### ab_test_large_sample

**Run 1**: {"correct_method": true, "rubric_scores": [true, true, true], "accuracy": 1.0, "false_positives": []}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Great setup — this is about as clean as causal inference gets. Based on what you've described, this is a **randomized experiment (A/B test)** with a large sample. You don't need any fancy quasi-experimental machinery here.

Let me just confirm a couple of things before I save your plan:

---

**What you've told me so far:**
- ✅ **Objective**: Evaluation — did the new checkout flow increase conversion?
- ✅ **Treatment**: New checkout flow (randomly assigned)
- ✅ **Population**: 100,000 users (50k / 50k split)
- ✅ **Outcome**: Conversion rate
- ✅ **Assignment**: Random
- ✅ **Language**: Python

---

**Two quick questions before I finalize:**

1. **Post-treatment conditioning check**: Is your conversion rate measured on *all* 100k users, or only on users who reached a certain step in the checkout (e.g., "users who added to cart")? If the latter, I want to flag a potential trap before we proceed.

2. **Effect size intuition**: Do you expect the new flow to have a subtle or meaningful lift? This affects whether we'd recommend a simple t-test or add regression adjustment (e.g., CUPED) for variance reduction — which can meaningfully shorten the time to significance if the effect is small.

</details>

### multiple_viable_methods

**Run 1**: {"correct_method": true, "rubric_scores": [false, true, true], "accuracy": 0.6666666666666666, "false_positives": []}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Based on what you've described, this is a **Regression Discontinuity Design (RDD)** problem — specifically a **sharp RDD** where the 50-employee cutoff deterministically assigns treatment. The panel structure adds a useful bonus I'll get to.

But before anything else, there's a critical issue to flag.

---

## 🚨 Post-Treatment Conditioning Trap

**Which employee count are you using to classify businesses as treated vs. control?**

If you're using employee count *during or after* the policy, you have a post-treatment contamination problem. Here's why it matters:

The minimum wage increase likely caused some businesses to reduce headcount — perhaps deliberately to fall below 50 and escape the regulation, or as a cost-cutting response. If you classify a business as "control" because it has 45 employees *today*, but it had 55 employees *before the policy*, you've misclassified a treated unit into your control group.

**The fix**: Use pre-policy employee count (ideally the last observation before month 0) to assign treatment status. This freezes assignment before any behavioral response can contaminate it.

---

## Why RDD (Not DiD)

You have a clean threshold: businesses at 49 employees are structurally identical to businesses at 51 employees — same market, same pressures — except one gets the minimum wage shock and the other doesn't. That discontinuity is your identification. RDD exploits *that*, which is a stronger assumption than parallel trends.

DiD would compare 50+ vs. <50 businesses across all sizes, assuming they'd have evolved the same way absent the policy. That's a much larger leap — a restaurant chain with 200 employees is not a good counterfactual for a 30-person shop.

You'd use RDD primarily, and bring in the panel for variance reduction and pre-period validation.

---

## A Few Quick Questions to Finalize the Plan

**1. Manipulation risk**: Do businesses in your context have strong incentives to stay just below 50? (e.g., is the wage increase large? are there other regulatory thresholds at 50?) This will determine how seriously we need to take the density test.

**2. Fuzzy or sharp?**: Is the 50-employee rule strictly enforced? Or could some businesses above 50 have delayed compliance or gotten exemptions? If assignment isn't perfectly deterministic at the cutoff, we'd use fuzzy RDD (which looks a lot like IV).

**3. Variation near the cutoff**: Roughly how many businesses in your data fall within, say, ±10 employees of the threshold? RDD's power depends heavily on observations near the cutoff — a sparse region makes bandwidth selection tricky.

**4. Employee count over time**: Is the count measured monthly (so it fluctuates), or is it a baseline measure? If it varies, we need to decide which period anchors the running variable.

---

Once you answer these, I'll finalize the plan and save it. The method will almost certainly be **sharp RDD with panel structure** — but the devil is in the density of data near 50 and whether manipulation is a real concern in your context.

</details>

---

### no_randomization_rich_covariates

**Run 1**: {"correct_method": true, "rubric_scores": [true, true, true], "accuracy": 1.0, "false_positives": []}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Based on what you've described, this is a **matching / propensity score weighting / doubly-robust** problem — specifically, a selection-on-observables design. You have no randomization, no cutoff, no instrument, and no panel — but you do have rich pre-treatment covariates including **prior earnings**, which is the most important confounder in job training studies.

Let me ask a few questions to sharpen the plan.

---

**Q1 — Panel data?**
You mentioned "prior earnings" — is that a single snapshot (e.g., earnings in year *t-1*), or do you have multiple pre-treatment periods? If you have repeated observations per person over time, DiD could be stronger than matching.

**Q2 — Can you run an experiment?**
Is there any possibility of designing a randomized study going forward, or are you committed to this observational data?

**Q3 — What determines selection?**
You said participants self-selected. Do you know *why* people enrolled — e.g., were they unemployed, referred by a counselor, aware of the program? This matters for assessing whether your observables are likely sufficient to explain selection.

**Q4 — Objective?**
Is this a retrospective evaluation ("did the program work?") or informing a prospective decision ("should we scale it up / target it differently?")?

---

> ⚠️ **One concern to flag now**: Prior earnings is a powerful covariate — but job training self-selection is notorious for *selection on unobservables* (motivation, job search intensity, social networks). Even with rich demographics, the conditional independence assumption may be strained. This is one of the most-studied datasets in causal inference precisely because naive methods fail badly on it (see LaLonde 1986). I'll flag this prominently in the plan.

</details>

---

### post_treatment_conditioning

**Run 1**: {"correct_method": true, "rubric_scores": [true, true, true, true], "accuracy": 1.0, "false_positives": []}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## 🚨 Stop — Post-Treatment Conditioning Trap

Before anything else: **comparing openers vs non-openers will give you a biased estimate, and likely an inflated one.** Here's why this matters.

**Opening the email is caused by the email.** It's a behavior that happens *after* treatment, not before. People who open marketing emails are systematically different from those who don't — they're more engaged, more interested in your brand, more likely to buy *regardless* of the discount. When you compare these two groups, you're not measuring the email's effect. You're measuring the difference between highly-engaged and low-engagement customers, which was already there before you sent anything.

This is called **conditioning on a post-treatment variable** — and it's one of the most common mistakes in marketing analytics.

---

## The Right Comparison: Intent-to-Treat (ITT)

You have 100,000 customers who **received** the email. The valid causal question is:

> *What is the effect of* sending *the email on purchase amount?*

To answer this, you need a **control group** — customers who were eligible for the email but didn't receive it. Then you compare:

- **Treated**: received the email (all 100k, regardless of whether they opened it)
- **Control**: did not receive the email

This is the **Intent-to-Treat (ITT)** estimand, and it's what your business actually cares about: "Does sending this email campaign lift revenue?"

---

## A Few Questions to Find the Right Method

**Was there a holdout group** — customers who were randomly excluded from receiving the email?

- If **yes** → this becomes a clean **experiment (A/B test)**, and I can have you up and running in Python in minutes.
- If **no** → we need a quasi-experimental method (DiD, matching, or similar), and I'll need to understand your data structure.

Which is it?

</details>

---

### sharp_cutoff

**Run 1**: {"correct_method": true, "rubric_scores": [true, true, true], "accuracy": 1.0, "false_positives": []}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

This is a **Regression Discontinuity Design (RDD)** problem. Your university uses a hard GPA cutoff to assign scholarships — that's exactly the identification mechanism RDD exploits.

Here's the core intuition: students just below 3.5 and just above 3.5 are essentially similar people (similar ability, effort, background) — the only meaningful difference is who got the scholarship. That local comparison is what gives us causal identification, without any randomization needed.

Let me ask a few questions to sharpen the plan.

---

**1. Is the cutoff sharp or fuzzy?**

- **Sharp**: *Every* student at 3.5+ gets the scholarship, and *no one* below 3.5 does — no exceptions.
- **Fuzzy**: The scholarship probability jumps at 3.5, but there are exceptions (appeals, special cases, other criteria).

Which describes your university's policy?

---

**2. How is GPA measured and reported?**

Is it a continuous decimal (e.g., 3.4823) or rounded/reported in increments (e.g., 0.1 steps)? This matters because rounding can create artificial bunching at the cutoff — a red flag for manipulation.

---

**3. Do you have other student-level covariates?**

Things like demographics, family income, major, year of entry? These aren't required for RDD, but they help with:
- Verifying the cutoff is *the only* thing that discontinuously changes at 3.5
- Improving precision of the estimate

---

**4. What's the graduation outcome format?**

Binary (graduated / didn't graduate within N years)? Or time-to-graduation? And what's the typical graduation rate in your data — roughly what % graduate?

</details>

---

### single_treated_unit

**Run 1**: {"correct_method": true, "rubric_scores": [true, true, true], "accuracy": 1.0, "false_positives": []}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Based on what you've described, this is a **Synthetic Control (SCM)** problem. One treated unit (California), 49 potential donors, and a long pre-treatment window (40 quarters) — that's the textbook setup for synthetic control.

You *could* run DiD, but with a single treated unit, the parallel trends assumption is hard to defend — you'd essentially be picking one or a few control states arbitrarily. Synthetic control constructs a weighted composite of states that best matches California's pre-2020 employment trajectory, letting the data choose the comparison.

---

Let me ask a few questions to sharpen the plan:

**1. Business objective — evaluation or something else?**
I'm assuming this is *evaluation* (the law passed, you want to measure its effect retroactively). Is that right, or is this a prospective policy analysis?

**2. What's the exact outcome metric?**
Total employment? Employment rate? Employment in a specific sector (e.g., food service, retail) that's more likely to respond to minimum wage changes? Sector-level outcomes often give cleaner signals because aggregate employment can be swamped by other trends.

**3. How does 2020 interact with COVID for you?**
This is the elephant in the room. California's law took effect January 1, 2020 — and the pandemic hit in Q1/Q2 2020, causing an enormous employment shock across *all* states. That's a major threat to identification: the post-treatment period is contaminated by a concurrent event. Have you thought about how you want to handle this?

**4. Any staggered or partial rollout?**
California's minimum wage increases were actually phased in over several years (2016–2023 ramp-up). Is 2020 a specific threshold (e.g., the $13 → $14 jump), or the full policy? The exact treatment definition matters for pinpointing the discontinuity in the synthetic control fit.

</details>

---

### small_sample_panel

**Run 1**: {"correct_method": true, "rubric_scores": [true, true, true], "accuracy": 1.0, "false_positives": []}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Based on what you've described, this is a **Difference-in-Differences (DiD)** problem — classic two-group, single treatment date setup. You have the ideal ingredients: panel data, a clear treatment date, and untreated stores as your control group.

Let me ask a few quick questions to sharpen the plan.

---

**1. How were the 15 stores selected for the new layout?**

This is the most important question. For DiD to work, we need to believe that treated and control stores would have followed *similar revenue trends* had the layout never changed. If the 15 stores were chosen because they were already struggling (or already thriving), that parallel trends assumption gets shaky.

For example: were stores selected randomly, by a rollout schedule, by geography, or by some performance criterion?

---

**2. Do you have any concerns about spillovers?**

If treated and control stores are geographically close, customers might shift between them — a new layout at store A could pull customers away from store B. That would contaminate your control group.

---

**3. What's the nature of the revenue series — any strong seasonality?**

With 52 weeks of data you have 25 pre-treatment periods and 26 post-treatment periods. That's a solid setup. But if revenue has strong seasonal patterns (e.g., holidays, back-to-school), the model needs to account for that so we're not attributing a seasonal bounce to the layout change.

---

*(These three answers will determine whether we go with a simple 2x2 DiD, a TWFE model with controls, or whether there are any red flags to address before coding.)*

</details>

---

### staggered_rollout

**Run 1**: {"correct_method": true, "rubric_scores": [true, true, true], "accuracy": 1.0, "false_positives": []}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

This is a **staggered difference-in-differences (DiD)** problem — and an important detail: because your rollout happened in three waves, you'll need a **staggered DiD estimator**, not naive TWFE. I'll explain why that matters in a moment.

Let me ask a few questions to sharpen the plan.

---

**1. What's your outcome metric exactly?**

Weekly total sales revenue? Units sold? Average transaction value? This affects how you'll model seasonality and whether you need to log-transform.

---

**2. Do you have any stores that *never* received the new policy?**

The 250 stores from the June wave are the last to be treated — but were any stores left on the old policy entirely? Having a clean "never-treated" group strengthens identification considerably.

---

**3. What's your theory of how the policy affects sales?**

For example:
- Does a more generous return policy increase purchase confidence → higher sales?
- Or does it increase returns → net negative on revenue?

This matters for **anticipation effects** — did store managers or customers know the rollout was coming? If so, behavior may have shifted *before* the official start date, which violates a key DiD assumption.

---

**4. Any other changes that coincided with the waves?**

Promotions, store renovations, staff changes, regional pricing updates rolled out at the same time? Anything that happened to the January or March stores but not (yet) to others would be a serious threat.

---

**Why not naive TWFE?**

Just so you have the intuition now: in staggered designs, the classic two-way fixed effects estimator uses **already-treated units as controls** for later-treated units. If the policy effect varies across cohorts (e.g., January stores respond differently than June stores), this creates biased and potentially sign-reversed estimates. The modern fix — Callaway-Sant'Anna's `att_gt` in the `did` package — computes clean cohort-specific effects and then aggregates them properly.

</details>

---

### time_series_no_control

**Run 1**: {"correct_method": true, "rubric_scores": [true, true, true], "accuracy": 1.0, "false_positives": []}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Based on what you've described, this is an **interrupted time series** problem — the right tool is **CausalImpact** (Bayesian Structural Time Series).

Here's why it fits: you have one aggregate unit, a long pre-period (~23 months of daily data), a clean intervention date, and no control group. CausalImpact builds a counterfactual by extrapolating the pre-March trend forward, then compares it to what actually happened.

A few questions to sharpen the plan:

**1. Do you have any unaffected "control series"?**
CausalImpact works without one, but it gets substantially more accurate with related time series that *weren't* affected by the campaign — think: organic search impressions, traffic from a country where the ad didn't run, or a competitor's traffic if you have it. Do you have anything like that?

**2. Any other things that happened around March 1st?**
Concurrent events are the main threat here — a product launch, a PR spike, seasonality, a Google algorithm update. If something else changed at the same time, CausalImpact can't separate it from the campaign effect. Anything come to mind?

**3. What does your pre-period look like?**
Two years is great. But I want to flag one thing: if there were any **structural breaks or level shifts** in that pre-period (e.g., a big spike in mid-2025, or a sustained traffic drop), the model's counterfactual projection may be unreliable. Does the pre-period look relatively stable, or are there notable events baked in?

**4. What's the traffic pattern like?**
Strong weekly seasonality (weekday/weekend swings)? Clear upward trend over the 2 years? This affects how the model is configured.

These answers will let me finalize the plan and flag any validity concerns before you run a line of code.

</details>

---

### weak_instrument

**Run 1**: {"correct_method": true, "rubric_scores": [true, true, true], "accuracy": 1.0, "false_positives": []}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Based on what you've described, this is a textbook **Instrumental Variables (IV)** problem — specifically an encouragement design.

Here's the structure:

| Role | Variable |
|---|---|
| **Instrument (Z)** | Email assignment (random ✅) |
| **Treatment (D)** | Actually using the savings feature |
| **Outcome (Y)** | Total deposits |

The email gives you a clean instrument because it was randomly assigned, but you're right not to just compare emailees vs. non-emailees — that gives you the **intent-to-treat (ITT)** effect of *the email*, not of *actually using the feature*.

What IV does: it uses the 25pp jump in usage rates (30% − 5%) to estimate the **LATE** — the effect of using the savings feature for **compliers** (customers who used it *because* they got the email). Your first stage looks strong, which is a great sign.

Before I save a plan, two things I want to flag and one question:

---

🚨 **Exclusion restriction — worth thinking through**: The instrument (email) must affect deposits *only through* feature usage. Could the email nudge deposits by other means — e.g., reminding customers about the app, increasing engagement broadly? If yes, the IV estimate would be biased upward. How confident are you the email's only pathway is the savings feature itself?

⚠️ **LATE ≠ ATE**: The effect is estimated only for compliers. If you care about the effect for *all* customers who might use the feature, that's a harder question.

---

**One question to refine the plan**: Do you have panel data (deposits before and after the email campaign), or is this a single cross-sectional snapshot post-campaign? Panel data would let us add a DiD layer to soak up individual-level baseline differences and tighten the estimates.

</details>

---

### audit_did_nonparallel

**Run 1**: {"violation_detected": true, "flags_coverage": 1.0, "severity_correct": false}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

# Audit Report: Inventory System DiD

**Date**: 2026-04-03
**Method**: Difference-in-Differences (TWFE, single treatment date)
**Overall assessment**: 🟡 **Yellow** — Three fixable gaps before trusting the estimate. The core specification is sound; the evidence isn't there yet to support the headline number.

---

## Quick orientation — what the analysis gets right

Before the critiques: the specification (`sales ~ treated*post + store_FE + time_FE`) is appropriate for a **simultaneous rollout** (all 50 stores treated at the same month). This is classic 2×2 DiD, so the Callaway-Sant'Anna / staggered-adoption concerns do *not* apply here. Clustering at the store level is also the right call. These are meaningful defaults to get right.

Now the problems.

---

## Category 1: Assumption Violations

Going through every assumption from the DiD checklist.

---

### 🔴 Serious — Parallel trends: untested

This is the assumption your entire estimate rests on, and you said it was **never formally tested**. A significant p-value doesn't help if the treated and control stores were already diverging before month 12.

**Why it matters**: If treated stores were on a different sales trajectory *before* the inventory system arrived, DiD will attribute that pre-existing gap to the treatment. Your 3.2 estimate absorbs both the causal effect and any trend difference.

**Diagnostic code (R)**:

```r
library(fixest)
library(dplyr)

# Create relative time variable (month 12 = treatment onset)
df <- df |>
  mutate(rel_time = month - 12)
# rel_time runs from -11 (pre) to +12 (post); reference = -1

# Event study: interact relative-time dummies with treated indicator
es <- feols(sales ~ i(rel_time, treated, ref = -1) | store + month,
            data = df, cluster = ~store)

# Visual check — pre-treatment coefficients should hover near zero
# with no trending pattern
iplot(es,
      main   = "Event Study: Pre-Trends Check",
      xlab   = "Months Relative to Treatment (Month 12)")
abline(h = 0, lty = 2, col = "red")

# Joint significance test of ALL pre-treatment coefficients
# H0: β_{-11} = β_{-10} = ... = β_{-2} = 0  (parallel trends)
pre_names <- grep("rel_time::-", names(coef(es)), value = TRUE)
wald(es, keep = pre_names)
```

**What to look for**: Pre-treatment coefficients clustered around zero with no clear trend. A statistically significant Wald test (H₀ rejected) is a red flag. A visual "ramp up" before month 12 in the treated group is equally damning.

**Suggested fix**: Run this before reporting any result. If pre-trends are clean: add the event study plot to the paper and note the Wald p-value. If pre-trends are diverging: the estimate is biased and you need either a different control group, covariate-adjusted DiD, or a different method.

---

### 🟡 Serious — No anticipation: untested

If stores knew the inventory system was coming (rollout announced in, say, month 9), they may have deliberately run down inventory or deferred sales before month 12. That behavioral response before the official start date would show up as a *decline* in the pre-treatment period for treated stores — making pre-treatment trends look artificially negative, and the post-treatment jump look artificially large.

**Why it matters**: Anticipation inflates the DiD estimate by contaminating the baseline. Your 3.2 could include the "snap back" from deliberate pre-treatment behavior, not just the inventory system's effect.

**Diagnostic code (R)**:

```r
# Look specifically at the coefficients at t = -2, t = -3
# from the same event study above
summary(es)

# If β_{-2} or β_{-3} are significantly negative, anticipation is likely.
# "Ramp down before month 12, then ramp back up" = anticipation pattern.

# Robustness: re-estimate with reference period shifted to t = -3
# (shrinks the window where anticipation could hide)
es_alt <- feols(sales ~ i(rel_time, treated, ref = -3) | store + month,
                data = df, cluster = ~store)
iplot(es_alt, main = "Event Study: Alternative Reference (t = -3)")
```

**Suggested fix**: Document when stores learned about the rollout. If the announcement preceded the implementation by multiple months, use the announcement date as the treatment date, or use the `anticipation` parameter in `did::att_gt`.

---

### ⚪ Minor — Stable composition: unverified

You have 100 stores × 24 months. If some stores closed or entered the sample mid-study — and this is correlated with treatment — the composition of your groups drifts over time and introduces survivorship bias.

**Diagnostic code (R)**:

```r
# Check whether all stores are observed in all 24 months
total_periods <- 24

panel_check <- df |>
  group_by(store) |>
  summarize(n_months    = n_distinct(month),
            treated_grp = first(treated),
            .groups     = "drop") |>
  group_by(treated_grp) |>
  summarize(n_stores         = n(),
            fully_observed   = sum(n_months == total_periods),
            pct_balanced     = mean(n_months == total_periods),
            min_months_seen  = min(n_months))

print(panel_check)

# Chi-square test: does attrition differ by group?
panel_check_unit <- df |>
  group_by(store) |>
  summarize(n_months = n_distinct(month),
            treated  = first(treated)) |>
  mutate(attrited = n_months < 24)

chisq.test(table(panel_check_unit$treated, panel_check_unit$attrited))
```

**Suggested fix**: If the panel is balanced (all 100 × 24 observed), this is a non-issue — confirm and move on. If it's unbalanced, restrict to a balanced panel for the main specification and report the unbalanced version as a robustness check.

---

### ⚪ Minor — No spillovers (SUTVA): plausibility depends on context

If treated and control stores share a customer base, a local supply chain, or regional advertising, the inventory improvement at treated stores could draw customers *away* from nearby control stores — making control stores look worse, inflating your DiD estimate. Alternatively, supply-chain improvements might spill over to nearby stores regardless of treatment status.

**Diagnostic (no clean test, but suggestive)**:

```r
# If you have geographic data: check whether control stores near
# treated stores behave differently post-treatment vs. distant controls

# Create a "proximity" indicator if store location is available:
# df <- df |> mutate(near_treated = ...)

# Among control stores only:
# feols(sales ~ post * near_treated | store + month,
#       data = df[df$treated == 0, ], cluster = ~store)
# A significant post:near_treated coefficient suggests spillover.
```

**Suggested fix**: If stores are geographically dispersed and sell non-competing goods, spillovers are unlikely — state this explicitly. If stores compete locally (e.g., retail chains in overlapping catchments), acknowledge the threat and try the geographic proximity test above.

---

## Category 2: Identification Threats

### 🔴 Serious — Treatment selection mechanism undocumented

How were the 50 treated stores chosen? This is arguably the **most important contextual question** in the audit, and the analysis summary doesn't address it.

**Why it matters**: Parallel trends only protects you against *time-varying* confounders. If the treated stores were systematically selected — e.g., underperforming stores got the new system as a rescue effort, or high-performing stores were piloted first — you have a level-difference in unobservables that parallel trends cannot fix, even if pre-period *trends* look identical.

**Scenarios and their implications**:
| Selection mechanism | Bias direction |
|---|---|
| Random / lottery assignment | No bias ✅ |
| High-performing stores first | Downward mean-reversion bias (estimate too large) |
| Struggling stores first | Upward mean-reversion bias (estimate too small or wrong sign) |
| Volunteer adoption | Selection on motivation (estimate for compliers only) |

**Diagnostic code (R)**:

```r
# Compare pre-treatment sales distribution by group
# Are treated stores systematically higher/lower sales beforehand?
pre_summary <- df |>
  filter(post == 0) |>
  group_by(store, treated) |>
  summarize(mean_sales = mean(sales), .groups = "drop")

# T-test: pre-treatment sales level differences
t.test(mean_sales ~ treated, data = pre_summary)

# Variance check: are treated stores more or less variable?
pre_summary |>
  group_by(treated) |>
  summarize(mean = mean(mean_sales),
            sd   = sd(mean_sales),
            p25  = quantile(mean_sales, 0.25),
            p75  = quantile(mean_sales, 0.75))
```

**Suggested fix**: Document the selection rule. If truly random or quasi-random (e.g., phased IT rollout by logistics region), state it explicitly. If based on performance criteria, consider a matching step before DiD (match on pre-treatment sales trajectory), or use a doubly-robust estimator.

---

## Category 3: Data Threats

### ⚪ Minor — Outcome scale not contextualized

The estimated ATT is **3.2 units of sales**. The pre-treatment sample shows stores with sales around 111–120. That's roughly a **2.7% lift** — meaningful in some contexts, noise in others. Without knowing the units of `sales` (dollars? units? thousands?), it's impossible to assess economic significance, and stakeholders may misread the headline number.

**Suggested fix**: Report the percentage effect alongside the level effect: `round(3.2 / mean_pre_treatment_sales * 100, 1)` and add units to the axis labels.

---

### ⚪ Minor — No check for measurement breaks at treatment date

Occasionally, new operational systems change *how* outcomes are recorded, not just the outcomes themselves. If the inventory system also changed how sales were logged (e.g., previously excluded returned goods, now includes them), the post-period "effect" could be a measurement artifact.

**Suggested fix**: Confirm with the data team that the `sales` variable is defined consistently across the pre- and post-treatment periods. Check if the data source or aggregation method changed at month 12.

---

## Category 4: Statistical Threats

### ⚪ Minor — Inference with 50 treated clusters is borderline

You have 100 stores as clusters (50 treated, 50 control). Conventional guidance is that cluster-robust SEs perform well with ≥ 50 clusters, so you're at the lower boundary. With fewer than ~30 clusters, cluster-robust SEs can under-reject. You're okay here, but worth a robustness check.

**Diagnostic code (R)**:

```r
library(fixest)

# Main model
main <- feols(sales ~ treated:post | store + month,
              data = df, cluster = ~store)
summary(main)

# Robustness: wild cluster bootstrap (more reliable with ~50 clusters)
# Requires the `fwildclusterboot` package
library(fwildclusterboot)

boot_res <- boottest(main,
                     clustid   = "store",
                     param     = "treated:post",
                     B         = 9999,
                     seed      = 42)
summary(boot_res)
# Compare p-values: if they diverge substantially, prefer the bootstrap
```

**Suggested fix**: If the wild bootstrap p-value is similar to your clustered SE p-value (both < 0.05), the inference is robust. If the bootstrap p-value is notably higher, report both and caveat.

---

### ⚪ Minor — No power analysis documented

With 50 treated stores and a 12-month pre-period, what was the minimum detectable effect (MDE)? Your result is significant, so ex-post power isn't the concern — but documenting the MDE strengthens the analysis by showing the test had reasonable sensitivity.

```r
# Rough MDE calculation for DiD
# Uses pwr package for a simple illustration
library(pwr)

# Estimate within-store sales SD from the pre-period
pre_sd <- df |>
  filter(post == 0) |>
  group_by(store) |>
  summarize(sd_sales = sd(sales)) |>
  pull(sd_sales) |>
  mean()

cat("Average within-store pre-treatment SD:", round(pre_sd, 2), "\n")
cat("Your effect size (Cohen's d approx):",
    round(3.2 / pre_sd, 3), "\n")
# Interpret: effect is X standard deviations of within-store sales variation
```

---

## Category 5: External Validity

### ⚪ Minor — 12-month post-period may capture novelty effects, not steady-state

Your post-treatment window is 12 months (months 13–24). If the sales lift at treated stores is partly driven by novelty (staff enthusiasm with the new system, temporary over-ordering to stock up) rather than lasting efficiency gains, the estimated ATT reflects a transient effect.

**Suggested fix**: Plot the event study post-treatment coefficients (from the code above). If the effect *fades* in months 10–12 post-treatment, the 3.2 estimate overstates the long-run effect. If it's stable or growing, that's reassuring.

---

### ⚪ Minor — ATT for this cohort of stores may not generalize to full rollout

You've estimated the ATT for these 50 specific stores. If the remaining stores differ in size, region, or operational maturity, the effect at full rollout could be higher or lower.

**Suggested fix**: Report the effect alongside a brief description of how the treated stores compare to the rest of the fleet on observable characteristics (size, pre-treatment sales, region). This makes the scope of the estimate transparent.

### audit_matching_post_treatment

**Run 1**: {"violation_detected": true, "flags_coverage": 1.0, "severity_correct": true}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

# Causal Audit: Job Training Program — Propensity Score Matching

**Date**: 2026-04-03
**Method audited**: Matching / PSM (ATT, 1:1 nearest neighbor)
**Overall assessment**: 🔴 **Red** — One fatal flaw invalidates the current estimates. Results should not be interpreted until it is fixed.

---

## Summary

The analysis contains a fatal post-treatment variable problem: `employment_status_6mo` is measured *after* treatment began, yet it is included in the propensity score model. This poisons every downstream result — the propensity scores, the matched pairs, the balance statistics, and the ATT estimate of $2,800. Two additional serious threats (unmeasured confounders, likely underestimated standard errors) also need attention, but the post-treatment variable must be fixed first.

---

## Findings

---

### 🔴 Fatal: Post-treatment variable in the propensity score model

**Category**: 1 — Assumption Violations / 2 — Identification Threats

**Explanation**

`employment_status_6mo` is measured 6 months *after the program started*. A propensity score model is supposed to capture *why someone selected into treatment before treatment happened*. By including a variable that is itself affected by the training program, you have introduced one or both of the following biases:

1. **Over-control / mediation bias**: If the program improves employment status, and employment status drives earnings, then conditioning on `employment_status_6mo` partially blocks the causal pathway you are trying to measure. You are matching treated and control units who look similar *because the treated ones were brought down to the control group's employment level* — artificially shrinking the estimated effect.

2. **Collider bias**: Even if `employment_status_6mo` isn't a mediator, there are almost certainly unmeasured variables (motivation, health, local job market shocks) that affect *both* employment status at 6 months *and* annual earnings. Conditioning on `employment_status_6mo` opens a backdoor path through those unmeasured variables, creating spurious correlation between treatment and outcome.

The direction and magnitude of the resulting bias is not recoverable without additional data. The reported ATT of $2,800 cannot be trusted as stated.

**A critical side-effect**: The SMD = 0.04 "perfect balance" result is computed on a contaminated propensity score. Balance on a wrong model is not evidence of a valid match — it is evidence of a well-executed invalid procedure.

**Diagnostic**

Rerun the analysis twice — once with `employment_status_6mo` included (current), once without (correct). If the estimates differ materially, the post-treatment variable is doing real damage.

```r
library(MatchIt)
library(cobalt)

# --- Correct spec: only pre-treatment covariates ---
m_correct <- matchit(
  treatment ~ age + education + prior_earnings + industry,
  data = df,
  method  = "nearest",
  distance = "glm",
  caliper = 0.2,
  estimand = "ATT"
)

# --- Contaminated spec: with post-treatment variable ---
m_contaminated <- matchit(
  treatment ~ age + education + prior_earnings +
              employment_status_6mo + industry,  # <-- problematic
  data = df,
  method  = "nearest",
  distance = "glm",
  caliper = 0.2,
  estimand = "ATT"
)

# Compare ATT estimates
md_correct     <- match.data(m_correct)
md_contaminated <- match.data(m_contaminated)

att_correct     <- lm(earnings ~ treatment + age + education +
                        prior_earnings + industry,
                      data = md_correct,
                      weights = weights)

att_contaminated <- lm(earnings ~ treatment + age + education +
                         prior_earnings + employment_status_6mo + industry,
                       data = md_contaminated,
                       weights = weights)

cat("ATT (correct spec):", coef(att_correct)["treatment"], "\n")
cat("ATT (contaminated): ", coef(att_contaminated)["treatment"], "\n")
cat("Difference:         ", abs(coef(att_correct)["treatment"] -
                                coef(att_contaminated)["treatment"]), "\n")
```

```python
# Python equivalent
from sklearn.linear_model import LogisticRegression
import pandas as pd, numpy as np

# Correct: only pre-treatment covariates
covs_correct      = ['age', 'education', 'prior_earnings', 'industry_enc']
# Contaminated: adds post-treatment variable
covs_contaminated = covs_correct + ['employment_status_6mo']

def estimate_att(df, covariates):
    X = df[covariates]
    D = df['treatment']
    ps = LogisticRegression(max_iter=1000).fit(X, D).predict_proba(X)[:, 1]
    df = df.copy()
    df['ps'] = ps
    # 1:1 nearest-neighbor match on treated units
    treated = df[D == 1].copy()
    control = df[D == 0].copy()
    matched_controls = []
    for _, t_row in treated.iterrows():
        diffs = np.abs(control['ps'] - t_row['ps'])
        best  = diffs.idxmin()
        matched_controls.append(control.loc[best])
        control = control.drop(best)          # no replacement
    matched = pd.concat([treated,
                         pd.DataFrame(matched_controls)], ignore_index=True)
    att = matched[matched['treatment']==1]['earnings'].mean() - \
          matched[matched['treatment']==0]['earnings'].mean()
    return att

att_c = estimate_att(df, covs_correct)
att_x = estimate_att(df, covs_contaminated)
print(f"ATT (correct spec):    ${att_c:,.0f}")
print(f"ATT (contaminated):    ${att_x:,.0f}")
print(f"Difference:            ${abs(att_c - att_x):,.0f}")
```

**Fix**: Remove `employment_status_6mo` from the propensity score model entirely. If employment status *before* the program is available (e.g., `employment_status_baseline`), that is a valid and valuable covariate — include it instead.

---

### 🟠 Serious: Unobserved confounders — unconfoundedness is not plausible on current covariates

**Category**: 1 — Assumption Violations

**Explanation**

Even after fixing the post-treatment variable, the covariates `{age, education, prior_earnings, industry}` likely leave important confounders unmeasured. Job training programs suffer from classic *self-selection*: people who enroll are systematically more motivated, more proactive about career advancement, and better-networked than similar non-enrollees with the same observable profile. None of those traits are in the model.

This means conditional independence — the assumption that (Y₀, Y₁) ⊥ D | X — is questionable even with a clean covariate set. The E-value tells you the minimum strength an unmeasured confounder would need to have to explain away the result.

**Diagnostic**

```r
library(EValue)

# Reported ATT = $2,800, SE = $600
# Convert to a standardized effect size for the E-value
# (approximate: assume earnings SD ~ $15,000)
sd_earnings <- 15000
d_cohen     <- 2800 / sd_earnings    # ≈ 0.187

# For continuous outcomes, convert Cohen's d to approximate risk ratio
# RR ≈ exp(0.91 * d) — a standard approximation
rr_est <- exp(0.91 * d_cohen)
rr_lo  <- exp(0.91 * (2800 - 1.96 * 600) / sd_earnings)

evalues.RR(est = rr_est, lo = rr_lo)
# E-value: minimum RR an unmeasured confounder must have with BOTH
# treatment and outcome to explain away the result.
# If this is small (< 1.5), a modest confounder is all it takes.
```

```r
# Coefficient stability test (Oster 2019)
# Large change as you add controls = unobservables likely matter too
model_sparse <- lm(earnings ~ treatment, data = df)
model_medium <- lm(earnings ~ treatment + age + education, data = df)
model_full   <- lm(earnings ~ treatment + age + education +
                     prior_earnings + industry, data = df)

cat("Coefficient on treatment:\n")
cat("  Sparse (no controls):", coef(model_sparse)["treatment"], "\n")
cat("  Medium controls:     ", coef(model_medium)["treatment"], "\n")
cat("  Full controls:       ", coef(model_full)["treatment"], "\n")
# If adding controls drops the coefficient dramatically, that's a red flag
```

**Fix**: Report the E-value alongside the ATT. If the E-value is low (< 1.5), be explicit that a weak confounder could overturn the result. Consider a sensitivity analysis using Rosenbaum bounds (`rbounds` in R). Where possible, add baseline motivation or prior job-seeking behavior as covariates.

---

### 🟠 Serious: Standard errors are likely underestimated

**Category**: 4 — Statistical Threats

**Explanation**

Standard errors for matching estimators are not straightforward. The SE = $600 reported here is almost certainly the OLS standard error from a regression on the matched sample — which ignores the uncertainty introduced by the matching step itself. This leads to confidence intervals that are too narrow and p-values that are too small. The Abadie-Imbens (2006) correction shows this bias can be substantial, especially with 1:1 nearest-neighbor matching.

**Diagnostic**

```r
library(MatchIt)

m_out <- matchit(
  treatment ~ age + education + prior_earnings + industry,
  data    = df,
  method  = "nearest",
  caliper = 0.2,
  estimand = "ATT"
)

# Option 1: Abadie-Imbens robust SE via marginal effects
library(marginaleffects)
fit  <- lm(earnings ~ treatment * (age + education + prior_earnings + industry),
           data    = match.data(m_out),
           weights = weights)

avg_comparisons(fit,
                variables = "treatment",
                vcov      = ~subclass,     # cluster on matched pairs
                newdata   = subset(match.data(m_out), treatment == 1),
                wts       = "weights")

# Option 2: Bootstrap SE — most conservative, most robust
set.seed(42)
boot_att <- replicate(500, {
  idx     <- sample(nrow(df), replace = TRUE)
  df_boot <- df[idx, ]
  m_boot  <- matchit(treatment ~ age + education + prior_earnings + industry,
                     data = df_boot, method = "nearest",
                     caliper = 0.2, estimand = "ATT")
  md_boot <- match.data(m_boot)
  coef(lm(earnings ~ treatment, data = md_boot, weights = weights))["treatment"]
})
cat("Bootstrap SE:", sd(boot_att), "\n")
cat("Bootstrap 95% CI:", quantile(boot_att, c(0.025, 0.975)), "\n")
```

```python
import numpy as np

# Bootstrap SE
np.random.seed(42)
boot_atts = []

for _ in range(500):
    df_boot = df.sample(n=len(df), replace=True).reset_index(drop=True)
    att = estimate_att(df_boot, covs_correct)   # reuse function from above
    boot_atts.append(att)

boot_se = np.std(boot_atts)
ci_lo, ci_hi = np.percentile(boot_atts, [2.5, 97.5])
print(f"Bootstrap SE: ${boot_se:,.0f}")
print(f"Bootstrap 95% CI: (${ci_lo:,.0f}, ${ci_hi:,.0f})")
```

**Fix**: Replace the naive OLS SE with either the clustered-on-matched-pairs SE (fast) or a bootstrap SE (most robust). If the bootstrap CI still excludes zero, the significance finding survives — but you need to check this.

---

### 🟡 Serious: SUTVA may not hold in local labor markets

**Category**: 2 — Identification Threats

**Explanation**

SUTVA requires that one person's treatment doesn't affect another's outcome. Job training programs frequently violate this in local labor markets: if a significant share of workers in a particular industry or region receives training, they compete for the same jobs — improving *their* earnings partly by displacing untrained workers. This is a displacement effect. If your control group is drawn from the same local labor market as your treated group, some of those "control" workers may have lower earnings *because* the trained workers took jobs they would have gotten.

The result: the ATT is overestimated because the control group's counterfactual earnings are suppressed.

**Diagnostic**

```r
# Suggestive test: among control units, does share-treated-in-same-industry
# predict lower earnings? (requires industry x region data)
control_df <- df[df$treatment == 0, ]

spillover_test <- lm(
  earnings ~ share_treated_same_industry + age + education +
             prior_earnings,
  data = control_df
)
summary(spillover_test)
# Negative, significant coefficient on share_treated_same_industry
# is consistent with displacement/SUTVA violation
```

**Fix**: If industry × region data exist, check whether control units in high-treated-density cells have lower earnings. If displacement is plausible, consider using geographically distant control units, or explicitly model the spillover.

---

### 🟡 Minor: Propensity score model may need more flexibility

**Category**: 1 — Assumption Violations (Correct Specification)

**Explanation**

With `prior_earnings` and `age` as continuous variables, a linear logistic regression may miss nonlinear relationships. For example, the selection into training might be hump-shaped in earnings (people who are doing okay but not great are most likely to seek retraining). If the propensity score model misses this, balance will be overstated.

The reported max SMD = 0.04 is a good sign *for the included covariates*, but it only reflects balance after matching on a possibly misspecified model. It is not evidence of balance on all relevant dimensions.

**Diagnostic**

```r
# Try a more flexible specification after fixing the post-treatment issue
m_flex <- matchit(
  treatment ~ age + I(age^2) + education + I(education^2) +
              prior_earnings + I(prior_earnings^2) +
              age:prior_earnings + industry,
  data     = df,
  method   = "nearest",
  caliper  = 0.2,
  estimand = "ATT"
)

bal_flex <- bal.tab(m_flex, thresholds = c(m = 0.1))
print(bal_flex)

# Compare ATT between linear and flexible specs
```

**Fix**: Run the flexible spec above and compare ATT estimates. If they are close, the linear model was adequate. If they diverge, use the flexible spec or switch to a doubly-robust estimator (AIPW via `DoubleML` or `AIPW` package in R), which is consistent even if the propensity score model is mildly misspecified.

---

### 🔵 Minor: External validity is limited to compliers and this labor market context

**Category**: 5 — External Validity

**Explanation**

The ATT estimates the effect for the *specific workers who chose to enroll* in this program, in this labor market, at this time. Three limits on generalizability are worth flagging explicitly:

1. **Population**: Results for voluntary enrollees may not hold if the program is made mandatory (the marginal enrollee is different from the average voluntary enrollee).
2. **Labor market context**: If the study was conducted during a strong labor market, estimated effects may be larger than in a recession.
3. **Scale**: Effects estimated on a small pilot may shrink under full rollout due to general equilibrium effects (displacing other workers, saturating demand for trained labor in a region).

**Fix**: Report the sample's characteristics (age distribution, industry mix, enrollment period) alongside the ATT so readers can assess applicability to other settings.

---

## Prioritized Recommendations

| Priority | Action |
|---|---|
| 🔴 **Do first** | Remove `employment_status_6mo` from the propensity score model. Rerun all matching, balance checks, and estimation from scratch. |
| 🟠 **Do second** | Replace SE = $600 with bootstrap SE (500 reps) or clustered-on-matched-pairs SE. Verify the result survives. |
| 🟠 **Do third** | Compute and report the E-value. If it's below 1.5, you need either stronger identification or a more cautious interpretation. |
| 🟡 **Do fourth** | Check for displacement effects among control units in the same industry. |
| 🔵 **Report** | Add an explicit external validity caveat (who these results apply to, and under what conditions). |

### did_clean_parallel

**Run 1**: {"violation_detected": true, "flags_coverage": 1.0, "severity_correct": true}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Reading your setup

**Single treatment date (month 12), two groups, panel data → Classic 2×2 DiD with TWFE.** No staggered timing concerns here, so standard `PanelOLS` with entity + time fixed effects is appropriate.

Let me walk through all 5 stages.

---

## Stage 1: Setup ✅

| Element | Value |
|---|---|
| Treatment | New inventory system, activated at month 12 |
| Outcome | `sales` |
| Design | 50 treated stores, 50 controls, 24 months |
| Variant | Classic TWFE (single treatment date) |
| Language | Python |

One thing to flag immediately about your data structure: with entity fixed effects, `treated` (time-invariant) will be **fully absorbed** — including it as a regressor causes perfect multicollinearity. The estimand lives entirely in the `treated × post` interaction. I'll build that below.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

### 1. Parallel Trends ⚠️ *Partially testable*

> You've done a visual check — good start. But "looks parallel" isn't a statistical statement. We'll run a formal joint test on pre-treatment event-study coefficients. This matters because if the treated stores were already on a different growth trajectory *before* month 12, the DiD estimate would attribute that pre-existing divergence to the inventory system.

### 2. No Anticipation 🔲 *Check needed*

> Did stores know about the rollout before month 12? If managers started adjusting orders in months 10–11, those periods are "contaminated." The event study below will reveal this as non-zero coefficients at `t = -2, -1`.

### 3. Stable Composition 🔲 *Check needed*

> Did any stores open or close during the study? If treated stores that struggled dropped out after rollout, you'd only see survivors — biasing the estimate upward.

### 4. No Spillovers 🔲 *Context-dependent*

> Do your stores compete for the same customers? A treated store improving inventory could draw customers away from nearby control stores, making the control group look worse — inflating your estimate.

### 5. Functional Form ✅ *Not a concern here*

> Single treatment date, one cohort → standard TWFE is unbiased. The Callaway-Sant'Anna correction is only needed for staggered rollouts.

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

```python
# ── 0. Dependencies ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# pip install linearmodels pandas numpy matplotlib scipy

import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
import matplotlib.ticker as ticker
from linearmodels.panel import PanelOLS
from scipy import stats

# ── 1. Data prep ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# df already has: store, month, treated, post, sales

# Interaction term (the DiD estimand)
df['treated_post'] = df['treated'] * df['post']

# Time-to-treatment (for event study); reference period = month 11 (t = -1)
TREAT_DATE = 12
df['time_to_treat'] = df['month'] - TREAT_DATE

# Panel index
df_panel = df.set_index(['store', 'month'])

# ── 2. Composition check ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
total_months = df['month'].nunique()
balance = (df.groupby('store')
             .agg(n_months=('month', 'nunique'),
                  treated=('treated', 'max'))
             .reset_index())
balance['attrited'] = balance['n_months'] < total_months

print("=== Panel Balance ===")
print(balance.groupby('treated')[['n_months', 'attrited']].agg(['mean', 'sum']))

attrition_table = pd.crosstab(balance['treated'], balance['attrited'])
if attrition_table.shape == (2, 2):
    chi2, p, _, _ = stats.chi2_contingency(attrition_table)
    print(f"\nDifferential attrition test: chi2={chi2:.3f}, p={p:.4f}")
    if p < 0.05:
        print("⚠️  Significant differential attrition — flag as caveat.")
    else:
        print("✅  No significant differential attrition.")

# ── 3. Main DiD estimate ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# NOTE: 'treated' (time-invariant) is intentionally excluded — EntityEffects
#       absorbs it. Only the interaction term is identified.

mod = PanelOLS.from_formula(
    'sales ~ treated_post + EntityEffects + TimeEffects',
    data=df_panel
)
res = mod.fit(cov_type='clustered', cluster_entity=True)
print("\n=== Main DiD Result ===")
print(res.summary.tables[1])  # coefficient table

att       = res.params['treated_post']
ci_low    = res.conf_int().loc['treated_post', 'lower']
ci_high   = res.conf_int().loc['treated_post', 'upper']
pval      = res.pvalues['treated_post']

print(f"\nATT  = {att:.3f}  (95% CI: {ci_low:.3f}, {ci_high:.3f})  p={pval:.4f}")

# ── 4. Event study ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Creates dummies for each period, drops t = -1 as reference

all_periods = sorted(df['time_to_treat'].unique())
ref_period  = -1
ttp_periods = [p for p in all_periods if p != ref_period]

for p in ttp_periods:
    df[f'ttp_{p}'] = (df['time_to_treat'] == p).astype(int)

ttp_cols  = [f'ttp_{p}' for p in ttp_periods]
es_formula = 'sales ~ ' + ' + '.join(ttp_cols) + ' + EntityEffects + TimeEffects'

df_es = df.set_index(['store', 'month'])
es_mod = PanelOLS.from_formula(es_formula, data=df_es)
es_res = es_mod.fit(cov_type='clustered', cluster_entity=True)

# Extract coefficients and CIs
es_coefs  = [es_res.params.get(f'ttp_{p}', 0.0) for p in ttp_periods]
es_lower  = [es_res.conf_int().loc[f'ttp_{p}', 'lower']
             if f'ttp_{p}' in es_res.params.index else 0.0 for p in ttp_periods]
es_upper  = [es_res.conf_int().loc[f'ttp_{p}', 'upper']
             if f'ttp_{p}' in es_res.params.index else 0.0 for p in ttp_periods]

# Insert the reference period (t = -1) as a zero point
plot_periods = sorted(ttp_periods + [ref_period])
coef_map  = dict(zip(ttp_periods, es_coefs))
lower_map = dict(zip(ttp_periods, es_lower))
upper_map = dict(zip(ttp_periods, es_upper))

plot_coefs  = [coef_map.get(p, 0.0) for p in plot_periods]
plot_lower  = [lower_map.get(p, 0.0) for p in plot_periods]
plot_upper  = [upper_map.get(p, 0.0) for p in plot_periods]

err_low  = np.array(plot_coefs) - np.array(plot_lower)
err_high = np.array(plot_upper) - np.array(plot_coefs)

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(11, 5))
ax.errorbar(plot_periods, plot_coefs,
            yerr=[err_low, err_high],
            fmt='o-', color='steelblue', capsize=4, linewidth=1.5,
            label='Event-study coefficient (ref: t = -1)')
ax.axhline(0, color='black', linewidth=0.8, linestyle='--')
ax.axvline(-0.5, color='red', linewidth=1.0, linestyle=':', label='Treatment onset')
ax.fill_betweenx([-999, 999], min(plot_periods), -0.5,
                 color='gray', alpha=0.07, label='Pre-treatment window')
ax.set_xlim(min(plot_periods) - 0.5, max(plot_periods) + 0.5)
ax.yaxis.set_major_formatter(ticker.FuncFormatter(lambda x, _: f'{x:,.1f}'))
ax.set_xlabel('Months relative to treatment (month 12)', fontsize=11)
ax.set_ylabel('Effect on sales', fontsize=11)
ax.set_title('Event Study: Inventory System Rollout', fontsize=13, fontweight='bold')
ax.legend(fontsize=9)
plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig('event_study.png', dpi=150)
plt.show()
print("Event study saved → event_study.png")

# ── 5. Formal pre-trends test ───────────────────────────────────────────────────
# H₀: all pre-treatment coefficients are jointly zero
pre_cols = [f'ttp_{p}' for p in ttp_periods if p < 0]
pre_coefs_vals  = np.array([es_res.params[c]   for c in pre_cols if c in es_res.params])
pre_coefs_se    = np.array([es_res.std_errors[c] for c in pre_cols if c in es_res.params])

# Simple joint F-test approximation (chi-squared / k)
if len(pre_coefs_vals) > 0:
    joint_stat = np.sum((pre_coefs_vals / pre_coefs_se) ** 2)
    joint_p    = 1 - stats.chi2.cdf(joint_stat, df=len(pre_coefs_vals))
    print(f"\n=== Pre-Trends Joint Test ===")
    print(f"Chi2({len(pre_coefs_vals)}) = {joint_stat:.3f},  p = {joint_p:.4f}")
    if joint_p > 0.10:
        print("✅  Fail to reject H₀ — pre-treatment trends are consistent with parallel.")
    elif joint_p > 0.05:
        print("⚠️  Marginal evidence against parallel trends (p < 0.10).")
    else:
        print("🚨  Reject H₀ — pre-treatment coefficients are jointly significant.")
        print("    Parallel trends assumption may be violated.")
```

---

## Stage 4: Robustness / Falsification

Two checks I recommend for your design:

```python
# ── Robustness A: Placebo treatment date ────────────────────────────────────────
# Re-run DiD using only pre-treatment data (months 1–11),
# with a fake treatment date at month 6.
# If the inventory system caused the effect, this should find nothing.

df_pre    = df[df['month'] <= 11].copy()
df_pre['post_placebo']    = (df_pre['month'] >= 6).astype(int)
df_pre['treated_placebo'] = df_pre['treated'] * df_pre['post_placebo']

placebo_mod = PanelOLS.from_formula(
    'sales ~ treated_placebo + EntityEffects + TimeEffects',
    data=df_pre.set_index(['store', 'month'])
)
placebo_res = placebo_mod.fit(cov_type='clustered', cluster_entity=True)

p_att  = placebo_res.params['treated_placebo']
p_ci_l = placebo_res.conf_int().loc['treated_placebo', 'lower']
p_ci_h = placebo_res.conf_int().loc['treated_placebo', 'upper']
p_pval = placebo_res.pvalues['treated_placebo']

print("=== Placebo DiD (fake date = month 6, pre-period only) ===")
print(f"Placebo ATT = {p_att:.3f}  (95% CI: {p_ci_l:.3f}, {p_ci_h:.3f})  p={p_pval:.4f}")

if p_pval > 0.10:
    print("✅  No significant placebo effect — supports causal interpretation.")
else:
    print("⚠️  Significant placebo effect — review pre-trends carefully.")

# ── Robustness B: Different clustering ─────────────────────────────────────────
# Default: cluster by store (entity). Also try no clustering (heteroskedastic-robust).
res_robust = mod.fit(cov_type='robust')
att_robust_ci = res_robust.conf_int().loc['treated_post']
print(f"\n=== Robustness: Heteroskedastic-robust SE ===")
print(f"ATT = {res_robust.params['treated_post']:.3f}  "
      f"(95% CI: {att_robust_ci['lower']:.3f}, {att_robust_ci['upper']:.3f})")
print("Compare width to clustered CIs above. Wider cluster CIs = within-store correlation is real.")
```

### did_nonparallel_trends

**Run 1**: {"violation_detected": true, "flags_coverage": 1.0, "severity_correct": false}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup ✓

Classic **2×2 DiD** (single treatment date, two groups). I'll use TWFE with store + month fixed effects.

One thing to flag right away before we touch code:

> ⚠️ **With store fixed effects, never include `treated` as a standalone regressor** — it's time-invariant and gets fully absorbed by store FE, causing perfect multicollinearity. `treated:post` (the DiD term) is fine because it *does* vary over time.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through each one for your setup.

| # | Assumption | Your context | Testable? |
|---|---|---|---|
| 1 | **Parallel trends** | Would untreated stores have followed the same sales trend as treated stores? | ✅ Partially (pre-trends test) |
| 2 | **No anticipation** | Did treated stores change ordering/stocking behavior *before* month 12? | ✅ (event study at t = −2, −3) |
| 3 | **Stable composition** | Are all 100 stores observed all 24 months, or do some drop out? | ✅ (balance check) |
| 4 | **No spillovers** | Could treated stores affect sales at nearby control stores (e.g., shared customers)? | ❌ Untestable directly |
| 5 | **Functional form** | Single treatment date → standard TWFE is unbiased (no staggered adoption concern) | N/A |

Assumptions 1 and 2 are the most critical. The code below tests them.

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

```r
# ── 0. Packages ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
library(tidyverse)
library(fixest)
library(modelsummary)

# ── 1. Load data ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# df <- read_csv("your_data.csv")

# Quick sanity checks
stopifnot(n_distinct(df$store) == 100)
stopifnot(n_distinct(df$month) == 24)

# ── 2. Panel balance check (Stable Composition) ────────────────────────────────
balance <- df |>
  group_by(store) |>
  summarise(
    n_months  = n_distinct(month),
    is_treated = max(treated)
  ) |>
  group_by(is_treated) |>
  summarise(
    n_stores        = n(),
    fully_observed  = sum(n_months == 24),
    mean_months     = mean(n_months)
  )

print(balance)
# 🎯 Both groups should show 24 months. Unbalanced panel → flag attrition.

# ── 3. Visual parallel trends (raw) ────────────────────────────────────────────
df |>
  group_by(month, treated) |>
  summarise(mean_sales = mean(sales), .groups = "drop") |>
  ggplot(aes(month, mean_sales, color = factor(treated), group = factor(treated))) +
  geom_line(linewidth = 1) +
  geom_vline(xintercept = 11.5, linetype = "dashed", color = "gray40") +
  scale_color_manual(values = c("0" = "#6baed6", "1" = "#fd8d3c"),
                     labels = c("Control", "Treated")) +
  labs(title = "Raw Sales Trends by Group",
       subtitle = "Dashed line = treatment onset (month 12)",
       x = "Month", y = "Mean Sales", color = NULL) +
  theme_minimal()

# ── 4. Event study (Parallel Trends + No Anticipation test) ────────────────────
df <- df |>
  mutate(time_to_treat = month - 12)   # reference period: month before = −1

es <- feols(
  sales ~ i(time_to_treat, treated, ref = -1) | store + month,
  data    = df,
  cluster = ~store
)

iplot(
  es,
  main = "Event Study: Sales Around Inventory System Rollout",
  xlab = "Months Relative to Treatment (−1 = reference)",
  ylab = "Estimated Effect on Sales",
  ref.line = TRUE
)
# 🎯 Pre-treatment coefficients (t = −11 to −2) should hover near zero.
# A rising pre-trend → parallel trends violated.
# Significant effect at t = −2, −3 → anticipation concern.

# Joint test: are ALL pre-treatment coefficients jointly zero?
# Extract pre-period coefficient names
pre_names <- grep("time_to_treat::-", names(coef(es)), value = TRUE)
wald(es, keep = pre_names)
# 🎯 p > 0.05 → no evidence against parallel trends

# ── 5. Main DiD estimate ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Note: `treated` alone is absorbed by store FE.
# `post` alone is absorbed by month FE.
# We only need the interaction.
model_did <- feols(
  sales ~ treated:post | store + month,
  data    = df,
  cluster = ~store
)

summary(model_did)

# Clean results table
modelsummary(
  model_did,
  stars      = c("*" = 0.1, "**" = 0.05, "***" = 0.01),
  coef_rename = c("treated:post" = "DiD Estimate (ATT)"),
  gof_map    = c("nobs", "r.squared", "FE: store", "FE: month"),
  title      = "Effect of Inventory System on Sales"
)
```

---

## Stage 4: Robustness

Two checks I recommend for your setup:

```r
# ── Robustness 1: Placebo date (pre-period only) ───────────────────────────────
# Keep only pre-treatment months (1–11), assign a fake treatment at month 6.
# If DiD finds an effect here, pre-trends are problematic.

df_pre <- df |>
  filter(month <= 11) |>
  mutate(
    post_placebo = if_else(month >= 6, 1L, 0L)
  )

model_placebo <- feols(
  sales ~ treated:post_placebo | store + month,
  data    = df_pre,
  cluster = ~store
)

summary(model_placebo)
# 🎯 Coefficient should be small and not statistically significant.

# ── Robustness 2: Varying the estimation window ────────────────────────────────
# Narrow to ±4 months around treatment to reduce contamination from distant periods.

model_narrow <- feols(
  sales ~ treated:post | store + month,
  data    = df |> filter(between(month, 8, 16)),
  cluster = ~store
)

modelsummary(
  list("Full window" = model_did, "Narrow ±4mo" = model_narrow),
  stars       = TRUE,
  coef_rename = c("treated:post" = "DiD Estimate (ATT)"),
  title       = "Robustness: Estimation Window"
)
# 🎯 Similar estimates across windows → results are not driven by distant periods.
```

### experiments_noncompliance

**Run 1**: {"violation_detected": true, "flags_coverage": 1.0, "severity_correct": true}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup ✓

From your description:

| Element | Value |
|---|---|
| **Treatment** | `assigned_treatment` (randomized) / `received_treatment` (actual) |
| **Outcome** | `outcome` (continuous) |
| **N** | 2,000 (1,000 per arm) |
| **Design** | Individual-level RCT, two-sided non-compliance |
| **Stage** | Data in hand → estimation |
| **Language** | Python |

Two-sided non-compliance means we have **two estimands** to choose from:
- **ITT** (intention-to-treat): effect of *being assigned* to treatment — always valid under randomization
- **CACE/LATE** (complier average causal effect): effect of *actually receiving* treatment — requires IV, applies only to compliers

We'll estimate both. Let's check the assumptions first.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

### 1. Random Assignment
> *Each unit had a known, non-zero probability of being assigned to either group, and no one could influence their assignment.*

**Testable — yes.** We'll run a balance table and an AUC test after you share what covariates exist. For now, the randomization was stated as genuine — so this is plausible, but always worth verifying.

**⚠️ Ask yourself**: Was the randomization list generated programmatically? Was there any manual override? Even well-designed RCTs sometimes have implementation failures.

---

### 2. SUTVA (No Interference)
> *One person's treatment assignment doesn't affect another person's outcome.*

**Not directly testable.** But it's important: could a treated participant share the treatment with a control participant (e.g., a product they received, information, a drug)?

**⚠️ Ask yourself**: Could treated units influence control units — through word of mouth, shared households, professional networks, marketplace effects?

---

### 3. Compliance ← *Your key concern*
> *Everyone assigned to treatment receives it; no one in control receives it.*

You've already flagged this is violated — so the question is **how badly**, and **in which direction**.

- **One-sided non-compliance**: Only some treated units fail to comply (clean control group)
- **Two-sided non-compliance**: Some treated don't comply *and* some controls receive treatment anyway ← your case

The ITT is still valid. The CACE/LATE is recoverable via IV **if monotonicity holds** (see assumption 5 below).

---

### 4. No Differential Attrition
> *People don't drop out at different rates depending on which group they're in.*

Your data has `outcome` for all rows in the sample shown — but we should verify there's no missing outcome in the full dataset.

---

### 5. Monotonicity *(required for CACE/LATE)*
> *No "defiers" — no one who would take treatment if assigned to control but refuse if assigned to treatment.*

**Not testable** — it's an assumption. For most interventions it's plausible (why would someone do the opposite of their assignment?), but worth thinking about explicitly.

**⚠️ Ask yourself**: Is there any reason someone would *only* take the treatment if told not to? If yes, CACE/LATE is not identified.

---

### Assumption summary

| Assumption | Status | Notes |
|---|---|---|
| Random assignment | ✅ Plausible | Verify with balance table if covariates available |
| SUTVA | ❓ Unknown | Depends on your context — discuss spillover risk |
| Compliance | ⚠️ Violated | Two-sided — ITT valid, CACE needs IV |
| No differential attrition | ❓ Check | Verify no missing outcomes by arm |
| Monotonicity | ❓ Assumed | Required for CACE; typically plausible |

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

```python
# ─────────────────────────────────────────────
# Packages
# ─────────────────────────────────────────────
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
from linearmodels.iv import IV2SLS
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

# ─────────────────────────────────────────────
# 0. Load data
# ─────────────────────────────────────────────
# df = pd.read_csv("your_data.csv")  # ← replace with your file

# ─────────────────────────────────────────────
# 1. Compliance diagnostics
# ─────────────────────────────────────────────
print("=" * 50)
print("COMPLIANCE DIAGNOSTICS")
print("=" * 50)

# Compliance table: assignment × actual receipt
compliance_table = pd.crosstab(
    df["assigned_treatment"],
    df["received_treatment"],
    rownames=["Assigned"],
    colnames=["Received"],
    margins=True
)
print("\nCompliance table:")
print(compliance_table)

# Compliance rate among assigned-to-treatment
comply_treat = df.loc[df["assigned_treatment"] == 1, "received_treatment"].mean()

# Contamination rate among assigned-to-control
contam_control = df.loc[df["assigned_treatment"] == 0, "received_treatment"].mean()

print(f"\nCompliance rate (treatment arm):   {comply_treat:.1%}")
print(f"Contamination rate (control arm):  {contam_control:.1%}")

# ─────────────────────────────────────────────
# 2. ITT — Effect of assignment (always valid)
# ─────────────────────────────────────────────
print("\n" + "=" * 50)
print("INTENTION-TO-TREAT (ITT)")
print("=" * 50)

itt_model = smf.ols("outcome ~ assigned_treatment", data=df).fit(
    cov_type="HC3"  # heteroskedasticity-robust SEs
)
print(itt_model.summary2().tables[1])

itt_est = itt_model.params["assigned_treatment"]
itt_ci  = itt_model.conf_int().loc["assigned_treatment"]
print(f"\nITT estimate: {itt_est:.4f}  (95% CI: {itt_ci[0]:.4f}, {itt_ci[1]:.4f})")

# ─────────────────────────────────────────────
# 3. CACE/LATE — Effect on compliers via IV
#    Instrument: assigned_treatment
#    Endogenous: received_treatment
# ─────────────────────────────────────────────
print("\n" + "=" * 50)
print("CACE / LATE  (IV — assignment instruments actual treatment)")
print("=" * 50)

iv_model = IV2SLS.from_formula(
    "outcome ~ 1 + [received_treatment ~ assigned_treatment]",
    data=df
)
iv_res = iv_model.fit(cov_type="robust")
print(iv_res.summary)

cace_est = iv_res.params["received_treatment"]
cace_ci  = iv_res.conf_int().loc["received_treatment"]
print(f"\nCACE estimate: {cace_est:.4f}  (95% CI: {cace_ci[0]:.4f}, {cace_ci[1]:.4f})")

# First-stage F-statistic (instrument strength check)
# Rule of thumb: F > 10 is acceptable, F > 100 is strong
print(f"\nFirst-stage F-statistic: {iv_res.first_stage.diagnostics['f.stat']:.4f}")
print("  → F > 10: instrument is acceptable")
print("  → F > 100: instrument is strong")

# ─────────────────────────────────────────────
# 4. Quick sanity check: CACE ≈ ITT / compliance_rate
# ─────────────────────────────────────────────
wald_approx = itt_est / (comply_treat - contam_control)
print(f"\nWald approximation (ITT / net compliance): {wald_approx:.4f}")
print(f"IV estimate:                                {cace_est:.4f}")
print("  → These should be very close.")

# ─────────────────────────────────────────────
# 5. Attrition check
# ─────────────────────────────────────────────
print("\n" + "=" * 50)
print("ATTRITION CHECK")
print("=" * 50)

df["attrited"] = df["outcome"].isna().astype(int)
attrition = (
    df.groupby("assigned_treatment")
    .agg(n=("attrited", "count"), n_attrited=("attrited", "sum"))
    .assign(attrition_rate=lambda x: x["n_attrited"] / x["n"])
)
print(attrition)

from scipy.stats import chi2_contingency
table = pd.crosstab(df["assigned_treatment"], df["attrited"])
chi2, p, _, _ = chi2_contingency(table)
print(f"\nDifferential attrition test: chi2 = {chi2:.4f}, p = {p:.4f}")
if p < 0.05:
    print("  🚨 Significant differential attrition — results may be biased.")
else:
    print("  ✅ No significant differential attrition.")
```

---

## Stage 4: Falsification & Robustness

Two checks I recommend here:

### Check 1 — Permutation / Randomization Inference
Randomly shuffle the assignment label many times and rebuild the ITT distribution. If your actual estimate is in the tail of the permutation distribution, that's strong evidence it's real — not a fluke.

```python
print("=" * 50)
print("ROBUSTNESS: PERMUTATION / RANDOMIZATION INFERENCE")
print("=" * 50)

np.random.seed(42)
n_permutations = 2000
perm_estimates = []

for _ in range(n_permutations):
    df_perm = df.copy()
    df_perm["assigned_treatment"] = np.random.permutation(
        df["assigned_treatment"].values
    )
    perm_model = smf.ols("outcome ~ assigned_treatment", data=df_perm).fit()
    perm_estimates.append(perm_model.params["assigned_treatment"])

perm_estimates = np.array(perm_estimates)
p_ri = np.mean(np.abs(perm_estimates) >= np.abs(itt_est))

print(f"Actual ITT estimate:          {itt_est:.4f}")
print(f"Permutation distribution mean: {perm_estimates.mean():.4f}")
print(f"Randomization inference p:    {p_ri:.4f}")

plt.figure(figsize=(8, 4))
plt.hist(perm_estimates, bins=50, color="steelblue", alpha=0.7, label="Permuted ITT")
plt.axvline(itt_est, color="crimson", linewidth=2, label=f"Actual ITT = {itt_est:.3f}")
plt.axvline(-itt_est, color="crimson", linewidth=2, linestyle="--")
plt.xlabel("Permuted ITT Estimate")
plt.ylabel("Count")
plt.title("Randomization Inference — Permutation Distribution")
plt.legend()
plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig("permutation_inference.png", dpi=150)
plt.show()
print("Plot saved to permutation_inference.png")
```

### Check 2 — Instrument Strength (First Stage)
This is implicit in the IV output above but worth isolating. A weak instrument (F < 10) inflates the CACE standard error and can cause severe bias in small samples.

---

## One thing to think about now

The CACE/LATE only applies to **compliers** — people who take the treatment when assigned to it and don't take it when not assigned. If your compliance rate is low (say, 40%), the CACE generalizes to a small, potentially unrepresentative subgroup. The ITT, in contrast, generalizes to the full population you randomized. **Neither is wrong — they answer different questions.** Which one matters more depends on what decision you're making with this experiment.

</details>
