### iv_strong_instrument

**Run 1**: {"violation_detected": true, "flags_coverage": 1.0, "severity_correct": true}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

This is a classic Angrist (1990) setup — one of the most cited IV designs in economics. Let me walk you through all five stages.

---

## Stage 1: Setup Confirmation

| Role | Variable |
|---|---|
| Instrument (Z) | `draft_number` |
| Endogenous treatment (D) | `served` |
| Outcome (Y) | `earnings` |
| Covariates | None mentioned |

**One design question before we code**: `draft_number` is continuous (likely 1–365). There are two common approaches:

1. **Use it directly** as a continuous instrument — more power, but assumes a linear first-stage relationship
2. **Create a binary eligibility indicator** (`eligible = draft_number <= cutoff`) — the classic Angrist approach, more interpretable, but you need to know the cutoff used by the draft board

Do you know the cutoff? If not, we'll proceed with the continuous version and I'll flag both options in the code.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let's walk through each one for your specific context.

### ✅ Relevance — *Testable*
> Does draft lottery number predict military service?

The draft lottery was explicitly designed to determine who got called up — lower numbers were called first. We'd expect a **negative** first-stage relationship: lower `draft_number` → higher probability of `served`. This is testable and we expect a strong first stage. F-statistic target: **> 10** (ideally > 100).

### ⚠️ Exclusion Restriction — *Not directly testable*
> Does `draft_number` affect `earnings` **only** through `served`?

This is the hardest assumption in IV — always is. For the draft lottery, the classic argument is: your lottery number was randomly drawn from a bowl, so it couldn't directly change your earning potential. The number itself has no economic meaning — only its effect on whether you served matters.

**Potential violations to consider**:
- Could men with low numbers have preemptively invested in education to avoid service? (anticipation effects)
- Could draft-eligible men have experienced psychological stress affecting productivity even if they didn't serve?

These are generally considered minor in the literature. Do any of these seem plausible in your context?

### ✅ Independence — *Partially testable*
> Is `draft_number` as good as randomly assigned?

Yes — it literally was. The Vietnam-era draft lottery used randomized capsules. This is one of the cleanest instruments in economics. We'll still check balance on any available covariates (you have none, so we'll note this).

### ✅ Monotonicity — *Not testable, but very plausible*
> Does a lower draft number push everyone toward service — no "defiers"?

Almost certainly holds. There's no plausible story for someone *less* likely to serve because of a low draft number. One-sided non-compliance is the realistic worry (some with low numbers avoided service), which is exactly the fuzzy IV / LATE setup.

**Summary before we proceed:**

| Assumption | Status | Notes |
|---|---|---|
| Relevance | 🟡 Pending F-test | Strong prior, will confirm |
| Exclusion | 🟢 Plausible | Lottery is random; no direct earnings channel |
| Independence | 🟢 Strong | Literal randomization |
| Monotonicity | 🟢 Plausible | No defier story exists |

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

```r
# ── Packages ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
library(fixest)
library(modelsummary)

# ── Load your data ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# df <- read.csv("your_data.csv")  # adjust path

# ── [Optional] Binary eligibility instrument ──────────────────────────────────
# If you know the cutoff (e.g., 195 for Vietnam-era draft):
# df$eligible <- as.integer(df$draft_number <= 195)
# Then replace `draft_number` with `eligible` throughout.

# ── Stage 1: First stage ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Does draft_number predict military service?
# Expect: negative coefficient (lower number → more likely to serve)

first_stage <- feols(served ~ draft_number, data = df)
summary(first_stage)

# Extract first-stage F-statistic (the key diagnostic)
fs_f <- fitstat(first_stage, "f")$f$stat
cat("\n--- First-Stage F-statistic:", round(fs_f, 2), "---\n")
cat("Rule of thumb: F > 10 (Stock-Yogo); F > 100 (Lee et al. 2022)\n\n")

# ── Stage 2: Naive OLS (biased benchmark) ─────────────────────────────────────
# OLS likely understates the true effect: men who served may have had
# lower earnings potential even before service (negative selection into service)
ols_model <- feols(earnings ~ served, data = df)

# ── Stage 3: IV / 2SLS ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# fixest syntax: outcome ~ exogenous controls | endogenous ~ instrument
iv_model <- feols(earnings ~ 1 | served ~ draft_number, data = df)
summary(iv_model)

# ── Compare OLS vs. IV ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
modelsummary(
  list("OLS (biased)" = ols_model, "IV / 2SLS" = iv_model),
  stars   = TRUE,
  gof_map = c("nobs", "r.squared"),
  title   = "Effect of Military Service on Earnings"
)
```

**What to look for in the output:**
- **First-stage coefficient on `draft_number`**: Should be negative and significant
- **First-stage F**: If < 10, we have a weak instrument problem and need to address it before trusting the IV estimate
- **IV vs. OLS**: Classic finding in this literature is OLS is *downward* biased (negative selection into service), so IV > OLS

---

## Stage 4: Falsification & Robustness

```r
# ── Check 1: Reduced form ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Regress earnings directly on draft_number — skips the treatment entirely.
# If the instrument is valid and relevant, this should show a significant effect.
# The IV estimate ≈ reduced-form coefficient / first-stage coefficient.

reduced_form <- feols(earnings ~ draft_number, data = df)
summary(reduced_form)
cat("\nReduced-form interpretation: effect of draft_number on earnings\n")
cat("Should be: same sign as (IV estimate × first-stage coef)\n")

# ── Check 2: Sensitivity — does direction make sense? ─────────────────────────
# The IV estimate should be interpretable. Compare magnitudes:
iv_coef    <- coef(iv_model)["fit_served"]
ols_coef   <- coef(ols_model)["served"]
rf_coef    <- coef(reduced_form)["draft_number"]
fs_coef    <- coef(first_stage)["draft_number"]

cat("\n--- Sanity checks ---\n")
cat("OLS estimate:           ", round(ols_coef, 2), "\n")
cat("IV estimate (LATE):     ", round(iv_coef, 2), "\n")
cat("Reduced form / FS coef: ", round(rf_coef / fs_coef, 2),
    "  (should ≈ IV estimate)\n")

# ── Check 3: First-stage direction ────────────────────────────────────────────
# Confirm the expected negative relationship
cat("\n--- First-stage coefficient on draft_number:", 
    round(coef(first_stage)["draft_number"], 4), "---\n")
cat("Expected: negative (lower number → more likely to serve)\n")
```

> "The IV estimate of the effect of military service on earnings is **[X]** (95% CI: [lower, upper]). The naive OLS estimate was **[Y]** — the gap suggests [upward/downward] selection bias: men who served tended to have [higher/lower] earnings potential even before service.

It does not capture the effect for always-takers (volunteered regardless) or never-takers (avoided service regardless of number). If treatment effects are heterogeneous, this LATE may differ from the population ATE.

> **First-stage F = [F]**: [Strong / ⚠️ Weak] instrument."

### iv_weak_instrument

**Run 1**: {"violation_detected": true, "flags_coverage": 1.0, "severity_correct": false}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Great setup — this is a classic Card (1995) design. Let me walk you through the full IV analysis.

---

## Stage 1: Setup ✓

Here's what I have:

| Element | Variable |
|---|---|
| **Instrument (Z)** | `distance` (distance to college) |
| **Endogenous treatment (D)** | `education` (years of schooling) |
| **Outcome (Y)** | `earnings` |
| **Covariates** | None mentioned yet |
| **Language** | Python |

Before writing code, let's check the assumptions.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

### 1. Relevance (Strong First Stage) — *Testable*
**What it means here**: Does living farther from college actually reduce years of schooling? We need this relationship to be strong — a first-stage F-statistic well above 10 (ideally above 100).

**Why it matters**: A weak instrument biases your IV estimate *toward* OLS — defeating the entire purpose. If distance barely predicts education, the 2SLS estimate is unreliable and your confidence intervals are meaningless.

**Status**: We'll test this directly in Stage 3.

---

### 2. Exclusion Restriction — *Untestable*
**What it means here**: Distance to college affects earnings *only* through its effect on education — no direct path.

**The classic threat**: Distance to college is often correlated with **urban/rural location**, which affects earnings directly (urban wage premiums, local labor market quality, family income, etc.). Someone far from a college may earn less not because they got less education, but because they live in a rural area with fewer high-paying jobs.

> **❓ Can you think of any way `distance` might affect `earnings` other than through `education`?** For example: does your sample include any controls for urban/rural status, family background, or region?

Adding geographic controls (urban indicator, region FEs) can help soak up direct channels — but doesn't fully fix a violated exclusion restriction.

---

### 3. Independence (As-if Random) — *Partially testable*
**What it means here**: Distance to college should be uncorrelated with unobserved determinants of earnings (ability, family income, local labor market conditions). Families don't strategically locate near colleges in anticipation of their children's earnings potential — at least, not fully.

**Realistic concern**: Wealthier, more educated families tend to cluster in urban areas near colleges. If family background is unobserved, the instrument may be endogenous.

> **❓ Do you have any pre-treatment covariates** (family income, parental education, urban/rural, race) we could use to check balance and absorb some of this?

---

### 4. Monotonicity — *Largely untestable*
**What it means here**: Distance to college pushes everyone in the same direction — greater distance weakly *reduces* education for everyone. There are no "defiers" who pursue *more* education when farther from college.

**Plausibility**: High. It's hard to imagine distance *increasing* schooling for anyone. ✓

---

### Assumption Summary

| Assumption | Testable? | Status |
|---|---|---|
| Relevance | ✅ Yes | TBD — check F-stat |
| Exclusion Restriction | ❌ No | ⚠️ Urban/rural threat — needs argument |
| Independence | Partially | ⚠️ Wealth clustering concern |
| Monotonicity | Partially | ✅ Plausible |

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
from linearmodels.iv import IV2SLS
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

# ── 0. Load your data ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# df = pd.read_csv("your_data.csv")  # ← replace with your actual path

# ── 1. Exploratory: scatter of instrument vs treatment ───────────────────────
fig, axes = plt.subplots(1, 2, figsize=(12, 4))

axes[0].scatter(df['distance'], df['education'], alpha=0.3, s=20)
axes[0].set_xlabel("Distance to college")
axes[0].set_ylabel("Years of education")
axes[0].set_title("First stage: Z → D")

axes[1].scatter(df['distance'], df['earnings'], alpha=0.3, s=20)
axes[1].set_xlabel("Distance to college")
axes[1].set_ylabel("Earnings")
axes[1].set_title("Reduced form: Z → Y")

plt.tight_layout()
plt.show()

# ── 2. Naive OLS (biased baseline) ──────────────────────────────────────────
ols = smf.ols('earnings ~ education', data=df).fit()
print("=" * 55)
print("NAIVE OLS (likely upward-biased — ability confounding)")
print("=" * 55)
print(f"  Education coef : {ols.params['education']:>10.4f}")
print(f"  Std error      : {ols.bse['education']:>10.4f}")
print(f"  95% CI         : [{ols.conf_int().loc['education', 0]:.4f}, "
      f"{ols.conf_int().loc['education', 1]:.4f}]")
print(f"  R²             : {ols.rsquared:>10.4f}")

# ── 3. First stage: check instrument relevance ───────────────────────────────
first_stage = smf.ols('education ~ distance', data=df).fit()
print("\n" + "=" * 55)
print("FIRST STAGE: education ~ distance")
print("=" * 55)
print(f"  Distance coef  : {first_stage.params['distance']:>10.4f}")
print(f"  Std error      : {first_stage.bse['distance']:>10.4f}")
print(f"  p-value        : {first_stage.pvalues['distance']:>10.4f}")
print(f"  F-statistic    : {first_stage.fvalue:>10.2f}")
print(f"  R²             : {first_stage.rsquared:>10.4f}")

# ── Instrument strength check ────────────────────────────────────────────────
f_stat = first_stage.fvalue
if f_stat >= 100:
    strength = "✅ Strong instrument (F ≥ 100)"
elif f_stat >= 10:
    strength = "⚠️  Borderline — some weak-instrument bias possible (10 ≤ F < 100)"
else:
    strength = "🚨 WEAK INSTRUMENT — 2SLS estimates are unreliable (F < 10)"
print(f"\n  Assessment: {strength}")

# ── 4. 2SLS estimation ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────
iv = IV2SLS.from_formula(
    'earnings ~ 1 + [education ~ distance]',
    data=df
).fit(cov_type='robust')

print("\n" + "=" * 55)
print("2SLS / IV ESTIMATE: earnings ~ education")
print("(Instrument: distance to college)")
print("=" * 55)
print(iv.summary.tables[1])

# Extract key numbers
iv_coef = iv.params['education']
iv_se   = iv.std_errors['education']
iv_ci   = iv.conf_int().loc['education']
print(f"\n  IV  estimate   : {iv_coef:>10.4f}")
print(f"  Std error      : {iv_se:>10.4f}")
print(f"  95% CI         : [{iv_ci.iloc[0]:.4f}, {iv_ci.iloc[1]:.4f}]")

# ── 5. OLS vs IV comparison ──────────────────────────────────────────────────
print("\n" + "=" * 55)
print("COMPARISON: OLS vs IV")
print("=" * 55)
print(f"  OLS estimate   : {ols.params['education']:>10.4f}")
print(f"  IV  estimate   : {iv_coef:>10.4f}")
diff = iv_coef - ols.params['education']
direction = "OLS > IV → OLS likely upward-biased (ability confounding)" \
    if diff < 0 else "IV > OLS → consider other explanations"
print(f"  Difference     : {diff:>10.4f}  ({direction})")
```

---

## Stage 4: Falsification / Robustness

```python
# ── 6. Reduced form ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# The reduced form bypasses education entirely: Z → Y directly.
# If the instrument is valid and relevant, this should be significant.
# The ratio (reduced form) / (first stage) ≈ IV estimate — a useful sanity check.

reduced_form = smf.ols('earnings ~ distance', data=df).fit()
print("=" * 55)
print("REDUCED FORM: earnings ~ distance")
print("(Direct effect of instrument on outcome)")
print("=" * 55)
print(f"  Distance coef  : {reduced_form.params['distance']:>10.4f}")
print(f"  Std error      : {reduced_form.bse['distance']:>10.4f}")
print(f"  p-value        : {reduced_form.pvalues['distance']:>10.4f}")

rf_coef = reduced_form.params['distance']
fs_coef = first_stage.params['distance']
wald_ratio = rf_coef / fs_coef
print(f"\n  Wald ratio (RF/FS) : {wald_ratio:.4f}")
print(f"  IV estimate        : {iv_coef:.4f}")
print(f"  Match? (should be close): {'✅' if abs(wald_ratio - iv_coef) < 1 else '⚠️ Check'}")

# ── 7. Sensitivity to functional form ────────────────────────────────────────
# Log earnings is more standard in labor economics
df['log_earnings'] = np.log(df['earnings'])

iv_log = IV2SLS.from_formula(
    'log_earnings ~ 1 + [education ~ distance]',
    data=df
).fit(cov_type='robust')

iv_log_coef = iv_log.params['education']
iv_log_ci   = iv_log.conf_int().loc['education']
print("\n" + "=" * 55)
print("ROBUSTNESS: log(earnings) as outcome")
print("=" * 55)
print(f"  IV estimate (log Y) : {iv_log_coef:.4f}")
print(f"  95% CI              : [{iv_log_ci.iloc[0]:.4f}, {iv_log_ci.iloc[1]:.4f}]")
print(f"  Interpretation      : ~{iv_log_coef*100:.1f}% return per year of education")
```

## Stage 5: Interpretation (fill in after running)

Once you run the code, here's the template to interpret your results:

> *"The IV estimate of the effect of education on earnings is **[coef]** (95% CI: [lower, upper]). For comparison, the naive OLS estimate was **[OLS coef]** — the downward difference suggests OLS was upward-biased, consistent with unobserved ability confounding both education and earnings.*
>
> *The first-stage F-statistic is **[F]** — [strong/weak].*
>
> **LATE interpretation**: This estimate is not the average return to education for the full population. It applies only to **compliers** — individuals who got more education because they happened to live closer to a college, but would have gotten less education if they had lived farther away. These are likely individuals on the margin of attending college, for whom distance was a meaningful constraint.*
>
> *Caveats: The exclusion restriction requires that distance affects earnings only through education. The main threat is that distance proxies for urban/rural location, which directly affects earnings through local labor market conditions. Without controls for geography, this assumption is difficult to defend strongly.*"

---

**Run the code and share the output** — especially the first-stage F-stat — and I'll give you a complete, specific interpretation. That F-stat is the single most important number here.

</details>

---

### matching_good_overlap

**Run 1**: {"violation_detected": true, "flags_coverage": 1.0, "severity_correct": true}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup Confirmed

You're estimating the **ATE** (average treatment effect on *everyone*) with treatment assigned based on `x1` and `x2`. One thing to flag upfront before we write a single line of code:

> ⚠️ **ATT vs ATE with PSM**: Nearest-neighbor PSM naturally estimates the **ATT** (effect on the *treated*), because it finds controls *for* treated units. For the **ATE**, you need to match in *both directions* — treated → controls AND controls → treated — or use **IPW**, which is more efficient and purpose-built for ATE. I'll implement both so you can compare.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let's walk through each one for your context.

### 1. Conditional Independence (CIA) — *Untestable, most critical*

> "After conditioning on `x1` and `x2`, is treatment assignment independent of potential outcomes?"

You said treatment was assigned *based on* `x1` and `x2` — that's promising. **But**: if there's any other variable that drove who got treated AND affects `outcome`, CIA fails and no matching method can save you.

**Ask yourself**: Is there anything beyond `x1` and `x2` that predicted who got treated? Individual motivation? Time of assignment? Geographic factors?

### 2. Overlap / Positivity — *Testable* ✅

Every unit must have a nonzero probability of being treated or untreated given their covariates. We'll inspect this first before running anything else.

### 3. SUTVA — *Contextually assessed*

Does one person's treatment affect another person's outcome? (e.g., network effects, market spillovers?) Tell me if that's a concern in your setting.

### 4. Correct Specification — *Partially testable via balance* ✅

We'll check this via **standardized mean differences (SMDs)** after matching. If SMDs are below 0.1, the model was adequate for confounding removal — regardless of formal fit statistics.

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

Here's the full analysis. It runs the **overlap check first** — if that's broken, the estimates aren't trustworthy.

```python
import numpy as np
import pandas as pd
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
import matplotlib.gridspec as gridspec
from sklearn.linear_model import LogisticRegression
from sklearn.neighbors import NearestNeighbors
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
import warnings
warnings.filterwarnings("ignore")

# ── 0. Load data ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# df = pd.read_csv("your_data.csv")  # replace with your actual load
covariates = ["x1", "x2"]

# ── 1. Estimate propensity scores ─────────────────────────────────────────────
ps_model = LogisticRegression(max_iter=1000, random_state=42)
ps_model.fit(df[covariates], df["treatment"])
df["ps"] = ps_model.predict_proba(df[covariates])[:, 1]

print("=== Propensity Score Summary ===")
print(df.groupby("treatment")["ps"].describe().round(4))

# ── 2. Overlap check (DO THIS BEFORE ESTIMATION) ─────────────────────────────
fig, axes = plt.subplots(1, 2, figsize=(12, 4))

# Histogram overlap
axes[0].hist(df.loc[df["treatment"] == 1, "ps"], bins=40,
             alpha=0.5, label="Treated", color="steelblue", density=True)
axes[0].hist(df.loc[df["treatment"] == 0, "ps"], bins=40,
             alpha=0.5, label="Control", color="tomato", density=True)
axes[0].set_xlabel("Propensity Score")
axes[0].set_ylabel("Density")
axes[0].set_title("PS Overlap: Treated vs. Control")
axes[0].legend()

# Jitter plot (shows individual units)
for t, color, label in [(1, "steelblue", "Treated"), (0, "tomato", "Control")]:
    subset = df[df["treatment"] == t]
    jitter = np.random.uniform(-0.1, 0.1, len(subset))
    axes[1].scatter(subset["ps"], np.full(len(subset), t) + jitter,
                    alpha=0.3, color=color, s=10, label=label)
axes[1].set_yticks([0, 1])
axes[1].set_yticklabels(["Control", "Treated"])
axes[1].set_xlabel("Propensity Score")
axes[1].set_title("PS Distribution by Unit")
axes[1].legend()

plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig("overlap_check.png", dpi=150)
plt.show()

# Quantitative overlap flags
frac_extreme = (df["ps"] < 0.05).mean() + (df["ps"] > 0.95).mean()
print(f"\nFraction of units with extreme PS (< 0.05 or > 0.95): {frac_extreme:.3f}")
if frac_extreme > 0.1:
    print("⚠️  WARNING: >10% of units have extreme propensity scores. "
          "Overlap may be insufficient — inspect the histogram carefully.")

# ── 3a. IPW for ATE (recommended for ATE) ────────────────────────────────────
# ATE weights: 1/ps for treated, 1/(1-ps) for controls
# Trimming at [0.01, 0.99] to avoid explosive weights
df["ps_trimmed"] = df["ps"].clip(0.01, 0.99)
df["ipw_ate"] = np.where(
    df["treatment"] == 1,
    1 / df["ps_trimmed"],           # treated weight
    1 / (1 - df["ps_trimmed"])      # control weight
)

# Normalize weights within each group (stabilized IPW)
df.loc[df["treatment"] == 1, "ipw_ate"] /= df.loc[df["treatment"] == 1, "ipw_ate"].mean()
df.loc[df["treatment"] == 0, "ipw_ate"] /= df.loc[df["treatment"] == 0, "ipw_ate"].mean()

# Weighted regression for ATE
model_ipw = smf.wls("outcome ~ treatment", data=df, weights=df["ipw_ate"]).fit(
    cov_type="HC3"   # heteroskedasticity-robust SEs
)
ate_ipw = model_ipw.params["treatment"]
ci_ipw  = model_ipw.conf_int().loc["treatment"]
print(f"\n=== ATE via IPW ===")
print(f"  Estimate : {ate_ipw:.4f}")
print(f"  95% CI   : [{ci_ipw[0]:.4f}, {ci_ipw[1]:.4f}]")
print(f"  p-value  : {model_ipw.pvalues['treatment']:.4f}")

# ── 3b. PSM for ATT (as a reference / comparison) ────────────────────────────
# Nearest-neighbor 1:1 matching WITHOUT replacement
treated_idx   = df.index[df["treatment"] == 1].tolist()
control_idx   = df.index[df["treatment"] == 0].tolist()

nn = NearestNeighbors(n_neighbors=1, algorithm="ball_tree")
nn.fit(df.loc[control_idx, ["ps"]])
distances, indices = nn.kneighbors(df.loc[treated_idx, ["ps"]])

matched_control_idx = [control_idx[i[0]] for i in indices]

matched_df = pd.concat([
    df.loc[treated_idx].assign(matched_group="treated"),
    df.loc[matched_control_idx].assign(matched_group="control")
])

# ATT: simple difference-in-means on matched sample
att_psm = (matched_df[matched_df["treatment"] == 1]["outcome"].values
           - matched_df[matched_df["treatment"] == 0]["outcome"].values).mean()

# Bootstrap CI for ATT
np.random.seed(42)
n_treated = len(treated_idx)
boot_atts = []
for _ in range(1000):
    samp_idx = np.random.choice(n_treated, n_treated, replace=True)
    diffs = (matched_df[matched_df["treatment"] == 1]["outcome"].values[samp_idx]
             - matched_df[matched_df["treatment"] == 0]["outcome"].values[samp_idx])
    boot_atts.append(diffs.mean())
att_ci = np.percentile(boot_atts, [2.5, 97.5])

print(f"\n=== ATT via PSM (nearest neighbor, 1:1) ===")
print(f"  Estimate : {att_psm:.4f}")
print(f"  95% CI   : [{att_ci[0]:.4f}, {att_ci[1]:.4f}]")

# ── 4. Balance diagnostics ────────────────────────────────────────────────────
def compute_smd(data, treatment_col, covs, weight_col=None):
    rows = []
    for cov in covs:
        t_vals = data.loc[data[treatment_col] == 1, cov]
        c_vals = data.loc[data[treatment_col] == 0, cov]
        pooled_sd = np.sqrt((t_vals.var() + c_vals.var()) / 2)
        if pooled_sd == 0:
            continue
        if weight_col:
            w = data.loc[data[treatment_col] == 0, weight_col]
            c_mean_w = np.average(c_vals, weights=w)
            smd = (t_vals.mean() - c_mean_w) / pooled_sd
        else:
            smd = (t_vals.mean() - c_vals.mean()) / pooled_sd
        rows.append({"covariate": cov, "SMD": smd})
    return pd.DataFrame(rows)

balance_raw     = compute_smd(df, "treatment", covariates)
balance_ipw     = compute_smd(df, "treatment", covariates, weight_col="ipw_ate")
balance_matched = compute_smd(matched_df, "treatment", covariates)

balance_summary = (balance_raw.set_index("covariate")
                   .rename(columns={"SMD": "SMD_raw"})
                   .join(balance_ipw.set_index("covariate").rename(columns={"SMD": "SMD_IPW"}))
                   .join(balance_matched.set_index("covariate").rename(columns={"SMD": "SMD_PSM"})))
print("\n=== Covariate Balance (Standardized Mean Differences) ===")
print("  Target: |SMD| < 0.10 after adjustment")
print(balance_summary.round(4))

# Love plot
fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(8, 4))
y_pos = np.arange(len(covariates))
ax.scatter(balance_summary["SMD_raw"].abs(), y_pos - 0.2,
           color="gray", s=60, label="Unadjusted", zorder=3)
ax.scatter(balance_summary["SMD_IPW"].abs(), y_pos,
           color="steelblue", marker="D", s=60, label="IPW (ATE)", zorder=3)
ax.scatter(balance_summary["SMD_PSM"].abs(), y_pos + 0.2,
           color="darkorange", marker="s", s=60, label="PSM (ATT)", zorder=3)
ax.axvline(0.10, color="red", linestyle="--", linewidth=1, label="SMD = 0.10 threshold")
ax.axvline(0.25, color="red", linestyle=":", linewidth=1, label="SMD = 0.25 threshold")
ax.set_yticks(y_pos)
ax.set_yticklabels(covariates)
ax.set_xlabel("Absolute SMD")
ax.set_title("Love Plot: Covariate Balance")
ax.legend(fontsize=9)
ax.grid(axis="x", alpha=0.3)
plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig("love_plot.png", dpi=150)
plt.show()

# Warn on residual imbalance
bad_ipw = balance_summary[balance_summary["SMD_IPW"].abs() > 0.1]
if not bad_ipw.empty:
    print(f"\n⚠️  Residual imbalance after IPW (|SMD| > 0.10): {bad_ipw.index.tolist()}")
    print("   Consider adding polynomial or interaction terms to the PS model.")
```

---

## Stage 4: Robustness Checks

Two checks that matter most here:

### Check 1 — Propensity Score Trimming
If ATE changes substantially after trimming units with extreme PS, the overlap assumption is driving your results.

```python
# ── Robustness: ATE under stricter trimming ───────────────────────────────────
for trim in [0.05, 0.10, 0.15]:
    df_trim = df[(df["ps"] >= trim) & (df["ps"] <= (1 - trim))].copy()
    df_trim["ps_clip"] = df_trim["ps"].clip(trim, 1 - trim)
    df_trim["w_ate"] = np.where(
        df_trim["treatment"] == 1,
        1 / df_trim["ps_clip"],
        1 / (1 - df_trim["ps_clip"])
    )
    df_trim.loc[df_trim["treatment"] == 1, "w_ate"] /= df_trim.loc[df_trim["treatment"] == 1, "w_ate"].mean()
    df_trim.loc[df_trim["treatment"] == 0, "w_ate"] /= df_trim.loc[df_trim["treatment"] == 0, "w_ate"].mean()

    m = smf.wls("outcome ~ treatment", data=df_trim, weights=df_trim["w_ate"]).fit(cov_type="HC3")
    n_dropped = len(df) - len(df_trim)
    print(f"Trim [{trim:.2f}, {1-trim:.2f}] | n_dropped={n_dropped:3d} | "
          f"ATE={m.params['treatment']:.4f}  "
          f"CI=[{m.conf_int().loc['treatment',0]:.4f}, {m.conf_int().loc['treatment',1]:.4f}]")
```

### Check 2 — Alternative PS Model (add nonlinearity)
If adding `x1²`, `x2²`, or `x1·x2` to the PS model changes the ATE, the logistic regression was misspecified.

```python
# ── Robustness: nonlinear PS model ───────────────────────────────────────────
from sklearn.preprocessing import PolynomialFeatures

poly = PolynomialFeatures(degree=2, include_bias=False)
X_poly = poly.fit_transform(df[covariates])

ps_model_nl = LogisticRegression(max_iter=1000, random_state=42)
ps_model_nl.fit(X_poly, df["treatment"])
df["ps_nl"] = ps_model_nl.predict_proba(X_poly)[:, 1]

df["ps_nl_clip"] = df["ps_nl"].clip(0.01, 0.99)
df["w_nl"] = np.where(df["treatment"] == 1,
                      1 / df["ps_nl_clip"],
                      1 / (1 - df["ps_nl_clip"]))
df.loc[df["treatment"] == 1, "w_nl"] /= df.loc[df["treatment"] == 1, "w_nl"].mean()
df.loc[df["treatment"] == 0, "w_nl"] /= df.loc[df["treatment"] == 0, "w_nl"].mean()

m_nl = smf.wls("outcome ~ treatment", data=df, weights=df["w_nl"]).fit(cov_type="HC3")
print(f"\nATE (nonlinear PS): {m_nl.params['treatment']:.4f}  "
      f"CI=[{m_nl.conf_int().loc['treatment',0]:.4f}, "
      f"{m_nl.conf_int().loc['treatment',1]:.4f}]")
print(f"ATE (linear PS):    {ate_ipw:.4f}  — delta: {abs(m_nl.params['treatment'] - ate_ipw):.4f}")
```

Once you run this, paste your output and I'll help write the full interpretation. It will follow this structure:

> "The estimated ATE using IPW is **[X]** (95% CI: [lower, upper]). This means that, on average across all 1,000 individuals, receiving treatment is associated with a **[X]-unit change** in outcome. Covariate balance after weighting: [all SMDs < 0.10 / or flag residual imbalance]. This estimate is credible *only if* `x1` and `x2` capture all variables that drove treatment assignment. [Sensitivity finding from trimming check]."

**One key reminder**: Even with perfect balance, CIA is the load-bearing assumption here — it cannot be tested. The E-value (how strong an unobserved confounder would need to be to explain away your result) is worth computing once you have your estimate.

### matching_poor_overlap

**Run 1**: {"violation_detected": true, "flags_coverage": 1.0, "severity_correct": false}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1 — Setup Confirmed

Here's what I'm working with before writing a single line of code:

| Element | Value |
|---|---|
| **Treatment** | Training program (`treated`) |
| **Outcome** | `productivity` |
| **Covariates** | `age` (continuous), `tenure` (continuous), `department` (categorical → needs dummies) |
| **Assignment mechanism** | Self-selection |
| **Estimand** | ATT — *effect on participants* — since self-selection makes ATT the natural question ("did the program help the people who chose it?") |
| **Language** | Python |
| **Method** | Doubly-robust (primary) + IPW (secondary sanity check) |

⚠️ **One immediate flag**: `department` is stored as `int64` but is categorical. Feeding it as a raw number into a propensity score model would imply dept 3 is "between" dept 2 and dept 4 — meaningless. The code below one-hot encodes it.

---

## Stage 2 — Assumptions

Walk through these honestly before trusting any estimate.

### 1. Conditional Independence (CIA) — *untestable, most important*

**What it means here**: After controlling for age, tenure, and department, participants and non-participants would have had the *same* productivity trajectory without the program. In other words, you've captured every reason why someone chose to enroll.

**Is it plausible?** Almost certainly **partially violated**. Self-selection into a training program is usually driven by:
- Prior performance (high performers vs. low performers may select differently)
- Manager encouragement (a proxy for tenure/performance not in your data)
- Motivation / growth mindset (entirely unobserved)

Three covariates is a thin shield against self-selection. This doesn't make the analysis useless, but you should be honest about it.

👉 **Action**: After estimation, run a sensitivity analysis — how large would unmeasured confounding need to be to explain away the result?

---

### 2. Overlap / Positivity — *testable*

**What it means here**: For every combination of age, tenure, and department, there are both trained and untrained employees. If, say, senior employees in dept 2 *always* enroll, there's no comparable control for them.

The overlap histogram in the code below is your first diagnostic. **Stop and flag if you see separated distributions.**

---

### 3. SUTVA — *plausible but worth thinking about*

**What it means here**: One employee's participation doesn't affect another's productivity. This is probably fine unless:
- Trained employees share knowledge informally (positive spillover)
- Trained employees take on tasks from untrained colleagues (displacement)

If employees work in small teams where knowledge sharing is common, SUTVA is questionable.

---

### 4. Model Specification — *doubly-robust gives you a safety net*

**What it means here**: Either the propensity score model *or* the outcome model needs to be correctly specified — not both. That's the payoff of DR estimation. If your logistic regression for propensity scores is slightly wrong but your outcome model is right (or vice versa), you're still consistent.

Balance diagnostics (SMD, love plot) tell you whether the propensity score model was adequate.

---

**Assumption summary:**

| Assumption | Testable? | Status |
|---|---|---|
| CIA / Unconfoundedness | ❌ | ⚠️ Questionable — only 3 covariates vs. self-selection |
| Overlap | ✅ | Check histogram — unknown until you run it |
| SUTVA | ❌ | Probably fine; verify team knowledge-sharing norms |
| Model specification | Partially ✅ | DR gives a safety net; check balance SMDs |

---

## Stage 3 — Implementation

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
import warnings
warnings.filterwarnings("ignore")

from sklearn.linear_model import LogisticRegression
from sklearn.ensemble import GradientBoostingClassifier, GradientBoostingRegressor
from econml.dr import DRLearner
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf

# ── 0. Load data ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# df = pd.read_csv("your_data.csv")

# ── 1. Feature engineering ────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# department is categorical — one-hot encode, drop first to avoid collinearity
df_model = pd.get_dummies(df, columns=["department"], drop_first=True, dtype=float)

covariate_cols = (
    ["age", "tenure"]
    + [c for c in df_model.columns if c.startswith("department_")]
)

X = df_model[covariate_cols].values
D = df_model["treated"].values
Y = df_model["productivity"].values

print(f"Sample size  : {len(df_model)}")
print(f"Treated      : {D.sum()}  ({D.mean():.1%})")
print(f"Control      : {(1-D).sum()}  ({(1-D).mean():.1%})")
print(f"Covariates   : {covariate_cols}")

# ── 2. Propensity score estimation ────────────────────────────────────────────
ps_model = LogisticRegression(max_iter=1000)
ps_model.fit(X, D)
df_model["ps"] = ps_model.predict_proba(X)[:, 1]

roc_auc = __import__("sklearn.metrics", fromlist=["roc_auc_score"]).roc_auc_score(D, df_model["ps"])
print(f"\nPropensity model ROC-AUC: {roc_auc:.3f}")
if roc_auc > 0.9:
    print("  ⚠️  ROC-AUC > 0.90 — near-deterministic treatment. Check overlap carefully.")

# ── 3. Overlap check (run this BEFORE trusting estimates) ─────────────────────
fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(9, 5))
ax.hist(
    df_model.loc[df_model["treated"] == 1, "ps"],
    bins=40, alpha=0.6, label="Treated", density=True, color="steelblue"
)
ax.hist(
    df_model.loc[df_model["treated"] == 0, "ps"],
    bins=40, alpha=0.6, label="Control", density=True, color="salmon"
)
ax.set_xlabel("Propensity Score")
ax.set_ylabel("Density")
ax.set_title("Overlap: Propensity Score Distribution by Treatment Group")
ax.legend()
plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig("overlap.png", dpi=150)
plt.show()

print("\n── Propensity score ranges ──")
for grp, label in [(1, "Treated"), (0, "Control")]:
    ps_grp = df_model.loc[df_model["treated"] == grp, "ps"]
    print(f"  {label}: [{ps_grp.min():.3f}, {ps_grp.max():.3f}]  mean={ps_grp.mean():.3f}")

pct_extreme = (df_model["ps"] < 0.05).mean() + (df_model["ps"] > 0.95).mean()
print(f"  Fraction with PS < 0.05 or > 0.95: {pct_extreme:.3f}")
if pct_extreme > 0.10:
    print("  ⚠️  >10% of units have extreme propensity scores. Overlap is limited.")

# ── 4. IPW weights (ATT) ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# ATT weights: treated = 1, controls reweighted by ps/(1-ps)
df_model["ipw"] = np.where(
    df_model["treated"] == 1,
    1.0,
    df_model["ps"] / (1 - df_model["ps"])
)

# Stabilize: trim weights at 99th percentile of control weights
w_cap = np.percentile(df_model.loc[df_model["treated"] == 0, "ipw"], 99)
df_model["ipw_trimmed"] = df_model["ipw"].clip(upper=w_cap)
print(f"\nWeight cap (99th pct of control weights): {w_cap:.2f}")
print(f"Max trimmed weight: {df_model['ipw_trimmed'].max():.2f}")

# ── 5. Covariate balance diagnostics ─────────────────────────────────────────
def smd(treated_vals, control_vals, weights_control=None):
    """Standardized Mean Difference (positive = treated mean is higher)."""
    m_t = treated_vals.mean()
    s_pooled = np.sqrt(
        (treated_vals.var() + np.average(
            (control_vals - control_vals.mean()) ** 2,
            weights=weights_control
        )) / 2
    ) if weights_control is not None else treated_vals.std()
    if weights_control is not None:
        m_c = np.average(control_vals, weights=weights_control)
    else:
        m_c = control_vals.mean()
    return (m_t - m_c) / (s_pooled + 1e-8)

balance_rows = []
for cov in covariate_cols:
    t_vals = df_model.loc[df_model["treated"] == 1, cov].values
    c_vals = df_model.loc[df_model["treated"] == 0, cov].values
    c_wts  = df_model.loc[df_model["treated"] == 0, "ipw_trimmed"].values
    balance_rows.append({
        "covariate"   : cov,
        "SMD_before"  : smd(t_vals, c_vals),
        "SMD_after"   : smd(t_vals, c_vals, c_wts),
    })

bal = pd.DataFrame(balance_rows)
bal["balanced"] = bal["SMD_after"].abs() < 0.1

print("\n── Covariate Balance (|SMD|; target < 0.10) ──")
print(bal[["covariate", "SMD_before", "SMD_after", "balanced"]].to_string(index=False))

if not bal["balanced"].all():
    print("\n  ⚠️  Some SMDs remain above 0.10 after weighting.")
    print("     Consider adding interaction terms or using a flexible propensity model.")

# Love plot
fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(8, max(4, len(bal) * 0.6)))
y_pos = range(len(bal))
ax.scatter(bal["SMD_before"].abs(), y_pos, marker="o", s=60,
           label="Before weighting", color="salmon")
ax.scatter(bal["SMD_after"].abs(),  y_pos, marker="D", s=60,
           label="After IPW",        color="steelblue")
ax.axvline(0.1, color="gray", linestyle="--", alpha=0.7, label="SMD = 0.10")
ax.set_yticks(y_pos)
ax.set_yticklabels(bal["covariate"])
ax.set_xlabel("|Standardized Mean Difference|")
ax.set_title("Love Plot: Covariate Balance Before vs. After IPW")
ax.legend()
plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig("love_plot.png", dpi=150)
plt.show()

# ── 6. Doubly-Robust estimation (primary) ─────────────────────────────────────
# Consistent if EITHER propensity score OR outcome model is correct.
dr = DRLearner(
    model_propensity=GradientBoostingClassifier(n_estimators=200, random_state=42),
    model_regression=GradientBoostingRegressor(n_estimators=200, random_state=42),
    model_final=GradientBoostingRegressor(n_estimators=100, random_state=42),
    cv=5,
    random_state=42
)
dr.fit(Y=Y, T=D, X=X)

ate_point = dr.ate(X)
ate_lo, ate_hi = dr.ate_interval(X, alpha=0.05)
print("\n── Doubly-Robust ATE (primary estimate) ──")
print(f"  Point estimate : {ate_point:.3f}")
print(f"  95% CI         : [{ate_lo:.3f}, {ate_hi:.3f}]")

# ── 7. IPW weighted regression (secondary / sanity check) ─────────────────────
ipw_model = smf.wls(
    "productivity ~ treated",
    data=df_model,
    weights=df_model["ipw_trimmed"]
).fit(cov_type="HC3")

coef = ipw_model.params["treated"]
ci   = ipw_model.conf_int().loc["treated"]
print("\n── IPW ATT estimate (secondary) ──")
print(f"  Coefficient : {coef:.3f}")
print(f"  95% CI      : [{ci[0]:.3f}, {ci[1]:.3f}]")
print("\n  (DR and IPW estimates should be in the same ballpark.")
print("   A large divergence suggests model misspecification.)")
```

---

## Stage 4 — Robustness Checks

Run at least two of these before interpreting results:

```python
# ── Robustness 1: Propensity score trimming ───────────────────────────────────
# Drop units with extreme PS (outside [0.05, 0.95]) — poor common support
df_trim = df_model[(df_model["ps"] >= 0.05) & (df_model["ps"] <= 0.95)].copy()
print(f"Trimmed sample: {len(df_trim)} (dropped {len(df_model) - len(df_trim)})")

ipw_trim = smf.wls(
    "productivity ~ treated",
    data=df_trim,
    weights=df_trim["ipw_trimmed"]
).fit(cov_type="HC3")
print(f"Trimmed IPW estimate: {ipw_trim.params['treated']:.3f}  "
      f"CI: [{ipw_trim.conf_int().loc['treated', 0]:.3f}, "
      f"{ipw_trim.conf_int().loc['treated', 1]:.3f}]")
print("  If this differs meaningfully from the main estimate, overlap is driving results.")

# ── Robustness 2: Placebo outcome ─────────────────────────────────────────────
# Test: does the program "affect" a pre-treatment characteristic (age)?
# It SHOULD NOT. A significant result suggests residual confounding.
placebo_model = smf.wls(
    "age ~ treated",
    data=df_model,
    weights=df_model["ipw_trimmed"]
).fit(cov_type="HC3")
p_val = placebo_model.pvalues["treated"]
print(f"\nPlacebo outcome (age) — p-value: {p_val:.3f}")
if p_val < 0.05:
    print("  🚨 Significant 'effect' on age after weighting — residual imbalance detected.")
else:
    print("  ✅ No significant effect on placebo outcome — good sign.")

# ── Robustness 3: Vary propensity score flexibility ───────────────────────────
# Add interactions and squared terms; see if balance improves or estimate shifts
from sklearn.preprocessing import PolynomialFeatures

poly = PolynomialFeatures(degree=2, interaction_only=False, include_bias=False)
X_poly = poly.fit_transform(X)

ps_flex = LogisticRegression(max_iter=2000, C=0.5)
ps_flex.fit(X_poly, D)
df_model["ps_flex"] = ps_flex.predict_proba(X_poly)[:, 1]
df_model["ipw_flex"] = np.where(
    df_model["treated"] == 1, 1.0,
    df_model["ps_flex"] / (1 - df_model["ps_flex"])
)
w_cap_flex = np.percentile(df_model.loc[df_model["treated"]==0, "ipw_flex"], 99)
df_model["ipw_flex_trimmed"] = df_model["ipw_flex"].clip(upper=w_cap_flex)

ipw_flex = smf.wls(
    "productivity ~ treated",
    data=df_model,
    weights=df_model["ipw_flex_trimmed"]
).fit(cov_type="HC3")
print(f"\nFlexible PS estimate : {ipw_flex.params['treated']:.3f}  "
      f"CI: [{ipw_flex.conf_int().loc['treated', 0]:.3f}, "
      f"{ipw_flex.conf_int().loc['treated', 1]:.3f}]")
print("  Stable vs. main estimate → results not driven by propensity model form.")
```

---

## What to Watch For

| Signal | What it means |
|---|---|
| PS distributions barely overlap | The trained and untrained groups are too different to compare — treat results as unreliable |
| SMD > 0.25 after weighting | Serious imbalance — add polynomial/interaction terms to PS model or use CEM |
| DR and IPW estimates diverge by >50% | Both models may be misspecified |
| Placebo outcome is significant | Residual confounding is present |
| Trimming changes estimate dramatically | Results are overlap-sensitive — report both |

## Stage 1: Setup — Confirmed

This is a **Sharp RDD**:

| Element | Value |
|---|---|
| Running variable | `test_score` |
| Cutoff | 70 |
| Treatment | `scholarship` (deterministic: 1 if score ≥ 70) |
| Outcome | `gpa` |
| N | 2,000 students |
| Language | Python |

Before writing any code, let's walk through the assumptions — because they determine whether the estimate is credible, not just computable.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

### 1. No Manipulation of the Running Variable
**What it means here**: Could students have known the exact cutoff and intentionally scored just above 70? For example, teachers coaching borderline students, or score rounding practices.

- If yes → units just above and below 70 differ *systematically* in motivation/ability, not just by luck. The as-if-random assignment near the cutoff breaks down.
- You mentioned the distribution "looks smooth" — that's a good sign, but we'll formally verify with the **McCrary/rddensity test**.

> **Question**: Is there any institutional reason students could target the 70 cutoff — e.g., was the threshold publicly announced? Were scores rounded or adjusted?

---

### 2. Continuity of Potential Outcomes
**What it means here**: A student scoring 69.9 and one scoring 70.1 should have *similar potential GPAs* — the only difference is the scholarship. No other factor should jump at exactly 70.

- Testable by checking whether **pre-determined covariates** (things measured before the test, like demographics) are smooth through the cutoff.
- Your data only has `gpa` and `test_score` as non-ID columns — do you have any other pre-determined covariates (e.g., prior GPA, age, socioeconomic status)?

---

### 3. No Compound Treatments
**What it means here**: Is the scholarship the *only* thing that changes at the 70-point threshold? Or do other programs, honors tracks, or course placements also use this same cutoff?

> **Question**: Are there other policies tied to this exact score of 70?

---

### 4. Local Identification (interpretation caveat, not a violation)
**What it means**: The RDD estimate tells you the scholarship effect **for students near the 70-point cutoff** — not for students scoring 50 or 90. This is inherent to the design, not a flaw, but it shapes how you communicate results.

---

### Assumption Summary

| Assumption | Status | Action |
|---|---|---|
| No manipulation | ❓ Unverified | Run density test |
| Continuity (no compound treatment) | ❓ Unverified | Check other policies; run covariate tests if covariates exist |
| No other discontinuities at 70 | ❓ Contextual | Answer above question |
| Local identification | ✅ Acknowledged | Caveat in interpretation |

The first three are testable or context-dependent — I'll embed the diagnostics directly in the code below. While you think about those questions, here's the full implementation.

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

```python
# =============================================================
# Sharp RDD: Effect of scholarship on GPA
# Running variable: test_score | Cutoff: 70
# =============================================================

import numpy as np
import pandas as pd
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
from rdrobust import rdrobust, rdplot
from rddensity import rddensity, rdplotdensity

# --- 0. Load your data ---
# df = pd.read_csv("your_data.csv")
# Using the structure you shared:
# df columns: student_id, test_score, scholarship, gpa

Y = df["gpa"].values
X = df["test_score"].values
cutoff = 70

# =============================================================
# DIAGNOSTIC 1: Visual inspection — RD plot
# =============================================================
rdplot(Y, X, c=cutoff,
       title="RD Plot: Scholarship Effect on GPA",
       x_label="Test Score",
       y_label="GPA")
plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig("rd_plot.png", dpi=150)
plt.show()

# =============================================================
# DIAGNOSTIC 2: McCrary density test — check for manipulation
# =============================================================
# Tests whether the density of test_score has a jump at 70.
# A jump would suggest students are sorting themselves above the cutoff.
density_test = rddensity(X=X, c=cutoff)
print("\n=== McCrary Density Test ===")
print(density_test)

# Visual: density on both sides of the cutoff
fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(8, 5))
rdplotdensity(density_test, X,
              title="Density Test: Any Bunching at Score = 70?",
              xlabel="Test Score",
              ylabel="Density",
              ax=ax)
plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig("density_test.png", dpi=150)
plt.show()

# =============================================================
# MAIN ESTIMATE: Sharp RDD with bias-corrected robust inference
# =============================================================
# rdrobust uses MSE-optimal bandwidth selection by default (Calonico et al.)
# and reports bias-corrected, robust confidence intervals.
rd_main = rdrobust(Y, X, c=cutoff)
print("\n=== Main RD Estimate ===")
print(rd_main)

# =============================================================
# DIAGNOSTIC 3: Simple histogram — eyeball the distribution
# =============================================================
plt.figure(figsize=(8, 4))
plt.hist(X, bins=40, color="steelblue", edgecolor="white")
plt.axvline(x=cutoff, color="red", linestyle="--", linewidth=1.5, label="Cutoff (70)")
plt.xlabel("Test Score")
plt.ylabel("Count")
plt.title("Distribution of Test Scores")
plt.legend()
plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig("score_histogram.png", dpi=150)
plt.show()
```

**What to look for in the output:**

| Output | What it tells you |
|---|---|
| RD plot | Is there a visible jump in GPA at 70? |
| Density test p-value | > 0.05 → no evidence of manipulation ✅ |
| `rd_main` coefficient | The estimated scholarship effect on GPA |
| Bandwidth (`h`) | How wide a window rdrobust used around 70 |
| Robust 95% CI | Does it exclude zero? |

---

## Stage 4: Robustness Checks

Run these *after* verifying the main estimate ran cleanly:

```python
# =============================================================
# ROBUSTNESS 1: Bandwidth sensitivity
# Does the estimate hold at different bandwidths?
# =============================================================
opt_bw = rd_main.bws.iloc[0, 0]   # MSE-optimal bandwidth

bw_multipliers = [0.50, 0.75, 1.00, 1.25, 1.50]
print("\n=== Bandwidth Sensitivity ===")
print(f"{'Multiplier':<12} {'Bandwidth':<12} {'Estimate':<12} {'95% CI'}")
print("-" * 55)

for m in bw_multipliers:
    bw = opt_bw * m
    rd_bw = rdrobust(Y, X, c=cutoff, h=bw)
    coef  = rd_bw.coef.iloc[0, 0]
    ci_lo = rd_bw.ci.iloc[2, 0]   # robust CI lower
    ci_hi = rd_bw.ci.iloc[2, 1]   # robust CI upper
    label = "← optimal" if m == 1.00 else ""
    print(f"{m:<12.2f} {bw:<12.2f} {coef:<12.4f} [{ci_lo:.4f}, {ci_hi:.4f}] {label}")

# =============================================================
# ROBUSTNESS 2: Placebo cutoffs
# Run the RD at scores where NO treatment change occurs.
# Finding an "effect" at a placebo → model misspecification.
# Only test on the side away from the real cutoff.
# =============================================================
placebo_cutoffs = [55, 60, 65,   # left side
                   75, 80, 85]   # right side

print("\n=== Placebo Cutoff Tests ===")
print(f"{'Cutoff':<10} {'Estimate':<12} {'p-value (robust)'}")
print("-" * 40)

for c_placebo in placebo_cutoffs:
    try:
        rd_p = rdrobust(Y, X, c=c_placebo)
        coef   = rd_p.coef.iloc[0, 0]
        pval   = rd_p.pv.iloc[2, 0]   # robust p-value
        flag   = " ⚠️ p < 0.1" if pval < 0.1 else ""
        print(f"{c_placebo:<10} {coef:<12.4f} {pval:.4f}{flag}")
    except Exception as e:
        print(f"{c_placebo:<10} Error: {e}")

# =============================================================
# ROBUSTNESS 3: Donut hole test
# Exclude students very close to the cutoff (±1 point).
# If the estimate survives, minor bunching near 70 isn't driving results.
# =============================================================
donut = 1.0   # exclude ±1 point around cutoff
mask  = np.abs(X - cutoff) > donut

rd_donut = rdrobust(Y[mask], X[mask], c=cutoff)
print("\n=== Donut Hole Test (excluding ±1 point around cutoff) ===")
print(rd_donut)
```

## RDD Analysis: Scholarship Effect on GPA

Great setup — this is a **sharp RDD**. The scholarship is deterministic at the cutoff (score ≥ 70 = treated), so students just below and just above 70 are your comparison group.

Let me walk you through all five stages.

---

## Stage 1: Setup ✅

| Element | Value |
|---|---|
| Running variable | `test_score` |
| Cutoff | 70 |
| Treatment | `scholarship` (sharp — deterministic at cutoff) |
| Outcome | `gpa` |
| N | 2,000 students |
| Variant | Sharp RDD |

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Here are the four assumptions for your specific design, and what to watch for:

### 1. No Manipulation of the Running Variable 🔍
**What it means here**: Can students influence their test score to land just above 70? If students who scored 68–69 knew the cutoff and could retake the exam or get teachers to bump their grade, those near-cutoff students are *not* comparable. We'd expect a suspicious spike of students just above 70.

**Testable**: Yes — McCrary density test. Code below.

---

### 2. Continuity of Potential Outcomes 🔍
**What it means here**: If we removed the scholarship entirely, would GPA trend smoothly through the score-70 threshold? In other words, is there anything *else* that happens at score = 70 besides the scholarship?

**Partially testable**: Check whether pre-determined covariates (e.g., demographics, prior GPA) are smooth at the cutoff.

> **Question for you**: Do you have any pre-determined covariates in your dataset (e.g., student age, gender, prior academic record, family income)? If so, we should test them. If not, we proceed with the density test as our main diagnostic.

---

### 3. No Compound Treatments 🔍
**What it means here**: Does *only* the scholarship change at score = 70? For example, if students above 70 also get placed in an honors track, or become eligible for a different program, you'd be estimating the combined effect — not just the scholarship.

> **Question for you**: Is there anything else that uses this same 70-point cutoff (honors classes, dormitory eligibility, tuition discounts)?

---

### 4. Local Identification ⚠️ (inherent limitation)
**What it means here**: Your estimate will tell you the scholarship's effect *for students near the 70-point threshold* — not for the bottom or top of the score distribution. A student scoring 45 may respond very differently to the scholarship than one scoring 69. This isn't a violation, but it shapes how you interpret the result.

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

Here's the full analysis code. Run it in sequence and share the output — I'll interpret each piece with you.

```r
# ── 0. Packages ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# install.packages(c("rdrobust", "rddensity", "ggplot2"))

library(rdrobust)
library(rddensity)
library(ggplot2)

# ── 1. Load data ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# (adjust path to your file)
df <- read.csv("your_data.csv")

Y  <- df$gpa
X  <- df$test_score
cutoff <- 70

# ── 2. Visual inspection: histogram of running variable ───────────────────────
# Look for unusual bunching just above the cutoff
ggplot(df, aes(x = test_score)) +
  geom_histogram(binwidth = 1, fill = "steelblue", color = "white") +
  geom_vline(xintercept = cutoff, color = "red", linetype = "dashed", linewidth = 1) +
  labs(title = "Distribution of Test Scores",
       subtitle = "Red dashed line = scholarship cutoff (70)",
       x = "Test Score", y = "Count") +
  theme_minimal()

# ── 3. RD plot: visual evidence of the discontinuity ─────────────────────────
rdplot(Y, X, c = cutoff,
       title = "RD Plot: Scholarship Effect on GPA",
       x.label = "Test Score",
       y.label = "GPA")

# ── 4. McCrary density test: check for manipulation ──────────────────────────
density_test <- rddensity(X = X, c = cutoff)
summary(density_test)

# Plot the density
rdplotdensity(density_test, X,
              title = "Density Test at Cutoff",
              xlabel = "Test Score",
              ylabel = "Density")

# ── 5. Main RD estimate ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# rdrobust uses MSE-optimal bandwidth + robust bias-corrected inference
rd_main <- rdrobust(y = Y, x = X, c = cutoff)
summary(rd_main)
```

## Stage 4: Robustness Checks

Run these *after* you have the main estimate:

```r
# ── 7. Bandwidth sensitivity ─────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Does the estimate hold across different bandwidths?
opt_bw <- rd_main$bws[1, 1]

bw_grid <- opt_bw * c(0.5, 0.75, 1.0, 1.25, 1.5)

cat("\n=== Bandwidth Sensitivity ===\n")
for (bw in bw_grid) {
  rd_bw <- rdrobust(y = Y, x = X, c = cutoff, h = bw)
  cat(sprintf("BW: %5.2f | Estimate: %6.4f | 95%% CI: [%6.4f, %6.4f]\n",
              bw,
              rd_bw$coef[1],
              rd_bw$ci[3, 1],   # robust CI lower
              rd_bw$ci[3, 2]))  # robust CI upper
}

# ── 8. Placebo cutoffs ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Run the RD at score = 60 and 80, where there's NO actual treatment.
# If we see "effects" there, it suggests model misspecification.
placebo_cutoffs <- c(60, 80)

cat("\n=== Placebo Cutoff Tests ===\n")
for (pc in placebo_cutoffs) {
  # Only use data on ONE side of each placebo cutoff
  # (exclude observations that cross the real cutoff)
  if (pc < cutoff) {
    sub <- df[df$test_score < cutoff, ]
  } else {
    sub <- df[df$test_score >= cutoff, ]
  }
  rd_placebo <- rdrobust(y = sub$gpa, x = sub$test_score, c = pc)
  cat(sprintf("Placebo cutoff: %d | Estimate: %6.4f | p-value: %.3f\n",
              pc,
              rd_placebo$coef[1],
              rd_placebo$pv[3]))  # robust p-value
}

# ── 9. Donut hole test ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Exclude students within ±1 point of the cutoff (most likely to be manipulators)
df_donut <- df[abs(df$test_score - cutoff) > 1, ]
rd_donut <- rdrobust(y = df_donut$gpa, x = df_donut$test_score, c = cutoff)
cat("\n=== Donut Hole Test (excluding ±1 point of cutoff) ===\n")
summary(rd_donut)
```

### sc_poor_prefit

**Run 1**: {"violation_detected": true, "flags_coverage": 1.0, "severity_correct": false}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup ✓

Got it. Here's what I'm working with:

| Parameter | Value |
|---|---|
| Treated unit | State A |
| Treatment period | 20 |
| Pre-treatment periods | 1–19 (T₀ = 19) |
| Post-treatment periods | 20–40 (T₁ = 21) |
| Donor pool | 10 states |
| Language | R (`Synth` package) |

One quick note on your data: the `treated` column looks like a **unit-level flag** (State A = 1, donors = 0), not a period-level treatment indicator. That's fine — I'll handle treatment timing via `dataprep()` directly.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through the four key assumptions before generating code.

### 1. Pre-treatment fit quality
> *Can a weighted combo of donor states reproduce State A's pre-treatment trajectory?*

With **19 pre-treatment periods**, you have enough to optimize weights — though it's on the shorter side. We'll compute pre-treatment RMSPE after fitting and compare it to the post-treatment gap. If the synthetic line can't track State A before period 20, the counterfactual isn't credible.

**Status**: Testable — we'll flag it after running the model.

---

### 2. Convex hull
> *Does State A's outcome level sit within the range of donor states?*

If State A is much larger (or smaller) than all 10 donors on key predictors, no convex combination of them can match it — the weights would pile onto one donor and extrapolate. We'll inspect the donor weight distribution for concentration.

**Status**: Partially testable — watch for weights > 80% on a single donor.

---

### 3. No interference
> *Did State A's policy affect the donor states?*

For example: if the policy caused firms or residents to relocate from A to neighboring donor states, those donors' outcomes are contaminated — the synthetic control would then *underestimate* the true effect.

**Status**: Must be argued substantively. Does this policy have spillover potential? (e.g., a tax policy vs. a public health program)

---

### 4. No anticipation
> *Did State A start changing its behavior before period 20?*

If agents knew the policy was coming and adjusted early, the pre-treatment fit will look fine but the "pre-period" already contains some treatment effect, biasing the estimate downward.

**Status**: Check the trend plot visually — any unusual movement in periods 17–19?

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

Using the `Synth` package (canonical Abadie et al. implementation). I'm including pre-treatment outcome lags as predictors, which is best practice for matching on the full trajectory.

```r
# ── Dependencies ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
library(Synth)
library(tidyverse)

# ── 1. Prepare data ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Synth requires numeric unit IDs
df <- df %>%
  mutate(state_id = as.integer(factor(state, levels = sort(unique(state)))))

treated_id <- unique(df$state_id[df$state == "A"])
donor_ids  <- unique(df$state_id[df$state != "A"])

cat("Treated unit ID:", treated_id, "\n")
cat("Donor IDs:", donor_ids, "\n")

# ── 2. dataprep() ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Predictors: full pre-treatment mean + outcome lags at key periods
dataprep_out <- dataprep(
  foo                    = as.data.frame(df),
  predictors             = "outcome",
  predictors.op          = "mean",
  special.predictors     = list(
    list("outcome",  5, "mean"),   # early pre-period average
    list("outcome", 10, "mean"),   # mid pre-period
    list("outcome", 15, "mean"),   # late pre-period
    list("outcome", 19, "mean")    # last pre-treatment period
  ),
  dependent              = "outcome",
  unit.variable          = "state_id",
  time.variable          = "period",
  treatment.identifier   = treated_id,
  controls.identifier    = donor_ids,
  time.predictors.prior  = 1:19,
  time.optimize.ssr      = 1:19,
  time.plot              = 1:40
)

# ── 3. Estimate weights ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────
synth_out <- synth(dataprep_out)

# ── 4. Summary tables ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
synth_tables <- synth.tab(
  synth.res    = synth_out,
  dataprep.res = dataprep_out
)

# Donor weights — sorted descending
cat("\n── Donor Weights ──\n")
weights <- synth_tables$tab.w %>%
  arrange(desc(w.weights))
print(weights)

# Predictor balance: State A vs Synthetic
cat("\n── Predictor Balance ──\n")
print(synth_tables$tab.pred)

# Pre-treatment RMSPE
rmspe_pre <- synth_out$loss.v %>% sqrt()
cat("\nPre-treatment RMSPE:", round(rmspe_pre, 4), "\n")

# ── 5. Plots ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Plot 1: Treated vs Synthetic trajectory
path.plot(
  synth.res    = synth_out,
  dataprep.res = dataprep_out,
  Ylab         = "Outcome",
  Xlab         = "Period",
  Legend       = c("State A", "Synthetic A"),
  Legend.position = "bottomright",
  abline.v     = 20,   # treatment onset
  Main         = "State A vs. Synthetic Control"
)
abline(v = 20, col = "red", lty = 2)

# Plot 2: Treatment effect gap (treated − synthetic)
gaps.plot(
  synth.res    = synth_out,
  dataprep.res = dataprep_out,
  Ylab         = "Gap (Treated − Synthetic)",
  Xlab         = "Period",
  Main         = "Estimated Treatment Effect",
  abline.v     = 20
)
abline(v = 20, col = "red", lty = 2)
abline(h = 0, col = "gray", lty = 3)
```

## Stage 4: Falsification

Two robustness checks — I recommend running both.

### Check A: In-space placebo (permutation inference)

Apply the exact same method to each donor state as if *it* were the treated unit. Compute a pseudo p-value: what fraction of units has a post/pre RMSPE ratio as large as State A's?

```r
# ── In-space placebo ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Store results for each unit
store_gaps <- matrix(NA,
  nrow = length(1:40),
  ncol = length(donor_ids) + 1
)
colnames(store_gaps) <- c(treated_id, donor_ids)
rownames(store_gaps) <- 1:40

# Treated unit gap
store_gaps[, 1] <- dataprep_out$Y1plot - (dataprep_out$Y0plot %*% synth_out$solution.w)

# Placebo loop: treat each donor as if it were the treated unit
for (did in donor_ids) {
  
  dp_placebo <- dataprep(
    foo                    = as.data.frame(df),
    predictors             = "outcome",
    predictors.op          = "mean",
    special.predictors     = list(
      list("outcome",  5, "mean"),
      list("outcome", 10, "mean"),
      list("outcome", 15, "mean"),
      list("outcome", 19, "mean")
    ),
    dependent              = "outcome",
    unit.variable          = "state_id",
    time.variable          = "period",
    treatment.identifier   = did,
    controls.identifier    = c(treated_id, setdiff(donor_ids, did)),
    time.predictors.prior  = 1:19,
    time.optimize.ssr      = 1:19,
    time.plot              = 1:40
  )
  
  syn_placebo <- tryCatch(
    synth(dp_placebo, Margin.ipop = 0.005),
    error = function(e) NULL
  )
  
  if (!is.null(syn_placebo)) {
    store_gaps[, as.character(did)] <-
      dp_placebo$Y1plot - (dp_placebo$Y0plot %*% syn_placebo$solution.w)
  }
}

# ── RMSPE ratio for each unit ─────────────────────────────────────────────────
rmspe_ratio <- function(gaps, pre = 1:19, post = 20:40) {
  sqrt(mean(gaps[post]^2)) / sqrt(mean(gaps[pre]^2))
}

ratios <- apply(store_gaps, 2, rmspe_ratio, pre = 1:19, post = 20:40)
ratios <- sort(ratios, decreasing = TRUE)

cat("\n── Post/Pre RMSPE Ratios ──\n")
print(round(ratios, 3))

treated_rank <- which(names(ratios) == as.character(treated_id))
p_value      <- treated_rank / length(ratios)
cat("\nState A rank:", treated_rank, "of", length(ratios))
cat("\nPseudo p-value:", round(p_value, 3), "\n")

# ── Placebo plot ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
matplot(1:40, store_gaps,
  type = "l", lty = 1, col = "lightgray",
  ylim = range(store_gaps, na.rm = TRUE),
  xlab = "Period", ylab = "Gap",
  main = "In-Space Placebo Test"
)
lines(1:40, store_gaps[, as.character(treated_id)],
  col = "black", lwd = 2.5
)
abline(v = 20, col = "red", lty = 2)
abline(h = 0, col = "gray", lty = 3)
legend("topleft",
  legend = c("State A", "Donor placebos"),
  col    = c("black", "lightgray"),
  lty    = 1, lwd = c(2.5, 1)
)
```

### Check B: Leave-one-out donor sensitivity

Re-estimate dropping one donor at a time. If results swing wildly, a single donor is doing all the work.

```r
# ── Leave-one-out ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
loo_gaps <- matrix(NA, nrow = 40, ncol = length(donor_ids))
colnames(loo_gaps) <- donor_ids

for (i in seq_along(donor_ids)) {
  loo_donors <- donor_ids[-i]
  
  dp_loo <- dataprep(
    foo                    = as.data.frame(df),
    predictors             = "outcome",
    predictors.op          = "mean",
    special.predictors     = list(
      list("outcome",  5, "mean"),
      list("outcome", 10, "mean"),
      list("outcome", 15, "mean"),
      list("outcome", 19, "mean")
    ),
    dependent              = "outcome",
    unit.variable          = "state_id",
    time.variable          = "period",
    treatment.identifier   = treated_id,
    controls.identifier    = loo_donors,
    time.predictors.prior  = 1:19,
    time.optimize.ssr      = 1:19,
    time.plot              = 1:40
  )
  
  syn_loo <- tryCatch(
    synth(dp_loo, Margin.ipop = 0.005),
    error = function(e) NULL
  )
  
  if (!is.null(syn_loo)) {
    loo_gaps[, i] <- dp_loo$Y1plot - (dp_loo$Y0plot %*% syn_loo$solution.w)
  }
}

# Main gap
main_gap <- store_gaps[, as.character(treated_id)]

matplot(1:40, loo_gaps,
  type = "l", lty = 1, col = "lightblue",
  ylim = range(c(loo_gaps, main_gap), na.rm = TRUE),
  xlab = "Period", ylab = "Gap",
  main = "Leave-One-Out: Donor Sensitivity"
)
lines(1:40, main_gap, col = "black", lwd = 2.5)
abline(v = 20, col = "red", lty = 2)
abline(h = 0, col = "gray", lty = 3)
legend("topleft",
  legend = c("Main estimate", "Leave-one-out"),
  col    = c("black", "lightblue"),
  lty    = 1, lwd = c(2.5, 1)
)
```

## Stage 1: Setup

Good setup. Here's what I'm working with:

| Parameter | Value |
|-----------|-------|
| Pre-period | Months 1–36 (36 obs) |
| Post-period | Months 37–60 (24 obs) |
| Granularity | Monthly |

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through each one for your specific setup.

### 1. No concurrent confounding events 🔴 *Must argue, not test*

> Did anything else change around month 37 — a policy revision, external shock, data collection change, population shift?

This is the **single biggest threat** to ITS. The model cannot distinguish "policy effect" from "something else that also happened at month 37." Even if the pre-period looks clean, a single contemporaneous event invalidates the causal interpretation. I'll ask you to argue this explicitly before we interpret results.

---

### 2. Pre-period structural breaks 🔍 *Must check before anything else*

You said the pre-period "looks stable" — but a visual check can miss subtle level shifts or trend changes. A structural break in the pre-period means the model is trained on data from two different regimes, and its counterfactual projection will be wrong.

```r
library(strucchange)
library(tidyverse)

# Load your data
df <- read.csv("your_data.csv")

# Create ITS variables
df <- df %>%
  mutate(
    time_since = pmax(0, time - 36)  # 0 in pre-period, 1,2,3... after month 37
  )

pre_data <- df %>% filter(post == 0)

# --- Structural break tests on pre-period ---

# 1. CUSUM test: detects gradual parameter instability
cusum_test <- efp(outcome ~ time, data = pre_data, type = "OLS-CUSUM")
plot(cusum_test, main = "CUSUM Test — Pre-Period Stability")
# Red boundary lines: if the CUSUM path stays within bounds → no break

# 2. Chow-type breakpoint test (Bai-Perron)
bp_test <- breakpoints(outcome ~ time, data = pre_data, breaks = 2)
summary(bp_test)
plot(bp_test)
# Look for: are breakpoints flagged within the pre-period?

# 3. Visual inspection with loess
ggplot(pre_data, aes(x = time, y = outcome)) +
  geom_line(color = "steelblue") +
  geom_point(color = "steelblue", size = 1.5) +
  geom_smooth(method = "loess", span = 0.4, se = TRUE,
              color = "darkred", fill = "salmon", alpha = 0.2) +
  labs(title = "Pre-Period Outcome — Visual Stability Check",
       x = "Month", y = "Outcome") +
  theme_minimal()
```

> **What to look for**: Does the CUSUM path stay inside the red lines? Does the `breakpoints()` call flag any breaks? Does the loess curve show a sudden jump or direction change? If yes to any → stop, discuss before proceeding.

---

### 3. Stationarity 🔍 *Testable*

The segmented regression model assumes the pre-period fluctuations are roughly stationary around a trend. A random walk in the pre-period can produce a spurious-looking break.

```r
library(tseries)

# ADF test: H0 = unit root (non-stationary)
adf_result <- adf.test(pre_data$outcome)
cat("ADF p-value:", round(adf_result$p.value, 4), "\n")
# p < 0.05 → stationary ✓

# KPSS test: H0 = stationary (complementary)
kpss_result <- kpss.test(pre_data$outcome)
cat("KPSS p-value:", round(kpss_result$p.value, 4), "\n")
# p > 0.05 → stationary ✓

# ACF plot — slow decay = unit root concern
acf(pre_data$outcome, main = "ACF of Pre-Period Outcome")
```

> **Interpret together**: ADF rejects H0 *and* KPSS fails to reject H0 = stationary, proceed normally. If both point to non-stationarity, we'll need to difference before modeling.

---

### 4. Pre-period length adequacy ✅ *Already met*

36 monthly observations cover exactly 3 full annual cycles — the minimum for capturing seasonal patterns reliably. You're at the threshold. Not a concern for segmented regression; would matter more if adding a seasonal ARIMA component.

---

### 5. Pre-period model fit 🔍 *Check after estimation*

We'll assess MAPE in the pre-period once the model is fit. Target: < 10% acceptable, < 5% good.

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

### Option A — Segmented Regression (ITS)

Simple, interpretable. Estimates a **level change** and **slope change** at intervention.

```r
library(tidyverse)
library(sandwich)   # HAC standard errors
library(lmtest)     # coeftest

# --- Prepare data ---
df <- df %>%
  mutate(time_since = pmax(0, time - 36))

# --- Fit segmented regression ---
# time       = underlying time trend (same slope pre + post, baseline)
# post       = level shift at intervention
# time_since = slope change after intervention
model_its <- lm(outcome ~ time + post + time_since, data = df)

# Newey-West HAC standard errors (corrects for autocorrelation in residuals)
coef_hac <- coeftest(model_its, vcov = NeweyWest(model_its, lag = 4))
print(coef_hac)

# --- Key estimates ---
cat("\n--- Effect Estimates ---\n")
cat("Level change at month 37:     ", round(coef(model_its)["post"], 3), "\n")
cat("Slope change per month after: ", round(coef(model_its)["time_since"], 3), "\n")

# Cumulative effect at end of post-period (month 60 = 24 months post)
level_change <- coef(model_its)["post"]
slope_change <- coef(model_its)["time_since"]
cumulative <- sum(level_change + slope_change * 1:24)
cat("Cumulative effect (24 months):", round(cumulative, 3), "\n")

# --- Pre-period model fit ---
pre_fit <- df %>%
  filter(post == 0) %>%
  mutate(
    predicted = predict(model_its, newdata = filter(df, post == 0)),
    residual  = outcome - predicted
  )

mape_pre <- mean(abs(pre_fit$residual / pre_fit$outcome)) * 100
cat("\nPre-period MAPE:", round(mape_pre, 2), "%\n")
if (mape_pre < 5)  cat("→ Good fit\n")
if (mape_pre >= 5  & mape_pre < 10) cat("→ Acceptable fit\n")
if (mape_pre >= 10) cat("→ ⚠️ Poor fit — interpret with caution\n")

# --- Residual diagnostics ---
residuals_model <- residuals(model_its)
Box.test(residuals_model, lag = 12, type = "Ljung-Box")
# p > 0.05 → no significant autocorrelation in residuals ✓

acf(residuals_model, main = "ACF of Residuals")
```

**Counterfactual plot:**

```r
# Build counterfactual: what would have happened without the intervention?
df <- df %>%
  mutate(
    counterfactual = coef(model_its)["(Intercept)"] +
                     coef(model_its)["time"] * time,
    fitted = predict(model_its)
  )

ggplot(df, aes(x = time)) +
  geom_vline(xintercept = 36.5, linetype = "dashed", color = "gray40") +
  geom_line(aes(y = outcome),        color = "black",   linewidth = 0.8) +
  geom_line(aes(y = fitted),         color = "steelblue", linewidth = 0.8) +
  geom_line(aes(y = counterfactual), color = "darkred",
            linetype = "dashed",     linewidth = 0.8) +
  annotate("text", x = 37, y = max(df$outcome), hjust = -0.1,
           label = "Intervention", color = "gray40", size = 3.5) +
  labs(title = "Interrupted Time Series — Observed vs. Counterfactual",
       x = "Month", y = "Outcome",
       caption = "Blue = fitted model | Red dashed = counterfactual (no intervention)") +
  theme_minimal()
```

---

### Option B — CausalArima (ARIMA-based counterfactual)

Better at capturing autocorrelation structure; gives credible intervals.

```r
library(CausalArima)

# Convert to time series
outcome_ts <- ts(df$outcome, start = 1, frequency = 1)
dates_vec  <- df$time

result_arima <- CausalArima(
  y        = outcome_ts,
  dates    = dates_vec,
  int.date = 37,       # intervention at month 37
  nboot    = 1000
)

summary(result_arima)
plot(result_arima)

# Key numbers from output
cat("Point effect:", result_arima$effect$mean, "\n")
cat("95% CI: [", result_arima$effect$lower, ",", result_arima$effect$upper, "]\n")
```

> Start with Option A — it's more interpretable and the coefficients map directly to "how much did the level change" and "how much did the trend change." Use Option B to cross-validate.

## Stage 4: Robustness Checks

I'm proposing two checks. Run at least one.

### Check 1 — Placebo intervention date (most important)

Move the "intervention" to month 18 (mid-pre-period) and re-run the model. If you find a significant effect where there shouldn't be one, the model is picking up noise or a structural pattern — not a real policy effect.

```r
# Placebo at month 18
df_placebo <- df %>%
  filter(time <= 36) %>%  # pre-period only
  mutate(
    post_placebo       = as.integer(time >= 18),
    time_since_placebo = pmax(0, time - 17)
  )

model_placebo <- lm(outcome ~ time + post_placebo + time_since_placebo,
                    data = df_placebo)
coef_placebo <- coeftest(model_placebo, vcov = NeweyWest(model_placebo, lag = 4))
print(coef_placebo)

# Compare placebo level change to main estimate
cat("\nMain level change:   ", round(coef(model_its)["post"], 3), "\n")
cat("Placebo level change:", round(coef(model_placebo)["post_placebo"], 3), "\n")
# Placebo should be small and non-significant ✓
```

### Check 2 — Vary the pre-period length

Re-run using only the last 24 months of the pre-period (months 13–36). If the estimated effect changes substantially, the result may depend on early pre-period patterns rather than a real break.

```r
df_short_pre <- df %>%
  filter(time >= 13) %>%
  mutate(time_since = pmax(0, time - 36))

model_short <- lm(outcome ~ time + post + time_since, data = df_short_pre)
coef_short  <- coeftest(model_short, vcov = NeweyWest(model_short, lag = 4))

cat("Level change (full pre):  ", round(coef(model_its)["post"], 3), "\n")
cat("Level change (short pre): ", round(coef(model_short)["post"], 3), "\n")
# Should be qualitatively similar ✓
```
