### timeseries_structural_break

**Run 1**: {"violation_detected": false, "flags_coverage": 0.0, "severity_correct": false}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup

Good — you have **36 pre-treatment months** and **24 post-treatment months**. That's workable (≥ 30 is the minimum; 50+ is ideal, so flag this later).

You haven't mentioned control series, so I'll use **segmented regression (basic ITS)** — the right choice here. The model estimates two things at the intervention:
- A **level shift** (immediate jump or drop)
- A **slope change** (did the trend accelerate or decelerate?)

Your data already has `time` and `post`. We need to add one more variable: `time_since` — time elapsed *since* the intervention, which is 0 in the pre-period and counts up after. This is what captures the slope change.

One question before we go further: **is there known seasonality in the monthly outcome?** (e.g., consistent peaks in certain months each year). If yes, we'll add month dummies. If no, the basic model below is fine.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through each one for your setup:

### ① No concurrent confounding events 🔴 *Cannot be tested — must be argued*

> Did anything else change at month 37 that could explain a shift in your outcome?

This is the **single biggest threat** to ITS. A concurrent event (new regulation, economic shock, seasonal anomaly, another policy) at the same time as the intervention would make it impossible to isolate the policy's effect. The model has no way to detect this — you have to know your context.

*Action for you*: Think carefully about what else happened around month 37. If something did, we need to discuss whether to include a covariate or acknowledge it as a caveat.

---

### ② Pre-period model fit ⚠️ *Testable — we'll check after fitting*

> Does the segmented regression accurately capture the pre-intervention trend? If it can't explain the pre-period, the counterfactual projection is unreliable.

We'll measure **MAPE** on the pre-period after fitting. Target: < 10%. We'll also check residual autocorrelation (Ljung-Box test) — leftover autocorrelation means the model missed a pattern.

---

### ③ Stationarity ⚠️ *Testable — we'll run ADF + KPSS*

> Is the pre-intervention series roughly stable in its statistical properties, or does it have a unit root (random walk)?

Non-stationary series can produce spurious "effects." We'll use Newey-West (HAC) standard errors in the regression to handle autocorrelation, but we should still check.

---

### ④ Adequate pre-period length ⚠️ *Borderline*

> 36 months clears the 30-observation floor, but is below the 50+ recommended. With monthly data, you have exactly 3 seasonal cycles — right at the minimum if seasonality exists.

This means confidence intervals will be wider than ideal. Flag in interpretation.

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
from statsmodels.stats.diagnostic import acorr_ljungbox
from statsmodels.tsa.stattools import adfuller, kpss
from scipy.stats import shapiro

# ── 0. Reproduce your data structure ─────────────────────────────────────────
# Assumes df has columns: time (int), outcome (float), post (int 0/1)
# intervention_time = last pre-period time point (36 here)
intervention_time = 36

# ── 1. Build ITS variables ────────────────────────────────────────────────────
df = df.copy()
df['time_since'] = np.maximum(0, df['time'] - intervention_time)

# Verify structure
print(df[['time', 'post', 'time_since']].head(40).to_string())

# ── 2. Stationarity checks (pre-period only) ──────────────────────────────────
pre = df[df['post'] == 0]['outcome'].values

# ADF: H0 = unit root (non-stationary). p < 0.05 → stationary ✓
adf_stat, adf_p, *_ = adfuller(pre, autolag='AIC')
print(f"\nADF test:  stat = {adf_stat:.3f}, p = {adf_p:.3f}")
print("  → " + ("Stationary ✓" if adf_p < 0.05 else "⚠️  Possible unit root — consider differencing"))

# KPSS: H0 = stationary. p > 0.05 → stationary ✓
kpss_stat, kpss_p, *_ = kpss(pre, regression='c', nlags='auto')
print(f"KPSS test: stat = {kpss_stat:.3f}, p = {kpss_p:.3f}")
print("  → " + ("Stationary ✓" if kpss_p > 0.05 else "⚠️  Non-stationary signal"))

# ── 3. Main ITS model (HAC standard errors for autocorrelation) ───────────────
model = smf.ols('outcome ~ time + post + time_since', data=df).fit(
    cov_type='HAC', cov_kwds={'maxlags': 4}   # Newey-West, 4-lag rule of thumb
)
print("\n", model.summary())

# Key estimates
print("\n── Causal estimates ──")
print(f"  Pre-intervention slope (per month):   {model.params['time']:.3f}  "
      f"(p={model.pvalues['time']:.3f})")
print(f"  Level shift at intervention (month 37): {model.params['post']:.3f}  "
      f"(p={model.pvalues['post']:.3f})")
print(f"  Slope change after intervention:        {model.params['time_since']:.3f}  "
      f"(p={model.pvalues['time_since']:.3f})")

# ── 4. Pre-period model fit ───────────────────────────────────────────────────
pre_df = df[df['post'] == 0].copy()
pre_df['fitted'] = model.fittedvalues[pre_df.index]
pre_residuals = pre_df['outcome'] - pre_df['fitted']

mape = np.mean(np.abs(pre_residuals / pre_df['outcome'])) * 100
print(f"\nPre-period MAPE: {mape:.2f}%")
print("  → " + ("Good fit ✓" if mape < 5 else "Acceptable ✓" if mape < 10 else "⚠️  Poor fit — interpret with caution"))

# Ljung-Box: tests residual autocorrelation. p > 0.05 → no autocorrelation ✓
lb = acorr_ljungbox(pre_residuals, lags=10, return_df=True)
print(f"\nLjung-Box (lag 10): p = {lb['lb_pvalue'].iloc[-1]:.3f}")
print("  → " + ("No autocorrelation ✓" if lb['lb_pvalue'].iloc[-1] > 0.05
                 else "⚠️  Residual autocorrelation — HAC SEs help, but consider ARIMA"))

# Normality check
sw_stat, sw_p = shapiro(pre_residuals)
print(f"Shapiro-Wilk:       p = {sw_p:.3f}")
print("  → " + ("Residuals approx normal ✓" if sw_p > 0.05 else "⚠️  Non-normal residuals"))

# ── 5. Visualization ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
fig, axes = plt.subplots(2, 1, figsize=(12, 8))

# Panel 1: Observed vs counterfactual
ax = axes[0]
ax.plot(df['time'], df['outcome'], 'ko', markersize=4, label='Observed', zorder=3)

# Fitted line (actual model)
ax.plot(df['time'], model.fittedvalues, 'b-', linewidth=2, label='Model fit')

# Counterfactual: what would have happened without intervention
#   = intercept + time*slope (no post, no time_since terms)
df['counterfactual'] = (model.params['Intercept']
                        + model.params['time'] * df['time'])
post_df = df[df['post'] == 1]
ax.plot(post_df['time'], post_df['counterfactual'], 'r--',
        linewidth=2, label='Counterfactual (no intervention)')

ax.axvline(x=intervention_time + 0.5, color='gray', linestyle=':', linewidth=1.5,
           label='Intervention (month 37)')
ax.set_title('Interrupted Time Series — Observed vs Counterfactual')
ax.set_xlabel('Time (months)')
ax.set_ylabel('Outcome')
ax.legend()

# Panel 2: Pre-period residuals
axes[1].plot(pre_df['time'], pre_residuals, 'ko', markersize=4)
axes[1].axhline(0, color='gray', linestyle='--')
axes[1].set_title('Pre-Period Residuals (should look like random scatter)')
axes[1].set_xlabel('Time (months)')
axes[1].set_ylabel('Residual')

plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig('its_main.png', dpi=150)
plt.show()

print("\nPlot saved to its_main.png")
```

---

## Stage 4: Falsification

Two checks — run both:

```python
# ── Placebo 1: Fake intervention at month 18 (mid-pre-period) ────────────────
# If this also shows a "significant effect," the model is picking up noise.
placebo_time = 18
pre_only = df[df['post'] == 0].copy()
pre_only['post_placebo'] = (pre_only['time'] > placebo_time).astype(int)
pre_only['time_since_placebo'] = np.maximum(0, pre_only['time'] - placebo_time)

placebo_model = smf.ols(
    'outcome ~ time + post_placebo + time_since_placebo', data=pre_only
).fit(cov_type='HAC', cov_kwds={'maxlags': 4})

print("── Placebo test (fake intervention at month 18) ──")
print(f"  Level shift:  {placebo_model.params['post_placebo']:.3f}  "
      f"(p={placebo_model.pvalues['post_placebo']:.3f})")
print(f"  Slope change: {placebo_model.params['time_since_placebo']:.3f}  "
      f"(p={placebo_model.pvalues['time_since_placebo']:.3f})")
print("  → Placebo effect significant? "
      + ("⚠️  Yes — raises concern about spurious effects"
         if placebo_model.pvalues['post_placebo'] < 0.05
         else "No ✓ — consistent with model reliability"))

# ── Placebo 2: Vary pre-period start (sensitivity to pre-period length) ───────
results = []
for start in [1, 6, 12, 18]:
    sub = df[df['time'] >= start].copy()
    sub['time_since'] = np.maximum(0, sub['time'] - intervention_time)
    m = smf.ols('outcome ~ time + post + time_since', data=sub).fit(
        cov_type='HAC', cov_kwds={'maxlags': 4})
    results.append({
        'pre_start': start,
        'n_pre': sum(sub['post'] == 0),
        'level_shift': round(m.params['post'], 3),
        'level_p': round(m.pvalues['post'], 3),
        'slope_change': round(m.params['time_since'], 3),
        'slope_p': round(m.pvalues['time_since'], 3)
    })

print("\n── Sensitivity to pre-period start ──")
print(pd.DataFrame(results).to_string(index=False))
print("  → Results should be qualitatively stable across rows.")
```

**How to read the falsification results:**

| Check | Good sign | Concern |
|---|---|---|
| Placebo at month 18 | `p > 0.05` for both placebo estimates | Significant placebo "effect" |
| Pre-period sensitivity | Level shift sign/magnitude stable | Estimates flip sign or change drastically |

---

>
> Pre-period model fit: MAPE = [X]% — [good/acceptable/poor].
>
> Robustness: A placebo test at month 18 showed [no significant effect / a significant effect — caveat]. Results were [stable / sensitive] across different pre-period start points.
>

### did_basic_2x2

**Run 1**: {"has_code": true, "runs_without_error": true, "execution_error": null, "estimate": 4.828365714285701, "estimation_accurate": true, "diagnostic_coverage": 0.0, "must_include_results": {"parallel_trends": false, "confidence_interval": false, "clustered_standard_errors": false}}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup

You have a **classic DiD** setup:
- Single treatment date (month 6), two groups (stores 1–25 treated, 26–50 control)
- Panel: 50 units × 12 periods → use **TWFE** with store + month fixed effects

**Critical detail**: Since store-level fixed effects (EntityEffects) absorb all time-invariant unit characteristics, the `treated` dummy must *not* enter the regression directly — it would be perfectly collinear with the entity FEs. Only the interaction `treated × post` varies over time and identifies the effect.

---

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
from linearmodels.panel import PanelOLS

# ── 1. Treatment indicators ────────────────────────────────────────────────────
df['treated']      = (df['store'] <= 25).astype(int)   # time-invariant group flag
df['post']         = (df['month'] >= 6).astype(int)    # post-treatment period flag
df['treated_post'] = df['treated'] * df['post']         # DiD interaction — varies over time

# ── 2. Set panel index (required by linearmodels) ─────────────────────────────
df_panel = df.set_index(['store', 'month'])

# ── 3. TWFE estimation ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# 'treated' is NOT included — entity FEs absorb all time-invariant store traits.
# 'treated_post' is the sole regressor: it equals 1 only for treated stores post month 6.
mod = PanelOLS.from_formula(
    'sales ~ treated_post + EntityEffects + TimeEffects',
    data=df_panel
)
res = mod.fit(cov_type='clustered', cluster_entity=True)

# ── 4. Extract and report results ─────────────────────────────────────────────
att    = res.params['treated_post']
se     = res.std_errors['treated_post']
ci_lo  = res.conf_int().loc['treated_post', 'lower']
ci_hi  = res.conf_int().loc['treated_post', 'upper']
pval   = res.pvalues['treated_post']

print(res.summary)
print("\n── DiD Treatment Effect ─────────────────────────────────────")
print(f"  Estimate (ATT) : {att:+.4f}")
print(f"  Std. Error     : {se:.4f}")
print(f"  95% CI         : [{ci_lo:+.4f}, {ci_hi:+.4f}]")
print(f"  p-value        : {pval:.4f}")
print(f"ESTIMATE:{att}")
```

---

### What the output tells you

| Term | Meaning |
|---|---|
| `treated_post` coefficient | Average sales change in treated stores *relative to* what control stores experienced over the same period |
| Positive & significant | The display increased sales beyond any general month-6 trend |
| Entity FEs | Remove time-invariant store differences (e.g., store size, location) |
| Time FEs | Remove common shocks shared by all stores each month (e.g., seasonality) |
| Clustered SEs | Accounts for within-store serial correlation across months |

---

### Recommended next steps

1. **Pre-trends check** — run an event study to verify treated and control stores trended similarly before month 6
2. **Placebo test** — re-run with a fake treatment date (e.g., month 3) on pre-period data only; the estimate should be near zero
3. **`/causal-auditor`** — stress-test for threats to validity (spillovers between nearby stores, store-level attrition)

</details>

---

## 🏪 Scenario: RetailCo Store Layout Pilot

You're a data analyst at **RetailCo**, a mid-size retail chain with 100 brick-and-mortar locations across the country. In January of Year 2 (month 13 of a 24-month observation window), the company rolled out a redesigned store layout to **50 pilot stores** selected by regional managers. The remaining 50 stores kept the original layout and serve as your comparison group.

Your VP of Retail wants to know: **Did the new layout increase monthly revenue, and by how much?**

You have access to 24 months of store-level revenue data. Your job is to estimate the causal effect of the layout change.

> ⚠️ **One thing to keep in mind**: the pilot stores weren't randomly selected — regional managers picked them. Think about whether that matters for your identification strategy, and what assumption you'd need to check.

---

## 🐍 Python DGP Code

```python
import numpy as np
import pandas as pd

np.random.seed(303)

n_stores = 100
n_months = 24
treat_month = 13
true_att = 5.0  # thousands of dollars per month

store_id = np.repeat(np.arange(1, n_stores + 1), n_months)
month = np.tile(np.arange(1, n_months + 1), n_stores)

# First 50 stores are the pilot group
treated = (store_id <= 50).astype(int)
post = (month >= treat_month).astype(int)

# Store-level fixed effects (each store has its own baseline revenue)
store_fe = np.repeat(np.random.normal(0, 3, n_stores), n_months)

# Common time trend across all stores
time_trend = 0.2 * month

# Revenue in thousands of dollars
revenue = (
    50
    + store_fe
    + time_trend
    + true_att * treated * post
    + np.random.normal(0, 2, n_stores * n_months)
)

df = pd.DataFrame({
    "store_id": store_id,
    "month": month,
    "treated": treated,
    "post": post,
    "revenue": np.round(revenue, 2),
})

df.to_csv("retailco_layout.csv", index=False)
print(df.head(10))
print(f"\nDataset shape: {df.shape}")
print(f"\nTreated stores: {df[df['treated']==1]['store_id'].nunique()}")
print(f"Control stores: {df[df['treated']==0]['store_id'].nunique()}")

print(f"ESTIMATE:{true_att}")
```

3. **Estimate the ATT** using a two-way fixed effects (TWFE) DiD regression with store and month fixed effects.
4. **Interpret your coefficient** — does it match the true effect?

**Deliver**: A point estimate with a 95% confidence interval, and a brief statement on whether you trust the parallel trends assumption.

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## IV Practice Exercise — Python

---

### Scenario

You're a health economist at **Meridian Health**, a mid-size insurance company. The analytics team wants to evaluate whether enrolling in the **WellPath wellness program** actually reduces annual medical spending.

The problem: enrollment is voluntary. Healthier, more motivated members self-select into the program, so a naive comparison of enrollees vs. non-enrollees is badly confounded.

Last year, Meridian mailed **encouragement letters** to a random 50% of eligible members, urging them to join WellPath. Not everyone who got a letter enrolled — and a few members enrolled without one. Your manager hands you the dataset and asks:

> *"Among members who enrolled because of the letter, how much did the program actually reduce their annual medical spending?"*

---

### Your Deliverable

Estimate the **LATE** (Local Average Treatment Effect) of WellPath enrollment on annual medical spending (in $000s), using the encouragement letter as an instrument. Report:

1. Your LATE estimate and 95% confidence interval
2. First-stage F-statistic — and your verdict on instrument strength
3. The key assumptions you're buying into and whether the data supports them

---

### DGP Code

Run this to generate `wellness_trial.csv`:

```python
import numpy as np
import pandas as pd

np.random.seed(505)
n = 3000

# ── Instrument: randomly assigned encouragement letter ──────────────────────
letter = np.random.binomial(1, 0.5, n)

# ── Unobserved confounder: health motivation ─────────────────────────────────
# Higher motivation → more likely to enroll AND independently lowers spending.
# This is what makes naive OLS biased.
U = np.random.normal(0, 1, n)

# ── Treatment: WellPath enrollment ───────────────────────────────────────────
# Letter strongly predicts enrollment (you'll see this in the first stage).
# Motivation also predicts enrollment (the endogeneity source).
def sigmoid(x):
    return 1 / (1 + np.exp(-x))

p_enroll = sigmoid(-1.0 + 1.5 * letter + 1.0 * U)
enrolled = np.random.binomial(1, p_enroll)

# ── Outcome: annual medical spending ($000s) ──────────────────────────────────
# U directly lowers spending (motivated people are healthier regardless).
# Enrollment lowers spending too — but only for those who responded to the letter.
spending = 50 - 10 * enrolled - 8 * U + np.random.normal(0, 10, n)

df = pd.DataFrame({
    "member_id":  np.arange(1, n + 1),
    "letter":     letter,      # instrument (Z)
    "enrolled":   enrolled,    # endogenous treatment (D)
    "spending":   np.round(spending, 2),   # outcome (Y)
})

df.to_csv("wellness_trial.csv", index=False)
print(f"Dataset saved: {df.shape[0]} members, enrollment rate = {enrolled.mean():.1%}")

# ── True effect (don't peek until you have an estimate!) ─────────────────────
true_late = -10.0
print(f"ESTIMATE:{true_late}")
```

---

### Hints (expand only if stuck)

<details>
<summary>Hint 1 — What estimator to use</summary>

Two-Stage Least Squares (2SLS). In Python, `linearmodels.iv.model.IV2SLS` is the cleanest way. You can also do it manually: regress `enrolled` on `letter`, get fitted values, then regress `spending` on those fitted values.

</details>

<details>
<summary>Hint 2 — Checking the first stage</summary>

Run `enrolled ~ letter` and look at the F-statistic. Conventional threshold is F > 10; you should see something much higher here. A weak first stage inflates 2SLS variance and biases it toward OLS.

</details>

<details>
<summary>Hint 3 — The key assumptions to discuss</summary>

Three to address: (1) **Relevance** — does the letter predict enrollment? (2) **Independence** — was the letter truly randomly assigned? (3) **Exclusion restriction** — does the letter affect spending *only* through enrollment, or could it have a direct effect (e.g., letter nudges members to exercise regardless of WellPath)? The third one is the hardest to verify from data alone.

</details>

<details>
<summary>Hint 4 — Why OLS is biased here</summary>

Run OLS of `spending ~ enrolled` first and note the coefficient. Compare it to your 2SLS estimate. The gap is the bias from unmeasured health motivation — motivated people enroll *and* spend less, so OLS overstates the program's benefit.

</details>

---

### True LATE

The true effect baked into the DGP is **−$10,000** in annual medical spending among *compliers* — members who enrolled because they received the letter (they wouldn't have enrolled otherwise).

Note that this is a LATE, not an ATE. It says nothing about always-takers (enrolled regardless) or never-takers (never enrolled regardless).

Good luck — post your estimate and I'll debrief you on what the DGP confirms!

</details>

### experiments_simple

**Run 1**: {"has_code": true, "runs_without_error": true, "execution_error": null, "estimate": 0.031199999999999926, "estimation_accurate": true, "diagnostic_coverage": 0.0, "must_include_results": {"confidence_interval": false, "balance_check": false}}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup

You're at the **analysis stage** with individual-level randomization, a binary outcome, and no additional covariates in the sample. I'll run:

1. A quick assumption check (balance is limited here — no covariates — but attrition and group sizes can be verified)
2. Difference-in-means estimate
3. Logistic regression for a marginal effect check
4. Permutation test as a falsification/robustness step

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions (quick scan)

| Assumption | Status | Note |
|---|---|---|
| Random assignment | ✅ Assumed — A/B test | No covariates to balance-check; verify randomization code separately |
| SUTVA | ✅ Plausible | Users checking out independently; low spillover risk |
| No differential attrition | ⚠️ Will verify | Check group sizes match expected 5,000 each |
| Compliance | ✅ Assumed | Assignment = treatment in standard A/B setup |

---

## Stage 3 + 4: Implementation & Robustness

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
from scipy.stats import ttest_ind

# ── 0. Sanity checks ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

group_counts = df.groupby('treated')['user_id'].count()
print("Group sizes:\n", group_counts)

# Flag differential attrition if groups deviate from expected 5,000
for g, n in group_counts.items():
    if abs(n - 5000) > 50:          # >1% deviation from expected
        print(f"⚠️  Group {g} has {n} users (expected 5,000). Investigate attrition.")

# ── 1. Difference-in-means ──────────────────────────────────────────────────

treat = df.loc[df['treated'] == 1, 'conversion']
ctrl  = df.loc[df['treated'] == 0, 'conversion']

mean_treat = treat.mean()
mean_ctrl  = ctrl.mean()
ate_dm     = mean_treat - mean_ctrl

t_stat, p_val = ttest_ind(treat, ctrl)
n_treat, n_ctrl = len(treat), len(ctrl)

# 95% CI for difference in proportions
se_dm = np.sqrt(treat.var() / n_treat + ctrl.var() / n_ctrl)
ci_lo, ci_hi = ate_dm - 1.96 * se_dm, ate_dm + 1.96 * se_dm

print("\n── Difference-in-Means ─────────────────────────────")
print(f"  Control conversion rate : {mean_ctrl:.4f}")
print(f"  Treatment conversion rate: {mean_treat:.4f}")
print(f"  ATE (DiM)               : {ate_dm:.4f}")
print(f"  95% CI                  : [{ci_lo:.4f}, {ci_hi:.4f}]")
print(f"  t-stat: {t_stat:.3f}  |  p-value: {p_val:.4f}")

# ── 2. OLS regression (equivalent to DiM, gives HC3 SEs) ───────────────────

model = smf.ols('conversion ~ treated', data=df).fit(cov_type='HC3')

ate_ols = model.params['treated']
ci_ols  = model.conf_int().loc['treated']
p_ols   = model.pvalues['treated']

print("\n── OLS with HC3 Robust SEs ─────────────────────────")
print(f"  ATE (OLS)  : {ate_ols:.4f}")
print(f"  95% CI     : [{ci_ols[0]:.4f}, {ci_ols[1]:.4f}]")
print(f"  p-value    : {p_ols:.4f}")

# ── 3. Permutation test (falsification) ────────────────────────────────────

np.random.seed(42)
n_perms   = 2000
perm_ates = np.empty(n_perms)
outcomes  = df['conversion'].values

for i in range(n_perms):
    shuffled         = np.random.permutation(outcomes)
    perm_ates[i]     = shuffled[:n_treat].mean() - shuffled[n_treat:].mean()

perm_p = np.mean(np.abs(perm_ates) >= np.abs(ate_dm))

print("\n── Permutation Test ────────────────────────────────")
print(f"  Observed ATE : {ate_dm:.4f}")
print(f"  Permutation p: {perm_p:.4f}")
if perm_p > 0.05:
    print("  ⚠️  Effect not distinguishable from chance under permutation.")
else:
    print("  ✅ Effect is statistically rare under the null distribution.")

# ── 4. Primary output ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────

ate = ate_ols                        # HC3-robust OLS is the preferred estimate
print(f'\nESTIMATE:{ate}')
```

---

## Stage 5: Interpretation template

Once you run this, the output will tell you:

> *"The new checkout flow changed conversion rate by **[ATE]** percentage points (95% CI: [lo, hi]). The permutation test confirms [significance status]. With 5,000 users per group, the experiment has strong power to detect effects as small as ~0.5 pp (Cohen's h ≈ 0.01 at 80% power)."*

**Key caveats to check after running:**
- If group sizes deviate notably from 5,000 → investigate whether attrition was differential
- No covariates are available, so regression adjustment for precision gain is not possible here — the DiM and OLS estimates will be identical
- Effect is local to the experiment population and time window; external validity requires separate judgment

</details>

### iv_strong_instrument

**Run 1**: {"has_code": true, "runs_without_error": true, "execution_error": null, "estimate": -5581.812588667875, "estimation_accurate": true, "diagnostic_coverage": 0.0, "must_include_results": {"first_stage": false, "confidence_interval": false}}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
from linearmodels.iv import IV2SLS

# ── Instrument: binary eligibility indicator ──────────────────────────────────
df['draft_eligible'] = (df['draft_number'] > 50).astype(int)

print("=" * 60)
print("STAGE 1 — Instrument Relevance")
print("=" * 60)
first_stage = smf.ols('served ~ draft_eligible', data=df).fit()
print(first_stage.summary().tables[1])
print(f"\nFirst-stage F-statistic : {first_stage.fvalue:.2f}")
print(f"First-stage coef        : {first_stage.params['draft_eligible']:.4f}")

if first_stage.fvalue < 10:
    print("🚨 WEAK INSTRUMENT — F < 10. 2SLS estimates are unreliable.")
elif first_stage.fvalue < 100:
    print("⚠️  Moderate instrument — F < 100. Some bias possible.")
else:
    print("✅ Strong instrument — F ≥ 100.")

# ── 2SLS ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
print("\n" + "=" * 60)
print("STAGE 2 — 2SLS (IV) Estimation")
print("=" * 60)
iv_model = IV2SLS.from_formula(
    'earnings ~ 1 + [served ~ draft_eligible]',
    data=df
).fit(cov_type='robust')
print(iv_model.summary)

late        = iv_model.params['served']
se          = iv_model.std_errors['served']
ci_lo, ci_hi = iv_model.conf_int().loc['served']

# ── Naive OLS for comparison ──────────────────────────────────────────────────
print("=" * 60)
print("STAGE 3 — Naive OLS (for bias reference)")
print("=" * 60)
ols_model = smf.ols('earnings ~ served', data=df).fit()
ols_est   = ols_model.params['served']
print(ols_model.summary().tables[1])

# ── Reduced form (sanity check) ───────────────────────────────────────────────
print("=" * 60)
print("STAGE 4 — Reduced Form (instrument → outcome directly)")
print("=" * 60)
rf_model = smf.ols('earnings ~ draft_eligible', data=df).fit()
rf_est   = rf_model.params['draft_eligible']
print(rf_model.summary().tables[1])
print(f"\nReduced-form effect of eligibility on earnings: {rf_est:.2f}")
print(f"  ≈ First-stage × LATE: "
      f"{first_stage.params['draft_eligible']:.4f} × {late:.2f} "
      f"= {first_stage.params['draft_eligible'] * late:.2f}  (should be ~equal)")

# ── Summary ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
print("\n" + "=" * 60)
print("RESULTS SUMMARY")
print("=" * 60)
print(f"  Naive OLS estimate  : {ols_est:>10.2f}  (likely biased)")
print(f"  IV/2SLS LATE        : {late:>10.2f}  (effect for compliers)")
print(f"  Std error           : {se:>10.2f}")
print(f"  95% CI              : [{ci_lo:.2f}, {ci_hi:.2f}]")
print(f"  First-stage F       : {first_stage.fvalue:>10.2f}")
print()
print("  Who are compliers? Individuals who would serve when draft-eligible")
print("  (draft_number > 50) but would NOT serve otherwise.")
print("  LATE ≠ ATE — this estimate does not generalise to non-compliers.")

print(f'\nESTIMATE:{late}')
```

---

**What this code does and why each piece matters:**

| Step | What | Why |
|---|---|---|
| **Binary instrument** | `draft_number > 50 → draft_eligible` | IV needs a clear first-stage lever |
| **First-stage F** | Regress `served ~ draft_eligible` | F < 10 → weak instrument, estimates collapse toward OLS bias |
| **2SLS** | `IV2SLS` with robust SEs | Corrects for endogeneity in `served` (selection into service) |
| **OLS comparison** | Naive `earnings ~ served` | Reveals direction of selection bias (volunteers vs. draftees differ) |
| **Reduced form** | `earnings ~ draft_eligible` | Always-valid sanity check; RF ≈ first-stage × LATE if model is right |

**Interpreting the LATE**: The estimate captures the earnings effect of military service *for compliers* — individuals whose service decision was actually changed by the draft lottery. If draft eligibility also affects earnings through channels other than service (exclusion restriction violation), the estimate would be biased. That's the one assumption here that cannot be tested statistically and requires substantive argument.

</details>

---

### matching_ate

**Run 1**: {"has_code": true, "runs_without_error": true, "execution_error": null, "estimate": 5.0404, "estimation_accurate": false, "diagnostic_coverage": 1.0, "must_include_results": {"propensity_score": true}}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup

- **Treatment**: Binary (0/1), assigned based on age, income, education
- **Estimand**: ATE (average treatment effect for everyone)
- **Method**: Inverse Probability Weighting (IPW)
- **Language**: Python

## Stage 2: Key Assumptions (brief)

| Assumption | Assessment |
|---|---|
| **Conditional independence** | ⚠️ Plausible *if* age, income, education fully capture selection. Untestable — unobserved factors (e.g., motivation, health) could still confound. |
| **Overlap / positivity** | Will check via propensity score histogram. ATE is especially sensitive here — it requires overlap across the *full* sample. |
| **SUTVA** | Plausible for individual-level treatments with no spillovers. |
| **Correct specification** | Will diagnose via weighted covariate balance (SMD). |

---

## Stage 3: IPW Implementation

```python
import numpy as np
import pandas as pd
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
from sklearn.linear_model import LogisticRegression
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

# ── 1. Estimate propensity scores ────────────────────────────────────────────
covariates = ['age', 'income', 'education']

# Standardize features for better logistic regression convergence
from sklearn.preprocessing import StandardScaler
scaler = StandardScaler()
X = scaler.fit_transform(df[covariates])
D = df['treatment'].values

ps_model = LogisticRegression(max_iter=1000, random_state=42)
ps_model.fit(X, D)
df['ps'] = ps_model.predict_proba(X)[:, 1]

# ── 2. Overlap / positivity check ────────────────────────────────────────────
print("=== Propensity Score Summary ===")
print(f"Treated  — min: {df.loc[df.treatment==1,'ps'].min():.3f} | "
      f"max: {df.loc[df.treatment==1,'ps'].max():.3f} | "
      f"mean: {df.loc[df.treatment==1,'ps'].mean():.3f}")
print(f"Control  — min: {df.loc[df.treatment==0,'ps'].min():.3f} | "
      f"max: {df.loc[df.treatment==0,'ps'].max():.3f} | "
      f"mean: {df.loc[df.treatment==0,'ps'].mean():.3f}")
print(f"PS < 0.05: {(df.ps < 0.05).mean():.3f}  |  PS > 0.95: {(df.ps > 0.95).mean():.3f}")

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(8, 4))
ax.hist(df.loc[df.treatment==1, 'ps'], bins=40, alpha=0.55,
        label='Treated', color='steelblue', density=True)
ax.hist(df.loc[df.treatment==0, 'ps'], bins=40, alpha=0.55,
        label='Control',  color='salmon',    density=True)
ax.set(xlabel='Propensity Score', ylabel='Density',
       title='Propensity Score Overlap (ATE)')
ax.legend(); plt.tight_layout(); plt.savefig('ps_overlap.png', dpi=150)
plt.show()

# ── 3. IPW weights for ATE ───────────────────────────────────────────────────
# ATE weights: 1/ps for treated, 1/(1−ps) for control
df['ipw'] = np.where(df['treatment'] == 1,
                     1 / df['ps'],
                     1 / (1 - df['ps']))

# Stabilised weights (reduces variance from extreme PS)
n = len(df)
p_treated = df['treatment'].mean()
df['sipw'] = np.where(df['treatment'] == 1,
                      p_treated / df['ps'],
                      (1 - p_treated) / (1 - df['ps']))

print(f"\n=== IPW Weight Diagnostics ===")
print(f"Max raw weight   : {df['ipw'].max():.2f}  (>20 flags poor overlap)")
print(f"Max stable weight: {df['sipw'].max():.2f}")

# ── 4. Horvitz–Thompson ATE estimate ─────────────────────────────────────────
# Direct IPW estimator: E[Y(1)] − E[Y(0)]
mu1 = np.sum(df['treatment'] * df['outcome'] / df['ps']) / n
mu0 = np.sum((1 - df['treatment']) * df['outcome'] / (1 - df['ps'])) / n
ate_ht = mu1 - mu0

# Weighted regression for SE (stabilised weights)
model_ipw = smf.wls('outcome ~ treatment', data=df, weights=df['sipw']).fit()
ate_wls   = model_ipw.params['treatment']
ci_lo, ci_hi = model_ipw.conf_int().loc['treatment']

print(f"\n=== ATE Estimates ===")
print(f"Horvitz–Thompson  : {ate_ht:.4f}")
print(f"Weighted OLS      : {ate_wls:.4f}  (95% CI: [{ci_lo:.4f}, {ci_hi:.4f}])")
print(f"ESTIMATE:{ate_wls:.4f}")

# ── 5. Covariate balance diagnostics (SMD after weighting) ───────────────────
def smd_weighted(cov, data):
    t = data.loc[data.treatment == 1, cov]
    c = data.loc[data.treatment == 0, cov]
    w_c = data.loc[data.treatment == 0, 'sipw']
    pooled_sd = np.sqrt((t.var() + c.var()) / 2)
    smd_before = (t.mean() - c.mean()) / pooled_sd
    smd_after  = (t.mean() - np.average(c, weights=w_c)) / pooled_sd
    return round(smd_before, 3), round(smd_after, 3)

print("\n=== Covariate Balance (SMD) ===")
print(f"{'Covariate':<12} {'SMD Before':>12} {'SMD After':>10} {'Balanced?':>10}")
print("-" * 46)
for cov in covariates:
    before, after = smd_weighted(cov, df)
    flag = '✓' if abs(after) < 0.1 else ('⚠' if abs(after) < 0.25 else '🚨')
    print(f"{cov:<12} {before:>12.3f} {after:>10.3f} {flag:>10}")
```

---

## Stage 4: Robustness Checks

```python
# ── Robustness 1: Propensity score trimming (drop PS outside [0.05, 0.95]) ──
df_trim = df[(df['ps'] >= 0.05) & (df['ps'] <= 0.95)].copy()
model_trim = smf.wls('outcome ~ treatment', data=df_trim,
                     weights=df_trim['sipw']).fit()
ate_trim = model_trim.params['treatment']
print(f"\nTrimmed ATE (PS in [0.05,0.95], n={len(df_trim)}): {ate_trim:.4f}")
print(f"Change vs. main estimate: {abs(ate_trim - ate_wls):.4f}")

# ── Robustness 2: Doubly-robust check (AIPW) ─────────────────────────────────
# Outcome models for treated and control separately
from sklearn.linear_model import LinearRegression

m1 = LinearRegression().fit(X[D == 1], df.loc[df.treatment==1, 'outcome'])
m0 = LinearRegression().fit(X[D == 0], df.loc[df.treatment==0, 'outcome'])

mu1_hat = m1.predict(X)
mu0_hat = m0.predict(X)

# AIPW / doubly-robust ATE
aipw = (mu1_hat - mu0_hat
        + df['treatment'] * (df['outcome'] - mu1_hat) / df['ps']
        - (1 - df['treatment']) * (df['outcome'] - mu0_hat) / (1 - df['ps']))
ate_dr = aipw.mean()
print(f"Doubly-Robust (AIPW) ATE     : {ate_dr:.4f}")

# ── Summary ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
print(f"\n{'='*45}")
print(f"  Main IPW estimate  : {ate_wls:.4f}  [{ci_lo:.4f}, {ci_hi:.4f}]")
print(f"  Trimmed IPW        : {ate_trim:.4f}")
print(f"  Doubly-Robust      : {ate_dr:.4f}")
print(f"{'='*45}")
```

---

## Stage 5: How to Interpret Results

Once you run this, here's what to look for:

| Check | What it means |
|---|---|
| **PS overlap plot** | Distributions should substantially overlap. Separation → poor overlap → unreliable ATE. |
| **Max weight** | Values >20 signal extreme weights driven by poor overlap — stabilised weights help. |
| **SMD after weighting** | All should be <0.1 (✓). Values 0.1–0.25 ⚠️ suggest residual imbalance. |
| **Trimmed vs. main** | Large change (>10–15%) means your estimate is sensitive to units with poor overlap. |
| **DR ≈ IPW** | Agreement between methods strengthens credibility. Large gap → at least one model is misspecified. |

> ⚠️ **Reminder**: All three estimates assume **unconfoundedness** — that age, income, and education capture the full selection process. If there are unobserved drivers of treatment assignment (e.g., health status, job type), these estimates will be biased in ways no statistical test can detect.

</details>

## Sharp RDD: Scholarship Effect on College GPA

Here's the complete analysis. The cutoff is **70**, the running variable is `test_score`, and treatment (`scholarship`) is deterministic at the cutoff — classic sharp RDD.

```r
# ── Dependencies ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
library(rdrobust)
library(rddensity)
library(ggplot2)

# ── Setup ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
cutoff <- 70
Y <- df$gpa
X <- df$test_score

# ── Stage 1: Visual Inspection ────────────────────────────────────────────────
# RD plot — eyeball the discontinuity before any formal estimation
rdplot(Y, X, c = cutoff,
       title = "RD Plot: Scholarship Effect on GPA",
       x.label = "Test Score (Running Variable)",
       y.label = "College GPA")

# ── Stage 2: Manipulation Check (McCrary Density Test) ────────────────────────
# Is there bunching just above 70? If students can game the cutoff,
# units on either side are no longer comparable.
density_test <- rddensity(X, c = cutoff)
summary(density_test)

rdplotdensity(density_test, X,
              title = "McCrary Density Test",
              xlabel = "Test Score",
              ylabel = "Density")

# ── Stage 3: Main RD Estimate ─────────────────────────────────────────────────
# rdrobust uses MSE-optimal bandwidth selection and bias-corrected
# robust inference by default — preferred over manual OLS with fixed windows.
rd_main <- rdrobust(Y, X, c = cutoff)
summary(rd_main)

# Extract the bias-corrected robust point estimate and CI
rd_estimate  <- rd_main$coef["Bias-Corrected", "Coeff"]
rd_ci_lower  <- rd_main$ci["Robust", "CI Lower"]
rd_ci_upper  <- rd_main$ci["Robust", "CI Upper"]
rd_se        <- rd_main$se["Robust", "Std. Err."]
rd_bw        <- rd_main$bws["h", "left"]   # optimal bandwidth (symmetric here)
rd_n_left    <- rd_main$N_h[1]             # obs within bandwidth, left of cutoff
rd_n_right   <- rd_main$N_h[2]            # obs within bandwidth, right of cutoff

cat("ESTIMATE:", rd_estimate, "\n")
cat("95% Robust CI: [", round(rd_ci_lower, 4), ",", round(rd_ci_upper, 4), "]\n")
cat("Robust SE:", round(rd_se, 4), "\n")
cat("Optimal bandwidth (h):", round(rd_bw, 2), "\n")
cat("Obs within bandwidth — Left:", rd_n_left, "| Right:", rd_n_right, "\n")

# ── Stage 4: Robustness Checks ────────────────────────────────────────────────

## 4a. Bandwidth sensitivity
# If the estimate flips or changes dramatically with different bandwidths,
# the result is fragile and should be reported cautiously.
cat("\n── Bandwidth Sensitivity ──\n")
bw_multipliers <- c(0.5, 0.75, 1.0, 1.25, 1.5)

for (m in bw_multipliers) {
  bw_val <- rd_bw * m
  rd_bw_test <- rdrobust(Y, X, c = cutoff, h = bw_val)
  est <- rd_bw_test$coef["Bias-Corrected", "Coeff"]
  ci_l <- rd_bw_test$ci["Robust", "CI Lower"]
  ci_u <- rd_bw_test$ci["Robust", "CI Upper"]
  cat(sprintf("  BW = %.2f (x%.2f) | Estimate = %.4f | CI: [%.4f, %.4f]\n",
              bw_val, m, est, ci_l, ci_u))
}

## 4b. Placebo cutoffs
# Run RDD at cutoffs where no real treatment exists.
# Finding a spurious "effect" at a placebo cutoff signals misspecification.
cat("\n── Placebo Cutoff Tests ──\n")
placebo_cutoffs <- c(55, 60, 65, 75, 80, 85)

for (pc in placebo_cutoffs) {
  tryCatch({
    rd_placebo <- rdrobust(Y, X, c = pc)
    est <- rd_placebo$coef["Bias-Corrected", "Coeff"]
    ci_l <- rd_placebo$ci["Robust", "CI Lower"]
    ci_u <- rd_placebo$ci["Robust", "CI Upper"]
    cat(sprintf("  Placebo cutoff = %d | Estimate = %.4f | CI: [%.4f, %.4f]\n",
                pc, est, ci_l, ci_u))
  }, error = function(e) {
    cat(sprintf("  Placebo cutoff = %d | Insufficient data near cutoff\n", pc))
  })
}

## 4c. Polynomial order sensitivity
# Local linear (p=1) is the standard. Check local quadratic (p=2) for stability.
cat("\n── Polynomial Order Sensitivity ──\n")
for (p in c(1, 2)) {
  rd_poly <- rdrobust(Y, X, c = cutoff, p = p)
  est <- rd_poly$coef["Bias-Corrected", "Coeff"]
  ci_l <- rd_poly$ci["Robust", "CI Lower"]
  ci_u <- rd_poly$ci["Robust", "CI Upper"]
  cat(sprintf("  p = %d | Estimate = %.4f | CI: [%.4f, %.4f]\n",
              p, est, ci_l, ci_u))
}

## 4d. Donut hole test (exclude ±1 point around cutoff)
# Helps diagnose whether narrow manipulation right at the boundary is
# driving the result. Effect should survive if manipulation is minimal.
cat("\n── Donut Hole Test (exclude |test_score - 70| <= 1) ──\n")
df_donut <- df[abs(df$test_score - cutoff) > 1, ]
rd_donut <- rdrobust(df_donut$gpa, df_donut$test_score, c = cutoff)
est_donut <- rd_donut$coef["Bias-Corrected", "Coeff"]
ci_l_donut <- rd_donut$ci["Robust", "CI Lower"]
ci_u_donut <- rd_donut$ci["Robust", "CI Upper"]
cat(sprintf("  Donut estimate = %.4f | CI: [%.4f, %.4f]\n",
            est_donut, ci_l_donut, ci_u_donut))

# ── Stage 5: Summary ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
cat("\n════════════════════════════════════════\n")
cat("RDD RESULTS SUMMARY\n")
cat("════════════════════════════════════════\n")
cat(sprintf("Running variable   : test_score (cutoff = 70)\n"))
cat(sprintf("Outcome            : College GPA\n"))
cat(sprintf("Treatment          : Scholarship (sharp)\n"))
cat(sprintf("Estimand           : LATE at the cutoff\n\n"))
cat(sprintf("Point estimate     : %.4f GPA points\n", rd_estimate))
cat(sprintf("95%% Robust CI      : [%.4f, %.4f]\n", rd_ci_lower, rd_ci_upper))
cat(sprintf("Robust SE          : %.4f\n", rd_se))
cat(sprintf("Optimal bandwidth  : %.2f test-score points\n", rd_bw))
cat(sprintf("Effective N        : %d (left) + %d (right)\n", rd_n_left, rd_n_right))
cat("════════════════════════════════════════\n")
cat("⚠️  LOCAL ESTIMATE: This effect applies only to students scoring near\n")
cat("   70 points — not to the full population of scholarship recipients.\n")
```

---

### What the code does — and why

| Stage | What's happening | Why it matters |
|---|---|---|
| **RD plot** | Bins observations, fits local polynomials on each side | First sanity check — the discontinuity should be visually obvious |
| **McCrary test** | Tests for a density jump at 70 | If students can game scores to land just above 70, they're not comparable to those just below → fatal violation |
| **`rdrobust` main estimate** | MSE-optimal bandwidth + bias-corrected robust CI | Better than OLS in a fixed window: data-driven bandwidth, corrects for regularization bias |
| **Bandwidth sensitivity** | Re-estimates at 50%–150% of optimal BW | Stable estimates across bandwidths = credible result; wild swings = fragile |
| **Placebo cutoffs** | Looks for "effects" where none should exist | A spurious effect at a fake cutoff signals model misspecification |
| **Polynomial order** | Compares `p=1` (local linear) vs `p=2` (quadratic) | Standard is local linear; quadratic confirms robustness |
| **Donut hole** | Drops ±1 point around the cutoff | Guards against narrow bunching right at 70 |

### Key caveats going in

- **Local interpretation**: The estimate is the LATE *at the cutoff* (students scoring ≈ 70). Students scoring 50 or 90 likely experience different effects.
- **Compound treatment**: If any other program (e.g., tutoring, housing aid) also uses a score-70 cutoff, the estimate captures the combined effect. Worth checking.
- **Continuity assumption**: We can't directly test it, but the covariate smoothness checks (placebo outcomes) and density test give indirect evidence.

</details>

---

### sc_basic

**Run 1**: {"has_code": true, "runs_without_error": true, "execution_error": null, "estimate": 0.8066, "estimation_accurate": true, "diagnostic_coverage": 0.0, "must_include_results": {"pre_treatment_fit": false}}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup

You have a clean classic synthetic control setup:
- **1 treated unit** (Unit 1), **9 donors** (Units 2–10)
- **20 pre-treatment periods** — solid for pre-fit optimization
- **10 post-treatment periods** — enough to estimate the effect
- **Language**: Python, numpy/pandas/scipy only

This is a standard simplex-constrained SC. I'll implement weights via `scipy.optimize.minimize` with the convex constraints (w ≥ 0, Σw = 1), then compute the post-treatment gap.

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

```python
import numpy as np
import pandas as pd
from scipy.optimize import minimize
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

# ── 0. Config ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
TREATED_UNIT   = 1
TREATMENT_TIME = 21          # first post-treatment period
PRE_PERIODS    = list(range(1,  TREATMENT_TIME))         # 1–20
POST_PERIODS   = list(range(TREATMENT_TIME, 31))         # 21–30

# ── 1. Pivot to wide format ────────────────────────────────────────────────────
wide = df.pivot(index="time", columns="unit", values="outcome")
# wide.columns = [1, 2, ..., 10]

donor_ids  = [u for u in wide.columns if u != TREATED_UNIT]  # [2..10]
n_donors   = len(donor_ids)

Y_treated = wide[TREATED_UNIT].values                   # shape (30,)
Y_donors  = wide[donor_ids].values                      # shape (30, 9)

# Pre-treatment slices (index 0-based: periods 1-20 → rows 0-19)
pre_idx  = [t - 1 for t in PRE_PERIODS]
post_idx = [t - 1 for t in POST_PERIODS]

Y_pre_treated = Y_treated[pre_idx]                      # (20,)
Y_pre_donors  = Y_donors[pre_idx, :]                    # (20, 9)

# ── 2. Constrained optimization: find simplex weights ─────────────────────────
# Objective: minimize pre-treatment RMSPE
#   min_w  ||Y_pre_treated - Y_pre_donors @ w||²
#   s.t.   w >= 0,  sum(w) = 1

def objective(w):
    residuals = Y_pre_treated - Y_pre_donors @ w
    return np.sum(residuals ** 2)                       # MSE (monotone in RMSPE)

def objective_grad(w):
    residuals = Y_pre_treated - Y_pre_donors @ w
    return -2 * Y_pre_donors.T @ residuals

constraints = {"type": "eq", "fun": lambda w: np.sum(w) - 1}
bounds      = [(0.0, 1.0)] * n_donors
w0          = np.ones(n_donors) / n_donors              # equal-weight start

result = minimize(
    objective,
    w0,
    jac=objective_grad,
    method="SLSQP",
    bounds=bounds,
    constraints=constraints,
    options={"ftol": 1e-12, "maxiter": 1000}
)

if not result.success:
    print(f"⚠️  Optimizer warning: {result.message}")

weights = result.x
weights = np.clip(weights, 0, 1)
weights /= weights.sum()                                # re-normalize after clip

# ── 3. Build synthetic control series ─────────────────────────────────────────
Y_synthetic = Y_donors @ weights                        # (30,) — full series

# ── 4. Pre-treatment fit diagnostics ──────────────────────────────────────────
pre_residuals = Y_pre_treated - Y_synthetic[pre_idx]
rmspe_pre     = np.sqrt(np.mean(pre_residuals ** 2))
outcome_sd    = np.std(Y_pre_treated)

print("=" * 55)
print("SYNTHETIC CONTROL — Fit Diagnostics")
print("=" * 55)
print(f"Pre-treatment RMSPE   : {rmspe_pre:.4f}")
print(f"Outcome std dev (pre) : {outcome_sd:.4f}")
print(f"RMSPE / SD            : {rmspe_pre / outcome_sd:.4f}  "
      f"{'✅ good' if rmspe_pre/outcome_sd < 0.10 else '⚠️  review fit'}")

# ── 5. Donor weight table ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────
weight_df = (
    pd.DataFrame({"donor_unit": donor_ids, "weight": weights})
      .sort_values("weight", ascending=False)
      .reset_index(drop=True)
)
weight_df["weight_pct"] = weight_df["weight"].map("{:.1%}".format)
print("\nDonor Weights:")
print(weight_df[["donor_unit", "weight_pct"]].to_string(index=False))

# ── 6. Treatment effect estimate ──────────────────────────────────────────────
post_gap      = Y_treated[post_idx] - Y_synthetic[post_idx]  # per-period gap
gap_estimate  = np.mean(post_gap)                             # ATT (avg post gap)
rmspe_post    = np.sqrt(np.mean(post_gap ** 2))

print(f"\nPost-treatment avg gap (ATT) : {gap_estimate:.4f}")
print(f"Post-treatment RMSPE         : {rmspe_post:.4f}")
print(f"Post/Pre RMSPE ratio         : {rmspe_post/rmspe_pre:.2f}")

# ── Required output ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
print(f"ESTIMATE:{gap_estimate:.4f}")

# ── 7. Plots ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
times = sorted(df["time"].unique())

fig, (ax1, ax2) = plt.subplots(2, 1, figsize=(10, 8), sharex=True)

# Panel A: Treated vs Synthetic
ax1.plot(times, Y_treated,   color="black",  lw=2,   label="Unit 1 (Treated)")
ax1.plot(times, Y_synthetic, color="steelblue", lw=2,
         linestyle="--", label="Synthetic Unit 1")
ax1.axvline(TREATMENT_TIME - 0.5, color="red", linestyle=":", lw=1.5,
            label="Treatment onset")
ax1.set_ylabel("Outcome")
ax1.set_title("Treated vs. Synthetic Control")
ax1.legend()

# Panel B: Gap (treatment effect over time)
ax2.bar(times, Y_treated - Y_synthetic,
        color=["tomato" if t >= TREATMENT_TIME else "lightgray" for t in times],
        edgecolor="white")
ax2.axhline(0, color="black", lw=0.8)
ax2.axvline(TREATMENT_TIME - 0.5, color="red", linestyle=":", lw=1.5)
ax2.axhline(gap_estimate, color="tomato", linestyle="--", lw=1.5,
            label=f"Avg post gap = {gap_estimate:.2f}")
ax2.set_xlabel("Time Period")
ax2.set_ylabel("Gap (Treated − Synthetic)")
ax2.set_title("Estimated Treatment Effect Over Time")
ax2.legend()

plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig("sc_results.png", dpi=150, bbox_inches="tight")
plt.show()
print("Plot saved → sc_results.png")
```

---

## What This Does, and Why Each Choice Matters

| Step | Decision | Why |
|---|---|---|
| **Convex weights** | `w ≥ 0, Σw = 1` | Ensures the synthetic unit is an *interpolation*, not extrapolation — the core identifying assumption of SC |
| **SLSQP optimizer** | Gradient-aware, handles equality + box constraints | More reliable than gradient-free methods for this smooth quadratic objective |
| **Pre-treatment RMSPE / SD** | Ratio < 0.10 = good fit | If this ratio is large, the synthetic control can't faithfully mimic the treated unit's trajectory — the whole exercise breaks down |
| **Post/Pre RMSPE ratio** | Used for permutation inference | The treated unit's ratio should be an outlier vs. placebo units |
| **ATT = mean post-period gap** | Average treatment effect on the treated | Interpretable summary; the per-period bars let you see if the effect grows, fades, or is noisy |

---

## Stage 4: Recommended Robustness Checks

Once you run this and check the pre-treatment fit, I'd suggest:

1. **In-space placebo** — Apply the same optimization to each donor unit as a fake treated unit. If Unit 1's post/pre RMSPE ratio ranks in the top 10% of the distribution, the p-value ≤ 0.10.
2. **Leave-one-out** — Re-run dropping the highest-weight donor. If the estimate collapses, the result is fragile.
3. **In-time placebo** — Pretend treatment happened at period 11. There should be no gap in periods 11–20.

Want me to generate code for any of these?

</details>

### timeseries_causalimpact

**Run 1**: {"has_code": true, "runs_without_error": true, "execution_error": null, "estimate": 3.3292, "estimation_accurate": true, "diagnostic_coverage": 0.0, "must_include_results": {"pre_treatment": false, "confidence_interval": false}}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Interrupted Time Series — Segmented Regression

A few quick notes before the code:

**Assumptions being relied on (flag if any are violated):**
- ✅ Pre-period is 40 points — adequate for a basic model
- ⚠️ *No concurrent events at period 41?* This is untestable from data — you'll need to assert it from context
- ✅ Mild upward trend → included as `time` slope; HAC standard errors handle residual autocorrelation
- ✅ No specialized packages — pure `statsmodels` + `numpy`

---

```python
import numpy as np
import pandas as pd
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

# ── 0. Settings ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
INTERVENTION = 41          # first post-treatment period
N_POST       = len(df[df['period'] >= INTERVENTION])   # 20 periods

# ── 1. Build ITS variables ────────────────────────────────────────────────
#   time       : overall time index (captures pre-existing trend)
#   post       : 0 = pre-period, 1 = post-period  (level shift)
#   time_since : periods elapsed since intervention (slope change)
df = df.copy()
df['time']       = df['period']
df['post']       = (df['period'] >= INTERVENTION).astype(int)
df['time_since'] = np.maximum(0, df['period'] - INTERVENTION)
# time_since = 0 at period 41, 1 at 42 … 19 at 60

# ── 2. Quick pre-period diagnostics ──────────────────────────────────────
pre = df[df['post'] == 0]
print(f"Pre-period observations : {len(pre)}")
print(f"Pre-period mean outcome : {pre['outcome'].mean():.3f}")
print(f"Pre-period std outcome  : {pre['outcome'].std():.3f}")

# Simple trend check: correlation of outcome with time in pre-period
r = pre[['time', 'outcome']].corr().iloc[0, 1]
print(f"Pre-period trend corr   : {r:.3f}  {'(mild upward — expected)' if 0 < r < 0.7 else ''}")

# ── 3. Segmented regression ───────────────────────────────────────────────
# HAC (Newey-West) standard errors with lag=4 to handle autocorrelation
model = smf.ols('outcome ~ time + post + time_since', data=df).fit(
    cov_type='HAC', cov_kwds={'maxlags': 4}
)
print("\n", model.summary())

b_intercept  = model.params['Intercept']
b_time       = model.params['time']
b_post       = model.params['post']        # immediate level shift
b_time_since = model.params['time_since']  # change in slope

ci_post       = model.conf_int().loc['post']
ci_time_since = model.conf_int().loc['time_since']

print(f"\n── Effect decomposition ───────────────────────────────────────")
print(f"Immediate level shift (β_post)   : {b_post:+.3f}  "
      f"95% CI [{ci_post[0]:.3f}, {ci_post[1]:.3f}]")
print(f"Slope change per period          : {b_time_since:+.3f}  "
      f"95% CI [{ci_time_since[0]:.3f}, {ci_time_since[1]:.3f}]")

# ── 4. Average causal effect over the post-period ─────────────────────────
# At each post-period point t:
#   effect(t) = β_post + β_time_since × time_since(t)
# Average across all 20 post-period observations:
post_ts = df[df['post'] == 1]['time_since'].values   # [0, 1, ..., 19]
avg_effect = b_post + b_time_since * post_ts.mean()  # mean(time_since) = 9.5

print(f"\nAverage causal effect (post-period) : {avg_effect:+.4f}")
print(f"  (β_post={b_post:.4f}  +  β_time_since={b_time_since:.4f} × "
      f"mean_time_since={post_ts.mean():.1f})")
print(f"ESTIMATE:{avg_effect:.4f}")

# ── 5. Pre-period model fit (MAPE) ────────────────────────────────────────
df['fitted'] = model.fittedvalues
pre_fitted   = df[df['post'] == 0]
mape = (np.abs(pre_fitted['outcome'] - pre_fitted['fitted'])
        / np.abs(pre_fitted['outcome'])).mean() * 100
print(f"\nPre-period MAPE : {mape:.2f}%  "
      f"({'good' if mape < 5 else 'acceptable' if mape < 10 else 'poor — interpret with caution'})")

# ── 6. Counterfactual projection ──────────────────────────────────────────
# What would have happened without the intervention?
# Force post=0 and time_since=0 for counterfactual
df_cf = df.copy()
df_cf['post']       = 0
df_cf['time_since'] = 0
df['counterfactual'] = model.predict(df_cf)

# ── 7. Plot ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
fig, axes = plt.subplots(2, 1, figsize=(11, 8))

# Panel A: observed + fitted + counterfactual
ax = axes[0]
ax.scatter(df['period'], df['outcome'], color='black', s=20,
           alpha=0.7, label='Observed', zorder=3)
ax.plot(df['period'], df['fitted'],        color='steelblue',
        lw=2, label='Fitted')
ax.plot(df[df['post']==1]['period'],
        df[df['post']==1]['counterfactual'], color='steelblue',
        lw=2, ls='--', label='Counterfactual')
ax.axvline(INTERVENTION - 0.5, color='crimson', ls=':', lw=1.5,
           label=f'Intervention (period {INTERVENTION})')
ax.fill_between(df[df['post']==1]['period'],
                df[df['post']==1]['counterfactual'],
                df[df['post']==1]['fitted'],
                alpha=0.15, color='steelblue', label='Estimated gap')
ax.set_title('Interrupted Time Series — Segmented Regression')
ax.set_ylabel('Outcome')
ax.legend(fontsize=8)

# Panel B: per-period effect in post-period
post_df = df[df['post'] == 1].copy()
post_df['effect'] = b_post + b_time_since * post_df['time_since']

ax2 = axes[1]
ax2.bar(post_df['period'], post_df['effect'],
        color='steelblue', alpha=0.6, label='Per-period effect')
ax2.axhline(avg_effect, color='crimson', ls='--', lw=1.5,
            label=f'Avg effect = {avg_effect:.3f}')
ax2.axhline(0, color='black', lw=0.8)
ax2.set_xlabel('Period')
ax2.set_ylabel('Causal Effect')
ax2.set_title('Estimated Causal Effect per Post-Period')
ax2.legend(fontsize=8)

plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig('its_segmented_regression.png', dpi=150)
plt.show()
print("Plot saved → its_segmented_regression.png")
```

---

### How to read the output

| Term | Meaning |
|---|---|
| `β_post` | Immediate **level jump** at period 41 (intercept shift) |
| `β_time_since` | **Slope change** — each additional post-period adds this much extra (or less) |
| **Average causal effect** | `β_post + β_time_since × 9.5` — the mean effect across periods 41–60 |

The average effect accounts for the fact that a slope change accumulates over time — reporting only the level shift would understate (or overstate) the true average impact.

**Before trusting the estimate**, the key question to answer outside the code: *Was there anything else that changed at period 41?* That's the single assumption this method cannot test.

</details>

### auditor_subtle_flaw

**Run 1**: {"pedagogy": 1.0, "safety": 1.0, "actionable": 1.0, "overall": 1.0}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Audit Report: Mentoring Program → Promotion Rate

**Method audited**: Propensity Score Matching (ATT)
**Overall assessment**: 🔴 **Red — fatal issue present. Results cannot be trusted as-is.**

---

### Summary

The analysis has one fatal flaw that likely corrupts the entire estimate: `performance_rating_q3` is a **post-treatment variable** included as a covariate in the propensity score model. This is a form of collider bias / post-treatment conditioning that can bias the ATT in either direction. The excellent balance (SMDs < 0.05) is not a green light — it may be **artificially achieved** by conditioning on a variable that absorbed treatment variation. Two additional serious threats (unmeasured confounders, SUTVA violation) compound the problem. The 15pp estimate should not be reported without correction.

---

## Findings

---

### 🔴 FATAL: Post-treatment covariate in propensity score model

**Category**: 1 (Assumption Violations) + 3 (Data Threats)

**Explanation**: `performance_rating_q3` was measured *one quarter after the mentoring program started*. Mentoring can directly affect performance ratings, so this variable sits **on the causal path** from treatment to outcome (mentoring → Q3 rating → promotion). Including it in the propensity score model does two damaging things simultaneously:

1. **Blocks the mediated effect**: If part of mentoring's effect on promotion runs *through* improved performance ratings, conditioning on the rating strips that effect out. Your ATT will be artificially deflated — you're estimating the effect of mentoring *holding performance constant*, which is not what anyone asked.

2. **Opens a collider path**: If there's an unmeasured common cause of Q3 rating and promotion (e.g., employee ambition, manager favoritism), conditioning on Q3 rating creates a spurious association between mentoring and that unmeasured factor. This can bias the estimate in *either direction* — including inflating the 15pp.

The direction and magnitude of bias are unknowable without knowing the true causal structure. The good SMDs you're seeing should be treated with suspicion, not reassurance — excellent balance on a post-treatment variable is a warning sign, not a success.

**Fix**: Remove `performance_rating_q3` from the propensity score model entirely. If you want to control for prior performance, use **pre-treatment** ratings (Q1/Q2 from before the program started). Re-run matching and recheck balance.

**Diagnostic — check how much the PS changes when you drop the post-treatment variable**:

```r
library(MatchIt)
library(cobalt)

# Current (flawed) model — includes post-treatment covariate
m_flawed <- matchit(
  mentoring ~ age + tenure + department + education_level + performance_rating_q3,
  data = df, method = "nearest", estimand = "ATT", distance = "glm"
)

# Corrected model — pre-treatment only
m_corrected <- matchit(
  mentoring ~ age + tenure + department + education_level,
  data = df, method = "nearest", estimand = "ATT", distance = "glm"
)

# Compare propensity score distributions
par(mfrow = c(1, 2))
hist(m_flawed$distance[df$mentoring == 1], main = "Flawed PS (treated)",
     xlab = "PS", breaks = 30, col = "salmon")
hist(m_corrected$distance[df$mentoring == 1], main = "Corrected PS (treated)",
     xlab = "PS", breaks = 30, col = "steelblue")

# Compare balance
bal_flawed    <- bal.tab(m_flawed,    thresholds = c(m = 0.1))
bal_corrected <- bal.tab(m_corrected, thresholds = c(m = 0.1))
print(bal_flawed)
print(bal_corrected)
```

```python
from sklearn.linear_model import LogisticRegression
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np

covariates_flawed     = ['age', 'tenure', 'department', 'education_level', 'performance_rating_q3']
covariates_corrected  = ['age', 'tenure', 'department', 'education_level']

for label, covs in [("Flawed", covariates_flawed), ("Corrected", covariates_corrected)]:
    X = pd.get_dummies(df[covs], drop_first=True).values
    D = df['mentoring'].values
    model = LogisticRegression(max_iter=1000).fit(X, D)
    ps = model.predict_proba(X)[:, 1]
    print(f"\n{label} PS — Treated: [{ps[D==1].min():.3f}, {ps[D==1].max():.3f}]"
          f"  Control: [{ps[D==0].min():.3f}, {ps[D==0].max():.3f}]")
```

---

### 🟠 SERIOUS: Unmeasured confounders — selection into mentoring is non-random

**Category**: 1 (Assumption Violations) + 2 (Identification Threats)

**Explanation**: Conditional independence requires that *every* variable affecting both mentoring participation and promotion is in your model. Mentoring programs typically select on dimensions you haven't measured:

- **Employee motivation / ambition**: Driven employees seek out mentors *and* get promoted. Not in your model.
- **Manager sponsorship**: Managers nominate high-potential employees for mentoring. Manager quality affects both mentoring assignment and promotion decisions. Not in your model.
- **Whether participation was voluntary vs. assigned**: Employees who opted in are systematically different from those assigned — and you likely can't separate these.
- **Prior performance trajectory**: You have Q3 (contaminated), but not Q1/Q2 performance. A rising performer looks very different from a plateaued one with the same Q3 rating.

The E-value test below quantifies how strong an unmeasured confounder would need to be to explain away the 15pp effect. A small E-value (< 2) would be deeply concerning.

**Diagnostic — E-value sensitivity analysis**:

```r
library(EValue)

# Convert the 15pp ATT to a risk ratio approximation
# Baseline promotion rate among controls (replace with your actual value)
baseline_rate <- 0.20  # example: 20% baseline

att_pp        <- 0.15
treated_rate  <- baseline_rate + att_pp
rr_est        <- treated_rate / baseline_rate

# Approximate CI (replace se_att with your actual SE)
se_att   <- 0.04  # example SE — replace with your actual
rr_lo    <- (baseline_rate + att_pp - 1.96 * se_att) / baseline_rate
rr_hi    <- (baseline_rate + att_pp + 1.96 * se_att) / baseline_rate

evalues.RR(est = rr_est, lo = rr_lo, hi = rr_hi)
# Rule of thumb: E-value < 2 = easy to explain away; > 3 = more robust
```

```python
import numpy as np

def e_value(rr):
    return rr + np.sqrt(rr * (rr - 1))

baseline_rate = 0.20      # replace with actual
att_pp        = 0.15
treated_rate  = baseline_rate + att_pp
rr_est        = treated_rate / baseline_rate

print(f"Approximate RR: {rr_est:.2f}")
print(f"E-value: {e_value(rr_est):.2f}")
print("Interpretation: an unmeasured confounder would need RR >"
      f" {e_value(rr_est):.2f} with both treatment and outcome to explain away the result.")
```

**Fix**: Add pre-treatment performance ratings (Q1/Q2), and if possible, an indicator for voluntary vs. assigned participation. Consider whether a doubly-robust estimator (AIPW) would reduce sensitivity to the outcome model.

---

### 🟠 SERIOUS: SUTVA violation — mentored employees influence control group

**Category**: 1 (Assumption Violations)

**Explanation**: SUTVA requires that one employee's treatment doesn't affect another's outcome. In a mentoring program, this is structurally implausible: mentored employees share skills, advice, and visibility with colleagues in the same department. Your control group (non-mentored employees in the same departments) is likely *contaminated* — they may benefit indirectly from being around mentored peers. If so, the control group's promotion rate is *inflated*, and your 15pp ATT is an *underestimate* of the true effect.

The risk is highest within departments. You're controlling for department in the PS model, which means your matches are often within-department — exactly where spillovers are most likely.

**Diagnostic**:

```r
library(fixest)

# Among non-mentored employees, does fraction of mentored colleagues
# in the same department predict promotion?
control_df <- df[df$mentoring == 0, ]

spillover_test <- feols(
  promoted ~ frac_mentored_in_dept + age + tenure + education_level,
  data    = control_df,
  cluster = ~department
)
summary(spillover_test)
# Significant positive coefficient on frac_mentored_in_dept → spillover exists
```

**Fix**: Prefer matching across departments (or at least flag that within-department matching maximizes spillover risk). If spillover is confirmed, the estimand needs to be redefined — you're estimating a net effect that conflates direct treatment with peer contamination.

---

### 🟡 MINOR: ATT may not match the business question

**Category**: 2 (Identification Threats) + 5 (External Validity)

**Explanation**: ATT answers: *"What is the effect of mentoring on the employees who were actually mentored?"* That's a valid question if the goal is evaluating the program for its current participants. But if the goal is *"Should we expand this program to all employees?"*, you need the ATE — and your matched sample is restricted to the overlap region, which may not represent employees who have never been mentored (perhaps because they're very different in covariates). The 15pp should not be cited as the expected effect for a company-wide rollout without this caveat.

---

### 🟡 MINOR: Standard errors and sample size not reported

**Category**: 4 (Statistical Threats)

**Explanation**: You report p < 0.01 but not the SE, N, or how SEs are computed. After matching, standard OLS SEs understate uncertainty because matched units are not i.i.d. — they're paired. Bootstrap SEs on the matched sample or cluster-robust SEs at the match-pair level are more appropriate. Also: if the number of matched pairs is small (< 100), the 15pp estimate has wide uncertainty that the p-value alone doesn't convey.

```r
# After re-running with corrected covariates:
m_corrected <- matchit(
  mentoring ~ age + tenure + department + education_level,
  data = df, method = "nearest", estimand = "ATT"
)

matched_df <- match.data(m_corrected)

# Use subclass/pair-clustered SEs
att_model <- feols(
  promoted ~ mentoring,
  data    = matched_df,
  weights = ~weights,
  cluster = ~subclass   # clusters at the match pair level
)
summary(att_model)
```

---

## Recommendations (priority order)

| # | Action | Severity addressed |
|---|--------|--------------------|
| 1 | **Remove `performance_rating_q3`** from the PS model immediately. Substitute Q1/Q2 pre-treatment ratings if available. Re-run matching and recheck balance. | Fatal |
| 2 | **Run E-value sensitivity analysis** to quantify how exposed the result is to unmeasured confounders (motivation, manager quality). | Serious |
| 3 | **Test for SUTVA violation** using the within-department spillover regression. If significant, note that the 15pp is a lower bound on the true direct effect. | Serious |
| 4 | **Report bootstrap or pair-clustered SEs** alongside the point estimate. Clarify whether ATT is the right estimand for the decision being made. | Minor |
| 5 | If pre-treatment performance data is unavailable and unobserved selection remains severe, **consider a stronger design**: if the program had a capacity cutoff (e.g., only N slots), an RDD on the selection score would be far more credible than matching. | Design |

---

> ⚠️ One item added to `references/lessons.md`: matching skills should flag post-treatment covariates in the PS model as a fatal error before proceeding to balance checks — good balance on a post-treatment variable is misleading, not reassuring.

</details>

### auditor_thorough_analysis
