Good. No existing plan on file — I'll run the full audit from scratch. Let me work through all five categories.

---

# Audit Report: POS System DiD Analysis

**Date**: 2026-04-03
**Method audited**: Difference-in-Differences (Callaway-Sant'Anna)
**Overall assessment**: 🟡 **Yellow** — one potentially fatal threat (measurement artifact), two serious threats. The 12.3% estimate is defensible under certain conditions, but requires three targeted fixes before this analysis should be presented as causal evidence.

---

## Summary

The analysis is methodologically competent on the surface — flat pre-trends, no attrition, appropriate estimator, clustered SEs. But beneath that surface lie three threats that should concern you. First and most urgently: if your sales data *comes from the new POS system*, the 12.3% increase might be a recording artifact, not a real economic effect. Second, treatment assignment based on IT infrastructure readiness — decided 6 months before rollout — creates both a serious selection story and an anticipation contamination that the pre-trends test cannot fully rule out. Third, retail stores compete for customers, and you haven't checked whether treated stores captured market share from control stores.

---

## Findings

### 🔴 Potentially Fatal: Outcome measured by the instrument being tested

**Category**: Data Quality (Category 3)

**Explanation**: Your outcome is "weekly sales." Your treatment is a new POS (point-of-sale) system. Here's the uncomfortable question: *where does your weekly sales data come from?* If it comes from the POS system — as it typically does in retail — then you have a measurement equivalence problem. The old system recorded transactions one way; the new system records them another. Differences in how each system handles voids, returns, loyalty redemptions, or multi-tender transactions could mechanically inflate the sales figure. The new system isn't selling more; it's *counting* more. This would register as a 12.3% "increase" in the data with zero causal relationship to any real economic activity.

This is not a hypothetical concern. POS migrations are notorious for this. A classic tell: the effect appears immediately at week 26 rather than building over time as staff adapt.

**Diagnostic**:
```r
# 1. Plot the weekly ATT trajectory — does the effect jump on day 1 or build?
library(fixest)
library(did)

cs <- att_gt(yname = "sales", tname = "week", idname = "store_id",
             gname = "first_treat", data = df)

# Dynamic aggregation: effect by weeks-since-treatment
agg_dynamic <- aggte(cs, type = "dynamic")
ggdid(agg_dynamic,
      title = "Weekly ATT: Does effect appear immediately or build?")

# Red flag: a step-function jump at week 0 with no pre-period drift
# suggests measurement artifact, not behavioral change.

# 2. If you have an independent sales source (payment processor, bank deposits,
#    shipment/inventory data), correlate it with POS-recorded sales
# pre/post for treated stores. Divergence post-treatment = recording artifact.
```

**Suggested fix**: Find an *independent* verification of sales — payment processor records, daily bank deposits, inventory depletion data, or financial statements. Cross-validate treated stores' POS-recorded sales against this source pre and post. If they track closely pre-treatment but diverge post-treatment, you have a measurement problem. If independent data confirms the effect, you have a clean result. Without this validation, frame the 12.3% explicitly as "POS-recorded sales" and flag the measurement caveat prominently.

---

### 🟠 Serious: Treatment assignment is a proxy for unobserved store quality

**Category**: Identification (Category 2)

**Explanation**: Stores were assigned to treatment based on "IT infrastructure readiness." This is not random — it's a systematic characteristic. Now ask yourself: *what kind of stores have better IT infrastructure?* Probably larger stores, newer stores, stores in growing markets, or stores with more capable management. All of those characteristics correlate with sales trajectory.

Your parallel trends test passed — but that test only tells you trends were similar *during the 25 pre-treatment weeks you observed*. It doesn't rule out the possibility that IT-ready stores were on a fundamentally different trajectory that only becomes visible post-week 26. For example: if IT-ready stores are in growing neighborhoods, and that growth accelerates in week 26 for reasons unrelated to the POS system (a competitor closes, a new residential development opens nearby), DiD attributes the neighborhood growth to the POS upgrade.

The core backdoor path is: `IT Infrastructure Readiness ← Store Quality → Post-Week-26 Sales Growth`. This path is open and unblocked unless you can argue store quality has no differential effect on post-treatment trends.

**Diagnostic**:
```r
library(fixest)

# 1. Do treated and control stores look different on observable pre-treatment characteristics?
# (Larger pre-treatment difference = more reason to worry about unobservables)
pre_df <- df[df$week < 26, ]
balance <- pre_df |>
  dplyr::group_by(store_id) |>
  dplyr::summarize(
    treated = max(treated),
    avg_sales   = mean(sales),
    sales_vol   = sd(sales),          # volatility
    avg_items   = mean(items_sold, na.rm = TRUE),  # if available
    store_size  = first(store_size)   # if available
  )

# Compare means and SMDs
library(cobalt)
love.plot(treated ~ avg_sales + sales_vol + store_size,
          data = balance, binary = "std",
          thresholds = c(m = 0.1))

# 2. Sensitivity analysis: how strong would an unobserved confounder need to be
#    to explain away the 12.3% estimate?
# (If you have access to sensemakr)
library(sensemakr)
# Fit OLS version
ols_model <- lm(sales ~ treated * post + factor(store_id) + factor(week), data = df)
sens <- sensemakr(ols_model, treatment = "treated",
                  benchmark_covariates = "avg_pre_sales")  # strongest observed covariate
summary(sens)
```

**Suggested fix**: (1) Report a covariate balance table comparing treated vs. control stores on *all available pre-treatment observables* — size, location type, historical sales level, volatility. (2) Run a Rosenbaum-style sensitivity analysis asking: how strong would an unobserved confounder need to be to reduce the effect to zero? If the answer is "a confounder with R² ~ 0.01," your result is fragile. (3) If you have regional or market-level data, add market fixed effects to absorb geographic growth trends and check if the estimate moves.

---

### 🟠 Serious: Anticipation — treated stores knew for 6 months

**Category**: Assumption Violations (Category 1)

**Explanation**: Your pre-trends test checks whether outcomes were trending differently before treatment. But here's what it *cannot* detect: if treated stores started preparing for the new POS system (training staff, reorganizing inventory workflows, planning store layout changes) during the pre-period, and if that preparation improved their operational efficiency *before* the system went live, then your baseline for treated stores is already contaminated by anticipation.

The math is subtle: anticipation effects that don't show up in *sales* during the pre-period won't fail the parallel trends test. Staff training doesn't immediately change sales — but it might cause a jump *at week 26* when the system launches with a fully prepared team. You'd attribute the full effect to the POS system, when part of it is "we trained for 25 weeks." The counterfactual (what sales would look like without the POS announcement) would not include that training, so your estimate overstates the effect of simply *deploying* the POS system.

There's also a 6-month vs. 25-week tension: the decision was made ~26 weeks before rollout. If your data starts at week 1, treated stores have known since before your data begins. Anticipation that predates your sample is invisible to the event study — you cannot test for it.

**Diagnostic**:
```r
library(fixest)

# Test: are pre-treatment coefficients sloping upward at the END of the
# pre-period? (A ramp-up toward week 26 is a red flag for anticipation)
df$ttp <- df$week - 26  # time-to-treatment

es <- feols(sales ~ i(ttp, ref = -1) | store_id + week,
            data = df, cluster = ~store_id)

iplot(es,
      main = "Event Study: Check for anticipation ramp-up near week 0",
      xlab = "Weeks Relative to POS Launch")

# Look specifically at t = -4, -3, -2: are they rising?
# If yes: treated stores were changing behavior before treatment.

# Also: test a shifted baseline where you DROP the last 4 pre-weeks
# to see if effect estimate changes materially
df_trimmed <- df[df$ttp < -4 | df$ttp >= 0, ]
es_trimmed <- feols(sales ~ treated:post | store_id + week,
                    data = df_trimmed, cluster = ~store_id)
cat("ATT dropping last 4 pre-weeks:", coef(es_trimmed)["treated:post"], "\n")
```

**Suggested fix**: (1) Check whether your event study shows a creeping positive trend in the 4–8 weeks just before treatment — this is the classic anticipation signature. (2) Report the estimate using an "announcement date" as the treatment date (i.e., the week stores were officially notified), not the implementation date. (3) In the `did` package, use `anticipation = 4` (or whatever number of advance-notice weeks you believe is relevant) to formally allow anticipation in the C&S framework.

---

### 🟠 Serious: Retail spillovers — control stores may have lost customers to treated stores

**Category**: Assumption Violations (Category 1) / Identification (Category 2)

**Explanation**: SUTVA requires that treated stores' POS upgrade doesn't affect control stores' sales. In retail, that assumption is fragile. If the new POS system makes treated stores faster at checkout, better at managing inventory (fewer stockouts), or more effective at loyalty promotions, customers who previously split their spending between nearby treated and control stores might shift toward treated stores. The control stores' sales would *fall* — not because of anything about them, but because their nearby competitors improved. This makes the DiD estimate too large: you're comparing a rising treated group to a declining (not stable) control group.

You reported checking composition (no closures, no entry). That's necessary but not sufficient — SUTVA can be violated even with a stable panel.

**Diagnostic**:
```r
library(fixest)

# Test: among control stores, do those geographically closer to treated stores
# perform worse in the post period?
# (Requires store location / distance data)

control_df <- df[df$treated == 0, ]

# Create: post = (week >= 26)
control_df$post <- as.integer(control_df$week >= 26)

spillover_test <- feols(sales ~ post:dist_to_nearest_treated | store_id + week,
                         data = control_df, cluster = ~store_id)
summary(spillover_test)
# Negative coefficient on post:dist_to_nearest_treated would mean:
# control stores CLOSER to treated stores lost MORE sales — consistent with spillover.

# If you don't have distance data, at minimum plot control stores' average
# sales pre vs post. A visible decline is a red flag.
control_post <- df[df$treated == 0, ] |>
  dplyr::group_by(week) |>
  dplyr::summarize(avg_sales = mean(sales))

ggplot(control_post, aes(x = week, y = avg_sales)) +
  geom_line() +
  geom_vline(xintercept = 26, linetype = "dashed", color = "red") +
  labs(title = "Control store sales: any post-treatment dip?",
       x = "Week", y = "Avg Sales")
```

**Suggested fix**: If you have store location data, run the proximity test above. If control stores near treated stores decline more than distant control stores post-treatment, SUTVA is violated and you should either (a) restrict the control group to geographically distant stores, or (b) re-interpret the estimate as a "total effect including competitive displacement" rather than a pure POS system effect.

---

### 🟡 Minor: Callaway-Sant'Anna is equivalent to TWFE here — the stated rationale doesn't apply

**Category**: Statistical (Category 4)

**Explanation**: You used C&S because you were "being careful" about staggered adoption bias. Good instinct in general — but in *this specific design*, all 100 stores were treated simultaneously at week 26. There is only one cohort. C&S with a single cohort collapses to standard TWFE, so the results are numerically identical. You haven't done anything wrong, but the "careful" framing slightly misdiagnoses the risk. The staggered adoption concern (Goodman-Bacon, negative weights) simply doesn't apply when there's one treatment wave.

The actual thing worth being careful about here is *dynamic heterogeneity*: is the 12.3% a stable effect, or does it grow/shrink over the 26 post-treatment weeks? C&S's dynamic aggregation is genuinely useful for this — but it should be reported explicitly.

**Diagnostic**:
```r
library(did)

cs <- att_gt(yname = "sales", tname = "week", idname = "store_id",
             gname = "first_treat", data = df)

# Dynamic effects (is the 12.3% growing, stable, or fading?)
agg_dynamic <- aggte(cs, type = "dynamic")
ggdid(agg_dynamic, title = "ATT by Weeks Since Treatment")

# Simple aggregate (should match your reported 12.3%)
agg_simple <- aggte(cs, type = "simple")
summary(agg_simple)
```

**Suggested fix**: Report the dynamic treatment effects alongside the pooled ATT. If the effect is growing throughout the 26 post-weeks with no sign of plateau, your 12.3% underestimates the long-run effect. If it's fading, it may overstate persistence. This is important for any ROI calculation.

---

### 🟡 Minor: External validity — ATT applies to IT-ready stores only

**Category**: External Validity (Category 5)

**Explanation**: Your 12.3% ATT is the average effect for the 100 *treated* stores — the ones with sufficient IT infrastructure to be rolled out to first. If the company's next step is to upgrade the remaining 100 stores (who weren't ready yet), those stores may respond very differently: they may lack the infrastructure to use the system effectively, staff may be less technically capable, or the system may be deployed with more friction. The ATT from your "ready" stores is an optimistic upper bound on the ATE.

**Suggested fix**: Be explicit in the write-up: "This estimate represents the effect for stores with adequate IT infrastructure readiness. Effects on stores with lower readiness at full rollout may be substantially smaller." If possible, characterize *how different* the control stores are from treated stores on the infrastructure dimension, so decision-makers can judge how much to discount the estimate.

---

### 🟡 Minor: No check for concurrent operational changes (bundled treatment)

**Category**: Identification (Category 2)

**Explanation**: When a company rolls out a new POS system, it rarely happens in isolation. POS migrations typically come with: new staff training programs, updated pricing tools, new loyalty program integrations, changes to inventory management workflows, or vendor relationship changes. If any of these co-occurred with the POS deployment for treated stores (but not control stores), the 12.3% effect is the effect of the *bundle*, not the POS system alone.

**Suggested fix**: Document and disclose any co-occurring operational changes during the week-26 rollout for treated stores. If significant co-treatments occurred, reframe the estimand: "Effect of the POS system *plus associated operational changes*" — not "Effect of the POS system."

---

## Recommendations (Prioritized)

| Priority | Action | Finding |
|----------|--------|---------|
| 1 | **Validate sales data against an independent source** (payment processor, bank deposits, inventory) | Measurement artifact |
| 2 | **Run the anticipation check** — inspect event study near t = -4 to -1; try `anticipation = 4` in C&S | Anticipation contamination |
| 3 | **Run the proximity spillover test** if you have store location data; at minimum plot control store sales trend | SUTVA / spillovers |
| 4 | **Add a covariate balance table** comparing treated/control stores on observables; run sensitivity analysis | Selection on IT readiness |
| 5 | **Report dynamic ATT** — `aggte(cs, type = "dynamic")` — alongside the pooled 12.3% | Minor / interpretation |
| 6 | **Reframe external validity** explicitly in write-up — ATT ≠ effect on next wave of stores | External validity |

- [x] Diagnostic code generated for every serious/fatal finding

---

**Bottom line**: The parallel trends test passing is a necessary condition for validity — not a sufficient one. The biggest unresolved question isn't statistical, it's *substantive*: does your sales data come from the POS system being tested? If yes, that's your highest-priority validation before trusting the 12.3% number. After that, the anticipation and selection threats both point in the same direction — upward bias — so treat this estimate as a ceiling until you've addressed them.

### did_pedagogy_clean

**Run 1**: {"pedagogy": 0.6666666666666666, "safety": 0.0, "actionable": 1.0, "overall": 0.5555555555555555}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup ✓

Your design is clean — this is a **classic TWFE DiD**:

| Feature | Your data |
|---|---|
| Unit | `store` (100 stores) |
| Time | `month` (24 months) |
| Treatment | `treated` (50 stores get checkout system at month 12) |
| Outcome | `sales` |
| Variant | Single treatment date → TWFE with unit + time FE |

One important note before we touch code: **`treated` is time-invariant** (same value for every month of a store). With store fixed effects, you *cannot* include it as a standalone regressor — FE already absorb it. But the **interaction `treated:post`** varies over time, so that's exactly what we'll estimate.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk you through each one for your specific context — not just *what* to check, but *why* it matters.

### 1. Parallel Trends ⚠️ *Testable (partially)*
> **What it means here:** Without the new checkout system, would treated and control stores have had sales trending at the same rate during months 1–11? They don't need to have the *same* sales level — just the same *slope*.

**Why it matters:** If treated stores were already growing faster before month 12, DiD would credit that pre-existing growth to the checkout system.

**We can partially test this** using an event study on the pre-treatment months. We'll generate that code below.

### 2. No Anticipation ⚠️ *Testable*
> **What it means here:** Store managers didn't start changing operations *before* month 12 in anticipation of getting the new system (e.g., re-training staff, running promotions to "prepare" customers).

**Why it matters:** If treated stores' sales changed at month 10 or 11 because they *knew* they were getting the system, the pre-period is contaminated and our pre-trends test becomes unreliable.

**We'll check this** by looking at the event study coefficients at t = −2 and t = −3.

### 3. Stable Composition ⚠️ *Testable*
> **What it means here:** Stores didn't open or close because of the checkout system rollout. All 100 stores should be observed in all 24 months.

**Why it matters:** If weak treated stores closed after month 12, you'd only observe survivor stores, making the treatment group look artificially better.

### 4. No Spillovers (SUTVA) — *Untestable*
> **What it means here:** Customers don't shift between treated and control stores. If faster checkout at treated stores draws customers *away* from nearby control stores, control store sales drop — and DiD would overestimate the effect.

**Why it matters:** Control stores need to represent what treated stores *would have looked like* without treatment. Spillovers corrupt that.

**Ask yourself:** Are treated and control stores geographically intermixed, or are they in separate markets?

### 5. No Functional Form Bias ✅ *Not a concern here*
> This only applies to **staggered** rollouts (units treated at different times). Since all your treated stores get the system at the same month 12, standard TWFE is fine.

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

```r
# ── 0. Packages ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
library(tidyverse)
library(fixest)
library(modelsummary)

# ── 1. Load & inspect ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Replace with: df <- read_csv("your_data.csv")
# Assuming df is already loaded

# Quick sanity checks
cat("Stores:", n_distinct(df$store), "\n")         # expect 100
cat("Months:", n_distinct(df$month), "\n")          # expect 24
cat("Treatment split:\n")
df %>% distinct(store, treated) %>% count(treated)  # expect 50/50

# Is the panel balanced? Every store should have 24 rows.
balance_check <- df %>%
  group_by(store) %>%
  summarize(n_months = n_distinct(month), .groups = "drop")

cat("Panel balance — months per store:\n")
summary(balance_check$n_months)   # all should be 24

# ── 2. Visual pre-check: raw trends ─────────────────────────────────────────
# Before any regression, just look. Do groups trend similarly pre-month 12?
df %>%
  group_by(month, treated) %>%
  summarize(avg_sales = mean(sales), .groups = "drop") %>%
  mutate(group = ifelse(treated == 1, "Treated", "Control")) %>%
  ggplot(aes(x = month, y = avg_sales, color = group, group = group)) +
  geom_line(linewidth = 1) +
  geom_point(size = 2) +
  geom_vline(xintercept = 11.5, linetype = "dashed", color = "gray40") +
  annotate("text", x = 12.2, y = Inf, label = "Treatment",
           hjust = 0, vjust = 1.5, size = 3.5, color = "gray40") +
  scale_color_manual(values = c("Treated" = "#E63946", "Control" = "#457B9D")) +
  labs(title = "Average Sales by Group Over Time",
       subtitle = "Dashed line = treatment onset (month 12)",
       x = "Month", y = "Average Sales", color = NULL) +
  theme_minimal(base_size = 13)

# ── 3. Main DiD estimate (TWFE) ──────────────────────────────────────────────
# treated:post is the DiD interaction — the causal estimate of interest.
# Store FE absorb all time-invariant store characteristics (size, location, etc.)
# Month FE absorb all time-varying shocks common to all stores (seasonality, economy)
# DO NOT include `treated` alone — store FE already absorb it (time-invariant).

model_did <- feols(sales ~ treated:post | store + month,
                   data = df,
                   cluster = ~store)   # cluster at store level — where treatment varies

summary(model_did)

# Clean results table
modelsummary(
  model_did,
  stars    = c("*" = 0.05, "**" = 0.01, "***" = 0.001),
  coef_rename = c("treated:post" = "DiD Estimate (ATT)"),
  gof_map  = c("nobs", "r.squared", "FE: store", "FE: month"),
  title    = "Effect of Checkout System on Sales"
)

# Effect size: point estimate + 95% CI
est  <- coef(model_did)["treated:post"]
se   <- se(model_did)["treated:post"]
ci_l <- est - 1.96 * se
ci_u <- est + 1.96 * se

cat(sprintf(
  "\n DiD Estimate: %.3f (95%% CI: %.3f to %.3f)\n", est, ci_l, ci_u
))

# ── 4. Event study (pre-trends + dynamic effects) ────────────────────────────
# This does two jobs:
#   (a) PRE-TREATMENT: tests whether parallel trends held (coefficients near 0)
#   (b) POST-TREATMENT: shows how the effect evolves over time

df <- df %>%
  mutate(time_to_treat = month - 12)   # 0 = treatment month; -1 = reference period

# ref = -1 means month 11 is the baseline (coefficient fixed at 0)
event_study <- feols(
  sales ~ i(time_to_treat, treated, ref = -1) | store + month,
  data    = df,
  cluster = ~store
)

summary(event_study)

# Plot event study
iplot(
  event_study,
  main  = "Event Study: Effect of Checkout System on Sales",
  xlab  = "Months Relative to Treatment (0 = month 12)",
  ylab  = "Estimated Effect on Sales",
  col   = "#E63946",
  pt.join = TRUE
)
abline(h = 0, lty = 2, col = "gray50")
abline(v = -0.5, lty = 3, col = "gray50")
```

---

## Stage 4: Falsification & Robustness

Two checks I recommend — with code:

```r
# ── Robustness Check 1: Pre-trends formal test ───────────────────────────────
# Joint test: are ALL pre-treatment coefficients = 0?
# H0: no pre-existing trend differences between treated and control stores.
# If p < 0.05, parallel trends is in doubt.

pre_period_vars <- grep("time_to_treat::-", names(coef(event_study)), value = TRUE)
cat("Pre-treatment variables being tested:\n")
print(pre_period_vars)

# Wald test on all pre-treatment coefficients jointly
wald_result <- wald(event_study, keep = pre_period_vars)
print(wald_result)

cat("\nInterpretation: p-value > 0.05 → no evidence against parallel trends ✓\n")
cat("               p-value < 0.05 → pre-trends may exist — flag this ⚠️\n")

# ── Robustness Check 2: Placebo test (fake treatment date) ───────────────────
# Run DiD as if treatment happened at month 6 — using only pre-treatment data.
# If we find a significant "effect" here, something was already trending differently,
# which undermines the parallel trends assumption.

df_placebo <- df %>%
  filter(month <= 11) %>%          # pre-treatment data only
  mutate(post_placebo = if_else(month >= 6, 1L, 0L))   # fake cutpoint at month 6

model_placebo <- feols(sales ~ treated:post_placebo | store + month,
                       data    = df_placebo,
                       cluster = ~store)

est_placebo <- coef(model_placebo)["treated:post_placebo"]
cat(sprintf(
  "\n Placebo estimate: %.3f (should be near 0)\n", est_placebo
))

modelsummary(
  list("Main DiD" = model_did, "Placebo (pre-period)" = model_placebo),
  stars       = c("*" = 0.05, "**" = 0.01, "***" = 0.001),
  coef_rename = c("treated:post"         = "DiD Estimate",
                  "treated:post_placebo" = "Placebo Estimate"),
  title       = "Main Results vs. Placebo Test"
)
```

---

## What to watch for

| Output | What it means |
|---|---|
| Pre-treatment event study coefficients scattered near 0 | ✅ Parallel trends looks plausible |
| Pre-treatment coefficients trending upward/downward | 🚨 Parallel trends may be violated |
| Placebo estimate ≈ 0, p > 0.05 | ✅ No pre-existing differences |
| Placebo estimate large and significant | ⚠️ Something was already changing |
| Post-treatment coefficients stable over time | Effect is immediate and persistent |
| Post-treatment coefficients growing | Effect builds over time (maybe adoption lag) |

### did_pedagogy_messy

**Run 1**: {"pedagogy": 1.0, "safety": 1.0, "actionable": 1.0, "overall": 1.0}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup ✅

Your design is clear. Let me map it out before touching any code:

| Element | Your Study |
|---|---|
| **Treatment** | New training program |
| **Units** | 500 stores |
| **Cohorts** | Jan (n=100), Mar (n=200), Jun (n=200) |
| **Outcome** | Weekly sales |
| **Time series** | ~104 weekly periods |
| **Estimand** | Overall ATT (pooled across cohorts) |
| **Variant** | **Staggered DiD** |

One flag before we go further — you mentioned later-wave stores may have started informal training early. That's an **anticipation effect**, and it's the first assumption we need to reckon with.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through each one for your specific context.

---

### 1. Parallel Trends ⚠️ *Partially testable*

**What it means here**: Without the program, January stores, March stores, and June stores would have followed the same sales trajectory. The groups can differ in *level* (some stores are just bigger), but the *direction* of change must have been the same.

**Why it matters**: You have three cohorts that were likely chosen in a sequence — that raises a question: were later-wave stores different in some systematic way? If corporate picked January stores because they were already high-performers, their pre-program trends may look different from June stores.

**We can test this** using the pre-treatment periods in an event study. I'll generate that code in Stage 3. You'll want to see flat pre-treatment coefficients for all three cohorts.

---

### 2. No Anticipation 🚨 *You already flagged this*

**What it means here**: The March and June stores shouldn't have changed their sales behavior *before* their official wave started. But you told me they might have done informal training early — that's anticipation.

**Why it matters**: If a June store started informal training in April, its "pre-treatment" period is contaminated. Callaway-Sant'Anna uses the pre-treatment periods to identify the counterfactual. Contaminated pre-periods produce biased group-time ATTs — and they'll also make your pre-trends test look worse than it is.

**What we'll do**: The `differences` package (Python's Callaway-Sant'Anna implementation) has an `anticipation` parameter. We'll set `anticipation=1` or `anticipation=2` (weeks or months, depending on your unit) as a robustness check. I'll show you how to compare.

**Question before I write the code**: Is "anticipation" likely to be a few weeks, or could it go back a month or more? This affects how many periods to flag.

---

### 3. Stable Composition ✅ *Likely fine, worth a quick check*

**What it means here**: Stores shouldn't enter or leave your dataset because of the training program. If weak stores were closed after the program was announced, the surviving treated stores look better than they really are.

**Likely fine** for a corporate training rollout — stores don't usually close because of a training program. But we'll run a quick balance check.

---

### 4. No Spillovers (SUTVA) ⚠️ *Untestable, but flag-worthy*

**What it means here**: Trained stores shouldn't affect untrained stores' sales. This is more of a concern if stores compete for the same customers (overlapping geographies) or if staff rotate between stores.

**If stores are independent** (e.g., different metro areas, no staff rotation), you're fine. Do you have any reason to think stores could "infect" each other's outcomes?

---

### 5. Correct Functional Form 🚨 *This is the critical one for your design*

**What it means here**: Standard TWFE regression (`sales ~ treatment + EntityEffects + TimeEffects`) will give you a **biased estimate** here. Why? Because with three cohorts, TWFE implicitly uses already-treated stores as controls for later-treated stores — "forbidden comparisons." If the January stores respond differently to training than June stores (heterogeneous effects), TWFE can be wrong-signed.

**What we'll do**: Use **Callaway-Sant'Anna** — it only ever compares treated cohorts to stores that haven't been treated yet (or never-treated). This is the right estimator for your design. We'll also run TWFE and compare; if they diverge, that's informative about heterogeneity.

---

### Assumption Summary

| Assumption | Status | Action |
|---|---|---|
| Parallel trends | ⚠️ Testable — run pre-trends test | Flag if Jan stores differ systematically |
| No anticipation | 🚨 You flagged concern | Use `anticipation` parameter; robustness check |
| Stable composition | ✅ Likely fine | Quick panel balance check |
| No spillovers | ⚠️ Untestable | Flag if stores compete geographically |
| Correct functional form | 🚨 TWFE is biased here | Use Callaway-Sant'Anna throughout |

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

One question before the code: **what is your time variable?** Weekly periods work best as integers (1, 2, 3... 104) or ISO week numbers. And does every store appear in every week (balanced panel), or are there gaps?

Assuming a balanced panel with integer week numbers, here's the full analysis:

```python
# ── 0. Dependencies ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# pip install differences pandas numpy matplotlib

import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
import matplotlib.ticker as mticker
from differences import ATTgt

# ── 1. Data prep ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Expected columns:
#   store_id   : integer, unique store identifier
#   week       : integer, 1–104 (week 1 = first week of year 1)
#   sales      : numeric outcome
#   first_treat: integer, the week the store's wave went live
#                Set to 0 for never-treated stores (if any exist).
#                If ALL 500 stores are treated, set to 0 for the latest wave
#                as a proxy control — flag this as a limitation.

# ── Adapt these to your actual column names ──
df = pd.read_csv("your_data.csv")

# Map calendar dates to cohort wave weeks if needed:
# wave_map = {"January": 1, "March": 9, "June": 22}  # approximate week numbers
# df["first_treat"] = df["wave"].map(wave_map)
# Never-treated stores: df.loc[df["wave"].isna(), "first_treat"] = 0

# Quick sanity check
print("Stores per cohort:")
print(df.groupby("first_treat")["store_id"].nunique())
print(f"\nWeeks: {df['week'].min()} to {df['week'].max()}")
print(f"Total obs: {len(df):,}")

# ── 2. Panel balance check (Assumption 3) ──────────────────────────────────────
total_weeks = df["week"].nunique()
balance = (
    df.groupby("store_id")
    .agg(n_weeks=("week", "nunique"), cohort=("first_treat", "first"))
    .reset_index()
)
balance["fully_observed"] = balance["n_weeks"] == total_weeks
print("\nPanel balance by cohort:")
print(balance.groupby("cohort").agg(
    n_stores=("store_id", "count"),
    pct_fully_observed=("fully_observed", "mean")
).round(3))

# ── 3. Main estimation: Callaway-Sant'Anna ──────────────────────────────────────
# No anticipation assumed first (baseline model)
att_main = ATTgt(
    data=df,
    cohort_name="first_treat",
    time_name="week",
    unit_name="store_id",
    outcome_name="sales",
    anticipation=0,          # baseline: no anticipation
    control_group="notyettreated",  # only use not-yet-treated as controls
)
att_main.fit()

# Overall ATT (your primary estimand)
overall = att_main.aggregate("simple")
print("\n── Overall ATT (Callaway-Sant'Anna) ──")
print(overall)

# ── 4. Event study: pre-trends + dynamic effects ──────────────────────────────
es = att_main.aggregate("dynamic")  # relative-time aggregation
print("\n── Event Study Coefficients ──")
print(es)

# Plot event study
fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(12, 5))
es_df = es.summary_df()                     # columns: term, estimate, ci_lower, ci_upper
pre  = es_df[es_df["term"].astype(int) < 0]
post = es_df[es_df["term"].astype(int) >= 0]

for chunk, color in [(pre, "#e07b54"), (post, "#4c8cbf")]:
    t = chunk["term"].astype(int)
    ax.errorbar(t, chunk["estimate"],
                yerr=[chunk["estimate"] - chunk["ci_lower"],
                      chunk["ci_upper"] - chunk["estimate"]],
                fmt="o-", color=color, capsize=3, linewidth=1.5, markersize=5)

ax.axhline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8, linestyle="--")
ax.axvline(-0.5, color="gray", linewidth=0.8, linestyle=":")
ax.set_xlabel("Weeks Relative to Training Rollout")
ax.set_ylabel("Estimated Effect on Sales")
ax.set_title("Event Study: Training Program Effect\n(orange = pre-treatment, blue = post-treatment)")
ax.xaxis.set_major_locator(mticker.MaxNLocator(integer=True))
plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig("event_study.png", dpi=150)
plt.show()

# ── 5. By-cohort ATT ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
cohort_agg = att_main.aggregate("group")
print("\n── ATT by Cohort (wave) ──")
print(cohort_agg)
# If January cohort shows a very different ATT than June cohort,
# that's treatment effect heterogeneity — and it's why TWFE is unreliable here.

# ── 6. Comparison: TWFE (to illustrate bias) ──────────────────────────────────
from linearmodels.panel import PanelOLS

df["treated_post"] = (
    (df["first_treat"] > 0) & (df["week"] >= df["first_treat"])
).astype(int)

twfe = PanelOLS.from_formula(
    "sales ~ treated_post + EntityEffects + TimeEffects",
    data=df.set_index(["store_id", "week"])
)
twfe_res = twfe.fit(cov_type="clustered", cluster_entity=True)
print("\n── TWFE estimate (for comparison only — likely biased) ──")
print(f"  Coefficient: {twfe_res.params['treated_post']:.4f}")
print(f"  95% CI: [{twfe_res.conf_int().loc['treated_post', 'lower']:.4f}, "
      f"{twfe_res.conf_int().loc['treated_post', 'upper']:.4f}]")
print("  ⚠️  TWFE with staggered adoption and heterogeneous effects is biased.")
print("      Use the Callaway-Sant'Anna estimate above as your primary result.")
```

---

## Stage 4: Anticipation Robustness Check

Given your concern about informal training, run this directly after Stage 3:

```python
# ── Robustness: allow 1–4 weeks of anticipation ────────────────────────────────
results_by_anticipation = {}

for k in [0, 1, 2, 4]:
    att_k = ATTgt(
        data=df,
        cohort_name="first_treat",
        time_name="week",
        unit_name="store_id",
        outcome_name="sales",
        anticipation=k,
        control_group="notyettreated",
    )
    att_k.fit()
    overall_k = att_k.aggregate("simple")
    est = overall_k.summary_df()["estimate"].values[0]
    ci_lo = overall_k.summary_df()["ci_lower"].values[0]
    ci_hi = overall_k.summary_df()["ci_upper"].values[0]
    results_by_anticipation[k] = (est, ci_lo, ci_hi)
    print(f"anticipation={k} weeks → ATT = {est:.4f}  [{ci_lo:.4f}, {ci_hi:.4f}]")

# If estimates are stable across k=0,1,2,4 → anticipation is not driving results.
# If estimates shift meaningfully → the pre-period is contaminated and you should
# use k matching your domain knowledge of when informal training likely started.
```

**How to read it**: If the overall ATT is stable across `k=0` through `k=4`, you can report the main result with confidence. If it shifts substantially, use the `k` that matches your best knowledge of when informal training actually began — and flag it in the write-up.

### exercise_iv_quality

**Run 1**: {"pedagogy": 0.6666666666666666, "safety": 1.0, "actionable": 1.0, "overall": 0.8888888888888888}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Great goal. The hardest part of IV in practice isn't the math — it's knowing whether your instrument is actually valid. I'll give you a scenario where you have to decide.

---

## Your Exercise

**Scenario**: You're a health economist at a regional insurer. A colleague proposes studying whether exercise improves mental health. The problem: people who exercise more are also less stressed, higher-income, and have better baseline health — classic endogeneity. Your colleague suggests using **distance to the nearest gym** as an instrument. "People who live closer to a gym exercise more," he argues, "but proximity to a gym shouldn't directly affect your mental health — it just makes exercise more convenient."

You have a dataset of 3,000 members. Your manager wants to know: **Does exercise improve mental health, and by how much?**

**Your deliverable**: Estimate the effect of exercise on mental health using IV. Then answer: is this instrument valid? Walk through each IV assumption explicitly and show any diagnostics that support or challenge your conclusion.

---

## Setup — Run This First

```r
set.seed(1818)
n <- 3000

gym_distance  <- runif(n, 0.5, 15)
proximity     <- -gym_distance          # higher = closer to gym

unobserved    <- rnorm(n, 0, 1)

exercise_hrs  <- pmax(0, 3 + 0.3 * proximity - 1.5 * unobserved + rnorm(n, 0, 2))

mh_score <- 50 +
            10 * (exercise_hrs / sd(exercise_hrs)) +
             3 * (proximity   / sd(proximity))     -
             8 * unobserved                        +
            rnorm(n, 0, 5)

df <- data.frame(
  id            = 1:n,
  gym_distance  = round(gym_distance, 2),
  exercise_hrs  = round(exercise_hrs, 2),
  mental_health = round(mh_score,     2)
)

write.csv(df, "data.csv", row.names = FALSE)
```

---

## Suggested Packages

```r
library(tidyverse)
library(AER)       # ivreg()
library(fixest)    # feols() with IV syntax
library(modelsummary)
```

## What to Work Through

1. **OLS baseline** — estimate the naive effect of `exercise_hrs` on `mental_health`
2. **First stage** — regress `exercise_hrs` on `gym_distance`. Check the F-statistic
3. **2SLS estimate** — run the IV regression using `gym_distance` as the instrument
4. **Assumption audit** — go through each IV assumption one by one:
   - Relevance ✓ or ✗?
   - Independence ✓ or ✗?
   - Exclusion restriction ✓ or ✗?
5. **Your verdict** — is this instrument valid? What would you tell your colleague?

Take your time with step 4 — that's the whole exercise. Share your analysis when you're ready and I'll reveal what's actually in the data.

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Great setup — you've got the key ingredients for a clean experiment analysis. Before I generate code, three quick questions to make sure the analysis is correctly specified:

1. **Covariates**: Do you have baseline characteristics per user (e.g., device type, country, acquisition channel, prior app usage)? These aren't required, but they enable balance checks and precision gains.

2. **Compliance**: Did all users assigned to the new flow actually *see* it? Or could some have bypassed onboarding entirely (e.g., returning users, force-closes)? This determines whether we report ITT only, or also estimate the effect on users who actually received the treatment.

3. **Attrition / observability**: Are all 10,000 users observable at day 7 — meaning you can definitively label them retained or not? Or are some users "missing" from the outcome data (e.g., deleted accounts, data pipeline gaps)?

|---|---|
| **Design** | Individual-level RCT |
| **N** | 10,000 (≈5,000 per arm) |

| **Stage** | Analysis (data in hand) |
| **Language** | Python |

### experiments_pedagogy_messy

**Run 1**: {"pedagogy": 0.6666666666666666, "safety": 0.0, "actionable": 1.0, "overall": 0.5555555555555555}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Setup

You have two-sided non-compliance:
- **15% of treated assigned** refused the drug → one-sided defiance
- **5% of control assigned** obtained it elsewhere → contamination

This means **assignment ≠ receipt**. A simple comparison of `received_treatment` groups would be biased (self-selection into compliance). You have two valid causal quantities to estimate:

| Estimand | Question answered | Method |
|---|---|---|
| **ITT** | Effect of *being assigned* the drug | OLS on assignment |
| **CACE/LATE** | Effect of *actually receiving* the drug (for compliers) | IV: assignment → receipt |

Since you want "the treatment effect," you want **CACE**. Let's walk through assumptions first.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

**1. Random assignment** ✅ (by design — verify with balance table below)
> Randomization should make treatment and control groups comparable at baseline. Worth checking even with RCTs — small samples can have imbalance by chance.

**2. Compliance** ⚠️ *This is your core concern*
> Two-sided non-compliance: 15% of assigned-treated didn't comply; 5% of assigned-control crossed over. IV recovers the CACE for **compliers** — patients who take the drug if and only if assigned to it. The 15% who refused and the 5% who sought it out are not represented in this estimate.

**3. Exclusion restriction** — *the key IV assumption, untestable*
> Assignment affects outcome **only through** actual receipt. Would knowing you were assigned to the drug group change your outcome for any reason *other* than taking it? (Anxiety, placebo psychology, changed health behaviors?) In drug trials this is often plausible, but worth thinking through.

**4. SUTVA** — plausible in most clinical trials
> One patient's treatment doesn't affect another's outcome. Plausible here unless patients share medication.

**5. No differential attrition** — check below
> If patients assigned to drug dropped out of outcome measurement at different rates, the observed sample is no longer representative.

---

## Stage 3: Code

```r
library(fixest)
library(cobalt)
library(modelsummary)

# ── 0. Load data ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# df <- read.csv("your_data.csv")

# ── 1. Compliance summary ────────────────────────────────────────────────────
cat("=== Compliance table ===\n")
print(table(assigned = df$assigned_treatment,
            received  = df$received_treatment))

comply_treat   <- mean(df$received_treatment[df$assigned_treatment == 1])
contam_control <- mean(df$received_treatment[df$assigned_treatment == 0])

cat(sprintf("\nCompliance rate (assigned drug):     %.1f%%\n", comply_treat * 100))
cat(sprintf("Contamination rate (assigned placebo): %.1f%%\n", contam_control * 100))

# ── 2. Balance check — did randomization work? ──────────────────────────────
# Add baseline covariates (X1, X2, ...) when available
# Without pre-treatment covariates, verify group sizes are as expected
cat("\n=== Group sizes ===\n")
print(table(df$assigned_treatment))

# ── 3. Attrition check ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────
df$attrited <- as.integer(is.na(df$outcome))
attrition_test <- feols(attrited ~ assigned_treatment, data = df)
cat("\n=== Differential attrition test ===\n")
summary(attrition_test)
# p < 0.05 → differential attrition — a serious threat

# ── 4. ITT: effect of assignment (always valid) ──────────────────────────────
itt <- feols(outcome ~ assigned_treatment, data = df)

# ── 5. CACE/LATE via IV ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Instrument: assigned_treatment
# Endogenous variable: received_treatment
# Logic: assignment predicts receipt (first stage), and affects outcome
#        only through receipt (exclusion restriction)

cace <- feols(outcome ~ 1 | received_treatment ~ assigned_treatment,
              data = df)

# First-stage F-statistic — must be >> 10
cat("\n=== First-stage F-statistic ===\n")
print(fitstat(cace, "ivf"))

# ── 6. Results table ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
modelsummary(
  list("ITT (assignment)"  = itt,
       "CACE/LATE (IV)"    = cace),
  stars   = TRUE,
  gof_map = c("nobs", "r.squared"),
  title   = "Treatment Effect Estimates"
)
```

**What to look for in the output:**
- `fitstat(cace, "ivf")` → first-stage F. If F < 10, your instrument is weak and CACE is unreliable. With 85% vs 5% compliance, F should be very large here — this is a strong instrument.
- CACE > ITT in magnitude is expected: ITT is diluted by non-compliers; CACE scales it back up. Approximately: CACE ≈ ITT / (compliance_rate − contamination_rate) = ITT / (0.85 − 0.05) = ITT / 0.80.

---

## Stage 4: Robustness

```r
# ── Placebo outcome test ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# If you have a baseline outcome measured BEFORE treatment, the IV estimate
# on that variable should be zero. Any significant effect signals bias.

# cace_placebo <- feols(baseline_outcome ~ 1 | received_treatment ~ assigned_treatment,
#                       data = df)
# summary(cace_placebo)

# ── Sensitivity to exclusion restriction ─────────────────────────────────────
# How large would the direct effect of assignment (bypassing receipt) need
# to be to explain away your CACE estimate?
# Rule of thumb: compare ITT and reduced-form magnitudes to assess plausibility.

reduced_form <- feols(outcome ~ assigned_treatment, data = df)  # = ITT here
first_stage  <- feols(received_treatment ~ assigned_treatment, data = df)

cat("\n=== First stage coefficient ===\n")
coef(first_stage)["assigned_treatment"]  # should be ~0.80

cat("\n=== Manual CACE check ===\n")
cat("CACE ≈ ITT / first-stage =",
    coef(reduced_form)["assigned_treatment"] /
    coef(first_stage)["assigned_treatment"], "\n")
# Should match the IV estimate exactly — good sanity check
```

---

## Verification gate

Before interpreting, confirm from your output:
- [ ] Compliance table matches expectations (~850/1000 treated complied, ~50/1000 control crossed over)
- [ ] First-stage F >> 10
- [ ] CACE estimate and 95% CI quoted from output
- [ ] Manual CACE ≈ IV CACE (sanity check passes)

Share the output and I'll help you interpret the estimates in context.

---

**One thing to keep in mind**: CACE estimates the effect for **compliers only** — the patients who take the drug when assigned and don't when not assigned. If non-compliers (refusers, cross-overs) are systematically different from compliers (e.g., sicker patients are more likely to seek the drug outside the trial), the CACE may not generalize to the full population. Whether that matters depends on your policy question.

</details>

But the data you shared has these columns:

```
id, distance, education, earnings
```

That looks like a **returns-to-education** dataset — possibly the classic Card (1995) setup where distance to college instruments for years of schooling on wages. There's no tutoring variable, no test score variable, and no binary "center opened" indicator.

   - Is `earnings` actually test scores?

---

### ⚠️ Weak Instrument — Flag This Now

You said the first stage looks "okay but not amazing." That phrase is a yellow flag I want to address directly, because **weak instruments are the single most common way IV goes wrong.**

Here's why it matters:

| First-stage F | What it means |
|---|---|
| F < 10 | 🚨 Weak instrument. 2SLS estimates are biased toward OLS; confidence intervals are unreliable. |
| 10 ≤ F < 100 | ⚠️ Marginal. Some bias remains. Report the F explicitly. |
| F ≥ 100 | ✅ Strong. Modern standard (Lee et al. 2022). |

"Okay but not amazing" often means F is somewhere in the 10–30 range — which is in the danger zone. We'll get the exact number from the first-stage regression and interpret it carefully together.

### matching_pedagogy_clean

**Run 1**: {"pedagogy": 1.0, "safety": 1.0, "actionable": 1.0, "overall": 1.0}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Causal Matching Analysis — 5-Stage Walkthrough

Good setup to work with. Before diving in, one thing to flag: you described **five covariates** (age, BMI, blood pressure, disease severity, comorbidity count) but the data sample only shows **x1 and x2**. I'll write the code for x1 and x2 as shown — if your full dataset has more columns, swap them in everywhere below.

---

## Stage 1: Setup

**What I'm reading from your description:**

| Element | Value |
|---|---|
| Treatment | New drug (1) vs. standard care (0) |
| Outcome | Recovery time (continuous — lower = better) |
| Covariates | x1, x2 (age, BMI, BP, severity, comorbidity) |
| N | 2,000 (1,000 treated / 1,000 control — balanced) |
| Language | R |
| Estimand | **ATT** — "does the drug reduce recovery time for patients who received it?" This is the natural choice here: you care about the effect on patients who were actually treated. ATE would ask about the effect if you treated *everyone*, including patients who may have very different risk profiles. |

**Method recommendation: Propensity Score Matching (PSM) + Doubly-Robust as robustness check**

- PSM is transparent and easy to audit (you can show matched pairs).
- Given you have rich covariates and 2,000 observations, this is feasible.
- I'll also include a DR estimate — if the propensity or outcome model is misspecified, the other one can save you. With medical data, that safety net matters.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let's walk through each one for your specific context.

### 1. 🔴 Conditional Independence (CIA) — *Untestable, hardest to defend*

**What it means here**: After conditioning on age, BMI, BP, disease severity, and comorbidity count, the *reason* a patient received the new drug vs. standard care is unrelated to how quickly they would have recovered anyway.

**Why this is hard in medicine**: Physicians don't assign drugs randomly. They likely gave the new drug to patients where they expected it to help — or withheld it from the most severe cases. That selection logic is often based on clinical judgment that isn't fully captured in your covariates.

**Ask yourself**:
- Was drug assignment driven by a protocol, or physician discretion?
- Is there any variable a physician might have used (lab value, prior drug history, patient preference) that you *don't* have in your data?
- Does disease severity score fully capture case severity, or are there residual dimensions?

> ⚠️ If you can name even one plausible unmeasured confounder, matching cannot fully remove its bias. We'll run a sensitivity analysis in Stage 4 to quantify how bad this could be.

---

### 2. ✅ Overlap / Positivity — *Testable — run this first*

**What it means**: Every patient profile (covariate combination) should have a nonzero chance of receiving either treatment. If some patient types *always* get the drug, there's no control group to compare them to.

**Why it matters here**: Balanced 50/50 allocation is encouraging, but it doesn't guarantee overlap within covariate strata. Run this before anything else:

```r
library(MatchIt)
library(ggplot2)

# Quick overlap check before full analysis
ps_check <- matchit(treatment ~ x1 + x2, data = df,
                    method = NULL, distance = "glm")

df$ps <- ps_check$distance

ggplot(df, aes(x = ps, fill = factor(treatment))) +
  geom_histogram(alpha = 0.5, position = "identity", bins = 50) +
  scale_fill_manual(values = c("steelblue", "tomato"),
                    labels = c("Control", "Treated")) +
  labs(title = "Propensity Score Overlap — Pre-flight Check",
       x = "P(Treatment | Covariates)", y = "Count", fill = "Group") +
  theme_minimal()

# Numeric summary
cat("=== Treated ===\n"); summary(df$ps[df$treatment == 1])
cat("=== Control ===\n"); summary(df$ps[df$treatment == 0])
cat("\nProportion with PS < 0.05:", mean(df$ps < 0.05), "\n")
cat("Proportion with PS > 0.95:", mean(df$ps > 0.95), "\n")
```

**What to look for**: The two histograms should substantially overlap. If treated patients cluster near PS = 0.9+ with controls near 0.1–0.2, you have a serious overlap problem and matching estimates will be unreliable.

---

### 3. ✅ SUTVA — *Plausible here*

**What it means**: One patient's drug assignment doesn't affect another's recovery time.

**In this context**: Patients recovering from illness rarely interfere with each other's outcomes — this is much more defensible than, say, a vaccine trial where herd immunity creates spillovers. I'll flag this as **low concern** unless patients share resources (ICU beds, nursing time) that could create indirect effects.

---

### 4. ⚠️ Correct Model Specification

**What it means**: The propensity score model (logistic regression on x1 + x2) correctly captures how drug assignment was determined. If the true selection mechanism is nonlinear and we use a linear logit, the weights will be off.

**Diagnosis is balance, not model fit**: A correctly specified propensity score model produces balanced covariates after matching. We check this with standardized mean differences (SMD) and love plots — *not* with model R² or AIC.

> Rule of thumb: **SMD < 0.1** after matching = good. **SMD > 0.25** = respecify the model.

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

Complete analysis code. Run sections in order.

```r
# ── 0. Packages ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
library(MatchIt)          # Matching
library(cobalt)           # Balance diagnostics
library(marginaleffects)  # ATT estimation from matched data
library(ggplot2)          # Plots
library(dplyr)            # Data manipulation

# ── 1. Pre-flight: Overlap Check ─────────────────────────────────────────────
ps_check <- matchit(treatment ~ x1 + x2, data = df,
                    method = NULL, distance = "glm")

df$ps <- ps_check$distance

p_overlap <- ggplot(df, aes(x = ps, fill = factor(treatment))) +
  geom_histogram(alpha = 0.5, position = "identity", bins = 50) +
  scale_fill_manual(values = c("steelblue", "tomato"),
                    labels = c("Control", "Treated")) +
  labs(title = "Propensity Score Overlap",
       subtitle = "Check: distributions should substantially overlap",
       x = "P(Treatment | x1, x2)", y = "Count", fill = "Group") +
  theme_minimal()
print(p_overlap)

# Extremes — flag if large proportions near 0 or 1
cat("Proportion PS < 0.05:", round(mean(df$ps < 0.05), 4), "\n")
cat("Proportion PS > 0.95:", round(mean(df$ps > 0.95), 4), "\n")

# ── 2. Propensity Score Matching (ATT) ───────────────────────────────────────
# Nearest-neighbor 1:1 matching on PS, with caliper to avoid bad matches
m_out <- matchit(
  treatment ~ x1 + x2,
  data      = df,
  method    = "nearest",
  distance  = "glm",        # logistic PS
  estimand  = "ATT",
  ratio     = 1,
  replace   = FALSE,
  caliper   = 0.1,          # max 0.1 SD distance — prevents poor matches
  std.caliper = TRUE
)

summary(m_out, un = FALSE)  # How many matched? How many dropped?

# ── 3. Covariate Balance Diagnostics ─────────────────────────────────────────
# Balance table: SMD should be < 0.1 after matching
bal_tab <- bal.tab(m_out, thresholds = c(m = 0.1, v = 2))
print(bal_tab)

# Love plot: visual before/after
love.plot(
  m_out,
  thresholds  = c(m = 0.1),
  abs         = TRUE,
  var.order   = "unadjusted",
  title       = "Covariate Balance: Before and After Matching"
)

# ── 4. Extract Matched Data & Estimate ATT ───────────────────────────────────
m_data <- match.data(m_out)

# Regression on matched data — outcome model adjusts for any residual imbalance
# Cluster SEs by matched pair (subclass) to account for matching structure
outcome_model <- lm(
  outcome ~ treatment + x1 + x2,
  data    = m_data,
  weights = weights
)

# ATT estimate with cluster-robust SEs
att_psm <- avg_comparisons(
  outcome_model,
  variables = "treatment",
  vcov      = ~subclass,
  newdata   = subset(m_data, treatment == 1),
  wts       = "weights"
)
print(att_psm)

# ── 5. Doubly-Robust Estimate (Robustness Check) ─────────────────────────────
# Reweight using IPW for ATT, then regress
df$ipw_att <- ifelse(
  df$treatment == 1, 1,
  df$ps / (1 - df$ps)   # ATT weights: treated = 1, control = ps/(1-ps)
)

# Trim extreme weights to stabilize (99th percentile cap)
weight_cap <- quantile(df$ipw_att[df$treatment == 0], 0.99)
df$ipw_att_trim <- pmin(df$ipw_att, weight_cap)

cat("\nWeight distribution (control group):\n")
summary(df$ipw_att_trim[df$treatment == 0])

# Weighted balance check
bal_ipw <- bal.tab(treatment ~ x1 + x2, data = df,
                   weights = df$ipw_att_trim,
                   thresholds = c(m = 0.1))
print(bal_ipw)

# Doubly-robust outcome model
dr_model <- lm(outcome ~ treatment + x1 + x2,
               data = df, weights = ipw_att_trim)

# ATT estimate
att_dr <- avg_comparisons(
  dr_model,
  variables = "treatment",
  vcov      = "HC3",
  newdata   = subset(df, treatment == 1),
  wts       = "ipw_att_trim"
)
print(att_dr)

# ── 6. Summary Comparison ────────────────────────────────────────────────────
cat("\n=== Effect Estimates Comparison ===\n")
cat("PSM estimate:  ", round(att_psm$estimate, 3),
    " [", round(att_psm$conf.low, 3), ",", round(att_psm$conf.high, 3), "]\n")
cat("DR estimate:   ", round(att_dr$estimate, 3),
    " [", round(att_dr$conf.low, 3), ",", round(att_dr$conf.high, 3), "]\n")
cat("(Negative = drug reduces recovery time = good)\n")
```

---

## Stage 4: Falsification & Sensitivity Analysis

Run **at minimum** checks 1 and 2 before interpreting results.

### Check 1: Sensitivity to Unobserved Confounding (Rosenbaum Bounds)

This answers: *"How strong would an unobserved confounder need to be to fully explain away our result?"*

```r
library(rbounds)

# Extract matched outcomes
treated_outcomes <- m_data$outcome[m_data$treatment == 1]
control_outcomes <- m_data$outcome[m_data$treatment == 0]

# Rosenbaum bounds — Gamma = 1 means no hidden bias
# Gamma = 2 means an unobserved confounder could double the odds of treatment
psens(
  x      = treated_outcomes,
  y      = control_outcomes,
  Gamma  = 2,       # Test up to Gamma = 2 (fairly strong confounder)
  GammaInc = 0.1
)
# Look at the p-value column: at what Gamma does the result become insignificant?
# Gamma > 1.5 = moderately robust; Gamma < 1.2 = fragile
```

### Check 2: Alternative Matching Specifications

Results should be qualitatively stable across reasonable specification choices:

```r
# Full matching (uses all observations, no discard)
m_full <- matchit(treatment ~ x1 + x2, data = df,
                  method = "full", estimand = "ATT", distance = "glm")

m_full_data <- match.data(m_full)
model_full <- lm(outcome ~ treatment + x1 + x2,
                 data = m_full_data, weights = weights)
att_full <- avg_comparisons(model_full, variables = "treatment",
                            vcov = ~subclass,
                            newdata = subset(m_full_data, treatment == 1),
                            wts = "weights")

# 2:1 matching (each treated gets 2 controls — more power, less balance)
m_2to1 <- matchit(treatment ~ x1 + x2, data = df,
                  method = "nearest", estimand = "ATT",
                  distance = "glm", ratio = 2)

m_2to1_data <- match.data(m_2to1)
model_2to1 <- lm(outcome ~ treatment + x1 + x2,
                 data = m_2to1_data, weights = weights)
att_2to1 <- avg_comparisons(model_2to1, variables = "treatment",
                            vcov = ~subclass,
                            newdata = subset(m_2to1_data, treatment == 1),
                            wts = "weights")

cat("\n=== Specification Sensitivity ===\n")
cat("1:1 PSM:       ", round(att_psm$estimate, 3), "\n")
cat("Full matching: ", round(att_full$estimate, 3), "\n")
cat("2:1 PSM:       ", round(att_2to1$estimate, 3), "\n")
cat("DR (IPW):      ", round(att_dr$estimate, 3), "\n")
# Large spread across specs = fragile result; tight clustering = robust
```

### Check 3: Propensity Score Trimming

```r
# Restrict to region of common support (PS between 0.1 and 0.9)
df_trimmed <- df %>% filter(ps >= 0.1 & ps <= 0.9)
cat("Observations retained after trimming:", nrow(df_trimmed),
    "of", nrow(df), "\n")

m_trimmed <- matchit(treatment ~ x1 + x2, data = df_trimmed,
                     method = "nearest", estimand = "ATT",
                     distance = "glm", ratio = 1)

m_trimmed_data <- match.data(m_trimmed)
model_trimmed <- lm(outcome ~ treatment + x1 + x2,
                    data = m_trimmed_data, weights = weights)
att_trimmed <- avg_comparisons(model_trimmed, variables = "treatment",
                               vcov = ~subclass,
                               newdata = subset(m_trimmed_data, treatment == 1),
                               wts = "weights")
cat("Trimmed estimate:", round(att_trimmed$estimate, 3), "\n")
```

|---|---|
| Overlap histogram shows separation | ⚠️ Treated & control patients are very different — matched results will extrapolate |

| PSM and DR estimates diverge by > 20% | ⚠️ At least one model is misspecified — inspect both |

| Severe cases systematically in treated group | 🚨 Likely confounding by indication — matching alone can't fix this |

## Two Red Flags Before We Write a Line of Code

You've already spotted one problem. There are actually two, and both are serious:

---

### 🚨 Red Flag 1: Missing Confounders (Conditional Independence)

You said enrollees tend to be **healthier and higher income** — but those variables are **not in your dataset**. Looking at your columns: `age`, `tenure`, `department`.

This matters enormously. Voluntary wellness program enrollment is driven by exactly the kind of factors that also drive productivity: health motivation, socioeconomic resources, conscientiousness. If you can't measure them, you can't condition on them.

**No matching method — no matter how sophisticated — can adjust for a confounder you can't measure.**

Before going further: **Do you have health status or income data anywhere?** If not, the CIA assumption is likely violated from the start, and we need to be upfront about that in any results.

---

### 🚨 Red Flag 2: Poor Overlap (You Already Know This)

You told me the propensity score distributions barely overlap. This means there are treated employees with no comparable controls — and any estimate in those regions is extrapolation, not identification.

Let's **quantify** this right now before doing anything else. Run this:

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
from sklearn.linear_model import LogisticRegression
from sklearn.preprocessing import StandardScaler

# ── 1. Estimate propensity scores ──────────────────────────────────────────
covariates = ['age', 'tenure', 'department']
X = df[covariates].values
D = df['treated'].values

scaler = StandardScaler()
X_scaled = scaler.fit_transform(X)

ps_model = LogisticRegression(max_iter=1000, random_state=42)
ps_model.fit(X_scaled, D)
df['ps'] = ps_model.predict_proba(X_scaled)[:, 1]

# ── 2. Overlap summary ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────
treated_ps = df.loc[df['treated'] == 1, 'ps']
control_ps = df.loc[df['treated'] == 0, 'ps']

print("=== Propensity Score Summary ===")
print(f"\nTreated  (n={len(treated_ps):,}): "
      f"min={treated_ps.min():.3f}, "
      f"median={treated_ps.median():.3f}, "
      f"max={treated_ps.max():.3f}")
print(f"Control  (n={len(control_ps):,}): "
      f"min={control_ps.min():.3f}, "
      f"median={control_ps.median():.3f}, "
      f"max={control_ps.max():.3f}")

# Region of common support
cs_lower = max(treated_ps.min(), control_ps.min())
cs_upper = min(treated_ps.max(), control_ps.max())
print(f"\nCommon support region: [{cs_lower:.3f}, {cs_upper:.3f}]")

in_support = df['ps'].between(cs_lower, cs_upper)
print(f"Treated units IN common support:  "
      f"{in_support[df['treated']==1].sum():,} "
      f"/ {(df['treated']==1).sum():,} "
      f"({100*in_support[df['treated']==1].mean():.1f}%)")
print(f"Control units IN common support:  "
      f"{in_support[df['treated']==0].sum():,} "
      f"/ {(df['treated']==0).sum():,} "
      f"({100*in_support[df['treated']==0].mean():.1f}%)")

# Fraction with extreme scores
print(f"\nFraction with PS < 0.05: {(df['ps'] < 0.05).mean():.3f}")
print(f"Fraction with PS > 0.95: {(df['ps'] > 0.95).mean():.3f}")

# ── 3. Overlap plot ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(9, 5))
ax.hist(control_ps, bins=60, alpha=0.55, color='steelblue',
        density=True, label='Control (untreated)')
ax.hist(treated_ps, bins=60, alpha=0.55, color='firebrick',
        density=True, label='Treated (enrolled)')
ax.axvline(cs_lower, color='black', linestyle='--', linewidth=1,
           label=f'Common support [{cs_lower:.2f}, {cs_upper:.2f}]')
ax.axvline(cs_upper, color='black', linestyle='--', linewidth=1)
ax.set_xlabel('Propensity Score', fontsize=12)
ax.set_ylabel('Density', fontsize=12)
ax.set_title('Propensity Score Overlap: Wellness Program Enrollment',
             fontsize=13)
ax.legend()
plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig('overlap_check.png', dpi=150)
plt.show()
print("\nPlot saved to overlap_check.png")
```

---

### What the Output Will Tell Us

| What you see | What it means |
|---|---|
| Treated PS mostly > 0.7, control mostly < 0.3 | Severe overlap failure — groups are structurally different |
| < 50% of treated units in common support | Matching will discard most treated units; ATT estimate is for a small, unrepresentative subset |
| Extreme scores clustered near 0 or 1 | IPW weights will be explosive — do not use raw IPW |

---

### Three Questions to Answer Before We Proceed

1. **Do you have health status or income data?** Even proxies — insurance claims, salary bands, benefit tier, prior sick days — would help. Without them, we have a known omitted variable problem.

2. **What's your estimand?** ATT (effect on those who actually enrolled) is more defensible here than ATE, because at least the treated units exist in reality. Do you want to know "did the program help those who chose it?" or "would it help everyone?"

3. **What decision rides on this estimate?** If leadership is deciding whether to expand or mandate the program, the stakes affect how transparent we need to be about limitations.

### planner_pedagogy_ambiguous

**Run 1**: {"pedagogy": 1.0, "safety": 0.5, "actionable": 0.0, "overall": 0.5}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

This is a rich setup — and it actually contains **two distinct causal questions** that point to two complementary methods. Let me give you my read immediately, then refine with a couple of questions.

---

## My Recommendation: Synthetic Control + Fuzzy RDD (two-pronged)

You have two causal questions embedded here, and your data happens to support strong identification for both.

---

### Question 1: Did the city-wide algorithm change affect driver earnings?
→ **Synthetic Control (SCM)**

Your data structure is nearly textbook for this:
- **1 treated unit** (the city where the algorithm changed)
- **49 donor units** (the other cities, untreated)
- **18 months of panel data** (~12 pre-treatment, 6 post-treatment)

SCM constructs a weighted combination of the 49 control cities that best matches your treated city's pre-treatment earnings trajectory. The gap after month 12 is your causal estimate. The key advantage over DiD here is that you have *one treated city* — DiD with a single treated unit produces unreliable standard errors, whereas SCM is designed exactly for this case.

**Python package**: `scpi` (supports prediction intervals via conformal inference)

---

### Question 2: What's the effect of *opting in* to the new algorithm on individual driver earnings?
→ **Fuzzy RDD** (or equivalently, IV with score ≥ 80 as the instrument)

The score-80 threshold creates a natural experiment within the treated city:
- Drivers **just above 80** were eligible to opt in → ~30% did
- Drivers **just below 80** were not eligible

Because only 30% of eligibles actually opted in, this is a **fuzzy** discontinuity — the score cutoff sharply increases the *probability* of treatment, but doesn't determine it. That gives you a LATE: the effect of opting in on earnings **for drivers who opted in because they crossed the threshold** (compliers near the cutoff).

**Python package**: `rdrobust`

---

### Why both? They answer different things.

| | SCM | Fuzzy RDD |
|---|---|---|
| **Unit of analysis** | City aggregate | Individual driver |
| **Estimand** | City-level policy effect | LATE for compliers near score 80 |
| **Who it describes** | All drivers in the treated city | Drivers near the score threshold who opted in because they were eligible |
| **Threat to validity** | Pre-treatment fit quality, no concurrent shocks | Manipulation of driver score around 80, enough density near cutoff |

These are complementary, not redundant. SCM tells you whether the policy *worked at scale*; Fuzzy RDD tells you whether the algorithm itself (for those who chose it) changed individual earnings.

---

## Before I build the full plan, two questions:

1. **Scope of the city-level change**: When the algorithm "changed in one city" — did it roll out to *all drivers* in that city eventually, or only to those who opted in? This affects whether SCM is estimating the full rollout effect or just the opt-in effect at city scale.

2. **Driver score endogeneity**: Is there any reason drivers might have been able to game their score near the 80 threshold in anticipation of this opt-in program? If so, we'd need a McCrary density test urgently before trusting the RDD.

Once you answer those, I'll save a formal analysis plan with your Python implementation path.

</details>

---

### planner_pedagogy_clear

**Run 1**: {"pedagogy": 1.0, "safety": 1.0, "actionable": 0.0, "overall": 0.6666666666666666}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Based on what you've described, this is a **difference-in-differences (DiD)** problem — specifically a classic two-group DiD with store and time fixed effects (TWFE). You have everything you need: a clear treatment group, a large control group, and a long panel. Let me ask a few questions to sharpen the plan.

---

**1. Were all 10 stores treated at the same time (same week), or did the rollout happen in waves?**

This matters because simultaneous rollout → standard TWFE works cleanly. Staggered rollout → we need Callaway-Sant'Anna or Sun-Abraham to avoid bias from "bad comparisons" (late-treated stores being used as controls for early-treated ones).

---

**2. How were the 10 stores selected for the pilot?**

For example: were they chosen because they were underperforming, high-traffic, in specific regions, or essentially at random? This determines how worried we need to be about **parallel trends** — the core assumption of DiD, which says that in the absence of the loyalty program, treated and control stores would have followed the same sales trajectory. If the 10 stores were cherry-picked for a specific reason, their pre-treatment trend may already differ from the controls.

---

**3. Do you have any store-level characteristics** (size, region, store format, customer demographics)?

These help us (a) understand why certain stores were chosen, and (b) run covariate-adjusted DiD for tighter estimates if the groups look different.

</details>
