### rdd_pedagogy_clean

**Run 1**: {"pedagogy": 0.3333333333333333, "safety": 0.5, "actionable": 1.0, "overall": 0.611111111111111}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup Confirmed

Great setup for an RDD. Let me confirm what I'm working with before we touch any code:

| Element | Value |
|---|---|
| Running variable | `test_score` (continuous score) |
| Cutoff | 70 |
| Treatment | Honors program (`scholarship` column) |
| Outcome | `gpa` |
| N | 5,000 students |
| Variant | **Sharp RDD** — admission is deterministic at the cutoff |

> 🔍 **One quick note**: your column is named `scholarship` but you described it as the honors program. I'll use `scholarship` in code — just make sure it's actually 1 for admitted and 0 for not admitted (it looks correct in your sample). If "scholarship" is a *separate* financial award that also happens to use this cutoff, that would be a compound treatment problem we'd need to address. Assuming it's your treatment indicator, we're good to proceed.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions — What Makes This Work (or Not)

Before writing a single line of analysis code, we need to reason through the three core assumptions of RDD. This is where most first-time analyses go wrong.

### Assumption 1 — No Manipulation of the Running Variable 🔑

**What it means in your context**: Can students influence their own test score to land just above 70? If a student who would have scored 68 can strategically push to 71 (e.g., by retaking, appealing, or if graders know the cutoff), then the students just above and just below 70 are *not comparable* — the ones above are systematically more motivated or connected.

**Why it's fatal if violated**: The entire logic of RDD is that students near the cutoff are essentially identical except for which side they fell on — like a coin flip. Manipulation destroys that.

**We test this with the McCrary density test**: If students are gaming the cutoff, you'll see an unusual *spike* in the density of `test_score` just above 70 and a suspicious *drop* just below it.

---

### Assumption 2 — Continuity of Potential Outcomes

**What it means**: If the honors program didn't exist, GPA would follow a smooth curve through the score = 70 threshold. No other factor causes GPA to jump at exactly 70.

**Why it matters**: The jump in GPA we observe at 70 is *only* interpretable as the program's effect if nothing else also changes at 70. We test this by checking that *pre-determined covariates* (things fixed before the test, like age or prior grades if you have them) are smooth through the cutoff.

---

### Assumption 3 — No Compound Treatments

**What it means**: Is the honors program the *only* thing that changes at score = 70? If, say, students above 70 also receive a separate scholarship, a different advisor, or priority housing, your estimate would capture all of those effects bundled together.

**Quick check for you**: Does anything else at your institution use the 70-point threshold? This is worth confirming before we interpret results.

---

## Stage 3: Full Implementation

Here's a complete, annotated R script. Since you're new to RDD, I've explained every step.

```r
# ============================================================
# RDD Analysis: Effect of Honors Program on GPA
# Running variable: test_score | Cutoff: 70
# ============================================================

# --- 0. Install and load packages ---
# Run install lines only once; comment out after first install
# install.packages("rdrobust")
# install.packages("rddensity")

library(rdrobust)   # Core RDD estimation and plots
library(rddensity)  # McCrary density test for manipulation
library(ggplot2)    # Extra plots

# --- 1. Load your data ---
# Adjust this line to match how your data is stored
df <- read.csv("your_data.csv")

# Quick sanity check
cat("Total students:", nrow(df), "\n")
cat("Students at or above cutoff:", sum(df$test_score >= 70), "\n")
cat("Students below cutoff:", sum(df$test_score < 70), "\n")
cat("Treated (scholarship=1):", sum(df$scholarship == 1), "\n")

# Verify treatment aligns with cutoff (sharp RDD check)
# If this table shows non-zero in both cells, you may have a fuzzy RDD
table(above_cutoff = df$test_score >= 70, treated = df$scholarship)


# ============================================================
# ASSUMPTION CHECK 1: Manipulation (McCrary Density Test)
# ============================================================
# H0: density of test_score is continuous at the cutoff
# A significant p-value (< 0.05) suggests students are
# manipulating scores to land just above 70

density_test <- rddensity(X = df$test_score, c = 70)
summary(density_test)

# Visual: you want both sides of the cutoff to look like a
# smooth, uninterrupted distribution — no spike or gap at 70
rdplotdensity(density_test, df$test_score,
              title    = "McCrary Density Test — Manipulation Check",
              xlabel   = "Test Score",
              ylabel   = "Density")

# Also useful: simple histogram with cutoff line
ggplot(df, aes(x = test_score)) +
  geom_histogram(binwidth = 2, fill = "steelblue", color = "white") +
  geom_vline(xintercept = 70, color = "red", linetype = "dashed", linewidth = 1) +
  annotate("text", x = 72, y = Inf, label = "Cutoff = 70",
           color = "red", vjust = 2, hjust = 0) +
  labs(title = "Distribution of Test Scores",
       x = "Test Score", y = "Number of Students") +
  theme_minimal()


# ============================================================
# STEP 2: RD Plot — Visual Inspection of the Discontinuity
# ============================================================
# This is the most important diagnostic plot in RDD.
# You should see a visible jump in GPA right at score = 70.
# If you don't see a jump visually, the statistical estimate
# is unlikely to be meaningful.

rdplot(y = df$gpa,
       x = df$test_score,
       c = 70,
       title   = "RD Plot: Effect of Honors Program on GPA",
       x.label = "Test Score",
       y.label = "Final GPA")


# ============================================================
# STEP 3: Main RDD Estimate
# ============================================================
# rdrobust does three things automatically:
#   1. Selects the optimal bandwidth (how far from 70 to use)
#   2. Fits local linear regression on each side of the cutoff
#   3. Computes bias-corrected, robust confidence intervals
#      (these are more reliable than classical CIs for RDD)

rd_main <- rdrobust(y = df$gpa,
                    x = df$test_score,
                    c = 70)
summary(rd_main)

# How to read the output:
# "Coef" = estimated jump in GPA at the cutoff
# "Robust" CI = the one to report (bias-corrected)
# "BW est." = bandwidth used (observations within this many
#              points of 70 were included)
# "N (h)" = effective sample size used in estimation


# ============================================================
# STEP 4: Bandwidth Sensitivity
# ============================================================
# One bandwidth = one fragile result. We check that the
# estimate doesn't flip sign or become huge/tiny with
# different bandwidths. If it does, the finding is fragile.

opt_bw <- rd_main$bws[1, 1]  # Save the optimal bandwidth

bandwidths <- c(opt_bw * 0.50,
                opt_bw * 0.75,
                opt_bw * 1.00,
                opt_bw * 1.25,
                opt_bw * 1.50)

cat("\n--- Bandwidth Sensitivity ---\n")
cat(sprintf("%-12s %-10s %-10s %-10s\n",
            "Bandwidth", "Estimate", "CI Lower", "CI Upper"))

for (bw in bandwidths) {
  rd_bw <- rdrobust(df$gpa, df$test_score, c = 70, h = bw)
  cat(sprintf("%-12.3f %-10.4f %-10.4f %-10.4f\n",
              bw,
              rd_bw$coef[1],
              rd_bw$ci[3, 1],
              rd_bw$ci[3, 2]))
}


# ============================================================
# STEP 5: Placebo Cutoffs
# ============================================================
# We run the RDD at score values where there is NO treatment
# change (50 and 60, both below the real cutoff, using only
# control-side data). We should NOT find a significant effect.
# If we do, something is wrong with the model.

# Placebo at score = 60 (using only students scoring < 70)
rd_placebo_60 <- rdrobust(
  y = df$gpa[df$test_score < 70],
  x = df$test_score[df$test_score < 70],
  c = 60
)
cat("\n--- Placebo Cutoff at 60 ---\n")
summary(rd_placebo_60)

# Placebo at score = 50 (using only students scoring < 70)
rd_placebo_50 <- rdrobust(
  y = df$gpa[df$test_score < 70],
  x = df$test_score[df$test_score < 70],
  c = 50
)
cat("\n--- Placebo Cutoff at 50 ---\n")
summary(rd_placebo_50)
```

---

## What to Look for in the Output

Run the code and share the results. Here's what we're checking at each step:

| Check | Green light 🟢 | Red flag 🔴 |
|---|---|---|
| `table()` of cutoff vs. treatment | Perfect alignment (sharp) | Non-zero in both cells (fuzzy) |
| McCrary density test p-value | > 0.05 | < 0.05 (manipulation suspected) |
| Density/histogram plot | Smooth bell curve through 70 | Spike just above 70, gap just below |
| RD plot | Visible jump in GPA at 70 | Flat line through cutoff |
| Bandwidth sensitivity | Stable estimate across widths | Sign flip or huge swings |
| Placebo cutoffs | Non-significant (p > 0.05) | Significant (model misspecification) |

# Synthetic Control for California's Smoking Ban

Great setup for synthetic control — one treated unit, many donors, long pre-treatment window. This is actually a close cousin of the most famous synthetic control paper ever written (Abadie et al. 2010 used California's smoking *consumption* data). You're in well-mapped territory.

Let me walk you through all five stages.

---

## Stage 1: Study Design

Based on what you've described:

| Element | Value |
|---|---|
| Treated unit | California |
| Treatment | Smoking ban in restaurants (effective Jan 1, 1998 → **Q1 1998**) |
| Outcome | Restaurant employment |
| Donor pool | Up to 49 other states |
| Pre-treatment periods | Q1 1990 – Q4 1997 (32 quarters — excellent) |
| Post-treatment periods | Q1 1998 – Q4 2005 (32 quarters) |
| Language | R |

**Two questions before I generate code:**

1. **What predictor variables do you have** beyond restaurant employment itself? Common choices: state GDP, population, unemployment rate, share of food service in total employment, any pre-treatment outcome lags. (If you only have employment, that's fine — we can use lagged outcome averages as predictors.)

2. **Do you want to exclude any states from the donor pool?** Some states passed their own restaurant smoking bans in the late 1990s / early 2000s (e.g., Delaware 1994, New York 2003, Connecticut 2003). Including them would contaminate the counterfactual — their employment trends reflect their *own* bans, not an untreated world.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through each one for your specific context.

---

### 1. Pre-treatment fit quality ✅ *Testable*

> Can a weighted average of other states reproduce California's restaurant employment trajectory from 1990–1997?

**Why it matters**: If the synthetic California can't track real California *before* the ban, there's no reason to trust it as a counterfactual *after* the ban. The gap you'd measure post-1998 would partly reflect model failure, not the policy effect.

California is large and distinctive (large Hispanic workforce, tourism economy, high cost of living). A good donor pool should still be able to match it — but this is the first thing we'll check numerically with RMSPE (root mean squared prediction error).

**Concern level**: ⚠️ Monitor. California is unusual but not unprecedented.

---

### 2. Convex hull ⚠️ *Partially testable*

> Do California's pre-treatment characteristics fall within the range spanned by the 49 donor states?

**Why it matters**: The synthetic control optimization assigns *non-negative weights that sum to 1* (a convex combination). If California's employment level or growth rate is more extreme than any donor, the method is forced to extrapolate — which can bias results.

California has the largest restaurant sector in absolute terms, so we'll check whether the *per-capita* or *share* version of your outcome is more tractable. If convex hull is violated, I'll recommend switching to the **augmented synthetic control** (`augsynth`), which adds a bias-correction layer.

**Concern level**: ⚠️ Depends on how you define the outcome (levels vs. per capita vs. share of employment).

---

### 3. No interference between units ❓ *Untestable — must argue*

> Did California's smoking ban affect other states' restaurant employment?

**Why it matters**: If restaurants in Nevada saw more business because California diners crossed the border to smoke, then Nevada's employment trajectory would be inflated — and a synthetic California partly built from Nevada would underestimate the counterfactual. That would make the ban *look worse* than it was.

**Plausibility**: Cross-border spillovers from a restaurant smoking ban are probably small at the state level — most restaurant visits are local. The main concern is other states *seeing California's ban and passing their own* (policy diffusion, not economic spillover). That's why excluding states with concurrent bans matters (question 2 above).

**Concern level**: ⚠️ Low for economic spillover; manageable by cleaning the donor pool.

---

### 4. No anticipation ❓ *Partially testable*

> Did restaurant employment in California start changing *before* 1998 because of the anticipated ban?

**Why it matters**: If restaurants began adjusting (hiring freezes, early closures) in 1996–1997 when the legislation was being debated, our pre-treatment period is already "contaminated." The pre-fit might look good but the treated series is already diverging from its potential outcome.

California's law (AB 13) was signed in 1994 but phased in — restaurants were included starting January 1998. There's a real 3-year anticipation window to watch for.

**How we'll test this**: The in-time placebo will help — we'll "pretend" treatment happened in 1996 Q1 and see if a gap appears before the real 1998 date.

**Concern level**: ⚠️ Worth checking given the multi-year phase-in.

---

### 5. No concurrent shocks 🔍 *Must argue*

> Did anything else happen to California's restaurant sector around 1998 that didn't happen to other states?

**Why it matters**: If California also raised its minimum wage in 1998, or there was a regional recession, or a major wildfire season disrupted tourism — any of these would confound the employment effect we attribute to the smoking ban.

California *did* raise its minimum wage in 1996 and 1998. That's a potential confounder you'll want to acknowledge. The synthetic control partially handles this if other states also had minimum wage changes — but if the timing was uniquely bad for California, it could bias results downward (making the ban look more damaging).

**Concern level**: 🚨 This is the most important substantive threat. Flag minimum wage policy explicitly in your write-up.

---

### Assumption Summary

| Assumption | Status | Concern |
|---|---|---|
| Pre-treatment fit | Testable → check RMSPE | ⚠️ Monitor |
| Convex hull | Partially testable | ⚠️ Check outcome definition |
| No interference | Untestable — argue it | ✅ Plausible if donor pool is cleaned |
| No anticipation | Check via in-time placebo | ⚠️ Phase-in period is risky |
| No concurrent shocks | Must argue | 🚨 Minimum wage is real threat |

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

Once you confirm your predictor variables and donor pool decisions, here's the full analysis. I'm using the `Synth` package (canonical implementation):

```r
# ── 0. Packages ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
library(Synth)
library(tidyverse)

# ── 1. Data prep ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Your data should have:
#   state_fips : numeric unit identifier (California = 6)
#   year_quarter: numeric time variable (e.g., 1990.1, 1990.2, ... 2005.4)
#                 OR use a sequential quarter index: 1 = Q1 1990
#   restaurant_emp : outcome
#   gdp_pc, unemp_rate, pop : predictors (adjust to what you have)

# Define key values
CA_FIPS       <- 6
TREAT_QUARTER <- 33   # Q1 1998 = quarter 33 if Q1 1990 = quarter 1
PRE_START     <- 1    # Q1 1990
PRE_END       <- 32   # Q4 1997
POST_END      <- 65   # Q4 2005

# States to EXCLUDE from donor pool (had own bans; adjust as needed)
# Delaware (10), New York (36), Connecticut (9), Maine (23), Massachusetts (25)
exclude_fips <- c(10, 36, 9, 23, 25)

donor_fips <- df %>%
  filter(!state_fips %in% c(CA_FIPS, exclude_fips)) %>%
  pull(state_fips) %>%
  unique()

# ── 2. Dataprep ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
dataprep_out <- dataprep(
  foo                   = as.data.frame(df),
  predictors            = c("gdp_pc", "unemp_rate", "pop"),
  predictors.op         = "mean",
  dependent             = "restaurant_emp",
  unit.variable         = "state_fips",
  time.variable         = "quarter_index",
  treatment.identifier  = CA_FIPS,
  controls.identifier   = donor_fips,
  time.predictors.prior = PRE_START:PRE_END,
  time.optimize.ssr     = PRE_START:PRE_END,  # minimize pre-treatment RMSPE
  time.plot             = PRE_START:POST_END,
  # Also include lagged outcome values as predictors
  special.predictors    = list(
    list("restaurant_emp", PRE_START:8,   "mean"),  # 1990–1992 avg
    list("restaurant_emp", 9:20,           "mean"),  # 1993–1995 avg
    list("restaurant_emp", 21:PRE_END,     "mean")   # 1996–1997 avg
  )
)

# ── 3. Fit synthetic control ──────────────────────────────────────────────────
synth_out <- synth(dataprep_out)

# ── 4. Pre-treatment fit diagnostics ─────────────────────────────────────────
# Treated vs. synthetic
path.plot(
  synth.res    = synth_out,
  dataprep.res = dataprep_out,
  Ylab         = "Restaurant Employment",
  Xlab         = "Quarter",
  Legend       = c("California", "Synthetic California"),
  Legend.position = "bottomleft"
)
abline(v = TREAT_QUARTER, lty = 2, col = "red")

# Gap plot (treatment effect over time)
gaps.plot(
  synth.res    = synth_out,
  dataprep.res = dataprep_out,
  Ylab         = "Gap (Treated − Synthetic)",
  Xlab         = "Quarter",
  Main         = "California Restaurant Employment Gap"
)
abline(v = TREAT_QUARTER, lty = 2, col = "red")
abline(h = 0, lty = 3, col = "gray50")

# ── 5. Donor weights ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
weight_table <- synth.tab(
  synth.res    = synth_out,
  dataprep.res = dataprep_out
)
# Unit weights (which states matter most)
print(weight_table$tab.w[weight_table$tab.w[,1] > 0.01, ])

# Predictor balance
print(weight_table$tab.pred)

# ── 6. Pre-treatment RMSPE ────────────────────────────────────────────────────
gaps_pre <- dataprep_out$Y1plot[PRE_START:PRE_END] -
            (dataprep_out$Y0plot[PRE_START:PRE_END, ] %*% synth_out$solution.w)

rmspe_pre <- sqrt(mean(gaps_pre^2))
cat("Pre-treatment RMSPE:", round(rmspe_pre, 3), "\n")
cat("Outcome mean (pre)  :", round(mean(dataprep_out$Y1plot[PRE_START:PRE_END]), 3), "\n")
cat("RMSPE / mean        :", round(rmspe_pre / mean(dataprep_out$Y1plot[PRE_START:PRE_END]), 4), "\n")
```

---

## Stage 4: Falsification Tests

Run all three — each tests a different threat.

### Test 1: In-space placebo (permutation test for significance)

The key inferential tool in synthetic control. Apply the exact same method to each donor state as if *it* were treated in Q1 1998. Compare California's post/pre RMSPE ratio to the distribution of donor ratios. That ratio is your permutation p-value.

```r
# ── In-space placebo ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

store_gaps <- matrix(NA,
  nrow = length(PRE_START:POST_END),
  ncol = length(donor_fips) + 1
)
colnames(store_gaps) <- c(CA_FIPS, donor_fips)
rownames(store_gaps) <- PRE_START:POST_END

# California gap (already computed above)
store_gaps[, "6"] <- dataprep_out$Y1plot - 
                     (dataprep_out$Y0plot %*% synth_out$solution.w)

# Loop over each donor as a placebo "treated" unit
for (placebo_fips in donor_fips) {
  
  placebo_donors <- setdiff(c(CA_FIPS, donor_fips), placebo_fips)
  
  dataprep_placebo <- tryCatch(dataprep(
    foo                   = as.data.frame(df),
    predictors            = c("gdp_pc", "unemp_rate", "pop"),
    predictors.op         = "mean",
    dependent             = "restaurant_emp",
    unit.variable         = "state_fips",
    time.variable         = "quarter_index",
    treatment.identifier  = placebo_fips,
    controls.identifier   = placebo_donors,
    time.predictors.prior = PRE_START:PRE_END,
    time.optimize.ssr     = PRE_START:PRE_END,
    time.plot             = PRE_START:POST_END,
    special.predictors    = list(
      list("restaurant_emp", PRE_START:8,  "mean"),
      list("restaurant_emp", 9:20,          "mean"),
      list("restaurant_emp", 21:PRE_END,    "mean")
    )
  ), error = function(e) NULL)
  
  if (is.null(dataprep_placebo)) next
  
  synth_placebo <- tryCatch(synth(dataprep_placebo, Margin.ipop = 0.005),
                             error = function(e) NULL)
  if (is.null(synth_placebo)) next
  
  store_gaps[, as.character(placebo_fips)] <-
    dataprep_placebo$Y1plot - 
    (dataprep_placebo$Y0plot %*% synth_placebo$solution.w)
}

# ── Plot all placebo gaps ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────
quarters <- PRE_START:POST_END

matplot(quarters, store_gaps,
  type = "l", col = "gray80", lty = 1, lwd = 0.8,
  xlab = "Quarter", ylab = "Gap (Employment)",
  main = "In-Space Placebos: All States"
)
lines(quarters, store_gaps[, "6"],
  col = "black", lwd = 2.5
)
abline(v = TREAT_QUARTER, lty = 2, col = "red")
abline(h = 0, lty = 3, col = "gray50")
legend("topleft",
  legend = c("California", "Donor placebos"),
  col = c("black", "gray80"), lwd = c(2.5, 0.8)
)

# ── Permutation p-value ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Drop donors with poor pre-fit (RMSPE > 5x California's pre-RMSPE)
# This is standard practice (Abadie et al. 2010)
compute_rmspe <- function(gaps, pre_idx) sqrt(mean(gaps[pre_idx]^2, na.rm = TRUE))
pre_idx  <- PRE_START:PRE_END - (PRE_START - 1)
post_idx <- TREAT_QUARTER:POST_END - (PRE_START - 1)

rmspe_pre_all  <- apply(store_gaps, 2, compute_rmspe, pre_idx)
rmspe_post_all <- apply(store_gaps, 2, compute_rmspe, post_idx)
ratio_all      <- rmspe_post_all / rmspe_pre_all

# Keep only units with pre-RMSPE within 5x California's
keep <- rmspe_pre_all <= 5 * rmspe_pre_all["6"]
ratio_clean <- ratio_all[keep]

p_value <- mean(ratio_clean >= ratio_clean["6"])
cat("California post/pre RMSPE ratio:", round(ratio_clean["6"], 3), "\n")
cat("Permutation p-value            :", round(p_value, 3), "\n")
cat("N donors in permutation test   :", sum(keep) - 1, "\n")
```

---

### Test 2: In-time placebo (no-effect check)

Pretend treatment happened in Q1 1995, using only pre-1998 data. There should be **no gap** opening at the fake treatment date — if there is, the pre-treatment relationship is unstable.

```r
# ── In-time placebo ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
PLACEBO_QUARTER <- 21  # Q1 1995 (quarter 21 if Q1 1990 = 1)

dataprep_time_placebo <- dataprep(
  foo                   = as.data.frame(df %>% filter(quarter_index < TREAT_QUARTER)),
  predictors            = c("gdp_pc", "unemp_rate", "pop"),
  predictors.op         = "mean",
  dependent             = "restaurant_emp",
  unit.variable         = "state_fips",
  time.variable         = "quarter_index",
  treatment.identifier  = CA_FIPS,
  controls.identifier   = donor_fips,
  time.predictors.prior = PRE_START:(PLACEBO_QUARTER - 1),
  time.optimize.ssr     = PRE_START:(PLACEBO_QUARTER - 1),
  time.plot             = PRE_START:(TREAT_QUARTER - 1),
  special.predictors    = list(
    list("restaurant_emp", PRE_START:8,                   "mean"),
    list("restaurant_emp", 9:(PLACEBO_QUARTER - 1),       "mean")
  )
)

synth_time_placebo <- synth(dataprep_time_placebo)

path.plot(
  synth.res    = synth_time_placebo,
  dataprep.res = dataprep_time_placebo,
  Ylab         = "Restaurant Employment",
  Xlab         = "Quarter",
  Main         = "In-Time Placebo: Fake Treatment Q1 1995"
)
abline(v = PLACEBO_QUARTER, lty = 2, col = "blue")

# No gap should appear after the blue line
```

---

### Test 3: Leave-one-out donor analysis

Re-run the synthetic control removing each high-weight donor one at a time. If removing a single state dramatically changes the estimate, the result is fragile.

```r
# ── Leave-one-out ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Identify top donors (weight > 5%)
top_donors <- weight_table$tab.w %>%
  filter(w.weights > 0.05) %>%
  pull(unit.numbers)

loo_gaps <- matrix(NA, nrow = length(PRE_START:POST_END), ncol = length(top_donors))
colnames(loo_gaps) <- top_donors

for (i in seq_along(top_donors)) {
  
  loo_donors <- setdiff(donor_fips, top_donors[i])
  
  dp_loo <- tryCatch(dataprep(
    foo                   = as.data.frame(df),
    predictors            = c("gdp_pc", "unemp_rate", "pop"),
    predictors.op         = "mean",
    dependent             = "restaurant_emp",
    unit.variable         = "state_fips",
    time.variable         = "quarter_index",
    treatment.identifier  = CA_FIPS,
    controls.identifier   = loo_donors,
    time.predictors.prior = PRE_START:PRE_END,
    time.optimize.ssr     = PRE_START:PRE_END,
    time.plot             = PRE_START:POST_END,
    special.predictors    = list(
      list("restaurant_emp", PRE_START:8,  "mean"),
      list("restaurant_emp", 9:20,          "mean"),
      list("restaurant_emp", 21:PRE_END,    "mean")
    )
  ), error = function(e) NULL)
  
  if (is.null(dp_loo)) next
  syn_loo <- tryCatch(synth(dp_loo), error = function(e) NULL)
  if (is.null(syn_loo)) next
  
  loo_gaps[, i] <- dp_loo$Y1plot - (dp_loo$Y0plot %*% syn_loo$solution.w)
}

# Plot: main gap vs. all leave-one-out gaps
main_gap <- dataprep_out$Y1plot - (dataprep_out$Y0plot %*% synth_out$solution.w)

matplot(quarters, loo_gaps,
  type = "l", col = "steelblue", lty = 2, lwd = 1,
  xlab = "Quarter", ylab = "Gap (Employment)",
  main = "Leave-One-Out: Remove Each Top Donor"
)
lines(quarters, main_gap, col = "black", lwd = 2.5)
abline(v = TREAT_QUARTER, lty = 2, col = "red")
abline(h = 0, lty = 3, col = "gray50")
legend("topleft", legend = c("Main estimate", "Leave-one-out"),
  col = c("black", "steelblue"), lwd = c(2.5, 1), lty = c(1, 2))
```

---

## Stage 5: What to Look For in Your Results

Once you run this, here's how to interpret:

**Pre-treatment fit**: Report RMSPE and RMSPE/mean. Under ~2% is excellent; under ~5% is acceptable. If it's higher, the synthetic control is unreliable and we should consider `augsynth`.

**The gap plot**: A negative gap post-1998 means the ban *reduced* restaurant employment vs. the counterfactual. A gap near zero means no detectable effect.

**Permutation p-value**: If California's post/pre RMSPE ratio ranks in the top 5% of all donor placebos, p ≤ 0.05. With 44 usable donors, the minimum achievable p is ~0.02.

**The minimum wage confound**: If you find a large negative effect, you'll need to either (a) argue the synthetic control absorbs state-level wage variation, or (b) add minimum wage as a predictor explicitly.

1. What predictors beyond restaurant employment do you have available?
2. Do you want to exclude specific states from the donor pool, or should I suggest a standard exclusion list based on states with pre-2005 smoking bans?

### sc_pedagogy_messy

**Run 1**: {"pedagogy": 1.0, "safety": 0.5, "actionable": 1.0, "overall": 0.8333333333333334}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1 — Setup

You're building a synthetic control for **one treated country** vs. **15 donors**, tracking **GDP growth** over **20 annual periods** (10 pre, 10 post). Python. 

**Before writing a line of code, there's a signal in your description that changes the recommended method.**

---

## Stage 2 — Assumptions

Let me walk through the four key assumptions for your setting, in order of concern.

---

### 🚨 Assumption 1: Convex Hull (VIOLATED — addressed below)

**What it means**: The standard synthetic control works by finding non-negative weights on donors that sum to 1 (a "convex combination"). This only works if your treated unit's pre-treatment behavior lies *within the range* of what the donor pool can span. If the treated country was unusually volatile — different from all donors — no weighted average of donors can reproduce that pattern.

**Why it matters for you**: You described the treated country as having "unusually volatile growth pre-treatment — different from all donors." This is the textbook convex hull violation. Standard SCM will:
- Force weights onto whichever donors are *least unlike* the treated unit
- Still produce a poor pre-treatment fit
- Make the post-treatment gap partially reflect model failure, not just the carbon tax effect

**What to do**: Use **`scpi_pkg`** with a **ridge-penalized** weight constraint (`"ridge"`), which relaxes the convex hull requirement by allowing the donor combination to extrapolate in a controlled, regularized way — rather than just failing silently. We'll run *both* the standard simplex and the ridge version and compare them. If they diverge substantially, the convex hull violation was materially biasing the standard estimate.

---

### ⚠️ Assumption 2: Pre-Treatment Fit Quality

**What it means**: Even with ridge weights, we need to verify the synthetic control tracks the treated unit's volatile pre-period reasonably well. We'll measure this with **RMSPE** (root mean squared prediction error). Rule of thumb: RMSPE should be small relative to the outcome's standard deviation.

**Testable**: Yes — we'll plot it and compute RMSPE explicitly.

---

### Assumption 3: No Interference Between Units

**What it means**: The 15 donor countries shouldn't be affected by the carbon tax in country A. If the tax causes carbon-intensive industries to relocate to donor countries (boosting their GDP), those donors are contaminated and the synthetic control underestimates the true effect.

**Testable**: Not formally, but we'll visually inspect donor trajectories around the treatment date and flag any donors that show unusual breaks.

**Your call**: Does country A trade heavily with the donors? Are there known spillover channels?

---

### Assumption 4: No Structural Breaks / Concurrent Events

**What it means**: The post-treatment gap should reflect the carbon tax, not something else that happened to country A at the same time (recession, election, trade shock).

**Testable**: In-time placebo — backdate the treatment to period 5 and check if a fake "gap" emerges. If it does, the pre-period relationship is unstable.

---

### Assumption Summary

| Assumption | Status | Action |
|---|---|---|
| Convex hull | 🚨 Likely violated | Use ridge weights; compare to simplex |
| Pre-treatment fit | ⚠️ At risk (volatile treated unit) | Compute RMSPE; inspect plot |
| No interference | Unknown | Argue substantively; inspect donor breaks |
| No structural breaks | Unknown | In-time placebo test |

---

## Stage 3 — Implementation

```python
# ── 0. Dependencies ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# pip install scpi-pkg matplotlib pandas numpy

import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
import matplotlib.gridspec as gridspec
from scpi_pkg.scdata import scdata
from scpi_pkg.scest import scest
from scpi_pkg.scpi  import scpi
from scpi_pkg.scplot import scplot

# ── 1. Load & inspect ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
df = pd.read_csv("your_data.csv")          # adjust path

TREATED_UNIT   = "A"
TREATMENT_TIME = 11                        # first post-treatment period
PRE_PERIODS    = list(range(1, 11))        # periods 1–10
POST_PERIODS   = list(range(11, 21))       # periods 11–20

donor_units = sorted(df.loc[df["state"] != TREATED_UNIT, "state"].unique().tolist())
print(f"Treated unit : {TREATED_UNIT}")
print(f"Donor pool   : {len(donor_units)} units → {donor_units}")

# ── 2. Convex hull diagnostic ─────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Is the treated unit's pre-treatment outcome range inside the donor envelope?
pre = df[df["period"].isin(PRE_PERIODS)]

treated_mean = pre.loc[pre["state"] == TREATED_UNIT, "outcome"].mean()
treated_std  = pre.loc[pre["state"] == TREATED_UNIT, "outcome"].std()
donor_means  = pre.loc[pre["state"] != TREATED_UNIT].groupby("state")["outcome"].mean()

print(f"\nTreated pre-treatment mean : {treated_mean:.2f}")
print(f"Treated pre-treatment std  : {treated_std:.2f}  ← high relative to donors?")
print(f"Donor mean range           : [{donor_means.min():.2f}, {donor_means.max():.2f}]")
print(f"Treated unit inside range? : {donor_means.min() <= treated_mean <= donor_means.max()}")

# Visual: pre-treatment trajectories
fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(11, 5))
for unit, grp in pre.groupby("state"):
    if unit == TREATED_UNIT:
        ax.plot(grp["period"], grp["outcome"], color="black", lw=2.5,
                label="Treated (A)", zorder=5)
    else:
        ax.plot(grp["period"], grp["outcome"], color="steelblue", alpha=0.3, lw=1)

ax.plot([], [], color="steelblue", alpha=0.5, lw=1, label="Donors")
ax.set_xlabel("Period"); ax.set_ylabel("GDP Growth")
ax.set_title("Pre-Treatment Trajectories: Treated vs Donor Pool\n(Does any donor track the treated unit's volatility?)")
ax.legend(); plt.tight_layout(); plt.savefig("pre_trajectories.png", dpi=150); plt.show()

# ── 3. Prepare scdata object (shared by both models) ─────────────────────────
scd = scdata(
    df          = df,
    id_var      = "state",
    time_var    = "period",
    outcome_var = "outcome",
    period_pre  = PRE_PERIODS,
    period_post = POST_PERIODS,
    unit_tr     = TREATED_UNIT,
    unit_co     = donor_units
)

# ── 4. Model A — Standard simplex SCM ────────────────────────────────────────
# Classic synthetic control: non-negative weights summing to 1.
# Expected to struggle here due to convex hull violation.
sc_simplex = scest(scd, w_constr={"name": "simplex"})
print("\n── Standard SCM (simplex) ──")
print(sc_simplex)

# Donor weights
w_simplex = pd.Series(
    sc_simplex.w.flatten(),
    index=donor_units,
    name="weight_simplex"
).sort_values(ascending=False)
print("\nDonor weights (simplex):")
print(w_simplex[w_simplex > 0.01].to_string())

dominant = w_simplex.iloc[0]
if dominant > 0.80:
    print(f"\n⚠️  WARNING: One donor has weight {dominant:.1%} — effectively a pairwise comparison.")

# ── 5. Model B — Ridge SCM (augmented-style, handles convex hull violation) ──
# Ridge relaxes the simplex constraint, allowing controlled extrapolation.
# This is the recommended model given the treated unit's unusual volatility.
sc_ridge = scest(scd, w_constr={"name": "ridge"})
print("\n── Ridge SCM (handles convex hull violation) ──")
print(sc_ridge)

w_ridge = pd.Series(
    sc_ridge.w.flatten(),
    index=donor_units,
    name="weight_ridge"
).sort_values(ascending=False)
print("\nDonor weights (ridge):")
print(w_ridge.to_string())

# ── 6. Pre-treatment fit: RMSPE comparison ────────────────────────────────────
def compute_rmspe(sc_obj, scd_obj, periods):
    """Compute RMSPE for given periods from a fitted scest object."""
    Y1   = scd_obj.A.values.flatten()          # treated outcomes (pre)
    Ydon = scd_obj.B.values                    # donor matrix (pre)
    w    = sc_obj.w.flatten()
    Ysyn = Ydon @ w
    residuals = Y1 - Ysyn
    return np.sqrt(np.mean(residuals**2))

rmspe_simplex = compute_rmspe(sc_simplex, scd, PRE_PERIODS)
rmspe_ridge   = compute_rmspe(sc_ridge,   scd, PRE_PERIODS)
outcome_sd    = df.loc[df["state"] == TREATED_UNIT, "outcome"].std()

print(f"\n── Pre-Treatment Fit ──")
print(f"Outcome std dev       : {outcome_sd:.4f}")
print(f"Simplex RMSPE         : {rmspe_simplex:.4f}  ({rmspe_simplex/outcome_sd:.1%} of SD)")
print(f"Ridge   RMSPE         : {rmspe_ridge:.4f}  ({rmspe_ridge/outcome_sd:.1%} of SD)")
print(f"Ridge improvement     : {(rmspe_simplex - rmspe_ridge)/rmspe_simplex:.1%}")
print()
if rmspe_ridge / outcome_sd > 0.10:
    print("⚠️  Ridge RMSPE > 10% of outcome SD — pre-treatment fit is imperfect.")
    print("   Results should be interpreted cautiously.")

# ── 7. Prediction intervals (use ridge model as primary) ─────────────────────
sc_ridge_pi = scpi(scd, w_constr={"name": "ridge"}, cores=1)
print("\n── Prediction Intervals (Ridge SCM) ──")
print(sc_ridge_pi)

# ── 8. Plot: treated vs synthetic, both models ───────────────────────────────
def build_trajectory(sc_obj, scd_obj, all_periods):
    """Reconstruct full synthetic trajectory from scest output."""
    # Pre-period
    w      = sc_obj.w.flatten()
    Y_pre  = scd_obj.B.values @ w
    # Post-period — use scpi output if available, else estimate manually
    Y_post = scd_obj.P.values @ w  if hasattr(scd_obj, "P") else np.full(len(POST_PERIODS), np.nan)
    return np.concatenate([Y_pre, Y_post])

treated_full = df[df["state"] == TREATED_UNIT].sort_values("period")["outcome"].values
periods      = sorted(df["period"].unique())

fig, axes = plt.subplots(1, 2, figsize=(14, 5), sharey=True)

for ax, sc_obj, label, color in zip(
    axes,
    [sc_simplex, sc_ridge],
    ["Standard SCM (simplex)", "Ridge SCM (recommended)"],
    ["tomato", "steelblue"]
):
    syn = build_trajectory(sc_obj, scd, periods)
    ax.plot(periods, treated_full, "k-",  lw=2.5, label="Treated (A)")
    ax.plot(periods, syn,          "--",  lw=2,   color=color, label="Synthetic")
    ax.axvline(TREATMENT_TIME - 0.5, color="gray", ls=":", lw=1.5, label="Treatment")
    ax.set_title(label); ax.set_xlabel("Period"); ax.set_ylabel("GDP Growth")
    ax.legend(fontsize=9)

plt.suptitle("Treated vs Synthetic Control — GDP Growth", y=1.02, fontsize=13)
plt.tight_layout(); plt.savefig("sc_trends.png", dpi=150); plt.show()

# ── 9. Gap plot (treatment effect over time) ─────────────────────────────────
syn_ridge = build_trajectory(sc_ridge, scd, periods)
gap       = treated_full - syn_ridge

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(11, 4))
ax.axhline(0, color="black", lw=1)
ax.axvline(TREATMENT_TIME - 0.5, color="gray", ls=":", lw=1.5, label="Treatment")
ax.fill_between(periods, gap, 0,
                where=(np.array(periods) >= TREATMENT_TIME),
                alpha=0.25, color="steelblue", label="Post-treatment gap")
ax.plot(periods, gap, "o-", color="steelblue", lw=2, ms=5)
ax.set_xlabel("Period"); ax.set_ylabel("Gap (Treated − Synthetic)")
ax.set_title("Treatment Effect: Treated minus Synthetic (Ridge SCM)")
ax.legend(); plt.tight_layout(); plt.savefig("sc_gap.png", dpi=150); plt.show()

avg_effect = gap[len(PRE_PERIODS):].mean()
cum_effect = gap[len(PRE_PERIODS):].sum()
print(f"\nAverage per-period effect (post) : {avg_effect:.4f}")
print(f"Cumulative effect (post)         : {cum_effect:.4f}")
```

---

## Stage 4 — Robustness Tests

### Test 1 — In-Space Placebo (permutation inference)

```python
# Apply the ridge SCM to every donor as if it were the treated unit.
# If country A's post/pre RMSPE ratio is extreme relative to donors,
# the effect is unlikely to be noise.

placebo_ratios = {}

for placebo_unit in donor_units:
    donor_pool_placebo = [u for u in donor_units if u != placebo_unit]
    if len(donor_pool_placebo) < 2:
        continue
    try:
        scd_p = scdata(
            df          = df,
            id_var      = "state",
            time_var    = "period",
            outcome_var = "outcome",
            period_pre  = PRE_PERIODS,
            period_post = POST_PERIODS,
            unit_tr     = placebo_unit,
            unit_co     = donor_pool_placebo
        )
        sc_p        = scest(scd_p, w_constr={"name": "ridge"})
        Y1_p        = scd_p.A.values.flatten()
        Ysyn_p_pre  = scd_p.B.values @ sc_p.w.flatten()
        rmspe_pre_p = np.sqrt(np.mean((Y1_p - Ysyn_p_pre)**2))

        Y1_post_p   = df.loc[(df["state"] == placebo_unit) &
                              (df["period"].isin(POST_PERIODS)), "outcome"].values
        Ysyn_p_post = scd_p.P.values @ sc_p.w.flatten() if hasattr(scd_p, "P") \
                      else np.full(len(POST_PERIODS), np.nan)
        rmspe_post_p = np.sqrt(np.mean((Y1_post_p - Ysyn_p_post)**2))

        if rmspe_pre_p > 0:
            placebo_ratios[placebo_unit] = rmspe_post_p / rmspe_pre_p
    except Exception as e:
        print(f"  Skipped {placebo_unit}: {e}")

# Treated unit's ratio
Y1_post   = df.loc[(df["state"] == TREATED_UNIT) &
                   (df["period"].isin(POST_PERIODS)), "outcome"].values
Ysyn_post = syn_ridge[len(PRE_PERIODS):]
rmspe_post_treated = np.sqrt(np.mean((Y1_post - Ysyn_post)**2))
ratio_treated = rmspe_post_treated / rmspe_ridge

all_ratios       = list(placebo_ratios.values()) + [ratio_treated]
rank_treated     = sum(r >= ratio_treated for r in all_ratios)
pseudo_pvalue    = rank_treated / len(all_ratios)

print(f"\n── In-Space Placebo ──")
print(f"Treated unit post/pre RMSPE ratio : {ratio_treated:.3f}")
print(f"Donor ratios (median)             : {np.median(list(placebo_ratios.values())):.3f}")
print(f"Rank among all units              : {rank_treated} / {len(all_ratios)}")
print(f"Pseudo p-value                    : {pseudo_pvalue:.3f}")

# Plot distribution of ratios
fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(9, 4))
ax.hist(list(placebo_ratios.values()), bins=10, color="lightgray",
        edgecolor="white", label="Donor placebos")
ax.axvline(ratio_treated, color="black", lw=2.5, label=f"Treated A (ratio={ratio_treated:.2f})")
ax.set_xlabel("Post/Pre RMSPE Ratio"); ax.set_ylabel("Count")
ax.set_title(f"Permutation Test — Pseudo p-value: {pseudo_pvalue:.3f}")
ax.legend(); plt.tight_layout(); plt.savefig("placebo_dist.png", dpi=150); plt.show()
```

### Test 2 — Simplex vs Ridge Comparison (convex hull check)

```python
# If simplex and ridge estimates diverge substantially,
# the standard SCM was being distorted by the convex hull violation.

print("\n── Model Comparison: Simplex vs Ridge ──")
syn_simplex = build_trajectory(sc_simplex, scd, periods)
gap_simplex = treated_full - syn_simplex
gap_ridge_arr = treated_full - syn_ridge

print(f"Simplex avg post-treatment effect : {gap_simplex[len(PRE_PERIODS):].mean():.4f}")
print(f"Ridge   avg post-treatment effect : {gap_ridge_arr[len(PRE_PERIODS):].mean():.4f}")
print(f"Divergence (abs diff in ATT)      : {abs(gap_simplex[len(PRE_PERIODS):].mean() - gap_ridge_arr[len(PRE_PERIODS):].mean()):.4f}")

if abs(gap_simplex[len(PRE_PERIODS):].mean() - gap_ridge_arr[len(PRE_PERIODS):].mean()) > 0.05 * outcome_sd:
    print("⚠️  Estimates diverge meaningfully — convex hull violation was material.")
    print("   Report the ridge estimate as primary; simplex as a sensitivity check.")
```

**Quick flag on sample size**: With 15 donors, the minimum achievable pseudo p-value from the permutation test is 1/16 ≈ **0.06**. You cannot achieve conventional p < 0.05. This is a hard limit — not a flaw in execution — and should be stated explicitly in any write-up.

### timeseries_pedagogy_clean

**Run 1**: {"pedagogy": 0.6666666666666666, "safety": 0.5, "actionable": 0.5, "overall": 0.5555555555555555}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup

Good news: with 42 pre-treatment months and 6 post-treatment months of monthly data, you have a solid foundation for an ITS analysis. Let me confirm the design before we proceed:

- **Intervention**: month 43 (first `post == 1`)
- **Pre-period**: 42 months ✅ (covers 3.5 annual cycles — enough to detect seasonality)
- **Post-period**: 6 months ⚠️ (short — effect estimates will carry wider uncertainty)
- **No control series** → CausalImpact without controls is weaker; **segmented regression ITS** is the most transparent choice here, with a `causalimpact` run as a robustness check

**Method selected: Segmented Regression (ITS)**
Models a level shift *and* a slope change at the intervention point — the two ways a campaign can affect revenue.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through each one for your specific context.

---

### 1. 🔴 No concurrent confounding events *(most critical — untestable)*

> "Did anything else change around month 43 — pricing, a competitor exiting, macroeconomic shifts, a product launch, seasonality?"

This is the **single biggest threat** to your analysis. A marketing campaign launched alongside a price cut or during a seasonal peak can't be disentangled from the series alone. You need to argue this from business knowledge, not data.

**Action needed**: Before we go further, please think through: *what else changed in your business or market around month 43?*

---

### 2. ⚠️ Pre-period model fit

> "Can the model learn your revenue's pattern from 42 months of pre-data well enough to project what would have happened without the campaign?"

This is testable. We'll check MAPE and residual autocorrelation after fitting.

---

### 3. ⚠️ Stationarity

> "Is your revenue series mean-reverting, or does it have a trend or drift? Non-stationary series can produce fake 'breaks' that look like campaign effects."

Testable with ADF + KPSS tests — code below.

---

### 4. ⚠️ Short post-period

> "With only 6 post-treatment months, your effect estimate will be noisy. You can estimate a level shift reliably, but a slope change (trajectory shift) will be very uncertain — not enough post-period data."

This isn't a fatal flaw, but it means **wide confidence intervals** and you should weight the level-shift estimate more than the slope estimate.

---

## Stage 3: Pre-Flight Diagnostics

Run this first — before the main model:

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
import matplotlib.patches as mpatches
from statsmodels.tsa.stattools import adfuller, kpss
from statsmodels.stats.diagnostic import acorr_ljungbox
from statsmodels.graphics.tsaplots import plot_acf

# ── Load your data ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# df = pd.read_csv("your_data.csv")  # replace with your actual path

# ── Basic structure check ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────
treatment_time = df.loc[df['post'] == 1, 'time'].min()  # first treated period
pre = df[df['post'] == 0]['outcome']
post = df[df['post'] == 1]['outcome']

print(f"Intervention point:       time = {treatment_time}")
print(f"Pre-period observations:  {len(pre)}")
print(f"Post-period observations: {len(post)}")
print(f"Pre-period mean:          {pre.mean():.2f}")
print(f"Post-period mean:         {post.mean():.2f}")
print(f"Naive before/after diff:  {post.mean() - pre.mean():.2f}  (unadjusted for trend)")

# ── 1. Structural break check: visual inspection ──────────────────────────────
fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(12, 4))
ax.plot(df['time'], df['outcome'], color='steelblue', linewidth=1.5, label='Revenue')
ax.axvline(treatment_time, color='crimson', linestyle='--', linewidth=1.5, label='Campaign launch')
ax.set_xlabel('Month'); ax.set_ylabel('Revenue'); ax.set_title('Revenue over time — inspect pre-period for level shifts or trend breaks')
ax.legend(); plt.tight_layout(); plt.show()

# ── 2. Stationarity: ADF + KPSS on pre-period only ───────────────────────────
print("\n── Stationarity tests (pre-period only) ──")
adf = adfuller(pre, autolag='AIC')
print(f"ADF statistic:  {adf[0]:.4f}  |  p-value: {adf[1]:.4f}")
print("  → p < 0.05: stationary ✅   p > 0.05: possible unit root ⚠️")

kpss_result = kpss(pre, regression='c', nlags='auto')
print(f"KPSS statistic: {kpss_result[0]:.4f} |  p-value: {kpss_result[1]:.4f}")
print("  → p > 0.05: stationary ✅   p < 0.05: non-stationary ⚠️")

# ── 3. Seasonal cycle coverage ────────────────────────────────────────────────
print(f"\n── Seasonal check ──")
print(f"Annual cycles in pre-period: {len(pre)/12:.1f}  (recommend ≥ 3 for monthly data)")

# ── 4. ACF of pre-period ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────
fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(10, 3))
plot_acf(pre, ax=ax, lags=24, title='ACF of pre-period revenue (check for seasonality at lag 12)')
plt.tight_layout(); plt.show()
```

**What to look for:**
| Check | Green | Yellow | Red |
|---|---|---|---|
| Pre-period plot | Stable trend/noise | Gradual drift | Obvious level jump mid-pre-period |
| ADF p-value | < 0.05 | 0.05–0.15 | > 0.15 |
| KPSS p-value | > 0.10 | 0.05–0.10 | < 0.05 |
| ACF lag 12 spike | None | Moderate | Strong (unmodeled seasonality) |

---

## Stage 4: Main Estimation

Once diagnostics pass (or you've noted the caveats):

```python
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
from scipy import stats

# ── Build ITS variables ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────
df = df.copy()
df['time_since'] = np.maximum(0, df['time'] - (treatment_time - 1))
# time_since = 0 in pre-period, counts up from 1 in post-period
# This captures slope change (trajectory shift) after launch

# ── Segmented regression with HAC standard errors ────────────────────────────
# HAC = heteroscedasticity and autocorrelation consistent
# maxlags=4 is a reasonable default for monthly data
model = smf.ols('outcome ~ time + post + time_since', data=df).fit(
    cov_type='HAC', cov_kwds={'maxlags': 4}
)
print(model.summary())

# ── Extract and interpret key coefficients ────────────────────────────────────
intercept   = model.params['Intercept']
slope_pre   = model.params['time']
level_shift = model.params['post']
slope_change = model.params['time_since']

print("\n── Causal effect decomposition ──")
print(f"Pre-period slope (monthly trend):   {slope_pre:+.3f} per month")
print(f"Level shift at campaign launch:     {level_shift:+.3f}  (immediate step change)")
print(f"Slope change after campaign:        {slope_change:+.3f} per month  (trajectory shift)")
print(f"  → Combined effect at month +6:   {level_shift + 6*slope_change:+.3f}")

# ── Confidence intervals ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────
ci = model.conf_int()
print(f"\n95% CI — Level shift:  [{ci.loc['post', 0]:.3f}, {ci.loc['post', 1]:.3f}]")
print(f"95% CI — Slope change: [{ci.loc['time_since', 0]:.3f}, {ci.loc['time_since', 1]:.3f}]")

# ── Counterfactual vs observed plot ──────────────────────────────────────────
df['counterfactual'] = (intercept
                        + slope_pre * df['time']
                        # post=0 and time_since=0 for the counterfactual
                        )
df['fitted'] = model.fittedvalues

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(13, 5))
ax.plot(df['time'], df['outcome'],      color='steelblue',  lw=2,   label='Observed revenue')
ax.plot(df['time'], df['counterfactual'], color='gray',     lw=2,   linestyle='--', label='Counterfactual (no campaign)')
ax.plot(df['time'], df['fitted'],        color='darkorange', lw=1.5, linestyle=':', label='Model fit')
ax.axvline(treatment_time, color='crimson', linestyle='--', lw=1.5, label='Campaign launch')

# Shade the effect region
post_df = df[df['post'] == 1]
ax.fill_between(post_df['time'], post_df['counterfactual'], post_df['outcome'],
                alpha=0.2, color='green', label='Estimated effect')

ax.set_xlabel('Month'); ax.set_ylabel('Revenue')
ax.set_title('Interrupted Time Series: Campaign Effect on Revenue')
ax.legend(); plt.tight_layout(); plt.show()

# ── Residual diagnostics ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────
residuals = model.resid
pre_resid = residuals[df['post'] == 0]

lb = acorr_ljungbox(pre_resid, lags=10, return_df=True)
print("\n── Residual diagnostics (pre-period) ──")
print(lb[['lb_stat', 'lb_pvalue']])
print("  → All p > 0.05: residuals are white noise ✅")

mape = np.mean(np.abs(pre_resid / df.loc[df['post']==0, 'outcome'])) * 100
print(f"\nPre-period MAPE: {mape:.2f}%")
print("  → < 5%: good fit ✅   5–10%: acceptable ⚠️   > 10%: poor fit 🚨")
```

---

## Stage 5: Robustness Checks

Two checks — run both:

### 5a. Placebo intervention date (fake treatment in pre-period)
```python
# Use month 21 as a fake launch date (midpoint of pre-period)
placebo_time = 21
df_placebo = df[df['time'] < treatment_time].copy()  # pre-period only
df_placebo['post_placebo']    = (df_placebo['time'] >= placebo_time).astype(int)
df_placebo['time_since_placebo'] = np.maximum(0, df_placebo['time'] - (placebo_time - 1))

m_placebo = smf.ols('outcome ~ time + post_placebo + time_since_placebo',
                    data=df_placebo).fit(cov_type='HAC', cov_kwds={'maxlags': 4})

print("── Placebo test (fake launch at month 21) ──")
print(f"Placebo level shift:  {m_placebo.params['post_placebo']:+.3f}  "
      f"(p = {m_placebo.pvalues['post_placebo']:.3f})")
print(f"Real   level shift:   {level_shift:+.3f}")
print("\n✅ Good: placebo effect is small and/or non-significant")
print("🚨 Concern: if placebo is also 'significant', the model is picking up noise or a structural break")
```

### 5b. Alternative model — CausalImpact (no controls)
```python
# pip install causalimpact
from causalimpact import CausalImpact

# CausalImpact needs a DatetimeIndex; construct synthetic monthly dates
dates = pd.date_range('2022-01-01', periods=len(df), freq='MS')
ci_data = pd.Series(df['outcome'].values, index=dates, name='y')

pre_period  = [dates[0], dates[treatment_time - 2]]   # up to last pre-period month
post_period = [dates[treatment_time - 1], dates[-1]]

ci = CausalImpact(ci_data, pre_period, post_period)
print(ci.summary())
print(ci.summary(output='report'))
ci.plot()

# Compare to segmented regression
print(f"\n── Cross-method comparison ──")
print(f"Segmented regression level shift:   {level_shift:+.3f}")
print(f"CausalImpact avg effect (per month): see 'Average' row above")
print("✅ If both point in the same direction with similar magnitude, evidence is stronger")
```

### timeseries_pedagogy_messy

**Run 1**: {"pedagogy": 1.0, "safety": 1.0, "actionable": 1.0, "overall": 1.0}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Pre-Flight Check: Structural Break Detected 🚨

Before touching the intervention effect, I need to flag something that could invalidate the entire analysis. The data collection methodology change around months 15–20 is a **structural break in your pre-treatment period**. This is the single most dangerous data issue for ITS.

**Why it matters**: CausalImpact / segmented regression learns what "normal" looks like from the pre-period, then projects that forward as the counterfactual. If the pre-period contains two different regimes (pre- and post-methodology-change), the model is trained on broken data. The counterfactual will be a blend of both regimes — and the estimated treatment effect will absorb whatever distortion the methodology change introduced.

---

## Step 1: Diagnose the Break Formally

Let's first quantify what you're seeing visually, before deciding how to proceed.

```r
library(tidyverse)
library(strucchange)   # install.packages("strucchange")

# ── Load your data ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Replace this with your actual import (e.g. read_csv("data.csv"))
df <- your_data_frame

# ── 1. Visual inspection ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────
ggplot(df, aes(x = time, y = outcome)) +
  geom_line(color = "steelblue", linewidth = 0.8) +
  geom_point(size = 1.5) +
  geom_vline(xintercept = c(15, 20), linetype = "dashed", color = "orange",
             alpha = 0.7) +
  geom_vline(xintercept = 37, linetype = "dashed", color = "red") +
  annotate("rect", xmin = 15, xmax = 20, ymin = -Inf, ymax = Inf,
           fill = "orange", alpha = 0.1) +
  annotate("text", x = 18, y = max(df$outcome, na.rm = TRUE),
           label = "Methodology\nchange", size = 3, color = "darkorange") +
  annotate("text", x = 38, y = max(df$outcome, na.rm = TRUE),
           label = "Regulation", size = 3, color = "red", hjust = 0) +
  labs(title = "Outcome over time — pre-period break check",
       x = "Month", y = "Outcome") +
  theme_minimal()

# ── 2. Formal structural break test (Bai-Perron / Chow via strucchange) ────────
# Fit a simple linear trend on the pre-period only
pre <- df |> filter(time < 37)

# Test for breakpoints in the pre-period trend
bp <- breakpoints(outcome ~ time, data = pre)
summary(bp)

# This reports estimated breakpoint location(s) and confidence intervals.
# A breakpoint near months 15-20 confirms your visual impression.

# ── 3. Chow test at the suspected break (say, month 18) ───────────────────────
pre <- pre |>
  mutate(post_method = as.integer(time >= 18),        # adjust if you know exact month
         time_since_method = pmax(0, time - 18))

chow_model_full <- lm(outcome ~ time + post_method + time_since_method, data = pre)
chow_model_restricted <- lm(outcome ~ time, data = pre)

anova(chow_model_restricted, chow_model_full)
# Significant F-test → confirmed structural break
cat("\nEstimated break location(s):\n")
print(confint(bp))
```

---

## Step 2: Choose a Path Forward

Run the diagnostics above, then we face three options. Here are the honest trade-offs:

| Option | What it does | Pre-period N | Main risk |
|--------|-------------|-------------|-----------|
| **A. Truncate pre-period** to months 21–36 | Use only the stable post-methodology window | **~16 obs** 🚨 Too short for reliable ITS | Model can't learn seasonality or trend reliably |
| **B. Model the break explicitly** | Add a dummy + slope-change for the methodology shift in the regression | 36 obs ✓ | Assumes the break was a clean level/slope shift — requires knowing the exact month |
| **C. Abandon ITS** | Rethink the design | — | — |

**My honest read**: Option A alone is problematic — 16 pre-period observations is below the 30-minimum threshold, and we'd lose the ability to detect anything with confidence. Option B is the most defensible *if* you can nail down the exact break month and if the methodology change can be characterized as a level shift (not a change in how the underlying construct is measured, which would be worse).

Option B would look like this in the segmented regression:

```r
# Option B: model the methodology change explicitly
df <- df |>
  mutate(
    post_method   = as.integer(time >= 18),      # ← adjust to actual break month
    slope_method  = pmax(0, time - 18),           # ← slope change after break
    time_since_tx = pmax(0, time - 36)            # ← slope change after regulation
  )

model <- lm(outcome ~ time               # pre-period baseline trend
                    + post_method        # level shift at methodology change
                    + slope_method       # slope change at methodology change
                    + post              # level shift at regulation (treatment)
                    + time_since_tx,    # slope change at regulation
            data = df,
            # Cluster-robust SEs to handle autocorrelation
            )

library(sandwich); library(lmtest)
coeftest(model, vcov = vcovHAC(model, maxlag = 4))
```

---

## Before We Proceed

I need two things from you:

1. **Do you know the exact month the methodology changed?** (Knowing whether it's month 15, 17, or 20 matters for placing the break correctly.)
2. **What kind of change was it?** A *level shift* (e.g., different data source that reads systematically higher/lower) is modelable. A *construct change* (e.g., you started measuring something fundamentally different) is much harder to model around — and might mean the pre- and post-change series aren't comparable at all.

Run the diagnostic code and share the output — particularly the Bai-Perron breakpoint estimate and the ANOVA result — and we'll decide together which path is viable.

</details>

--- Step 1 ---
# Difference-in-Differences Analysis
### 100 stores × 24 months · Single treatment date · R

---

## Stage 1 — Setup ✅

Your design maps cleanly to **classic TWFE DiD**:

| Feature | Your data |
|---|---|
| Treatment date | Single (month 12) |
| Groups | 2 (50 treated, 50 control) |
| Structure | Balanced panel |
| Variant | **TWFE with unit + time fixed effects** |

One quick question before code: **What's your data structure?** I'll assume the column names below — correct them if needed:

| Column | Assumed name |
|---|---|
| Store ID | `store_id` |
| Month | `month` |
| Sales (outcome) | `sales` |
| Treatment group dummy (time-invariant) | `treated` |

---

## Stage 2 — Assumptions

Let me walk through each one for your specific context.

---

### 1. Parallel Trends ⚠️ *Testable — we'll run it*

> **What it means for you:** Without the new checkout system, would treated and control stores have followed the same sales trajectory? Their *levels* can differ (busy flagship vs. small outlet), but the *month-to-month trend* must be the same.

This is the **core assumption** of DiD. If treated stores were already growing faster *before* month 12, the DiD estimate will absorb that pre-existing advantage and overstate the checkout system's impact. We can't prove it holds post-treatment, but we can check it in months 1–11.

➡️ We'll run a formal pre-trends test in Stage 3.

---

### 2. No Anticipation ⚠️ *Partially testable*

> **What it means for you:** Did store managers change behavior *before* month 12 — say, if they knew the rollout was coming in month 10? If so, sales might already be shifting in months 10–11, contaminating our "pre-treatment" baseline.

Look at your event study plot: if the coefficient at t = −2 or t = −1 (relative to month 12) is significantly non-zero, anticipation is likely.

---

### 3. Stable Composition ✅ *Likely satisfied — worth confirming*

> **What it means for you:** Did any stores open or close because of the checkout system rollout? If weak stores were excluded from treatment, or closed after treatment, survivorship bias inflates your estimate.

With 100 stores observed over 24 months, this is probably fine — but we'll run a quick balance check.

---

### 4. No Spillovers (SUTVA) ✅ *Assumed — worth flagging*

> **What it means for you:** Does the new checkout system at a treated store draw customers *away* from nearby control stores? If so, control stores look worse post-treatment not because the system failed them, but because you redirected their customers.

This is untestable without geography data. Flag it as a caveat if stores compete in overlapping markets.

---

### 5. Correct Functional Form ✅ *Fine for your design*

> **What it means for you:** TWFE bias from heterogeneous effects is a concern with *staggered* rollouts. Since all your treated stores get treated at the same time (month 12), there's only one cohort — no forbidden comparisons. Standard TWFE is valid.

---

## Stage 3 — Implementation

```r
# ── 0. Packages ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
library(tidyverse)
library(fixest)
library(modelsummary)

# ── 1. Data preparation ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Rename to match your actual column names if different
df <- your_data |>
  rename(
    store_id = store_id,   # panel unit
    month    = month,      # time period (1–24)
    sales    = sales,      # outcome
    treated  = treated     # 1 = treatment group, 0 = control (time-invariant)
  ) |>
  mutate(
    post          = as.integer(month >= 12),          # post-treatment indicator
    treated_post  = treated * post                    # DiD interaction term
  )

# ── 2. Sanity check: panel balance ───────────────────────────────────────────
panel_check <- df |>
  group_by(store_id) |>
  summarise(
    n_months      = n_distinct(month),
    is_treated    = max(treated),
    attrited      = n_months < 24
  )

cat("Panel balance:\n")
print(table(panel_check$is_treated, panel_check$attrited,
            dnn = c("Treated", "Attrited")))

# ── 3. Parallel trends: raw plot ─────────────────────────────────────────────
df |>
  group_by(month, treated) |>
  summarise(mean_sales = mean(sales), .groups = "drop") |>
  mutate(group = ifelse(treated == 1, "Treated", "Control")) |>
  ggplot(aes(x = month, y = mean_sales, color = group, group = group)) +
  geom_line(linewidth = 1) +
  geom_vline(xintercept = 11.5, linetype = "dashed", color = "gray40") +
  labs(
    title  = "Raw Trends: Treated vs. Control Stores",
    x      = "Month",
    y      = "Mean Sales",
    color  = NULL,
    caption = "Dashed line = treatment onset (month 12)"
  ) +
  theme_minimal()

# ── 4. Main DiD model (TWFE) ──────────────────────────────────────────────────
# NOTE: Do NOT include `treated` here — unit FE already absorb all
# time-invariant store characteristics, including which group a store is in.
# `treated_post` is fine because it varies over time.
model_main <- feols(
  sales ~ treated_post | store_id + month,
  data    = df,
  cluster = ~store_id
)

summary(model_main)

# Formatted table
modelsummary(
  model_main,
  stars      = TRUE,
  coef_rename = c("treated_post" = "Treatment × Post"),
  gof_map    = c("nobs", "r.squared"),
  title      = "DiD Estimate: Effect of New Checkout System on Sales"
)

# ── 5. Event study (pre-trends test + dynamic effects) ───────────────────────
df <- df |>
  mutate(
    time_to_treat = month - 12   # relative to treatment onset; t = -1 is ref
  )

model_es <- feols(
  sales ~ i(time_to_treat, treated, ref = -1) | store_id + month,
  data    = df,
  cluster = ~store_id
)

# Plot: pre-treatment coefs should hover near zero
iplot(
  model_es,
  main    = "Event Study: Effect of Checkout System on Sales",
  xlab    = "Months Relative to Treatment",
  ylab    = "Estimated Effect on Sales",
  ref.line = TRUE
)
abline(v = -0.5, lty = 2, col = "gray40")

# Formal joint test: H₀ = all pre-treatment coefficients = 0
# Extract pre-treatment coefficient names
pre_coef_names <- grep("time_to_treat::-", names(coef(model_es)), value = TRUE)
cat("\nJoint test of pre-treatment coefficients (H₀: all = 0):\n")
wald(model_es, keep = pre_coef_names)
```

---

## Stage 4 — Falsification & Robustness

Run **at least two** of these:

```r
# ── A. Placebo test: fake treatment at month 6 (pre-period only) ──────────────
df_pre <- df |>
  filter(month < 12) |>   # use only pre-treatment data
  mutate(
    post_placebo         = as.integer(month >= 6),
    treated_post_placebo = treated * post_placebo
  )

model_placebo <- feols(
  sales ~ treated_post_placebo | store_id + month,
  data    = df_pre,
  cluster = ~store_id
)
cat("\nPlacebo DiD (fake treatment at month 6 — should be near zero):\n")
summary(model_placebo)

# ── B. Specification robustness: different clustering ────────────────────────
model_robust <- feols(
  sales ~ treated_post | store_id + month,
  data  = df,
  vcov  = "hetero"   # heteroskedasticity-robust SEs instead of clustered
)

# Compare estimates side-by-side
modelsummary(
  list(
    "Main (Clustered)"   = model_main,
    "Hetero SEs"         = model_robust,
    "Placebo at Month 6" = model_placebo
  ),
  stars       = TRUE,
  coef_map    = c(
    "treated_post"         = "Treatment × Post",
    "treated_post_placebo" = "Placebo × Post"
  ),
  title = "Robustness Checks"
)
```

```markdown
### DiD: Log transformation not suggested for sales/revenue outcomes
**Layer**: Auditor
**Trigger**: User runs DiD with a sales, revenue, or count outcome
**Mistake**: Skill ran analysis on raw levels without suggesting log transformation or checking skewness
**Rule**: For monetary/count outcomes, always suggest testing log(outcome) alongside levels. Explain that the choice changes interpretation (absolute vs. percentage effect) and affects residual behavior.
**Source**: Auditor finding, 2026-04-03

### DiD: Treatment assignment mechanism not discussed
**Layer**: Auditor
**Trigger**: User provides pre-assigned treatment/control groups without explaining selection
**Mistake**: Skill proceeded to parallel trends testing without asking how treatment was assigned
**Rule**: Before any assumption checks, ask (or prompt the user to document) how units were selected into treatment. This context is essential for judging whether parallel trends is credible — a pre-trends test can pass even when selection was non-random.
**Source**: Auditor finding, 2026-04-03

### DiD: Wild cluster bootstrap not recommended when treatment is group-level
**Layer**: Auditor
**Trigger**: DiD with binary treatment assigned to a group (all treated units share the same treatment value in each period)
**Mistake**: Skill recommended only standard cluster-robust SEs; added heteroskedastic SEs as robustness check but not wild cluster bootstrap
**Rule**: When treatment is assigned at the group level (e.g., all 50 treated stores share the same treated_post value), recommend wild cluster bootstrap via fwildclusterboot. Standard cluster-robust SEs can be anti-conservative in this setting.
**Source**: Auditor finding, 2026-04-03
```
