<details><summary>Response</summary>

--- Step 1 ---
Here's your DiD practice exercise. I'll use DGP-03 from the library — a clean 2×2 setup with one realistic wrinkle baked in: you'll need to verify parallel trends before trusting any estimate.

---

## 🏪 Scenario: BrewPoint Coffee — Mobile App Pilot

You're a data analyst at **BrewPoint Coffee**, a mid-size chain with 100 locations across the US. In month 13 of a 24-month observation window, the ops team rolled out a new **in-store mobile app ordering system** to 50 pilot stores. The other 50 stores kept the standard counter-ordering process throughout.

Your VP of Operations wants to know: **did the mobile app increase monthly store revenue?**

You have access to a panel dataset covering all 100 stores across 24 months. Each row is one store-month observation.

**Your deliverable**: Estimate the ATT (average treatment effect on the treated) of the mobile app rollout on monthly revenue. Report your estimate, standard error, and explain which assumptions you verified and how.

---

## 🐍 Python Code — Generate `exercise_data.csv`

```python
import numpy as np
import pandas as pd

np.random.seed(303)
n_stores = 100
n_months = 24
treat_month = 13

store_id = np.repeat(np.arange(1, n_stores + 1), n_months)
month = np.tile(np.arange(1, n_months + 1), n_stores)

# Stores 1–50 receive the mobile app at month 13
treated = (store_id <= 50).astype(int)
post = (month >= treat_month).astype(int)

# Store-level fixed effects (each store has its own baseline revenue)
store_fe = np.repeat(np.random.normal(0, 3, n_stores), n_months)

# Common time trend across all stores
time_trend = 0.2 * month

# Treatment effect (what you're trying to recover)
revenue = (
    50
    + store_fe
    + time_trend
    + 5.0 * treated * post           # <-- the causal effect
    + np.random.normal(0, 2, n_stores * n_months)
)

df = pd.DataFrame({
    "store_id": store_id,
    "month": month,
    "treated": treated,
    "post": post,
    "revenue": np.round(revenue, 2),
})

df.to_csv("exercise_data.csv", index=False)
print(df.head(10))
print(f"\nShape: {df.shape}")
print(f"Treated stores: {df['treated'].sum() // n_months}")
print(f"Control stores: {(1 - df['treated']).sum() // n_months}")
```

Run this and you'll have `exercise_data.csv` with 2,400 rows (100 stores × 24 months).

---

## ✅ True Treatment Effect

> **ATT = $5,000/month** per treated store

That's the causal lift from the mobile app. Your DiD estimate should land near **5.0** (revenue is in thousands).

---

## 💡 What to check before running the regression

| Check | Why it matters |
|---|---|
| **Pre-trends plot** | If treated and control stores diverged *before* month 13, parallel trends fails and your DiD is biased |
| **Balance at baseline** | Verify treated/control stores look similar in months 1–12 |
| **Event study** | Run dynamic DiD (one dummy per month relative to treatment) to see if the effect is immediate or grows |
| **Cluster SEs** | Revenue within a store is autocorrelated — cluster standard errors at the store level |

---

When you've got an estimate, share it and I'll debrief you: what the DGP looked like under the hood, whether your assumptions held, and where your estimate landed relative to the truth. Good luck! 📊

--- Step 2 ---
## Stage 1: Study Design Confirmed

Before touching the data, let me lock in what kind of DiD problem this is:

| Design element | This exercise |
|---|---|
| Treatment date | Single (month 13) |
| Groups | Two (50 treated, 50 control) |
| Data structure | Balanced panel (100 stores × 24 months) |
| Estimand | ATT — effect on the treated stores |
| DiD variant | **Classic 2×2 / TWFE** — no staggered timing, so standard two-way FE is appropriate here |

This is the cleanest possible DiD setup. No staggered adoption, no compositional change, no obvious spillover risk (stores in different locations). That said, parallel trends still needs to be *verified*, not assumed.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions — Walking Through Each One

### 1. Parallel Trends 🔍 *Testable in the pre-period*

**What it means here**: Without the mobile app, treated stores (1–50) and control stores (51–100) would have followed the same revenue trajectory from months 1–24. Their revenue *levels* can differ — that's absorbed by store fixed effects — but the *trend* must be the same.

**Why it matters**: If treated stores were already growing faster before month 13, your DiD will attribute that pre-existing gap to the app. The estimate will be biased upward.

**How to test**: Event study plot. Pre-treatment coefficients (months 1–12) should scatter around zero with no systematic trend. We'll also run a joint significance test.

**My prior**: The DGP uses `time_trend = 0.2 * month` applied equally to all stores. Parallel trends should hold by construction — but let's verify it empirically as you would in a real analysis.

---

### 2. No Anticipation ✅ *Plausible*

**What it means here**: Did stores *change their behavior before month 13* because they knew the app was coming? For example, did pilot stores ramp up inventory or staffing in month 12 in anticipation of the launch?

**Why it matters**: If treated stores started improving in month 11–12 before the app launched, the pre-period looks "too clean" and you're underestimating the true effect by excluding early treatment response.

**Assessment**: The DGP has the effect switch on exactly at month 13 with no ramp-up. In the event study, coefficients at t = −1, −2 should be indistinguishable from zero. Plausible — app rollouts are often confidential until launch.

---

### 3. Stable Composition ✅ *Plausible*

**What it means here**: Did stores drop out of the sample because of the treatment? A store that struggled with the app might have closed — removing it from your data creates survivorship bias.

**Assessment**: With 100 stores × 24 months = 2,400 rows in a balanced panel, this looks fine. We'll confirm the panel is actually balanced before estimating.

---

### 4. No Spillovers (SUTVA) ✅ *Plausible*

**What it means here**: Did the mobile app in pilot stores steal customers from control stores nearby? If yes, control stores' revenue *falls* because of the treatment, making the treatment look more effective than it is.

**Assessment**: With 100 stores across the US, most treated/control pairs are likely far apart. Geographic separation makes spillovers unlikely. We'll note this as an assumption rather than run a formal test (it's inherently untestable without location data).

---

### 5. Correct Functional Form ✅ *Not a concern here*

**What it means here**: For staggered adoption, TWFE can be biased because early-treated units become implicit controls for later-treated units — some weights go negative. Here, there's only one treatment cohort (all treated stores go live at month 13), so this problem doesn't arise.

**Assessment**: Safe to use standard TWFE. No need for Callaway-Sant'Anna.

---

**Assumption summary before we run anything:**

| Assumption | Status | How verified |
|---|---|---|
| Parallel trends | 🔍 Test first | Event study + joint pre-test |
| No anticipation | ✅ Plausible | Check t = −1, −2 coefficients |
| Stable composition | ✅ Plausible | Confirm balanced panel |
| No spillovers | ✅ Plausible | Geographic separation assumed |
| Correct functional form | ✅ Not applicable | Single cohort design |

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

Here's the complete analysis. Run this after generating `exercise_data.csv`.

```python
# =============================================================
# BrewPoint Coffee — DiD Analysis
# =============================================================
import numpy as np
import pandas as pd
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
import matplotlib.gridspec as gridspec
from linearmodels.panel import PanelOLS
import warnings
warnings.filterwarnings("ignore")

# ------------------------------------------------------------------
# 0. Load and inspect
# ------------------------------------------------------------------
df = pd.read_csv("exercise_data.csv")

print("=== Data structure ===")
print(df.shape)
print(df.dtypes)
print(df.head())

# Confirm balanced panel
obs_per_store = df.groupby("store_id")["month"].count()
assert (obs_per_store == 24).all(), "Unbalanced panel — investigate before proceeding"
print(f"\n✅ Balanced panel: {df['store_id'].nunique()} stores × {df['month'].nunique()} months")
print(f"   Treated stores: {df[df['treated']==1]['store_id'].nunique()}")
print(f"   Control stores: {df[df['treated']==0]['store_id'].nunique()}")

# ------------------------------------------------------------------
# 1. Pre-trends visualization (raw means)
# ------------------------------------------------------------------
monthly_means = (df.groupby(["month", "treated"])["revenue"]
                   .mean()
                   .reset_index()
                   .rename(columns={"revenue": "mean_revenue"}))

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(10, 5))
for group, label, color in [(1, "Treated (mobile app)", "#2563eb"),
                              (0, "Control (standard)", "#dc2626")]:
    subset = monthly_means[monthly_means["treated"] == group]
    ax.plot(subset["month"], subset["mean_revenue"],
            marker="o", markersize=4, label=label, color=color)

ax.axvline(x=12.5, color="gray", linestyle="--", linewidth=1.5, label="App launch (month 13)")
ax.set_xlabel("Month")
ax.set_ylabel("Mean Monthly Revenue ($000s)")
ax.set_title("BrewPoint Coffee: Raw Revenue Trends by Group")
ax.legend()
ax.grid(alpha=0.3)
plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig("01_raw_trends.png", dpi=150)
plt.show()
print("Saved: 01_raw_trends.png")

# ------------------------------------------------------------------
# 2. Main TWFE estimate
# NOTE: Do NOT include 'treated' (time-invariant) with EntityEffects.
#       Entity FE already absorbs all time-invariant store characteristics.
#       The interaction treated*post varies over time — that's our estimand.
# ------------------------------------------------------------------
df["treated_post"] = df["treated"] * df["post"]
df_panel = df.set_index(["store_id", "month"])

mod = PanelOLS.from_formula(
    "revenue ~ treated_post + EntityEffects + TimeEffects",
    data=df_panel
)
res = mod.fit(cov_type="clustered", cluster_entity=True)
print("\n=== Main TWFE Estimate ===")
print(res.summary.tables[1])

att = res.params["treated_post"]
se  = res.std_errors["treated_post"]
ci_lo, ci_hi = res.conf_int().loc["treated_post"]
print(f"\n  ATT:  {att:.4f}")
print(f"  SE:   {se:.4f}")
print(f"  95% CI: [{ci_lo:.4f}, {ci_hi:.4f}]")
print(f"  True effect: 5.0  |  Your estimate: {att:.4f}  |  Bias: {att - 5.0:.4f}")

# ------------------------------------------------------------------
# 3. Event study — dynamic treatment effects
#    Reference period: month 12 (one period before treatment)
# ------------------------------------------------------------------
treat_month = 13
df["rel_month"] = df["month"] - treat_month   # ranges from -12 to +11

# Create event study dummies (exclude ref period = -1)
ref_period = -1
rel_periods = sorted(df["rel_month"].unique())
rel_periods_no_ref = [p for p in rel_periods if p != ref_period]

for p in rel_periods_no_ref:
    df[f"es_{p}"] = ((df["rel_month"] == p) & (df["treated"] == 1)).astype(int)

es_cols = [f"es_{p}" for p in rel_periods_no_ref]
formula_es = "revenue ~ " + " + ".join(es_cols) + " + EntityEffects + TimeEffects"

df_panel_es = df.set_index(["store_id", "month"])
mod_es = PanelOLS.from_formula(formula_es, data=df_panel_es)
res_es = mod_es.fit(cov_type="clustered", cluster_entity=True)

# Collect coefficients (insert 0 at reference period)
coefs = []
for p in rel_periods:
    if p == ref_period:
        coefs.append((p, 0.0, 0.0, 0.0))
    else:
        col = f"es_{p}"
        c = res_es.params[col]
        lo, hi = res_es.conf_int().loc[col]
        coefs.append((p, c, lo, hi))

es_df = pd.DataFrame(coefs, columns=["rel_month", "coef", "ci_lo", "ci_hi"])

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(12, 5))
pre  = es_df[es_df["rel_month"] < 0]
post = es_df[es_df["rel_month"] >= 0]

for subset, color, label in [
    (pre,  "#94a3b8", "Pre-treatment"),
    (post, "#2563eb", "Post-treatment"),
]:
    ax.errorbar(
        subset["rel_month"], subset["coef"],
        yerr=[subset["coef"] - subset["ci_lo"], subset["ci_hi"] - subset["coef"]],
        fmt="o-", color=color, capsize=3, linewidth=1.5, label=label
    )

ax.axhline(y=0,    color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.axvline(x=-0.5, color="gray",  linestyle="--", linewidth=1.2, label="Treatment starts")
ax.axhline(y=5.0,  color="#16a34a", linestyle=":", linewidth=1.5, label="True ATT = 5.0")
ax.set_xlabel("Months Relative to App Launch")
ax.set_ylabel("Estimated Coefficient ($000s)")
ax.set_title("Event Study: Dynamic Treatment Effects — BrewPoint Mobile App")
ax.legend()
ax.grid(alpha=0.3)
plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig("02_event_study.png", dpi=150)
plt.show()
print("Saved: 02_event_study.png")

# ------------------------------------------------------------------
# 4. Joint pre-trends test (H0: all pre-treatment coefficients = 0)
# ------------------------------------------------------------------
pre_cols = [f"es_{p}" for p in rel_periods_no_ref if p < 0]
from linearmodels.iv.model import _OLS  # for Wald test

# Extract pre-treatment params and covariance
pre_params = res_es.params[pre_cols].values
pre_cov    = res_es.cov.loc[pre_cols, pre_cols].values

# Wald statistic: beta' * V^{-1} * beta ~ chi2(k)
from numpy.linalg import inv
wald_stat = pre_params @ inv(pre_cov) @ pre_params
k = len(pre_cols)
from scipy.stats import chi2
p_val = 1 - chi2.cdf(wald_stat, df=k)
print(f"\n=== Pre-Trends Joint Test ===")
print(f"  H0: all {k} pre-treatment coefficients = 0")
print(f"  Wald statistic: {wald_stat:.3f} (χ² with {k} df)")
print(f"  p-value: {p_val:.4f}")
if p_val > 0.05:
    print("  ✅ Fail to reject H0 — pre-trends look parallel")
else:
    print("  🚨 Reject H0 — evidence of non-parallel pre-trends")
```

---

## Stage 4: Robustness Check

```python
# ------------------------------------------------------------------
# 5. Placebo test: fake treatment date at month 7 (pre-period only)
#    If DiD finds an "effect" before month 13, that's a red flag.
# ------------------------------------------------------------------
df_pre = df[df["month"] <= 12].copy()
fake_treat_month = 7
df_pre["fake_post"]         = (df_pre["month"] >= fake_treat_month).astype(int)
df_pre["fake_treated_post"] = df_pre["treated"] * df_pre["fake_post"]

df_pre_panel = df_pre.set_index(["store_id", "month"])
mod_placebo = PanelOLS.from_formula(
    "revenue ~ fake_treated_post + EntityEffects + TimeEffects",
    data=df_pre_panel
)
res_placebo = mod_placebo.fit(cov_type="clustered", cluster_entity=True)

placebo_att = res_placebo.params["fake_treated_post"]
placebo_p   = res_placebo.pvalues["fake_treated_post"]
print(f"\n=== Placebo Test (fake treatment at month 7, pre-period only) ===")
print(f"  Placebo estimate: {placebo_att:.4f}")
print(f"  p-value:          {placebo_p:.4f}")
if placebo_p > 0.05:
    print("  ✅ No spurious effect detected — supports parallel trends")
else:
    print("  ⚠️  Significant placebo effect — parallel trends may be violated")

# ------------------------------------------------------------------
# 6. Summary table
# ------------------------------------------------------------------
print("\n" + "="*55)
print("RESULTS SUMMARY")
print("="*55)
print(f"  Main DiD estimate:      {att:>8.4f}  (SE = {se:.4f})")
print(f"  95% CI:                 [{ci_lo:.4f}, {ci_hi:.4f}]")
print(f"  True ATT:               {5.0:>8.4f}")
print(f"  Bias:                   {att - 5.0:>8.4f}")
print(f"  Pre-trends p-value:     {p_val:>8.4f}")
print(f"  Placebo estimate:       {placebo_att:>8.4f}  (p = {placebo_p:.4f})")
print("="*55)
```

---

## Stage 5: Interpretation Template

Once you run this and have numbers, here's how to report it:

> **The mobile app increased monthly store revenue by approximately $X,000** (95% CI: [$Y,000, $Z,000]) per treated store. This estimate comes from a difference-in-differences design comparing 50 pilot stores to 50 control stores across 24 months, with store and month fixed effects absorbing time-invariant heterogeneity and common time shocks respectively.
>
> **Assumptions**: Pre-treatment revenue trends were statistically parallel across groups (joint pre-trends test: p = [value]). A placebo test using a fake treatment date in the pre-period found no spurious effect ([value], p = [value]), further supporting the design's validity. Standard errors are clustered at the store level to account for within-store revenue autocorrelation.
>
> **Caveats**: (1) Parallel trends in the pre-period is necessary but not sufficient — we cannot verify that trends would have remained parallel absent the app. (2) If treated and control stores share geographic markets, there may be competitive spillovers not captured here.

Given the DGP, here's what you *should* see:

| Output | Expected |
|---|---|
| Main ATT | ~5.0, likely within ±0.5 |
| Pre-trend coefficients | Scattered near 0, no systematic drift |
| Joint pre-trends p-value | > 0.05 (fail to reject H0) |
| Post coefficients | Step up to ~5.0 at t=0, flat thereafter |
| Placebo estimate | Near 0, p > 0.05 |

--- Step 1 ---
# IV Analysis: Effect of Private Tutoring on Test Scores

Great setup. Distance to tutoring center is a classic Card-style instrument — the idea being that students closer to a center face lower travel costs, making them more likely to get tutoring, but distance itself doesn't affect test scores directly.

Let me walk through all five stages.

---

## Stage 1: Setup Confirmed

| Element | Value |
|---|---|
| **Endogenous treatment** | Private tutoring (take-up) |
| **Instrument** | Distance to nearest tutoring center |
| **Outcome** | Test scores |
| **N** | 3,000 students |
| **Language** | R |

Before I generate code, two quick questions — answers will affect the analysis:

1. **Do you have covariates** — things like student age, gender, household income, school type, urban/rural indicator? These matter a lot for defending the exclusion restriction here (more on that in Stage 2).
2. **Is tutoring binary** (used / didn't use) **or continuous** (hours of tutoring)?

I'll proceed with the assumption of **binary tutoring** and **no covariates yet** — you can slot them in when you answer. Flag if your setup differs.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through each one in your specific context.

---

### ① Relevance — *Is distance a strong predictor of tutoring take-up?*

**What it means here**: Students closer to a tutoring center should have a meaningfully higher probability of using private tutoring. This is plausible — shorter distance = lower travel cost and friction.

**Why it matters**: If the first-stage F-statistic is below 10, the instrument is too weak. Your 2SLS estimate would be biased toward OLS (the thing you're trying to fix) and your confidence intervals would be misleading. With F < 10, IV is worse than useless.

✅ **Testable** — we'll inspect the first-stage F directly in the code.

---

### ② Exclusion Restriction — *Does distance affect test scores ONLY through tutoring?*

**What it means here**: The instrument must have no direct path to test scores. This is the most important assumption to interrogate in your context, because distance to tutoring centers is **geographically correlated** with several things that independently affect test scores:

- **Urban vs. rural**: Urban students have tutoring centers nearby *and* tend to attend better-resourced schools.
- **Neighborhood socioeconomic status**: Wealthier neighborhoods attract more tutoring businesses *and* have better home study environments, more parental education, etc.
- **School quality**: School density correlates with tutoring center density.

🚨 **This is a real threat** — not a theoretical nitpick. If you don't have controls for income, urbanicity, or school quality, the exclusion restriction is hard to defend.

❌ **Not directly testable** — must be argued on substantive and contextual grounds.

**My question for you**: Can you think of any way a student's distance to a tutoring center could affect their test scores *other than* through whether they use tutoring? If yes, list them — and let's think about whether you have variables to control for those channels.

---

### ③ Independence — *Is distance "as-if randomly assigned"?*

**What it means here**: Students and their families shouldn't have chosen where to live *because of* the tutoring center. If wealthier, academically-motivated families self-select into neighborhoods near tutoring centers, then distance is correlated with unobserved drivers of test scores — and it's a bad instrument.

⚠️ **Partially testable** — we can check if distance is balanced on observables (income, parental education, school type). An imbalanced instrument is a red flag.

---

### ④ Monotonicity — *Does shorter distance push everyone toward more tutoring?*

**What it means here**: Being closer to a tutoring center should increase tutoring take-up for every student — or at least not decrease it for anyone. There are no obvious "defiers" here (students who *stop* getting tutoring when a center opens nearby). This assumption is **plausible** in your context.

✅ **Plausible** — proceed with LATE interpretation.

**What LATE means here**: Your IV estimate will be the effect of tutoring on test scores specifically for **compliers** — students who *would* get tutoring if the center is close but *wouldn't* if it's far. This is not the effect for students who get tutoring regardless of distance (always-takers) or who never get tutoring no matter how close the center is (never-takers). Keep this in mind when interpreting.

---

### Assumption Summary

| Assumption | Status | Notes |
|---|---|---|
| Relevance | ✅ Testable — check F-stat | Plausible on face value |
| Exclusion restriction | ⚠️ Requires argument | Urban/SES channels are a real threat; covariates help |
| Independence | ⚠️ Partially testable | Check balance; residential sorting is a concern |
| Monotonicity | ✅ Plausible | No obvious defiers |

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

Here is the complete analysis code. **Slot in your actual variable names** where indicated.

```r
# ============================================================
# IV Analysis: Effect of Private Tutoring on Test Scores
# Instrument: Distance to nearest tutoring center
# ============================================================

library(tidyverse)
library(fixest)
library(modelsummary)
library(cobalt)      # for balance checks
library(AER)         # for Wu-Hausman endogeneity test

# ── 0. Load data ─────────────────────────────────────────────
# df <- read_csv("your_data.csv")
# 
# Expected columns (rename to match yours):
#   tutoring   — binary: 1 if student received private tutoring
#   test_score — continuous: outcome variable
#   distance   — continuous: km to nearest tutoring center
#   X1, X2     — any covariates (income, urban dummy, school type, etc.)

# ── 1. Descriptive Overview ───────────────────────────────────
cat("=== Descriptive Statistics ===\n")
df |>
  select(tutoring, test_score, distance) |>
  summary() |>
  print()

cat("\nTutoring take-up rate:", mean(df$tutoring), "\n")
cat("Mean distance (km):", mean(df$distance), "\n")

# Quick visual: does distance predict tutoring?
ggplot(df, aes(x = distance, y = tutoring)) +
  geom_point(alpha = 0.3) +
  geom_smooth(method = "lm", color = "steelblue") +
  labs(title = "First-stage relationship: Distance → Tutoring take-up",
       x = "Distance to nearest tutoring center (km)",
       y = "Tutoring take-up (0/1)") +
  theme_minimal()

# ── 2. Naive OLS (for comparison — likely biased) ────────────
# Biased because motivated families self-select into tutoring
ols_model <- feols(test_score ~ tutoring,
                   # + X1 + X2 if you have covariates
                   data = df,
                   vcov = "HC1")

cat("\n=== OLS Estimate (likely upward-biased) ===\n")
summary(ols_model)

# ── 3. First Stage: Check Instrument Relevance ───────────────
first_stage <- feols(tutoring ~ distance,
                     # + X1 + X2 if you have covariates
                     data = df,
                     vcov = "HC1")

cat("\n=== First Stage: Distance → Tutoring ===\n")
summary(first_stage)

# Extract F-statistic
fs_f <- fitstat(first_stage, "f")$f$stat
cat("\n*** First-stage F-statistic:", round(fs_f, 2), "***\n")
cat("Stock-Yogo rule of thumb: F > 10 (minimum), ideally F > 100\n")

if (fs_f < 10) {
  cat("🚨 WEAK INSTRUMENT: F < 10. 2SLS estimates are unreliable.\n")
  cat("   Do NOT interpret IV results without addressing this.\n")
} else if (fs_f < 100) {
  cat("⚠️  Moderate instrument: F is above 10 but below 100.\n")
  cat("   Some bias toward OLS may remain. Report this as a limitation.\n")
} else {
  cat("✅ Strong instrument: F > 100.\n")
}

# ── 4. 2SLS / IV Estimation ───────────────────────────────────
iv_model <- feols(test_score ~ 1 | tutoring ~ distance,
                  # Add covariates: test_score ~ X1 + X2 | tutoring ~ distance
                  data = df,
                  vcov = "HC1")

cat("\n=== IV / 2SLS Estimate ===\n")
summary(iv_model)

# ── 5. Results Table ─────────────────────────────────────────
modelsummary(
  list("OLS (biased)" = ols_model,
       "IV / 2SLS"    = iv_model),
  stars = TRUE,
  gof_map = c("nobs", "r.squared"),
  title = "Effect of Private Tutoring on Test Scores"
)

# ── 6. Wu-Hausman Test for Endogeneity ────────────────────────
# H0: tutoring is exogenous (OLS is consistent)
# Rejection → tutoring IS endogenous → IV is needed
iv_aer <- ivreg(test_score ~ tutoring | distance,
                # covariates: test_score ~ tutoring + X1 + X2 | distance + X1 + X2
                data = df)

cat("\n=== Wu-Hausman Endogeneity Test ===\n")
summary(iv_aer, diagnostics = TRUE)
# Look at the "Wu-Hausman" row
# p < 0.05 → tutoring is endogenous, IV is warranted
# p > 0.05 → can't reject exogeneity; OLS may be fine
```

---

## Stage 4: Falsification & Robustness

Run all three of these — they take 5 minutes and greatly strengthen (or challenge) your findings.

```r
# ── Robustness 1: Reduced Form ───────────────────────────────
# Regress test scores directly on distance (skipping tutoring).
# If the instrument is valid, this should be significant and negative
# (farther distance → lower tutoring → lower scores).
# The ratio of reduced form / first stage ≈ your IV estimate.

reduced_form <- feols(test_score ~ distance,
                      data = df, vcov = "HC1")

cat("\n=== Reduced Form: Distance → Test Score ===\n")
summary(reduced_form)

rf_coef  <- coef(reduced_form)["distance"]
fs_coef  <- coef(first_stage)["distance"]
cat("Implied IV estimate (RF / FS):", round(rf_coef / fs_coef, 4), "\n")
cat("(Should match your 2SLS estimate above)\n")


# ── Robustness 2: Balance Check ───────────────────────────────
# Check if distance is correlated with observables.
# A valid instrument should be (roughly) independent of pre-treatment
# characteristics. Significant correlations are red flags.

# Replace X1, X2, X3 with your actual covariates
balance_vars <- c("X1", "X2", "X3")  # e.g. c("income", "urban", "school_type")

if (length(balance_vars) > 0 && all(balance_vars %in% names(df))) {
  cat("\n=== Balance: Is Distance Correlated with Observables? ===\n")
  
  balance_results <- map_dfr(balance_vars, function(v) {
    m <- feols(as.formula(paste(v, "~ distance")), data = df, vcov = "HC1")
    tibble(
      variable    = v,
      coef        = round(coef(m)["distance"], 4),
      p_value     = round(pvalue(m)["distance"], 4),
      significant = pvalue(m)["distance"] < 0.05
    )
  })
  
  print(balance_results)
  
  n_sig <- sum(balance_results$significant)
  if (n_sig > 0) {
    cat("\n⚠️ ", n_sig, "covariate(s) are significantly correlated with distance.\n")
    cat("   This raises concerns about the independence assumption.\n")
    cat("   Consider adding these as controls in the IV model.\n")
  } else {
    cat("\n✅ No significant correlations with observables. Independence looks plausible.\n")
  }
}


# ── Robustness 3: Sensitivity to Controls ────────────────────
# Add and remove covariates. A robust IV estimate shouldn't change much.
# Large swings suggest the instrument is proxying for something else.

# Example — run only if you have covariates:
# iv_with_controls <- feols(test_score ~ X1 + X2 | tutoring ~ distance,
#                            data = df, vcov = "HC1")
# 
# modelsummary(list("IV (no controls)"   = iv_model,
#                   "IV (with controls)" = iv_with_controls),
#              stars = TRUE,
#              title = "Sensitivity to Controls")
```

## One Thing to Do Before Running

The **exclusion restriction** is the weakest link here. Before you run the code, try to answer:

> *Do students in your data live in areas where distance to tutoring centers is correlated with neighborhood wealth, school quality, or urban density?*

If yes — and it probably is — the analysis will be much more credible with those variables as controls. They don't fix a violated exclusion restriction, but they reduce the threat by closing some of the backdoor paths. Let me know what covariates you have available.

--- Step 2 ---
I don't have a write tool available in this session — here's the full report to save at `docs/causal-plans/2026-04-03-tutoring-iv/audit.md`:

---

```markdown
# Audit Report: Effect of Private Tutoring on Test Scores (IV)

**Date**: 2026-04-03
**Method audited**: Instrumental Variables (2SLS) — Distance to tutoring center
**Overall assessment**: 🔴 Red — two fatal issues must be fixed before interpreting results

## Summary

The analysis has two issues that invalidate current inference: standard errors
use HC1 (observation-level) instead of clustering at school/district, which almost
certainly understates standard errors and overstates significance; and the
first-stage F-statistic is extracted from a standalone OLS model rather than the
IV model object, which gives incorrect values once controls are added. Beyond
these, the exclusion restriction has an unaddressed structural threat — endogenous
center placement — and the binary first stage runs OLS without acknowledging LPM
limitations. Fix the two fatal issues before drawing conclusions.

## Findings

### Fatal: F-statistic extracted from wrong object
**Category**: Assumption Violations (Relevance)
**Explanation**: The code uses `fitstat(first_stage, "f")` on a standalone OLS
regression. The correct effective F for IV in fixest is `fitstat(iv_model, "ivf")`,
extracted from the IV model object. These diverge once covariates are added because
the IV-specific F accounts for the endogenous regressors and instruments jointly.
Using the standalone version gives an optimistic F and may lead you to conclude the
instrument is adequate when it isn't.
**Diagnostic**:
```r
iv_model <- feols(test_score ~ 1 | tutoring ~ distance,
                  data = df, cluster = ~school_id)
fitstat(iv_model, "ivf")          # correct: IV-effective F
fitstat(iv_model, "ivf", type = "ivreg2")  # with Stock-Yogo critical values
```
**Suggested fix**: Always extract F from the IV model object, not a separate
first-stage regression. Re-run after adding any covariates.

---

### Fatal: No clustering — standard errors are wrong for school data
**Category**: Statistical Threats
**Explanation**: Every model uses `vcov = "HC1"`, which treats all 3,000
observations as independent. Students in the same school share unobserved
characteristics (teacher quality, peer effects, school culture), creating
within-school residual correlation. HC1 underestimates standard errors; in
education data, clustering at school level typically inflates SEs by 20–60%. A
result significant at p=0.03 with HC1 can easily become p=0.12 after clustering.
This can change the headline result.
**Diagnostic**: Replace `vcov = "HC1"` with `cluster = ~school_id` in every
feols call. If no school ID exists, use district or geographic cluster.
```r
# All models should use:
iv_model   <- feols(test_score ~ 1 | tutoring ~ distance,
                    data = df, cluster = ~school_id)
first_stage <- feols(tutoring ~ distance,
                     data = df, cluster = ~school_id)
```
**Suggested fix**: Add school/district cluster variable to the dataset before
running. If unavailable, acknowledge it explicitly as a limitation and explain
why HC1 is being used.

---

### Serious: Exclusion restriction — endogenous center placement not flagged
**Category**: Assumption Violations (Exclusion Restriction)
**Explanation**: The method skill correctly flagged the SES/urban backdoor but
missed the structural source of that correlation: tutoring centers open where
academic demand is high, which is where motivated families live. Distance is
partially a function of unobserved academic motivation — the same latent variable
driving test scores. Controlling for income and urbanicity helps but does not
close this path; the market placement mechanism responds to motivation *conditional*
on SES.
**Diagnostic**: Use historical center locations if available (centers that
predated the students' enrollment). Run Conley bounds to quantify how much direct
effect of distance would flip the result:
```r
library(ivmodel)
iv_fit <- ivmodel(Y = df$test_score, D = df$tutoring, Z = df$distance)
# Anderson-Rubin robust CI — valid even with weak instruments and
# approximate exclusion violations
AR.test(iv_fit, beta0 = 0)
```
**Suggested fix**: Ask whether historical center locations (pre-dating the sample)
are available. If not, document the endogenous placement threat explicitly and
run Conley bounds sensitivity.

---

### Serious: Balance check silently skips with no covariates
**Category**: Assumption Violations (Independence)
**Explanation**: The balance check uses placeholder variable names `c("X1", "X2",
"X3")`. When none exist in `df`, the `all(...%in% names(df))` condition is FALSE
and the block silently skips — no output, no warning. A user running this code
sees nothing and may assume the check passed.
**Diagnostic**:
```r
if (!all(balance_vars %in% names(df))) {
  warning(paste("Balance check skipped — these variables not in df:",
                paste(balance_vars[!balance_vars %in% names(df)],
                      collapse = ", "),
                "\nReplace with real covariate names before running."))
}
```
**Suggested fix**: Add the guard above. More importantly, obtain income,
urbanicity, and school quality variables before running the analysis — the
independence assumption cannot be evaluated without them.

---

### Serious: Binary first stage uses OLS without acknowledgment
**Category**: Assumption Violations (Relevance) / Statistical Threats
**Explanation**: Tutoring is binary. The first stage is a linear probability
model (LPM) — OLS on a 0/1 outcome. Predicted probabilities can fall outside
[0,1]; errors are heteroskedastic by construction. This is common practice but
should be checked and acknowledged.
**Diagnostic**:
```r
fitted_probs <- fitted(first_stage)
cat("Predicted probs outside [0,1]:",
    sum(fitted_probs < 0 | fitted_probs > 1), "/", nrow(df), "\n")
```
If >5% are out of bounds, use the control function approach with a probit
first stage:
```r
probit_fs <- glm(tutoring ~ distance, data = df, family = binomial("probit"))
df$cf_resid <- residuals(probit_fs, type = "response")
cf_model <- feols(test_score ~ tutoring + cf_resid, data = df, cluster = ~school_id)
# Coefficient on cf_resid: test for endogeneity
```
**Suggested fix**: Check the share of out-of-bounds predictions. Report LPM
limitation. If substantial, switch to the control function approach.

---

### Minor: Missing data not addressed
**Category**: Data Threats
**Explanation**: No missing data check is included. In school data, non-response
is higher for students who move or drop out — precisely those at the margin of
tutoring take-up. If missingness correlates with distance, estimates are biased.
**Diagnostic**:
```r
df$any_missing <- rowSums(is.na(df)) > 0
miss_test <- feols(any_missing ~ distance, data = df, vcov = "HC1")
summary(miss_test)
# Significant = selective missingness tied to instrument
```
**Suggested fix**: Run the above before the main analysis. If missingness is
non-random with respect to the instrument, consider multiple imputation or
at minimum document the pattern.

---

### Minor: LATE complier population understated
**Category**: External Validity
**Explanation**: The compliers for distance-as-instrument are students for whom
logistics is the binding constraint — likely middle-SES, moderately motivated
students. This LATE is well-suited for evaluating "should we build more tutoring
centers?" but is not informative about "should we subsidize tutoring costs?"
(different complier population). The method skill mentions LATE but doesn't map it
to the specific policy question.
**Suggested fix**: State explicitly which policy question the LATE answers, and
which it doesn't. If the policy is demand-side (subsidies), note that a separate
identification strategy with a cost-based instrument would be needed.

---

### Minor: Online tutoring as temporal validity threat
**Category**: External Validity / Assumption Violations (Monotonicity)
**Explanation**: If the data is post-2020, online tutoring penetration is
non-trivial. Distance to a physical center becomes a weaker instrument (weakening
relevance) and may create non-monotonicity for rural students who switch to online
when physical options are far (potential defiers).
**Suggested fix**: Confirm data vintage. If post-2020, add a check for whether
distance effect varies by urban/rural subgroup and whether the first stage holds
in rural areas specifically.

## Recommendations (priority order)

1. **Fix clustering** — swap `vcov = "HC1"` for `cluster = ~school_id` in all
   models. Re-run and re-check significance. This is non-negotiable for education
   data.
2. **Fix F-statistic extraction** — use `fitstat(iv_model, "ivf")`, not
   `fitstat(first_stage, "f")`. Re-evaluate instrument strength.
3. **Obtain covariates** — income, urbanicity, school quality. Without them, you
   cannot run the balance check, the exclusion restriction is hard to defend, and
   the IV estimate absorbs uncontrolled confounders.
4. **Check for historical center locations** — if available, switch to
   pre-sample distance as the instrument. This directly addresses endogenous
   placement.
5. **Check LPM fit** — run the out-of-bounds prediction diagnostic. If material,
   switch to the control function approach.
6. **Run Conley bounds** — quantify how robust the exclusion restriction needs to
   be. Even if you can't prove it, show the result survives small violations.
7. **Add missing data audit** — run before analysis, not after.
```

---

Two lessons to add to `references/lessons.md` — the method skill missed both:

```markdown
### IV: Wrong F-statistic object in fixest
**Layer**: Auditor
**Trigger**: IV analysis using fixest with manual first-stage verification
**Mistake**: Extracted F-statistic via `fitstat(first_stage, "f")` on a separate
OLS regression instead of `fitstat(iv_model, "ivf")` on the IV model object.
These diverge when controls are present; the standalone version is optimistic.
**Rule**: Always extract the IV-effective F from the IV model object using
`fitstat(iv_model, "ivf")`. Never use the standalone first-stage F for reporting.
**Source**: Auditor finding, 2026-04-03

### IV: Clustering omitted in school-level data
**Layer**: Auditor
**Trigger**: IV analysis with student-level outcome data, no clustering specified
**Mistake**: Used `vcov = "HC1"` throughout without clustering at school or
district level. In nested education data this understates standard errors by
20–60%, potentially changing headline results.
**Rule**: Default to `cluster = ~school_id` (or district) for any student-level
analysis. If no clustering variable exists, flag it explicitly — never silently
use HC1 as a substitute.
**Source**: Auditor finding, 2026-04-03
```

---

**Bottom line**: Don't interpret the IV estimate yet. The two fatal issues (wrong SEs, wrong F-stat) need to be fixed first — your significance levels and instrument strength assessment are both potentially wrong. Once you have school IDs for clustering and the corrected F-stat, come back and we can do Stage 5 interpretation on solid ground.

</details>

### workflow_planner_to_did

**Run 1**: {"handoff_quality": 0.8, "questions_passed": 4, "questions_total": 5}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

--- Step 1 ---
Based on what you've described, this is a **difference-in-differences (DiD)** problem — and a well-structured one. You have many units, a solid pre-treatment window (~15 months before January), and a clean control group. Let me ask a couple of questions to nail down the exact variant.

**1. Did all 200 treated stores go live at the same time in January, or did the rollout happen at different weeks/dates across stores?**

- Same date for all → classic two-way fixed effects (TWFE), simplest and cleanest
- Different dates → staggered DiD, which requires a more careful estimator (Callaway-Sant'Anna or Sun-Abraham) to avoid biased estimates

**2. How were the 200 stores selected for the first wave?** For example:
- Randomly chosen
- Largest/highest-volume stores first
- Specific regions
- Stores that were struggling

This matters because DiD's key assumption is **parallel trends** — that treated and control stores would have followed similar sales trajectories without the intervention. If the first wave was systematically different (e.g., all big-city stores), we'd need to verify that pre-treatment trends were actually parallel, or consider reweighting.

---

While you answer, here's a preview of what the plan will look like either way:

| Your answer | Recommended path |
|---|---|
| Same rollout date + random/near-random selection | Classic TWFE with `fixest` — strong design |
| Same rollout date + non-random selection | TWFE + pre-trends event study — need to verify parallel trends carefully |
| Staggered rollout dates | Callaway-Sant'Anna (`did` package) — avoids forbidden comparisons |

--- Step 2 ---
I'll proceed, making two working assumptions I'll flag clearly:

> **Assumed**: All 200 stores launched in **January on the same date** (classic TWFE, not staggered). Store selection was **non-random** — so I'll treat parallel trends as something to verify rigorously, not assumed away. Using **R**. Correct any of these and I'll adjust the code.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through each one for your specific context — retail stores, new checkout feature, sales outcome.

---

### 1. Parallel Trends ⚠️ *Verify before trusting results*

**What it means here**: Without the new checkout feature, your 200 treated stores and the control stores would have had sales growing (or declining) at the same rate over time. The groups can differ in *level* — maybe treated stores are bigger — but their *trajectory* must be the same.

**Why it matters**: If treated stores were already on a growth path before January (say, they're in faster-growing markets), DiD will mistakenly credit the checkout feature for a trend that was already there.

**How to test it**: We'll run an event study — plotting the treatment effect at each month relative to January. The pre-January coefficients should hover near zero with no trend. We'll also run a joint significance test on those pre-treatment coefficients.

**Your risk**: Moderate-to-high if stores were selected on performance or region. With 15 months of pre-data, you're in good shape to *detect* a violation — that's better than having only 2-3 months and being blind to it.

---

### 2. No Anticipation ✅ *Likely fine, but worth checking*

**What it means here**: Stores (or customers) didn't change behavior *before* January because they knew the new feature was coming. For example, if staff were trained in December and already changing checkout behavior, the "pre-treatment" period is contaminated.

**Why it matters**: If anticipation exists, the t = -1 or t = -2 event study coefficients will be non-zero in the *same direction* as the post-treatment effect — a telltale ramp-up pattern.

**Your risk**: Low, unless the feature rollout was publicly announced or stores received advance notice that changed their operations. The event study will show this if it exists.

---

### 3. Stable Composition ✅ *Usually fine for retail stores*

**What it means here**: The same stores are in your dataset before and after January. Stores aren't entering the treated group because they're doing well, or dropping out because they closed.

**Why it matters**: If poorly-performing stores closed *because of* (or *despite*) the feature, you'd only observe the survivors — biasing results upward.

**How to test it**: Check that your panel is balanced — every store has observations in every month. We'll add a quick balance check to the code.

---

### 4. No Spillovers (SUTVA) ⚠️ *Flag if stores share customers*

**What it means here**: A customer who shops at a treated store doesn't *stop* shopping at a control store because of it. If treated and control stores compete in the same market, the checkout improvement might steal customers from control stores — making control stores look worse, and inflating your estimate.

**Why it matters**: This would make the treatment effect look larger than it really is — you'd be measuring "treated stores gain + control stores lose" not just "treated stores gain."

**Your risk**: Depends on store geography. If treated and control stores are in different cities, you're probably fine. If they're in the same malls, flag this as a concern.

**This assumption is untestable** in the strict sense, but we can do a sanity check: look at whether control stores near treated stores behave differently from control stores far away.

---

### 5. Correct Functional Form ✅ *Not a concern for single-date rollout*

**What it means here**: Since all 200 stores launched on the same date, standard TWFE is unbiased. The Callaway-Sant'Anna / Sun-Abraham concerns only apply to *staggered* rollouts where different cohorts are treated at different times.

---

### Assumption Summary

| Assumption | Status | Action |
|---|---|---|
| Parallel trends | ⚠️ Verify | Event study + pre-trends joint test |
| No anticipation | ✅ Likely fine | Check t = -2, t = -3 coefficients |
| Stable composition | ✅ Likely fine | Balanced panel check |
| No spillovers | ⚠️ Flag if stores compete | Proximity check if data available |
| Functional form | ✅ Not a concern | Single date = standard TWFE is fine |

No fatal violations up front — but parallel trends needs to be earned with the data, not assumed. Let's build that test into the code.

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

Complete analysis in R. Adapt `outcome`, `unit`, `time`, `treated`, and `first_treat` to your actual column names.

```r
# ============================================================
# DiD Analysis: New Checkout Feature → Store Sales
# Method: Classic TWFE + Event Study (fixest)
# ============================================================

library(tidyverse)
library(fixest)
library(modelsummary)

# ── 0. Load your data ────────────────────────────────────────
# Expected columns:
#   unit        — store ID (integer or character)
#   time        — month (integer, e.g. 1 = Jan 2024, or a date)
#   treated     — 1 if store is in the treated group, 0 if control
#   post        — 1 if time >= treatment date, 0 otherwise
#   outcome     — sales metric (e.g., weekly revenue, transaction count)
#   treat_date  — the treatment period for treated stores (same value for all)
#                 0 or NA for never-treated control stores (required for sunab)

# df <- read_csv("your_data.csv")

# ── 1. Data integrity checks ─────────────────────────────────
cat("=== Panel Balance ===\n")
balance <- df |>
  group_by(unit) |>
  summarise(
    n_periods  = n_distinct(time),
    treated    = max(treated),
    .groups    = "drop"
  ) |>
  group_by(treated) |>
  summarise(
    n_stores       = n(),
    mean_periods   = mean(n_periods),
    fully_observed = sum(n_periods == max(df |> pull(time) |> n_distinct())),
    .groups        = "drop"
  )
print(balance)
# What to look for: treated and control groups should have similar
# numbers of fully-observed stores. Large gaps = attrition risk.

# ── 2. Main TWFE Estimate ────────────────────────────────────
# Interaction treated:post is the DiD coefficient.
# Unit FE absorbs all time-invariant store characteristics (size, location, etc.)
# Time FE absorbs economy-wide shocks hitting all stores equally.
# NOTE: Do NOT include a standalone `treated` dummy — unit FE already absorbs it.

model_main <- feols(
  outcome ~ treated:post | unit + time,
  data    = df,
  cluster = ~unit      # cluster SEs at store level (standard for DiD)
)

summary(model_main)
# Key output:
#   treated:post — the ATT (average treatment effect on the treated)
#   Look at: coefficient, SE, 95% CI, and p-value

# Clean results table
modelsummary(
  model_main,
  stars      = TRUE,
  gof_map    = c("nobs", "r.squared"),
  title      = "DiD: Effect of Checkout Feature on Sales"
)

# ── 3. Effect Size with 95% CI ───────────────────────────────
att   <- coef(model_main)["treated:post"]
ci    <- confint(model_main)["treated:post", ]
se    <- se(model_main)["treated:post"]

cat(sprintf(
  "\nATT: %.4f  |  SE: %.4f  |  95%% CI: [%.4f, %.4f]\n",
  att, se, ci[1], ci[2]
))

# If outcome is log(sales), interpret as: exp(att) - 1 ≈ % change
# If outcome is raw sales: ATT is the absolute dollar / unit lift

# ── 4. Event Study (Pre-Trends + Dynamic Effects) ────────────
# This is your most important diagnostic. Pre-treatment coefficients
# should be statistically indistinguishable from zero.
# Reference period = -1 (the month immediately before launch).

df <- df |>
  mutate(time_to_treat = time - treat_date)  # months relative to launch
                                              # adjust if treat_date is in date format

event_study <- feols(
  outcome ~ i(time_to_treat, ref = -1) | unit + time,
  data    = df,
  cluster = ~unit
)

# Visual diagnostic
iplot(
  event_study,
  main  = "Event Study: Sales Around Checkout Feature Launch",
  xlab  = "Months Relative to Launch (0 = January)",
  ylab  = "Estimated Effect on Sales",
  col   = c("steelblue", "steelblue"),
  pt.pch = 19
)
abline(h = 0, lty = 2, col = "gray50")
abline(v = -0.5, lty = 3, col = "tomato", lwd = 1.5)

# What to look for:
#   Pre-treatment (x < 0): coefficients should be near zero, no trend
#   Post-treatment (x >= 0): should show the effect, ideally stable or growing
#   A pre-trend sloping up/down = parallel trends violation

# ── 5. Joint Test of Pre-Treatment Coefficients ──────────────
# H0: all pre-treatment event study coefficients = 0
# If p < 0.05, you have a pre-trends problem.

pre_names <- grep("time_to_treat::-", names(coef(event_study)), value = TRUE)
cat("\n=== Pre-Trends Joint Test ===\n")
wald(event_study, keep = pre_names)
# p > 0.05 → no statistical evidence against parallel trends
# p < 0.05 → pre-trends detected; results should be interpreted with caution

# ── 6. Results Summary ───────────────────────────────────────
cat("\n========================================\n")
cat("RESULTS SUMMARY\n")
cat("========================================\n")
cat(sprintf("Treated stores:   %d\n", df |> filter(treated == 1) |> pull(unit) |> n_distinct()))
cat(sprintf("Control stores:   %d\n", df |> filter(treated == 0) |> pull(unit) |> n_distinct()))
cat(sprintf("Pre-periods:      %d months\n", df |> filter(post == 0) |> pull(time) |> n_distinct()))
cat(sprintf("Post-periods:     %d months\n", df |> filter(post == 1) |> pull(time) |> n_distinct()))
cat(sprintf("\nEstimated ATT:    %.4f\n", att))
cat(sprintf("95%% CI:          [%.4f, %.4f]\n", ci[1], ci[2]))
cat(sprintf("p-value:          %.4f\n", pvalue(model_main)["treated:post"]))
cat("========================================\n")
```

---

## What to look for when you run this

| Output | What it tells you |
|---|---|
| `treated:post` coefficient | The average sales lift from the new feature |
| Pre-treatment event study coefficients near zero | Parallel trends holds ✅ |
| Pre-treatment coefficients trending up/down | Parallel trends violated 🚨 |
| Joint pre-test p > 0.05 | No statistical evidence of pre-trends ✅ |
| Post-treatment coefficients growing over time | Effect is building — customers warming up to feature |
| Post-treatment coefficients shrinking | Novelty effect fading |

---

Once you run this, share the output (especially the event study plot and the pre-trends test p-value) and I'll move to **Stage 4: Falsification checks** — including a placebo test using a fake treatment date in your pre-period.

</details>

### workflow_planner_to_matching

**Run 1**: {"handoff_quality": 0.8, "questions_passed": 4, "questions_total": 5}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

--- Step 1 ---
Based on what you've described, this is a **matching / doubly-robust (observational)** problem — specifically, you'll want inverse probability weighting (IPW) or a doubly-robust estimator like AIPW.

But before we go further, I want to flag a concern that shapes everything:

---

**🚨 Self-selection is the central threat here**

"Opted in" means customers chose the premium service themselves. That choice almost certainly correlates with things that also drive purchase frequency — engagement level, income, how much they already liked the product. Your rich behavioral data may capture a lot of this, but *may not capture all of it*.

This puts you in the **weakest identification strategy** available: selection on observables / conditional independence. The estimate is only valid if your covariates include *everything* that drives both opt-in and purchase frequency. That's a strong claim.

I want to make sure you go in with clear eyes on that.

---

A few questions to sharpen the plan:

**1. What drives opt-in?** Do you know the mechanism — was it shown to everyone and some clicked "yes"? Targeted to certain segments? Paid? This tells us how much selection bias to expect and which covariates matter most.

**2. What's in your behavioral data?** Specifically: do you have *pre-treatment* purchase frequency (before they opted in)? That's the single most important covariate — it captures baseline engagement directly.

**3. What's your estimand?** Do you want:
- **ATT** — effect on customers who *did* opt in (most defensible, since you're estimating within the treated group)
- **ATE** — effect for the average customer (requires more extrapolation)

**4. Any natural cutoffs or nudges?** For instance, was there a price threshold, eligibility score, or promotional push that made some people much more likely to opt in than others? That could unlock a stronger strategy (RDD or IV).

--- Step 2 ---
I don't have your answers to questions 1–4 yet, which affect both the assumption walkthrough and the code. But I can walk the assumptions now — they're the same regardless — and I'll generate code with placeholder variable names you can swap in. I'll default to **R** and **ATT** (most defensible for an opt-in design); tell me if you want Python or ATE and I'll regenerate.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

I'll walk through each one for your specific setup — customers self-selecting into a premium service, outcome is purchase frequency.

---

### Assumption 1 · Conditional Independence (Unconfoundedness)

**What it means for you:** After conditioning on your behavioral covariates, opt-in is "as good as random" — there's no remaining variable that drove both the decision to opt in *and* purchase frequency. In plain terms: you've measured everything that matters about why a customer chose the premium service.

**Why it matters:** This is the load-bearing assumption for the entire analysis. If a motivated, high-engagement customer is more likely to both opt in *and* buy more — and you haven't fully captured "engagement level" in your covariates — your estimate of the premium service's effect will be inflated. You'll be partly estimating the "engaged customers buy more" effect, not the "premium service causes buying more" effect.

**Is it plausible?** That depends heavily on what's in your behavioral data. The single most important covariate is **pre-treatment purchase frequency** — it directly captures baseline engagement. If you have that, plus tenure, browsing/session behavior, category affinity, and demographic proxies, the assumption becomes more defensible. If you're missing any of those, it weakens.

⚠️ **This is NOT testable** — you can never rule out an unmeasured confounder. We'll run sensitivity analysis (Rosenbaum bounds / E-value) in Stage 4 to quantify how strong an unmeasured confounder would need to be to overturn your result.

**Concern level:** 🚨 High — self-selection on motivation/engagement is the classic threat here. Proceed, but this caveat must travel all the way into the final interpretation.

---

### Assumption 2 · Overlap / Positivity

**What it means for you:** Every customer — opted-in and not — must have had a nonzero probability of going either way. If there's a segment that *always* opts in (say, power users with engagement score = max), there's no comparable control group for them, and matching breaks down.

**Why it matters:** Without overlap, matching or weighting is forced to extrapolate into covariate space where no comparable units exist. IPW weights for those units become extreme (huge numerically), which destabilizes the entire estimate. A few extreme weights can dominate your weighted regression.

**Is it plausible?** Likely yes for most customers, but worth checking around the edges — high-engagement segments may have near-deterministic opt-in. We'll look at the propensity score overlap histogram before trusting any estimates.

**Testable:** ✅ Yes — we'll plot propensity score distributions for opted-in vs. not, and check for regions with no overlap.

**Concern level:** ⚠️ Medium — watch for high-engagement users who almost universally opted in.

---

### Assumption 3 · SUTVA (No Interference)

**What it means for you:** One customer's opt-in decision doesn't change another customer's purchase frequency. The premium service given to Customer A doesn't spill over to Customer B.

**Why it matters:** If premium members share referral codes, or if the premium tier creates network effects (e.g., premium members recruit friends who then buy more), then the "control" group's outcomes are contaminated. Your estimate conflates the direct effect of the service with indirect network effects.

**Is it plausible?** Generally yes for individual purchase behavior — customers are buying independently. The main exception would be referral programs tied to the premium tier. Do you have one of those?

**Testable:** Partially — if you have social/referral network data, you could test whether control users connected to treated users behave differently.

**Concern level:** 🟢 Low, unless referral/social features are built into the premium tier.

---

### Assumption 4 · Correct Model Specification (and why DR helps)

**What it means for you:** The propensity score model (what predicts opt-in) or the outcome model (what predicts purchase frequency) needs to be correctly specified. If you use logistic regression but the true opt-in propensity is nonlinear, the weights will be off, and you'll have residual confounding even after weighting.

**Why it matters:** A misspecified propensity score model doesn't fully remove confounding — some covariate imbalance will remain after weighting. A misspecified outcome model will give biased estimates even with perfect weights.

**The doubly-robust safety net:** This is exactly why I'm recommending **AIPW (Augmented IPW)** rather than plain IPW. Doubly-robust estimation combines *both* a propensity score model *and* an outcome model. The estimate is consistent if **either** model is correctly specified — you get two chances. It's more robust to partial misspecification than either model alone.

**Testable:** ✅ Yes — we check covariate balance (standardized mean differences) after weighting. If SMDs are below 0.1 for all covariates, the propensity model was adequate. We'll visualize this with a love plot.

**Concern level:** ⚠️ Medium — with self-selection, the propensity model needs to be flexible. I'll use gradient boosting for both models (more robust than logistic regression).

---

### Assumption Summary

| Assumption | Testable? | Concern | Status |
|---|---|---|---|
| Conditional independence | ❌ No | 🚨 High | Plausible *if* pre-treatment purchase frequency + full behavioral history are in covariates. Unmeasured engagement motivation is the key threat. |
| Overlap / positivity | ✅ Yes | ⚠️ Medium | Check high-engagement segments. Will inspect PS distribution before proceeding. |
| SUTVA / no interference | ❌ Partial | 🟢 Low | Acceptable unless premium tier includes referral mechanics. |
| Correct specification | ✅ Partial (via balance) | ⚠️ Medium | Mitigated by using doubly-robust estimation + flexible ML models. |

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

I'm using `MatchIt` + `cobalt` for diagnostics, and `AIPW` from the `AIPW` package for the doubly-robust estimate. Swap the variable names marked with `# ← YOUR DATA` for your actual column names.

```r
# ============================================================
# AIPW (Doubly-Robust) Analysis
# Treatment: opted into premium service
# Outcome:   purchase frequency (post-treatment)
# Estimand:  ATT
# ============================================================

library(tidyverse)
library(MatchIt)
library(cobalt)
library(AIPW)
library(sl3)          # SuperLearner backend for AIPW
library(ggplot2)

# ---- 0. Load data ------------------------------------------
# df must have:
#   treatment     : 1 = opted into premium, 0 = did not
#   outcome       : post-treatment purchase frequency
#   pre_freq      : pre-treatment purchase frequency  ← most important
#   tenure        : account age in days (or months)
#   engagement    : e.g. session frequency, pages viewed
#   [other covariates]

df <- read_csv("your_data.csv")     # ← YOUR DATA

treatment_var <- "treatment"        # ← YOUR DATA
outcome_var   <- "outcome"          # ← YOUR DATA
covariates    <- c(                 # ← YOUR DATA
  "pre_freq",
  "tenure",
  "engagement_score",
  "age",
  "income_proxy"
)

# ---- 1. Overlap check: BEFORE any estimation ---------------
# Estimate propensity scores with logistic regression first
ps_formula <- as.formula(
  paste(treatment_var, "~", paste(covariates, collapse = " + "))
)

ps_model <- glm(ps_formula, data = df, family = binomial)
df$ps    <- predict(ps_model, type = "response")

# Overlap histogram — inspect this carefully before proceeding
ggplot(df, aes(x = ps, fill = factor(.data[[treatment_var]]))) +
  geom_histogram(alpha = 0.55, position = "identity", bins = 60) +
  scale_fill_manual(values = c("#4472C4", "#ED7D31"),
                    labels = c("Control (not opted in)", "Treated (opted in)")) +
  labs(
    title    = "Propensity Score Overlap",
    subtitle = "Inspect for separation — poor overlap = unreliable estimates",
    x = "Propensity Score", y = "Count", fill = NULL
  ) +
  theme_minimal(base_size = 13)

# Numeric summary
cat("── Propensity score summary ──────────────────────\n")
cat("Treated:\n"); print(summary(df$ps[df[[treatment_var]] == 1]))
cat("Control:\n"); print(summary(df$ps[df[[treatment_var]] == 0]))
cat(sprintf("\nFraction with PS < 0.05 : %.3f\n", mean(df$ps < 0.05)))
cat(sprintf("Fraction with PS > 0.95 : %.3f\n", mean(df$ps > 0.95)))

# ⚠️  If treated PS and control PS distributions barely overlap,
#     STOP and discuss with the team before proceeding.

# ---- 2. Covariate balance check (via MatchIt diagnostics) --
# We use MatchIt with method = NULL just for the balance report —
# no matching is performed, this is purely diagnostic.
m_null <- matchit(ps_formula, data = df,
                  method = NULL, estimand = "ATT")

cat("\n── Raw balance (before any adjustment) ────────────\n")
print(bal.tab(m_null, thresholds = c(m = 0.1)))

love.plot(m_null,
          thresholds  = c(m = 0.1),
          title       = "Covariate Balance — Unadjusted",
          colors      = c("#4472C4", "#ED7D31"),
          shapes      = c("circle", "triangle"),
          sample.names = c("Control", "Treated"))

# ---- 3. AIPW estimation ------------------------------------
# Doubly-robust: combines IPW weights with outcome model.
# Consistent if EITHER the PS model OR the outcome model is correct.

# Build SuperLearner libraries (flexible, non-parametric)
lrnr_stack <- make_learner_stack(
  "Lrnr_glm",
  "Lrnr_ranger",      # random forest
  "Lrnr_xgboost"      # gradient boosting
)

set.seed(42)

aipw_fit <- AIPW$new(
  Y          = df[[outcome_var]],
  A          = df[[treatment_var]],
  W          = df[, covariates],
  Q.SL.library = lrnr_stack,
  g.SL.library = lrnr_stack,
  k_split    = 5,       # 5-fold cross-fitting
  verbose    = TRUE
)

aipw_fit$fit()
aipw_fit$summary(g.bound = 0.025)  # trim extreme PS weights at 2.5%

# Extract ATT estimate
results <- aipw_fit$result
cat("\n── AIPW Estimate (ATT) ─────────────────────────────\n")
cat(sprintf("ATT estimate : %.4f\n", results$ATT_estimates["Risk_difference",  "Estimate"]))
cat(sprintf("95%% CI       : [%.4f, %.4f]\n",
            results$ATT_estimates["Risk_difference", "2.5%"],
            results$ATT_estimates["Risk_difference", "97.5%"]))

# ---- 4. Weighted balance check (post-adjustment) -----------
# Verify that IPW weighting achieved balance
df$ipw_weight <- ifelse(
  df[[treatment_var]] == 1,
  1,
  df$ps / (1 - df$ps)
)

m_weighted <- weightit(
  ps_formula,
  data    = df,
  method  = "ps",
  estimand = "ATT"
)

cat("\n── Balance after IPW weighting ─────────────────────\n")
print(bal.tab(m_weighted, thresholds = c(m = 0.1)))

love.plot(m_weighted,
          thresholds  = c(m = 0.1),
          title       = "Covariate Balance — Before vs After IPW",
          colors      = c("#4472C4", "#ED7D31"),
          shapes      = c("circle filled", "triangle filled"),
          sample.names = c("Unadjusted", "IPW Weighted"))
```

> **Before trusting any estimate**: check the overlap plot from Step 1, and confirm that the love plot from Step 4 shows SMDs below 0.1 for all covariates. If any SMD exceeds 0.25, the propensity model needs to be re-specified.

---

## Stage 4: Robustness Checks

Three checks I'd recommend for this design:

```r
# ============================================================
# Robustness Check 1: E-value (sensitivity to unmeasured confounding)
# ============================================================
# How strong would an unmeasured confounder need to be to explain
# away the estimated ATT?

library(EValue)

# Convert your ATT to a risk ratio first (or use the additive E-value)
# If ATT is on a count/rate scale, standardize by control mean:
control_mean <- mean(df[[outcome_var]][df[[treatment_var]] == 0])
ATT_point <- results$ATT_estimates["Risk_difference", "Estimate"]
ATT_lo    <- results$ATT_estimates["Risk_difference", "2.5%"]

# Approximate RR = 1 + (ATT / control_mean)
RR_est <- 1 + ATT_point / control_mean
RR_lo  <- 1 + ATT_lo    / control_mean

cat("\n── E-value (unmeasured confounding sensitivity) ────\n")
evalues.RR(est = RR_est, lo = RR_lo)

# Interpretation: the E-value tells you the minimum strength
# (on the RR scale) that an unmeasured confounder would need to
# have — with BOTH treatment assignment AND the outcome — to
# fully explain away the result. A small E-value = fragile result.

# ============================================================
# Robustness Check 2: Propensity score trimming
# ============================================================
# Restrict to units with PS in [0.05, 0.95] — excludes extremes
# where overlap is poorest. If results change a lot, overlap was
# doing work and the untrimmed estimate is unreliable.

df_trimmed <- df %>% filter(ps >= 0.05 & ps <= 0.95)
cat(sprintf("\nTrimmed sample: %d of %d units retained (%.1f%%)\n",
            nrow(df_trimmed), nrow(df), 100 * nrow(df_trimmed) / nrow(df)))

aipw_trimmed <- AIPW$new(
  Y          = df_trimmed[[outcome_var]],
  A          = df_trimmed[[treatment_var]],
  W          = df_trimmed[, covariates],
  Q.SL.library = lrnr_stack,
  g.SL.library = lrnr_stack,
  k_split    = 5,
  verbose    = FALSE
)
aipw_trimmed$fit()
aipw_trimmed$summary(g.bound = 0.025)

cat("\n── Trimmed sample estimate ──────────────────────────\n")
res_trimmed <- aipw_trimmed$result
cat(sprintf("ATT (trimmed) : %.4f\n",
            res_trimmed$ATT_estimates["Risk_difference", "Estimate"]))
cat(sprintf("95%% CI        : [%.4f, %.4f]\n",
            res_trimmed$ATT_estimates["Risk_difference", "2.5%"],
            res_trimmed$ATT_estimates["Risk_difference", "97.5%"]))

# ============================================================
# Robustness Check 3: Placebo outcome (pre-treatment period)
# ============================================================
# Re-run the ENTIRE analysis but replace the outcome with
# purchase frequency from a period BEFORE the premium service
# launched (or before opt-in was possible). The "effect" should
# be zero — finding one suggests residual confounding.

# Requires: df$pre_freq_placebo = purchase freq in pre-period

if ("pre_freq_placebo" %in% names(df)) {
  aipw_placebo <- AIPW$new(
    Y          = df[["pre_freq_placebo"]],     # ← placebo outcome
    A          = df[[treatment_var]],
    W          = df[, covariates],
    Q.SL.library = lrnr_stack,
    g.SL.library = lrnr_stack,
    k_split    = 5,
    verbose    = FALSE
  )
  aipw_placebo$fit()
  aipw_placebo$summary()

  cat("\n── Placebo outcome check ────────────────────────────\n")
  cat("Expected: near-zero estimate (this period predates treatment)\n")
  cat("Finding a large effect = residual confounding\n")
  res_placebo <- aipw_placebo$result
  cat(sprintf("Placebo ATT : %.4f\n",
              res_placebo$ATT_estimates["Risk_difference", "Estimate"]))
} else {
  cat("\n⚠️  No pre_freq_placebo column found. If you have a pre-treatment\n")
  cat("   purchase frequency column from a period before opt-in was available,\n")
  cat("   add it as the outcome here. This is the most valuable robustness check.\n")
}
```

## Verification Gate

Before I write up the interpretation, I need you to confirm:

- [ ] Overlap plot ran — describe what you see (good overlap vs. separation)
- [ ] Love plot ran — are all SMDs below 0.1 after weighting?
- [ ] AIPW estimate — what's the point estimate and CI?
- [ ] At least one robustness check ran — does the result hold?

Share the outputs and I'll write the plain-language interpretation with appropriate caveats. If anything looks off (separation in the PS plot, large SMDs, big shift after trimming), we should discuss before calling it a result.
